# The Color It Answers To

# Chapter 1

Patent Row smelled the way a liar's mouth smelled after a hot day: sweet at first, then yeasty, then rotten once you got close enough to know better. The digesters had been running hard since noon. By late afternoon every wall on the avenue sweated through its posters. New handbills curled over old ones. Blue paint bled into red. Paste shone in the heat. The whole street looked as if it had been licked and left out.

Silas Vale liked it best when it looked that way.

He stood on a tea crate outside a rented patent stall, a mahlstick under one arm and a lettering brush between nicotine-yellowed fingers, while the crowd considered disappointing him on purpose. They had that look. Patent Row wore it most days now. Too many inventors. Too many miracle glazes, steam salves, eel tonics, glow powders, rat jars, widow's corset stays, and bridge bolts said to laugh at rust. Every week wanted a sensation. Every week half the street pretended it had not been fooled the week before.

The woman paying him for today's miracle held a squat tin in both hands as if it might leak through the seams by shame alone. Mrs. Clary Dobb had glue blisters along the heels of her thumbs from pasting her own notices and a clove stem tucked in one corner of her mouth because the digester smell made her stomach pitch. Her best hat had a wilted jet bead fringe that clicked when she nodded.

"Mr. Vale," she said for the third time, "I paid for memorable. I did not pay for vulgar."

Silas did not look down at her. Looking down made clients think they had him. He looked past her into the crowd instead, where two ferry clerks in rolled sleeves had stopped to watch, and a pair of boardinghouse girls were already grinning behind their hands, and a newsboy had tucked his remaining sheets under one arm so he would not miss the fall if there was one.

"Those are neighboring districts, Mrs. Dobb," he said. "There's no clean road from one to the other on this street."

The newsboy barked a laugh. One of the ferry clerks called, "He's right there."

Clary Dobb's mouth flattened around the clove. "It's for brass polish."

"Everything's for brass polish until it isn't." He crouched, held out his free hand, and she surrendered the tin. The paste inside was green-brown and glossy as river scum under moonlight. It smelled of oil, salt, and something sharp enough to strip skin if a fool kept his hand in it. Useful. Maybe. Not his problem. His work was not truth. His work was the half second before a stranger reached for a purse because a word had made the object in front of them feel inevitable.

He dipped two fingers in the polish and turned toward the crowd with the smear glistening on his skin.

"Look at this," he said. "Look honest."

"Honest?" said one of the boardinghouse girls. She was carrying a bundle of cuffs and had a damp ring at the throat of her cheap blue dress. "On Patent Row?"

"You can go to church for the other thing." He pointed with the glistening fingers at a tarnished handbell hanging beside the stall. "What did she call it before she paid me?"

Clary Dobb answered with the pained obedience of a woman saying her own weak child's name in public. "Dobb's Algal Brass Restorative."

The crowd groaned on schedule.

Silas let them. He could feel the moment ripening. It came up through a crowd the way heat came up off brick: first a shimmer, then a pressure. He knew the exact instant their meanness became hunger. That was his actual talent. The brush mattered. The ear mattered more.

He stepped off the crate, took the handbell off its hook, and rubbed the paste across the dull metal with the heel of his palm. The green-brown sludge turned black for a breath, then thinned under his pressure and woke a bright gold underneath. The ferry clerks leaned in. Even Clary Dobb forgot the clove for a second and let it rest in the wet corner of her lip.

"There," Silas said. "That is not restorative. That is not algal. That is not anything any living mouth wants to ask for twice."

He handed the bell to the boardinghouse girl. "Hold that up."

She did, laughing already.

"What she's selling," Silas said, climbing back onto the crate, "is not polish. If it were only polish, every shipwright's boy from here to Ferrymouth would make it in a bucket and she'd be ruined by Thursday. What she's selling is the exact shine a front latch gets when mourning has lasted long enough and company may call again."

The crowd made the small scandalized noise he wanted.

Silas looked at Clary Dobb then, finally, and gave her the sort of smile that made nervous people think he was on their side and sensible people check their pockets.

"She's selling Widow's Rub."

There was a beat. A real one. Patent Row took good names the way a boxer took a clean punch: with surprise first, pain or pleasure a moment later.

Then the newsboy yelped, "Widow's Rub!" and one of the ferry clerks slapped his friend's shoulder, and the boardinghouse girl nearly dropped the bell laughing. A driver sitting high on a dray farther down the avenue shouted, "I'll take a tin for my landlady, see if she shines herself into mercy." Somebody whistled. Somebody booed. The booing was not hostile. It was appetite in work clothes.

Clary Dobb turned the phrase in her mouth once. Silas watched the horror in her face give way by increments to arithmetic.

"Widow's Rub," she said again, with less pain and more ownership.

"There you are." Silas set a fresh board against the front of the stall and loaded his brush. "Now keep up."

He did not usually enjoy the painting more than the naming. Painting was slower, and slower work meant the part of him that kept accounts woke up and started clawing at the door. Havelock Pigments had become too patient lately. Patience from a creditor was never mercy. It was measurement. But a live crowd changed the whole business. Then the brush was not a tool of trade but proof. If he only shouted, they could call him a drunk with luck. If he lettered the thing in front of them, in one unbroken series of strokes while the word was still hot in their mouths, that was craft. That was a man earning the superstition around him.

He laid down the W with a fat arrogant downstroke and a lean uplift that made the ferry clerk breathe out through his teeth. The I and D snapped together. By the time he got to the apostrophe-less S he could hear the rhythm of the word settling into the crowd. Wid-ow's-rub. Three beats, one slide. Dirty enough to travel. Simple enough for a child. Strong enough for a shop window.

He painted the second word larger.

When he stepped back, the board read:

WIDOW'S RUB

in cream letters on an eel-black field, with a thin green line under the whole thing like river light under a closed door.

The boardinghouse girl clapped. The newsboy started chanting it toward the people still walking past as if the product had existed for years and he had merely been patient enough to find the proper corner from which to sell it.

"Widow's Rub! Widow's Rub! Makes your brass behave!"

"That last part is yours," Silas told him.

"You'll thank me when it catches."

"If it catches, I'll deny knowing you."

But it had already caught. A dockman bought a tin for the joke and then bought a second because the bell truly had shone. One of the ferry clerks asked whether it worked on buckles. Clary Dobb, who five minutes earlier had looked near to apologizing for existing, began answering with an authority she plainly had not possessed until the name arrived to dress her in it. She tucked the clove behind one ear, squared her shoulders, and lied with improving skill.

"Works on buckles, hinges, lamp collars, and any brass a decent house keeps above the touch of damp."

"You've never had a decent house in your life," said the boardinghouse girl.

"Then I'm overdue one."

More laughter. More hands going into pockets.

Silas felt the little inner lift he always felt at such moments, a rising at the back of the neck that had almost nothing to do with pride and everything to do with power. Not moral power. Not respectable power. Nothing any judge would write down. Just the indecent fact that a thing could be worthless at four o'clock and saleable at four ten because he had put the right dirt on it.

That was what Patent Row paid for. Not genius, exactly. Nerve, timing, and a willingness to put his mouth where other people's shame lived.

Clary Dobb pressed coins into his palm before she forgot herself and became cautious again. Her hand was damp and trembling. "If the matrons of Gilt Crescent come asking for it," she said, "I shall send flowers to whatever church still admits you."

"Send money instead."

"I'm not ungrateful, Mr. Vale."

"No," he said, weighing the coins without counting them yet. "Only improved."

He slipped the money into his trouser pocket, tucked the brush behind one ear, and considered departure. A crowd loved you best in the first six minutes. Stay past ten and they began asking for charity. Another line. Another trick. Another proof that the rabbit was really in the hat and not in your sleeve all along.

***

He had just taken the crate under one arm when something struck the back of the fresh sign with a wet smack.

The sound was meaty enough that half the crowd laughed before they turned.

Above them, an errand boy on the narrow gallery between two buildings had frozen with a split bucket still in his hands. He could not have been more than twelve. One suspender hung loose, and his left ear stuck out from his hair as if it wanted to escape him first. He had that guilty stillness children got when they knew apology would only make adults more accurate.

"Oh, hell," he said.

The bucket's contents had slid down the back of the signboard and through a crack in the side frame, then burped around to the front in three slow glistening tongues. At first it looked like ordinary drain slurry: black water, pale mash, a rind of green filth where too many things had been mixed and heated and forgotten. Patent Row saw that all day and cursed it with no particular imagination.

Then the green rind shivered.

Silas stopped moving.

The stuff on the sign began climbing.

It did not do it fast. That would have been easier to understand. It lifted itself by the patient half-millimeters of something testing a surface, throwing out thin frilled edges and finding grip on the drying paint. One tongue spread over the lower corner of the W. Another reached toward the green underline and brightened there. The black water dripped away. What remained had the live, treacherous sheen of spill-born moss, the kind that showed up after digester surges and gutter floods and made three blocks of fools insist they had discovered a new strain. Usually it flared, stank, and died.

This one did not look interested in dying.

The crowd drew closer. Patent Row could smell novelty faster than fire.

"What's that, then?" the ferry clerk said.

The boy on the gallery found his voice. "Sweepings from Latcher's lamp room! I only meant to tip them in the cut!"

"Well, you tipped them in my sales pitch," Clary Dobb snapped.

The boardinghouse girl held the polished bell up higher as if that might scare the slime back into obedience. "It is eating the widow."

"A fitting courtship," said the driver on the dray.

Another laugh. The newsboy was already bouncing on the balls of his feet. "Name it, Vale."

Silas did not answer.

He had seen live gutter growth before. Enough to know when the crowd wanted fright and when it wanted fun. This was still fun. Barely. The moss had climbed across the curve of the W and was now throwing a faint green wash over the cream paint, not dissolving it, not covering it, only altering the light on it. The effect was pretty in the cheap dangerous way a broken bottle could be pretty in sun.

"Go on," said one of the ferry clerks. "You named the polish. Name the leavings."

"I don't christen every damp mistake on the avenue."

"You will if we clap," said the boardinghouse girl, and the crowd rewarded her with exactly that.

Silas felt the pressure change. It had been admiration a moment ago. Now it was appetite with teeth. Patent Row had decided he was not only useful. He was reusable.

He hated that part. He loved it too. Those were neighboring districts as well.

The moss had reached the downstroke of the R in RUB. Its frilled edges were paling, not green now but a translucent shell color, the sort of blush a cheap pearl tried to fake and never quite managed. The whole board looked suddenly indecent in a way he had not planned. Widow's Rub, haloed by shy luminous filth.

Silas was aware of the entire street in separate pieces: the damp on the tea crate handle under his palm, the clove breath still leaking off Clary Dobb beside him, the creak of the gallery above where the boy dared not move, the far ferry bell from Ferrymouth carrying through the hot air, and underneath all of it the crowd waiting for his mouth to turn accident into commerce.

He should have walked away. That would have been the wise thing, and wise things were not always beneath him. Sometimes they even happened.

But the crowd was looking at the pinking edges of the moss, then back at him, and a joke arrived whole.

He snorted. "Look at it."

"We're looking," Clary Dobb said through her teeth.

"No, really look. The little whore is blushing."

That got the proper shock. Three women laughed. The errand boy on the gallery made a strangled noise, scandalized on principle and delighted in spite of himself.

Silas pointed with the crate handle. "Came out of a gutter, climbs where it's not invited, throws on a pretty face the instant strangers crowd near. That's not drain-light. That's bridelight."

The newsboy said it first, because boys always did. "Bridelight!"

The boardinghouse girl repeated it with her hand over her mouth. One of the ferry clerks tried it in a mock-genteel accent. The driver on the dray bellowed it down the avenue to people who had not seen any of this and needed only the shape of the word to want in.

"Bridelight!"

"Bridelight on Dobb's board!"

"Oi, come see the bridelight!"

The word went out in layers. Street cry. Laughter. Questions. A woman passing with two hat boxes paused long enough to say, "What is bridelight?" and the newsboy, with the speed of the professionally ignorant, answered, "The newest thing in Nacre Bend, missus," before turning and shouting it again.

Silas felt the first prickle of wrongness then.

The moss at the bottom of the board was no longer only pale. It was pink. Not a trick of the lowering sun. Not paint reflection. Pink from inside itself, a flushed nacre color that thickened along the frilled margins and pooled bright where the word had been repeated loudest. Another tongue of growth slipped over the D and answered with the same change. The green underneath thinned as if retreating from embarrassment.

Clary Dobb stepped back so hard her hat fringe clicked against her cheek. "That wasn't there."

"No," Silas said.

He kept his voice short when he was truly paying attention. The performance went out of it all at once. "It was not."

The boardinghouse girl reached toward the sign, then stopped an inch short when the warmth hit her knuckles. "It's hot."

"Don't touch it," Silas said, and there was enough flat fact in his tone that she obeyed.

The crowd obeyed only by becoming louder.

"Bridelight!"

"Look there, it likes it!"

"Say it again!"

They did. God help them, they did. Half of Patent Row spent its days trying to prove that repetition could make a bad thing profitable. The avenue had trained itself for exactly this kind of mistake.

By the time Silas pushed through them to get closer to the board, the moss had gone from shame-pink to full flushed rose along the lower half of the sign, as if someone had lit a coal behind the paint. The green line he had laid under WIDOW'S RUB now glowed like a river weed seen through wine.

The errand boy on the gallery said, almost reverently, "It heard him."

Nobody answered because that sentence had gone farther into the room than any of them wanted to admit.

Silas looked down into the gutter. A second clot of the spilled stuff had landed there among cigarette stubs, horse droppings, and shredded handbills. It had caught in the ironwork around a drain mouth. While the crowd shouted over the sign, that smaller clot had begun to pink as well.

He could have called it trickery if there had only been one patch.

He could not call two patches trickery with a straight face, and on Patent Row a man needed at least the option.

Clary Dobb grabbed his sleeve. "Mr. Vale. If this ruins my board-"

"If it ruins your board, you will have the most visited ruined board in the district by supper."

"That's not reassuring."

"It wasn't meant to be."

The coins in his pocket suddenly felt too light.

He should have gone then. He knew he should. The crowd was turning from customer to witness, and witnesses never paid enough. But now people were arriving from farther down the avenue and asking the word before they asked the cause. Bridelight had jumped the original moment already. It was moving on its own legs.

Silas set the crate down again.

That was his mistake, or only the first one he had made in full daylight.

***

For the next hour Patent Row did what it did whenever something truly new entered the air: it converted astonishment into commerce before astonishment could change its mind.

A pie seller dragged her barrow ten yards closer to Clary Dobb's stall and announced hot eel hand-pies for bridelight viewing. Two boys started taking wagers on whether the glow would turn the whole sign pink by sunset. A man from a competing polish shop arrived pretending concern and stayed to see whether he might buy whatever remained if Clary Dobb fainted. Someone tried to scrape a thread of the moss into a bottle and yelped when the glass grew warm in his fingers.

Silas endured it because leaving had become harder than staying. Also because he was having a good time, which was the uglier truth. His nerves were up and singing. A thing he had said in a half-joke had altered a living surface in front of witnesses. He knew enough to be afraid and not enough to be bored.

That was almost happiness.

He took a second fee from a soapmaker three doors down for naming a floral wash while the crowd kept one eye on the glowing board. He called the wash Churchstep because it promised cleanliness without innocence, and the soapmaker nearly kissed him. The ease of that second christening should have calmed him. It did the opposite. Ordinary work felt suddenly cheap. He kept looking back at the pink glow under WIDOW'S RUB the way a gambler kept glancing at a horse that had started speaking in a human voice.

The moss did not spread fast. It spread steadily. That was worse.

By the time the sun slid low enough to catch on the ridge above Gilt Crescent, a lace of the same flushed pink had appeared in the mortar seam between Clary Dobb's stall and the neighboring print shop. A strip of gutter growth under a hatter's window answered to the word when a passing apprentice shouted it just to see. Women in work aprons began saying bridelight with practical skepticism, as if naming it right away might keep the thing from becoming somebody else's property first. A pair of schoolboys came running from halfway up the avenue swearing there was some on a bridge piling too, and one of them had chalk on his cuffs from writing the word where he should not.

Silas finally counted his coins while pretending not to care. Enough for supper, enough for tobacco, enough to make Havelock's clerk stop using that soft voice for one more day. Not enough to satisfy the sharper hunger the glowing board had woken in him. He tucked the coins back away feeling cheated by success itself.

That was when Damaris Pike found him.

She emerged from the doorway of the Evening Tin Sheet with printer's smut on two fingers and widow's black gone shiny at the elbows from years of wearing and refusing to replace. Her front tooth had a neat chip out of it that caught the pink glow when she smiled. She had a folded proof tucked under one arm and a stub of pencil anchored in hair that had once been pinned for respectability and had since decided against the effort.

"Don't go home yet," she said. "I can smell tomorrow from here."

Silas took his brush from behind his ear and used the handle to scratch at a drying spot of paint on his wrist. "I was considering drink before destiny. It is a known preference."

"Take both." She looked past him at Clary Dobb's stall, where the crowd had only thickened. "Tell me you didn't plan it. If you tell me you planned it, I've got to print that. If you tell me you didn't, I can imply worse."

"You make a widow's trade look honest."

"And you make honesty sound underdressed. There, we're square." She leaned in, close enough for him to smell ink, clove cigarettes, and the stale starch of old mourning. "How long till it reaches the ridge, do you think?"

"You are asking the wrong bastard."

"No, I'm asking the bastard who put the word in its mouth."

"Its mouth?" Silas looked back at the board. "You have upgraded slime into a witness faster than I expected."

"Witnesses sell. Slime sells too, but less well above the second floor." Her eyes flicked over him with the quick accounting habit of an editor: hair loose from the heat, coat too thin at the cuffs, pocket weighted by one small payment and not enough more. Nothing in her face softened. Damaris Pike did not trade in softness. She traded in angle. "I want three mornings out of this at least. Four if children start frightening one another with it. Give me a line."

"A line for what?"

"For the headline, naturally. I can't put BRIDELIGHT STIRS PATENT ROW unless you hand me something nastier beneath it."

"You already had the word before you came over."

"That is because I employ boys who can hear."

He grinned despite himself. "All right. Print this: It blushes under scrutiny and climbs where it's not wanted. Which makes it more qualified for society than half the ridge."

Damaris's chipped tooth flashed. She wrote it down in a rattling burst, then paused only long enough to look at him over the proof sheet. "If that thing reaches a church wall by morning, I shall own this week."

"If it reaches a church wall by morning, the church may own us."

"That has never stopped me."

She tucked the pencil back into her hair and snapped her fingers at a printer's devil hovering in the doorway. "Ned. Set BRIDELIGHT big enough to make shopkeepers swear."

The boy darted away at once, already mouthing the word as he ran, as if speaking it were part of setting type. Silas watched him go and had the uncomfortable thought that the street had just found another throat.

"Do not look ill," Damaris said. "You have the face of a man who has finally done something worth regretting. It is attractive on you. Temporarily."

"I'm always attractive. Regret's only weather."

"Keep telling women that. It saves them time."

She turned her head slightly, not enough for a full glance, and Silas followed the line of her attention.

Gideon Wren was standing across the avenue in front of a carriage he had no need to have stepped out of except that stepping out of it improved the composition. Lean, silvering at the temples, gloved despite the heat, coat dark and immaculate where everyone else's cuffs had already surrendered to the day. Patent Row's damp seemed to respect him against its nature. Even his boots looked wiped by a servant between each puddle.

Silas had seen him before, because anybody who sold names or wall space in Nacre Bend eventually saw Gideon Wren, but rarely this near. Up close the man's civility had structure to it. You could have set a ledger edge against it and trusted the line.

Damaris muttered, "There is the undertaker of pleasure itself."

"You say that like you have dined with him."

"Twice. He chews as if appetite were a rumor about poorer people."

Gideon crossed the avenue without haste. People shifted for him. Not out of affection. Out of the same instinct that made pedestrians step aside for a horse likely to kick: respect misfiled under caution.

"Mr. Vale," he said.

He inclined his head first to Silas, then to Damaris. "Mrs. Pike."

"Mr. Wren," Damaris said. "Do come purchase the moon. The avenue is nearly out of ordinary commodities."

"You print jokes because you cannot invoice silence," Gideon said mildly. "I am here on more practical business."

Damaris made a small bow with two fingers. "My condolences."

Silas let himself enjoy that for a breath, then turned to Gideon. "If the business is polish, you're late. The widow's been rubbed."

Gideon's mouth moved almost not at all. On another face it would not have counted as a smile.

"So I hear. And now there is this." He looked toward the glowing signboard. He did not gape. That was one of the many expensive things about him. "I understand the name is yours."

"The shout was mine. The rest belongs to the gutter, apparently."

"Nothing belongs to the gutter for long once it has proven marketable."

Damaris snorted. "Now there is a sermon for the age."

Gideon ignored her with enough polish to make the exclusion visible. "Mr. Vale, novelty becomes value only when it can be asked for twice without confusion. A street can shout many things. A city must be taught."

The line put a cold little hook into Silas somewhere under the ribs. Not because it was frightening. Because it was true in the way that ugly commercial truths often were.

He shrugged to hide that he had felt it. "You may teach the city what you like. I only supply conversation."

"Conversation is an undisciplined distribution channel."

"That sounds lonely."

"It sounds profitable." Gideon glanced at the widening crowd and then back to Silas. "If the growth persists past tonight, I should like a word with you tomorrow."

"You are having one now."

"A better one, then."

Damaris clicked her tongue. "Be careful, Silas. Men like him don't buy mouths. They buy the hours around them and call the arrangement civilization."

Gideon turned to her at last. "Mrs. Pike, you mistake stewardship for theft because theft is the only durable grammar your paper recognizes."

"And you mistake a leash for architecture."

Silas almost laughed. The two of them were lovely together in the way acid and silver were lovely when brought too near. He filed that away. Everything on Patent Row could be turned to use if you had enough shamelessness and somewhere to write it.

Gideon returned his attention to Silas. "Tomorrow morning, then. If the phenomenon remains."

"And if it does not?"

"Then you have still proved yourself interesting in public, which is no small thing."

That was almost flattering. More importantly, it sounded like money dressed for church.

Silas said, "I keep irregular mornings."

"I do not." Gideon touched two fingers to the brim of an invisible hat, because his actual hat remained in the carriage where a man like him left unnecessary objects until they were required for theater. "Do not let Mrs. Pike print you into a corner before then."

He went back across the avenue. The crowd closed after him. Even his exit seemed ironed.

Damaris watched him go and let out a breath through her nose. "There. Now the day has turned expensive."

"You make everything sound like a debt."

"Everything is a debt. Some of it merely dresses better." She folded the proof under her arm again. "If he offers you a retainer, ask for more than your appetite says. Appetite is a cut-rate negotiator."

That made Silas glance at her sharply. She only smiled and turned away.

"Tomorrow's sheet," she said over her shoulder. "Buy it from a child, not a man. The children shout clearer."

Then she was gone into the print shop, where type and rumor were already beginning their night work.

Silas stood a moment longer in the guttered light, listening.

Patent Row had changed key.

The usual evening clatter was still there: dray wheels knocking through ruts, hawkers dragging out the last of the day, ferry bells from the river, glass clinking in upstairs rooms, some idiot singing half a chorus from a music hall song he did not know well enough to survive. But over and through it now came the new word. Not everywhere. Not yet. Enough places to matter.

"Bridelight down by the print shop!"

"No, look, there is some by the saddler too!"

"Mind your hem, it's warm-"

"Who named it?"

"Patent Row Silas."

He hated hearing that name in strangers' mouths. He always had. It made him sound both larger and cheaper than a man ought to be.

He drifted with the crowd instead of leaving it, which was another mistake. The dusk thickened. Lamps along the avenue began to come up one by one, ordinary household green in most chimneys, but where the spill-born patches had taken hold the light was altered. Under the hatter's window a line of moss in the mortar joints had gone the same flushed shell pink as Clary Dobb's sign. On the brick flank of the print shop, a damp patch no one had noticed at noon now glowed soft rose around the edges of a handbill for a boxing exhibition. Farther down, on the iron brace of a pedestrian bridge over the drainage cut, a smear of living film had brightened as if somebody had laid bridal ribbon under soot.

Every new sighting made people say the word again.

Every saying seemed to feed the thing it named.

Silas stopped at the mouth of an alley where two shopgirls and a coal runner were staring at a strip of pink glow climbing the wall above a busted crate. The coal runner had one shoe tied with cord and a healed burn on the back of his hand shaped like a crooked flower. He looked over when Silas drew near and did not recognize him, which was almost restful.

"You think it will spread?" the boy asked nobody in particular.

One of the shopgirls answered before Silas could decide whether to lie. "Everything does if rich men notice."

That was a better line than most paying clients ever bought from him. He nearly told her so. Then the patch on the wall pulsed once, a soft flush through the pink, and all three of them laughed in the same startled key people used when a dog unexpectedly spoke.

Silas did not laugh.

He looked back up the avenue. Clary Dobb's glowing board had become a small lantern in the crowd. Beyond it the ridge windows of Gilt Crescent held the last gold of the sun. Below, Patent Row was taking on its night colors: algae green under common lamps, river black in the gutters, and now, in scattered answer across brick and iron and damp timber, that impossible bridal pink.

He tried the ordinary explanations in order because he was not an idiot. Reflection. Mineral in the sweepings. Trick of warm paint under a cooling sky. Coordinated fraud by persons unknown and too underfunded to deserve the effort.

Each explanation failed on contact with his eyes.

He put his hand against the alley wall beside the glowing strip and felt the heat in it, not enough to burn, enough to declare itself alive. The brick sweated under his palm. Pink light climbed over the web of old mortar and made the ridges look almost tender.

He had named products before. He had named pests, washes, varnishes, appetite tonics, a line of lamp collars so ugly the name had been the only honest part of them. He had put words on things until they sold faster, shone brighter in shop windows, or seemed better bred than their materials justified.

He had never before watched the thing itself lean toward the word.

That should have sobered him clean through.

Instead a dangerous pleasure opened low in his gut, hot and bright and mean with possibility. If a shouted word could do this on a street corner, what could a printed one do by morning? What could a whole city be talked into? He thought of walls. Lamps. Ferry rails. Whole neighborhoods lit one color because the right phrase had caught. Then he thought of the same force escaping whoever first named it, answering not to a patron or a shop but to the nearest hundred mouths, and the pleasure turned at the edges.

This was bigger than a successful christening.

That meant it was worth more money than anything he had touched in months.

It also meant money might not be the first hand on it.

A girl half a block away shouted, "Bridelight!" just to test the air. Three voices answered. On the bridge brace the pink deepened.

Silas took his hand off the wall.

For the first time since he had started selling words, he felt the old stupid superstition trying to rise in him, the childish conviction that language was not only trade or wit or leverage but a kind of handling. A way of touching the live world without laying skin on it.

He did not like the thought. He liked it far too much.

Night settled another notch. Patent Row glowed back.

Silas stood in the damp alley mouth with Clary Dobb's payment in one pocket and somebody else's future trouble moving pink through the walls around him, and he could not tell whether he had just made the best joke of his life or the first one that would keep answering after he shut up.


# Chapter 2

By noon the city had his joke in its teeth.

Silas climbed Lantern Steps through air that smelled of wet lime, lamp smoke, and the sweet warm rot the Sallow River breathed into every lower street after rain. He had no clean reason to be there. A sensible man would have stayed on Patent Row, taken another lap past the windows where people were already pointing at last night's pink bloom, and let the word he'd flung off a signboard do what words did in Nacre Bend, which was travel farther than their author deserved and pick up worse company on the way. But sense had never kept him in tobacco or paid Havelock Pigments on time. Curiosity had done better. Vanity had done best of all. He wanted to see whether bridelight had truly climbed into the stair district or whether the morning's talk had only painted rumor over damp stone.

It had climbed.

Every third public lamp along the rail showed some stain of it. Not a steady color, not yet. A blush under the green, a watered rose caught in the glass, the sort of tint a man could call charming if he had no intention of carrying soup, babies, or his own half-drunk body down slick steps after dark. A fishwoman in a tarred apron laughed the word to her sister, and the lamp over their heads flushed a shade brighter as if it liked applause. Two boys half his nephew's age had pink chalk on their cuffs and were arguing over who had first heard him say it. A clerk from Gilt Crescent, clean collar already losing the fight with humidity, had come down only to stare and say the name with the reverence some men reserved for a new racehorse.

Silas ought to have felt pleased. He did, for about half a landing at a time. Then the feeling kept slipping on something he could not name. The pink did not sit on the lamps the way paint sat on a board. It lived too near the surface. It seemed to gather and thin with the breath of whoever was nearest. He knew color. He knew fraud in color better than most honest men knew prayer. This was neither.

He saw the woman before he understood she was waiting for him.

She stood in the middle of the stair where the steps widened enough for two tenants to quarrel without tumbling into the drainage cut, compact and planted and impossible to edge around. A dark work scarf was wrapped hard over her hair. The hem of her skirt was wet to mid-calf. Two lamp trays hung from her hands by their bent wire handles, one in each fist, as if she had brought him a pair of arguments too heavy for language alone. The inside of her right wrist showed a pale crescent scar when she shifted her grip, old heat laid down clean and deep. Behind her, crowding the landing rail, waited four women and a girl with a cracked chimney glass hugged to her apron. They had the patient appetite of people who had come to watch a man be corrected.

"Silas Vale," the woman said.

He touched two fingers to his hat brim. "When the law insists on particulars."

"Good. Then you won't pretend to be somebody less useful."

Her voice did not rise. It landed flat and exact, the kind of voice used to calling problems by the right size before somebody made them expensive. She stepped down one stair and set one tray on the stone between them. The metal hit with a blunt wet knock. A ribbon of pink slime shivered over the lip and dropped onto his boot.

Up close, the tray had gone bad in ways no lamp keeper could fake for sport. The shallow belly had warped inward on one side as if heat had licked it too long. The wire cage over the moss bed had buckled. One rivet had sweated itself loose and left a rusty tear in the tin. The growth inside was alive, if that was the word for something that glowed while smelling faintly of pond scum and hot pennies. It held the same flushed pink that had charmed Patent Row the night before, only here it looked indecent, cosmetic, a cheap rouge streaked across work metal.

Silas crouched before he could stop himself. Trade habit. Damage spoke in materials before it spoke anywhere else.

"That tray was steady yesterday," the woman said. "Cool enough for a landing lamp. This morning it bowed."

One of the women at the rail, a narrow laundress with one lens missing from her spectacles and blueing powder dusted up both forearms, sucked her teeth and added, "Took the skin off my kettle thumb too. If you're collecting compliments, have that one."

Silas looked from the bent tray to the woman's thumb. A fresh blister shone there, high and angry. He pushed at the tray rim with one paint-stained nail. The metal gave under his touch where it should have held.

"Might be bad mash," he said. "Or a cheap plate sold by a liar with softer tin than sense."

The woman in the scarf did not blink. "I grow beds for six houses and the public lamps on this run. Do not explain my own trays to me."

There it was, the first hard little pleasure of the exchange, because she had said do not as a verdict rather than an accident. Not a woman carried away by grievance. A woman spending formality like coin because she meant him to feel the count of it.

He rose. "Then I'll skip theory and move straight to apology for the unknown tinner."

"Don't dress it." She nudged the tray with her boot until the warped belly rang again on stone. "I know your kind of joke when it walks indoors."

He smiled because people expected him to smile, and because once he stopped smiling in public a great deal of his life would have to be re-negotiated from the mud up. "My kind of joke usually buys drinks before it ruins cookware."

No one laughed. The girl with the cracked chimney glass shifted it higher against her chest as if afraid he might charm that away too.

The woman said, "Inez Cordero."

Not an introduction offered for courtesy. A label set down because work required clear bins.

He gave her a tiny nod. "Miss Cordero."

"Miss will do until you learn something. Look there."

She pointed past him, not at the tray but at the public lamp bolted to the landing post. Its glass belly had gone pink in streaks, color snagged inside green like blood working through river weed. Beneath it, tied to the post with a strip of wet cloth, sat a hand-sized spare cutting in a shallow tin saucer. Silas had not noticed it because he had been looking at the room of faces instead of the test laid out for him.

Inez bent, lifted the saucer, and held it level between them. The moss in it was greener than the tray growth, with a pink creep already nibbling in from one edge.

"This piece never left the landing," she said. "No Patent Row wall, no crowd around it, no drunks kissing it for luck. Just here. Last night the stair boys came up yelling your word because they'd heard it below and thought pink was a grand joke. By midnight this piece matched the rail lamps."

Silas heard himself say, "You make it sound jealous."

"I make it sound busy."

She turned her head toward the women behind her. "Say it."

They did not sing. They did not sway or perform a little ward rite for his benefit. They said bridelight the way neighbors called for more coal, or asked whose child was loose on the steps, or warned one another about a rotten plank. Flat, repeated, public. The laundress said it around the clench of pain in her thumb. The girl said it with unhappy dutifulness. Even a woman in a patched men's coat, who had been picking fish scales out of her cuff with the point of a hatpin, gave it one dry repetition.

Bridelight. Bridelight. Bridelight.

The saucer in Inez's hand answered at once. Pink ran across the green in a wet fast blush and the heat off it touched Silas's knuckles. Overhead the public lamp brightened with the same false warmth, a flattering color fit for a ballroom painting and useless on a stair slick with runoff.

Silas did not say anything. His mouth was suddenly occupied with staying shut.

Inez watched him watch it. Then she tipped her chin toward the women again.

"Now the house name."

The answer came back in a different rhythm, more settled because it belonged to use and everyone there already knew where it fit.

"Stepblue."

They said it twice. Three times. Not loud. Together.

The pink withdrew as if drawn through the flesh of the moss by an unseen thread. Blue spread in its place, not sky blue or the hard enamel of rich parlor tiles, but the laundry blue women cut with water to bully yellow out of shirts. Cool color. Working color. In the saucer it deepened until the whole patch held a sober mourning cast that made the landing stones look suddenly farther apart and more honest about their wetness. The lamp over them followed, its blush draining into the same grave blue. Even the heat came off differently. A moment before Silas had felt it on the back of his fingers like a flirt. Now it laid itself flat and broad, useful rather than insistent.

The woman in the patched coat said, "That's what keeps old Mr. Dane from pissing off the edge on his way down."

That got one bitter snort of laughter from the rail. It broke just enough tension for Silas to hear the river below and the hiss of damp gas off the lamp bed.

"Stepblue," he repeated.

Inez shifted the saucer to her other hand. "For stairs. Broad throw, low burn. Doesn't cook sleeves when a drunk leans too near. Doesn't make shadows jump. Doesn't draw every fool moth in the cut."

"And bridelight does."

"Bridelight preens." Her lip moved by the smallest degree, not a smile and not near one. "Pretty thing for a signboard. Rotten thing for a landing."

The words hit him harder because she had not raised her voice to throw them. He wanted to answer with something easy and knife-bright, something that would turn the whole little tribunal back into spectacle where he knew his footing. Three fine lines rose ready in him, all of them variations on the city wanting entertainment and names never staying where a man put them. All of them died before they reached his tongue. The blue in the lamp was still settling. He could smell cooled tin, damp stone, the rank sweetness of live growth. Nothing about it felt like theater except him.

"You taught it a trick," he said at last, and hated how thin it sounded.

"No." Inez set the saucer back beneath the lamp. "We taught it a job. You gave it a courtship name and now every idiot with lungs is making it blush."

One of the women behind her, the laundress with the missing lens, let out a short ugly laugh. "And while it blushes, I get to boil washing by luck."

Silas looked at her blister again. Then at the warped tray. Then back at Inez. He had spent years turning words into surfaces men could profit from. A polish. A gleam. A little difference between one thing for sale and the next thing for sale. He knew the thrill of hearing a crowd pick up a phrase he'd made and carry it forward as if they had invented it themselves. That thrill was still in him, stupidly alive. So was something uglier now, something like the instant after a brush slips and drags a whole loaded stripe across finished lettering. Not pain yet. The knowledge that pain has already been arranged.

"All right," he said. "You've shown me a moody lamp."

The women at the rail made small sounds of disgust. Inez did not.

"No," she said again, cleaner than before. "I've shown you that your mouth can reach a stair from Patent Row. Now pick that tray up."

He blinked. "Why?"

"Because it answers to you, and because you're coming inside."

She put the bent tray's handle into his hand before he could object. The wire bit his palm. The thing was heavier than it looked, wet and badly balanced. Heat still lived in one warped flank. Pink slime smeared his cuff. The smell rose stronger from this close, a stink of wet copper and pond weed and something singed underneath.

He did not drop it. The whole landing was waiting to see whether he would.

Inez took the second tray, turned without checking whether he followed, and started down the next run of steps with the quick sure footing of somebody who trusted slick wood and wet stone because they had spent years teaching her their terms. Silas adjusted his grip and went after her, feeling all at once like a man carrying his own cleverness after it had spoiled in the pan.

***

The stair district narrowed as she led him downward. Landings gave way to short plank bridges flung over runnels of gray water. Laundry lines crossed above like low pennants of surrender. The houses were packed close enough that windows argued with one another. Somewhere below, hidden by walls, a ferry bell knocked twice. Above, farther up the steps, someone was still trying out bridelight for amusement. The word came down thin through the damp and made the tray in Silas's hand flush at the edges.

Inez saw him notice. "Keep moving."

"I'm moving."

She glanced back. "You can contract if you're tired, Mr. Vale. The tray won't think less of you."

He barked one laugh in spite of himself. "That's crueler than it needed to be."

"No. Crueler is later if you still think this is a novelty."

She took him first into a one-room kitchen on a middle landing where the door swelled in its frame and had to be shoulder-butted open. The woman inside had a watch chain clipped to her apron though the watch itself was long gone; the empty chain swung against her belly every time she stirred the pot on the lamp stand. She did not look up when they entered. She was too busy scraping the bottom of a kettle with a spoon and swearing at it under her breath.

"Mrs. Rusk," Inez said.

Mrs. Rusk yanked the lid off the kettle and showed Silas supper in two different moods. One side of the stew had thickened into a paste blackening at the rim. The other side still sloshed pale and underdone, turnip cubes floating hard as stones in broth that had not decided whether it meant to warm or sulk.

"There," Mrs. Rusk said. "You can letter that if you like. Call it half-starved, half-burnt."

Under the kettle, the lamp bed shone with a muddy battle between blue and pink. The heat at the stand came in little shoves, enough to sting the back of Silas's hand when he tested the air, then fall away so suddenly the spoon in Mrs. Rusk's pot stopped rattling.

"It used to hold steady?" he asked.

Mrs. Rusk gave him a look sharp enough to skin eels with. "I don't cook by prayer, Mr. Vale."

Inez said, "Stepblue under a kettle gives you slow even heat. Not quick, not fashionable, but you can leave a widow's dinner on it while you mend a stocking and it won't take revenge. Since this morning it keeps answering to whatever's loudest outside."

Silas looked from the lamp to the window, where damp voices moved past in the lane. He could not blame draft or bad tin here. The kettle stand had not warped. The trouble was in the bed itself, in the way the color kept trying to climb into blush whenever a shout came down from the higher steps. Mrs. Rusk caught the spoon against the side of the pot and showed him the bottom. A black crescent of scorch ran across it like a grin.

"My boarders work the early ferry," she said. "If I feed them mush and cinder they'll eat it and still come back because men will chew anything with salt. But I lose the broth and I lose the lamp bed, and then I lose the next day's fuel because it takes more to set another tray right. So when people tell me a pretty name is good for the district, I hope they choke on it before supper."

She turned back to the kettle. That was all the attention he was going to get from her, and more than he deserved. Inez did not gild it with commentary. She waited until he had seen enough of the room's damp walls, the patched sleeve hanging to dry over the chair back, the narrow shelf with three potatoes on it and no fourth, and then she led him out again.

On the landing, he shifted the warped tray to his other hand. The wire handle had printed itself into his palm. Pink slime was drying tacky on his cuff.

"How many house names?" he asked.

Inez kept walking. "Depends what you're asking the bed to do."

"That many?"

"More."

"Patent Row would go blind trying to sell more."

"Patent Row doesn't carry boiling water down stairs."

It was not a clever line. That was why it stayed with him. She was never trying to win the exchange the way he won exchanges. She was moving through facts and leaving him either to keep up or to look foolish in public. It was a nastier kind of authority than charm. More expensive too.

The second room was narrower and darker, a mending space wedged behind a public rail lamp so close to the wall that its blue used to fall clean through the window. Now the light inside had a lavender dirtiness to it, an uncertain cast that made every shadow look rubbed with bruised milk. A woman sat at a worktable by the sill with a clove bud tucked into one cheek. She had short practical fingers and a way of biting the thread only at the side of her mouth, as if she distrusted the center. On the table before her lay two cuffs, one trimmed for mourning, one for ordinary work, and under the bad light the ribbons on both had collapsed into nearly the same muddy dark.

"Bess Quill," Inez said.

Bess did not rise. She lifted the two cuffs between finger and thumb and held them toward Silas. "Which one loses me money?"

Silas took a step nearer. In clean light the difference would have been plain enough. One ribbon was black. The other was deep river blue. Here the lamp flattened them into cousins.

"You tell me," he said.

"I did," Bess said. "I told the woman collecting these that this one was mourning black and this one was navy for a warehouse clerk who thinks he's elegant. Then your rosy nonsense got into the rail lamp and I sent both out with the wrong bands. I got two coppers docked and one insult for my trouble. The insult I could have supplied myself."

She popped the clove from one cheek to the other and looked past him to the window. On the stair outside, two girls were arguing about whether pink made them look paler. Each time the word bridelight floated in, the rail lamp's cast warmed another fraction and the black ribbon on Bess's table thinned toward plum.

"You see it?" Bess asked.

He did. More than that, he knew exactly how small the margin was between a ribbon that said widow and a ribbon that said clerk, between being paid and being told to mind your eyes better next time. He had made a career in that margin. He suddenly hated seeing it from the other side of the table.

"I see it," he said.

Bess grunted. "Good. I was worried fame had blinded you."

Inez took him out before he could answer. That mercy annoyed him more than the insult. On the landing he opened his mouth for some defense, and nothing but river damp came in.

Below them the houses grew closer to Sump Ward in their smell, more ferment in the walls, more wet burlap, more living green where brick ought to have been clean. The tray in his hand had cooled under the last few repetitions of stepblue, then twitched warm again as a group of boys somewhere above started testing his word for sport. The city was learning it too fast. Not learning. Playing with it. The distinction had seemed important to him that morning. It was losing ground now.

"Do you know what angers me?" Inez said, not looking at him.

He almost said the list could not be short enough for a landing. Instead he said, "Tell me."

"Not that you named a thing in public. The city names in public all day. Not that men on Patent Row are greedy. If I waited to be shocked by greed I'd never sleep." She took the last three steps of a run without touching the rail. "It's that you people talk like use is a stain. Like the best thing a word can do is shine."

He tightened his grip on the tray handle. "You don't know what I talk like."

"I heard you last night from two districts away."

That shut him up more effectively than a slap might have. The insult was not that she had heard him. It was that of course she had. Of course the performance he thought belonged to Patent Row had crossed the city already, entered kitchens, ridden ferry bells, gone under doors. He had always known words traveled. He had not had to smell where they slept once they got there.

The last house sat half a level below the rail, reached by two wet boards and a threshold worn hollow at the center. A child had chalked a line of uneven stars beside the door, and someone older had tried to wash them off without the heart to finish it. Inside, a woman with two copper nails holding her hair in place was bent over a school satchel spread on the table. Beside her sat a girl of about nine sorting buttons by size into a cough-drop tin, serious as a clerk at inventory. One knee of her stocking had been darned in green thread so bright it might have come off a flag.

"Nora," Inez said.

The woman straightened. One of the copper nails slipped and she pushed it back through her hair with the side of her wrist. "If you've brought me another tray I haven't got room for it."

"I've brought the man whose mouth is in them."

Nora's eyes moved to Silas and stayed there a beat longer than was comfortable. Then she snorted once, not with amusement but recognition of a nuisance she had previously only met at a distance.

"Fine," she said. "He can watch, then. Ettie, keep sorting."

The girl did not look up. "They go odd then even if I do the holes first."

"Then do the holes first."

On the table, next to the satchel, a squat kettle sat over a low lamp bed whose light should have been blue and was not quite anything. Blue at the core, pink feathering through the edges, enough to make the room look fevered. A strip of leather for the satchel strap softened near the kettle in a dish of tallow.

Nora touched the kettle handle with one quick knuckle and hissed. "It ran cool all breakfast. Now it keeps jumping. I can't tell whether I'm warming glue or branding the table."

Inez moved closer, studying the bed. "Who has been saying what on this landing?"

"Everybody." Nora gave a sharp laugh. "A paper boy came through twice. One woman from Gilt Crescent came down just to gawp and ask if she looked washed out in the pink. Then the boys started parroting her."

Ettie finally looked up at Silas. She had a freckle at the tip of her nose and an expression of grave suspicion that would have suited a magistrate. "Did you make the bad name?"

Silas had been called worse by better people. The question still caught under his ribs.

"I made one name," he said.

"It's a bad one." She dropped a button in the tin and returned to her sorting. "It lies about heat."

He had no answer ready for that. This was the true obscenity of children. They could sometimes say the exact thing and leave a grown man's fine language standing outside in the rain.

From the stair beyond the window came the slap of quick feet and then a stretched street cry, raw with ambition and too loud for the close walls.

"Bridelight takes the upper lamps! Bridelight, latest sheet, bridelight!"

The boy outside repeated it because selling required repetition. Two voices behind him mocked it back. Then another. Public enough. More than enough.

The lamp on Nora's table answered before anyone in the room did. Pink rushed through the bed in a hot wet seam. The kettle gave a little metallic tick as the heat climbed. Nora swore. Inez moved. Silas saw both motions and did not understand either in time.

Ettie, still intent on her buttons, reached for the kettle ear by habit, meaning only to slide it aside so she could set the tin closer to the light. Under stepblue the ear would have been merely warm. Under the sudden blush it was a branded strip of metal.

She yelped high and hard and snatched her hand back. Two fingers, first and middle, flared red at once where the skin had caught. The cough-drop tin hit the floor and buttons scattered under the table like teeth.

Nora grabbed the child around the waist. Silas caught the wobbling kettle with the edge of his sleeve before it could overturn and pour over the satchel, his own palm kissing enough heat through the cloth to make him swear. Inez had already seized the basin from beside the washstand and thrust Ettie's hand into the cool water.

"Hold her," she snapped.

Nora did. Ettie screamed once more, then bit it down into sobbing breaths. The nails in Nora's hair shook with the force of her grip. Inez kept the child's fingers under water, speaking now in that same exact public voice she had used on the landing, only lower.

"Stay there. That's right. Don't pull it out yet. Nora, you have goosefat?"

"A scrape."

"Fetch it. Clean rag too."

Nora lurched toward the shelf. Silas stood stupidly with the kettle still in his hand, the heat of it coming through sleeve and skin, and understood that he was holding a household object like a bomb he'd laid himself.

Ettie's face had gone blotchy with pain. She stared at her own hand in the basin as if insulted by it. Buttons glittered across the floorboards. One had rolled into the chalk stars by the door. Silas set the kettle down before he dropped it and crouched, not because he knew what to do but because standing over a hurt child in a room this small felt obscene.

"I didn't know," he said, and even to himself it sounded thin, a line spoken after the audience had already gone home.

Inez did not look at him. "No. You didn't."

Nora came back with a crock and a rag torn from some cleaner garment than anything else in the room. Inez lifted Ettie's hand from the basin. Water ran down the child's wrist in bright beads. The pads of the first two fingers were already whitening at the center where the burn would blister worst. Not a maiming. Not a tragedy large enough for the papers. The precise household kind that made the rest of the day harder, then the next day too. Inez smeared the fat gently over the red skin while Ettie sucked air between her teeth and tried not to cry again.

"Looks ugly," Inez said. "It'll heal if she leaves it alone."

"She won't," Nora said automatically.

"I know."

That, more than the burn itself, turned something in the room. They were women who already knew the shape of one another's next complaint. This was not incident. It was maintenance, pain folded into the day's work and handled because no one was coming with medals for it. Silas had sold names into rooms like this without ever having to sit in one while they bled.

Ettie sniffed. "I hate that name."

Nora brushed the damp hair off her forehead. "You're allowed."

Inez tied the rag lightly around the two fingers and only then looked at Silas. Nothing in her face had softened. It had simply become more precise.

"Tell me again it's publicity," she said.

He looked at the bandage. At the scattered buttons. At the kettle ear that had done exactly what metal did when heat lied. His own palm throbbed where the sleeve had failed him, a small mean pulse he was embarrassed to feel in the same room as the child's pain.

"It isn't," he said.

The words sat there. Bare. Useless for repair, but at least not false.

Inez waited, perhaps for the joke that usually followed his honesty like a dog after a butcher. None came. He found he was too tired of hearing himself from a distance.

"What do you call it," he asked, "when it burns steady and doesn't lie?"

Nora gave him a baffled look, as if this were the first sensible question he had offered all day. Ettie, already calmer now that the first bite of pain had passed, resumed glaring at the lamp as if she meant to discipline it by eye.

Inez wiped her fingers on her wet apron. Goosefat and river water shone on her knuckles. "That depends," she said.

"On what?"

She held his gaze long enough for him to understand that the answer would cost him more than curiosity.

"On the room," she said. "On the hands using it. On whether a body needs light, heat, warning, or all three. That's the part you people never bother to hear. You want one good word you can print big. We use the one that gets us through the task in front of us."

Outside, farther up the landing now, the paper boy's cry thinned into distance. The lamp on Nora's table cooled by degrees. Blue worked back into the pink, cautious and resentful, like water reclaiming dye from cloth.

Silas looked at it until the colors stopped fighting long enough for him to see the room by it: the damp patched wall, the satchel strap, the child's bandaged fingers held away from her body with offended care. Then he looked at Inez, at the old burn scar on her wrist, at the steadiness with which she stood in a house not hers and was nonetheless obeyed before anyone thought to ask why.

He would have liked to call her furious and be done with it. Furious was easy. Furious was a mood. It asked nothing of him but endurance. Competence was worse. Competence could indict a man without raising its voice. Competence could make his whole trade look like a disease of decorative rich fools.

He set the warped tray down by the door. Pink slime had dried in a crescent on his cuff. His palm carried the hot memory of the kettle through the cloth. Somewhere above them, on the steps between Patent Row and the river, people were still trying his word in their mouths because it amused them.

When he spoke again, there was no performance left in it.

"All right," he said. "I hear you."

Inez's expression did not change. It did not need to. She had not dragged him from the steps to win an argument. She had dragged him to put him in the same room as consequence and make him breathe it.

"Good," she said. "Now try learning faster than the city talks."


# Chapter 3

By the time Silas reached Ferrymouth, the word had got there ahead of him.

Two boys on the slip were leaning over the pilings, taking turns spitting into the tide-black water and saying bridelight in different voices to see whether the pink on the slime would answer better to mockery or praise. It kept its own counsel, a faint blush along the barnacled wood. The ferry chain rattled. Somebody cursed over a dropped crate. The whole landing smelled of wet rope, eel blood, hot algae mash, and the thick sweet rot the river sweated up once the day had gone warm enough.

Silas stood a moment under Junie's lintel with the damp still on his coat and Inez Cordero still in his ears. He had spent the walk down telling himself that a kitchen was a different country from Lantern Steps, that stew and family would take the edge off being told, in front of half a stair district, what his mouth had done to other people's lamps. Then Junie shouted from inside, without looking up, "If that's you, wash first. I can smell Patent Row through the broth."

He went in grinning because that was the shape his face knew best.

Junie's place was only half a room larger than the stove that ruled it. Bench tables crowded the front, already slick with the next rush's elbows. The back shelf bowed under crockery, onions, a jar of pepper gone soft with steam, and the ledger Junie kept as though God Himself might one day ask to see the arithmetic. She moved between pot and board with her sleeves rolled and her hair pinned up by two bone pins and a carpenter's pencil. Flour rode one cheek. The old split in her left eyebrow flashed pale whenever the steam licked it clean.

"You look peeled," she said. "Somebody finally take a knife to the varnish?"

"Only a civic-minded woman with a tray and excellent aim."

"Good. Basin's there."

He washed in the tin bowl by the door. The water went cloudy at once, paint stain and street grit making a skin on top. Junie slapped a heel of bread into his hand before he had dried properly, then pointed at the stool by the stove with the same spoon she used to move dumplings, scold Otis, and threaten drunks.

"Sit. Eat while it's hot, then you can explain why boys are making kissing noises at my pilings."

"That's commerce," Silas said, taking the bowl she set down.

"That's infection."

The eel stew was thick enough to stand the spoon nearly upright. Junie cooked it with potatoes, onions, and enough black pepper to bully the river mud back into something a Christian could love. He had not realized how hungry he was until the first mouthful burned him. It made his eyes water. Junie saw and took satisfaction from it.

"There," she said. "Now you're honest for one second."

He ate. The room pressed warm around him. Outside, a ferry bell clanged twice and men answered it with boots on plank. Inside, Junie took the ledger down, wiped her thumb on her apron, and opened it in front of him like a priest opening a book of sins.

"Before you flirt with that stew," she said, "we'll do the ledger version of love."

"That sounds indecent already."

"Everything with you does. Here."

Her finger, square and flour-dusted, tapped the page.

"Six evening meals you said you'd settle after the varnish job on Cork Lane. Four breakfasts. Two crowns eight for linseed and rag because you came whining about a client who wanted a sign to look expensive and clean at the same time. One bottle of lamp wash you swore you'd replace. And Havelock sent a boy by with a note asking whether his forty-three crowns had taken up residence under my roof."

Silas kept his spoon moving because if he stopped he would have to look at her too directly. "Havelock has a poetic turn."

"Havelock has a knife for a face and only writes when he's close enough to blood to smell it." She turned a page. "Then there is me."

"I had hoped I'd been rolled into the family rate."

"You have. That's why you're eating first."

The joke did not move her much. Junie, tired enough, got faster instead of louder. He knew that speed. It meant she was past temper and into tally.

"Pell came this morning," she said. "Not to eat. To smile. I distrust a landlord who smiles in daylight."

Silas looked up. "What does he want now?"

"What he always wants. More for less, with a compliment tied around it." She reached under the ledger and pulled out two folded papers, both gone soft at the corners from being handled. "He's calling the landing desirable after dark. Says people are coming from up-ridge to look at the pink water and take supper by the slips, so Ferrymouth has entered a brighter market. Those were his words. Brighter market. One of them was a woman in gloves who left half her dumplings to go stare at the pilings like the river had shown her its ankles."

Silas took the paper. Pell's clerk hand had laid the words down neat and mean. Revised terms. Basement storage review. Fuel hookup surcharge pending. He could almost hear the clerk taking pleasure in the flourishes.

"That's extortion in decent boots."

"It's rent," Junie said. "Which is extortion taught to keep its nails clean."

She set the second paper over the first. It was worse because it was familiar: fuel credit, numbers, dates, little polite hooks that ended in a choke.

"Aram Pell and his friends are carrying twenty-nine crowns on the kitchen now, with the arrears still growing because mash went up again this week. Half a crown more on each barrel, and the man telling me so had the nerve to say I might make it back selling to sightseers."

"Mash went up because the digester men are thieves."

"Mash went up because every silk fool in Gilt Crescent wants a pink jar on the mantel and the suppliers would rather strain fuel for parlors than send ugly barrels to kitchens." She took the papers back before the damp from his fingers ruined them. "The city gets one pretty light and suddenly stew is luxury."

He tried a shrug. "It'll pass."

Junie gave him a look so flat it could have leveled lumber.

"No. A rainstorm passes. A ferry passes. A fit passes. What this does is sit down at your table and ask for seconds."

From the front room came a burst of laughter, then someone calling for three bowls and more bread. Junie answered automatically, "Coming," and did not move yet.

"I'm not saying this so you'll pull that wounded face and promise the moon," she said. "I know what your promises are worth before you've eaten. I'm saying it because whatever you called that slime on Patent Row did not stay on Patent Row. It has got into rent paper and fuel paper, and if you think that is still a joke because a crowd clapped at you, then you're dumber than you dress."

Silas opened his mouth with something slick and easy ready to go, some line about civic miracles and landlords breeding faster than rats. Junie waited. He could see the fatigue at the root of her, not theatrical, not noble, just a woman measuring whether this week's pot would stay hot enough to make next week's. The easy line turned to ash.

"How close?" he asked.

Junie nodded once. That was all the reward truth got.

"Close enough that I watered the stew at noon and told the men the river eels were lean this season. Close enough that Otis doesn't get boots again until autumn unless a saint learns sums. Close enough that if Pell touches the basement hooks, I cook slower and serve less and then I stop being a kitchen and become a room that used to feed people."

She took his empty bowl before he had quite finished and tipped the last inch into the pot. She wasted nothing.

"Now," she said, already turning back to the stove, "if you've got any cleverness left in you, use it to carry trays."

***

The supper rush came in damp and hungry.

Ferrymouth always ate hard after the late boats. Men with rope burns on their palms and river silt drying pale on their cuffs crowded shoulder to shoulder with warehouse girls, porters, a washerwoman who brought her own hot sauce in a medicine bottle, and three clerks who had started coming down from Patent Row since the pink on the pilings made the district look dangerous in a way they could pay to admire and leave. Junie did not care where a mouth came from so long as it paid or had a tab honest enough to insult to its face.

Silas carried bowls because there was no graceful way not to. He moved between benches with stew heat slicking his neck and the smell of wet wool turning the room animal. Every time the door opened, the river shoved in a draft full of salt iron and lamp smoke. Every time it shut, the stove took the room back.

Otis arrived in the middle of it all, narrow-shouldered and freckled, one trouser knee dark with dock dirt as if the landing had signed him in mud. He came through the back with a slate under one arm and two chalk stubs in his pocket, kissed Junie on the sleeve because her hands were busy, and then saw Silas with the tray.

"Ma," he said, delighted, "Uncle Sile got demoted."

"He got hungry," Junie said.

"Same thing mostly," Silas told the boy.

Otis dropped onto the bench by the stove and started tracing letters into the steam on the tabletop with one blunt finger. He had copied sign hands since before he could read them straight, loving the curl of a flourish long before the word inside it meant anything. Silas watched him draw a crooked B, wipe it away, then try again.

"They were saying bridelight at school," Otis announced.

Junie snorted. "You're at school to learn numbers, not foolishness."

"I can learn both."

"That's the spirit of modern education," Silas said.

Otis ignored him. He was working the word in his mouth now, trying the corners of it. "Bride-light. Bride-blight. Bright-light. If everybody says a name wrong, does it answer wrong?"

Silas set down a bowl in front of a deckhand and nearly slopped broth onto the man's cuff.

"Sometimes," he said.

"What if you call it something mean?"

"Then it might get a bad temper."

"Like Ma?"

Junie did not look around from the stove. "Like the hand currently holding this spoon, yes."

The room laughed. Otis grinned, pleased with himself, then lowered his voice into a theatrical growl and addressed the damp windowpane. "Hear that, you nasty brideweed. Mind your manners."

From a front bench somebody called, "Brideweed's no good. My landlord's calling the alley behind our rooms Bridal Walk and wants another six bits a week for the privilege."

"You should charge him six bits each time he says it," Junie shot back.

Another voice said, "My little girl won't go to bed now unless the lamp's called bridelight first. Swears it burns prettier if you ask nice."

"Pretty doesn't boil potatoes," Junie said.

"Tell that to Gilt Crescent," Silas muttered.

But that was the thing. He heard the word moving through the room the way steam moved, taking shape against every colder surface it met. It was not solemn or magical. It was just everywhere: in a deckhand's complaint, in a mother's bedtime trick, in Otis testing syllables with chalk on his thumb. He had always loved that part, the way a phrase could jump the track you laid for it and go breeding in strangers. He had built a living on that leap. But here, with Junie knocking the ash down in the stove because she was trying to make fuel behave itself, it did not feel like applause. It felt like weather inside the house.

One of the Patent Row clerks, a nervous young man with a collar already wilted into surrender, lifted his spoon toward Silas as if toasting a performer.

"Mr. Vale, is it true the moss prefers romance?"

"It prefers damp masonry and a public with no discipline," Silas said.

"That sounds like romance to me," the clerk said, and got enough laughter to encourage him. "You ought to publish instructions. Ladies are buying cuttings off the back steps."

Junie swung around so fast the ladle clicked the pot.

"If any lady brings a live cutting in here for fashion, I will feed it to her. Eat your stew."

The clerk ducked his head and obeyed. The washerwoman with the medicine-bottle sauce laughed so hard she coughed into her kerchief and waved away concern with an irritated flick, as if pity was more offensive than choking.

Otis had abandoned the tabletop and moved on to his slate. He was writing names in a column now, mouthing them as he went.

"Bridelight," he said.

Scratch.

"Brideweed."

Scratch.

"Kissmoss."

Junie pointed with the ladle. "No."

"That one was off the ferry," he said defensively. "They were saying it by the railing."

"Then the ferry can raise you."

Silas took the slate from him, more sharply than he meant to. Otis blinked.

"Easy," Junie said.

The chalk dust came off on Silas's thumb. The letters were childish, all stems and panic, but they were letters. The boy was not performing for a crowd. He was sorting the city into marks he could keep. For one ridiculous second Silas saw the future of it, words handed around from bench to ferry to schoolroom to lamp hook until no one remembered which mouth had first sent them out. He had always counted on that forgetting. It was the way a man like him survived being borrowed by richer ones.

Otis watched his face and went quieter, not sulking yet, just uncertain.

"Did I do it wrong?"

Silas handed the slate back. "No. You did it exactly right."

"Then why are you looking like that?"

"Because your uncle's face has poor manners."

Otis accepted this as likely. He bent again to the slate, slower now. Junie shoved a bowl toward Silas without looking at him.

"Take table three before they mutiny," she said. Then, softer, only for him, "And stop acting surprised. Children put everything in their mouths. That's how the world gets inside."

He served the last of the rush with that lodged under his ribs like a fish bone. By the time the benches started emptying, the air in the kitchen had changed. It was not calmer, only thinner, the heat broken by the opening and shutting of the door. Outside, more people were strolling the landing than the hour deserved, pausing by the rail to glance at the blush on the pilings as if the river were running a show for them personally. Junie watched them through the front pane and made a face like she'd tasted soap.

"There," she said, wiping the lip of a bowl with her thumb. "Your admirers. Maybe they'll pay Pell for us."

"I'd sooner ask gulls."

"Gulls at least arrive hungry and honest."

***

Damaris Pike came in after the worst of it, carrying the damp evening in the hem of her widow's black and a stack of folded sheets under one arm. The black had gone shiny at the elbows and seams from hard wear and too much leaning over proofs. Ink stained the side of her right hand where she rested it when she wrote fast. She smiled at Silas with the chipped front tooth fully in view, as if damage was only another kind of typeface.

"If there is any coffee left that has not already confessed to murder," she said, "I will take it."

"You'll take what the pot thinks of you," Junie said.

"That's all any editor ever gets."

She settled at the end of the nearest bench without asking whether the room wanted a newspaper woman in it. Damaris seldom asked permission for entry into any scene she could profit by. She flattened her stack of papers with one palm, careful even in haste not to crease the columns she meant to use later, then looked past Junie to Silas as though she had been holding a place for him in her head all day.

"You have had an excellent week for ruining other people's certainty," she said. "Sit down."

"You say that as though it's a salaried post."

"Everything becomes one if I can print it often enough."

Junie set down a cup. The coffee looked punitive. Damaris drank, winced, and approved of it with a little nod.

"I've got a proposition," she said. "Not the respectable kind. If I wanted respectability I'd marry badly again."

"I thought widowhood was agreeing with you."

"It does. That's why I protect it."

She pulled one of the folded sheets free and pushed it across the table. The headline was his own trouble turned blocky and enthusiastic. It was not lies, exactly. It was worse, the sort of truth that had learned to dance in public.

"First installment sold out by noon," she said. "Newsboys were making up extra scandal in the street because I hadn't given them enough copy to mispronounce. The town wants the moss, but more than that it wants the argument around the moss. It wants to know what to call the damned thing next and whether the calling will stain somebody's curtains or marriage prospects. You're attached to the question now, unfortunately for your peace."

Silas folded the sheet shut before he had to read his own name again.

"I'm touched to hear it."

"You should be paid to hear it." She leaned in. Her voice quickened when she smelled circulation. "Give me a weekly series. Your rounds, your guesses, your failures, the district nonsense, the respectable panic, the way every grocer in town is suddenly pretending he discovered luminescent romance in a drainage problem. We make a serial of it. You're the man chasing a contrary bloom through a city too stupid and too greedy to agree with itself.
