I print six weeks and if the thing is still changing by then, I print six more."

"And what do I get besides libel performed in installments?"

"Money. Not enough, because I'm not a patent house. But money. Your name in every breakfast room worth stealing from. The right to keep your own mouth. And I don't ask exclusivity because I'm not an idiot. Exclusivity is for men who think ownership and understanding are the same appetite."

Junie, wiping down the next table, snorted without turning. Damaris flicked two fingers at her in acknowledgment.

"How generous of you to admit your limits," Silas said.

"I know my limits exactly. My presses can't force a city to obedience. They can only make disobedience impossible to ignore." She wrapped both hands around the coffee cup. For one brief second, the theatrics thinned. "Take the wrong money and you won't even have your own lies left."

That brought him fully to attention.

"Who's shopping?"

Damaris gave him a look of false innocence and real impatience.

"Who do you think? Wren has been buying wall space like a churchman buying candles ahead of a storm. He's not waiting to see whether this becomes useful. He already knows it's useful. He'll want one stable name with cuffs on it, a packet design, maybe a parlor story for the better rooms and some practical lie for the kitchens. He'll want you because the public has already put your mouth on the thing."

"You seem cheerful about that."

"I'm cheerful about anything that sells. That's not the same as blind." She took another swallow of coffee and shivered at the bitterness. "Listen to me closely, Silas. If you won't let me print you, then at least understand the condition you're in. You're not outside this story anymore. You're the part people point at with dirty fingers while the rich men measure shelf space."

He looked over at Junie. She had moved to the back shelf and was counting coins into stacks that kept collapsing against one another because the table was warped. She was too far to hear every word, but not far enough to miss the shape of them. Otis, on the bench by the stove, had fallen half asleep over his slate, his cheek marked white with chalk and the last unfinished word smudged under his hand.

Silas said, "What does your weekly series pay?"

Damaris named a number. It was not insulting. It also was not salvation.

He made a face.

"Exactly," she said. "Enough to matter in a boardinghouse, not enough to bribe a creditor with proper ambition. But it would keep you loose. Loose has value."

"Loose gets hungry."

"So does tied up. One of those conditions only fools respectable people."

He rubbed his thumb over the newsprint edge. It came away gray.

"You want sensation."

"Of course I do." She bared the chipped tooth again. "I'm in the sensation trade. But I also prefer a city with more than one throat. Wren prefers choir practice."

Junie came over then and lifted the empty cup before Damaris had quite finished it.

"If you're done buying my brother in theories," she said, "I need the bench back for tomorrow's potatoes."

"I'm trying to buy him in installments," Damaris said. "Entirely different sin."

"All his sins arrive whole."

Damaris rose, gathering the papers under her arm. At the door she paused and glanced once toward Otis asleep by the stove. Something in her face altered, not softer exactly, but less amused.

"If Wren comes," she said without ornament, "he won't come for a story. He'll come for a leash. Price that properly."

Then she was gone into the landing dusk, black hem catching a little of the damp, already halfway back to print in her mind.

Silas watched through the pane as she turned up toward Patent Row, quick even in widow's skirts, one hand holding her papers to her side like she was carrying a loaf that mattered.

Junie said, "Well?"

"Well what?"

"Don't play stupid indoors. It spreads. Are you going to write for her?"

"Maybe."

"You hate deadlines."

"I hate mornings. Deadlines merely accompany them."

Junie scraped Otis's slate clean with the side of her thumbnail and held it up so he could see the wet chalk paste. "I hate being fashionable. Looks like we all have our crosses."

***

They closed late because the gawkers at the rail kept drifting in for one last bowl, one last beer, one last excuse to sit near the pink water. By the time Junie shot the back bolt, the kitchen smelled of old steam, cabbage, dishwater gone gray with grease, and the coppery tang the fuel tank gave off when it was low enough to breathe air between gulps. Otis slept all the way through the washing-up, curled on the bench with his feet tucked under him and his hand still blotched white from chalk.

Silas was drying bowls when the knock came.

It was not the impatient hammer of a dockman or the sly scratch of somebody hoping to be admitted after hours. It was three measured taps, each one placed.

Junie and Silas looked at each other.

"If that's Pell, I'll throw the wash water on him," she said.

Silas opened the door.

Gideon Wren stood under the lintel as if the damp had been informed in advance not to touch him. His coat was dark and fine without showiness. His collar had survived the whole day unrumpled. Silver had begun to take the hair at his temples, and his gloves were the pale kid kind a sensible man would never wear within half a mile of Ferrymouth unless he trusted that dirt belonged to other people.

"Mr. Vale," he said. "Miss Vale. I am sorry for the hour."

Junie, elbow-deep in the wash basin, said, "No you're not, but come in if you're paying for the floor after."

If he minded the insult, he did not show it. Gideon removed one glove finger by finger before accepting the chair Silas pulled out. He laid a leather folder on the table and looked once around the kitchen, not with disgust, which would have been easier to dismiss, but with the assessing calm of a man who counted rooms by what leverage they held.

"Mrs. Pike has likely been here already," he said.

"Your spies are quick."

"Your routes are obvious."

Silas sat opposite him. Junie kept washing, metal clinking softly in the basin. The sound made the pause feel longer.

"If you've come to buy a bowl," Silas said, "we're shut."

"I have come to buy a solution."

"That sounds expensive."

"It is. Which is why I am here after dark rather than sending a clerk."

He opened the folder. The paper inside was thick, good rag stock, the sort of page meant to make a man feel he had already improved by touching it. An envelope sat on top, unsealed but heavy enough that the table noticed when Gideon set it down.

"The city is filling with provisional names," Gideon said. "Some comic, some sentimental, some merely stupid. That was tolerable for two days. It is less tolerable now. Households cannot cook by novelty. Merchants cannot package novelty. Investors certainly cannot scale it."

Junie barked a humorless laugh from the basin. "Funny. Households were cooking by one of his novelties before your investors smelled it."

Gideon inclined his head to her without turning. "Precisely. Which is why it must be stabilized before the damage compounds."

Silas rested his forearms on the table. The wood was still tacky from being wiped in haste.

"You say stabilize. I hear collar."

"Collars are for dogs. Markets require repetition." Gideon slid the top sheet across. "Verdancy House proposes a retainer. Exclusive. You will investigate, test, and devise the stable public designation for the spill-born chorus strain presently circulating under various street names. All public-facing applications of that designation, and any associated visual campaign, run through my office for the duration of the agreement."

Silas scanned the page. The language had teeth hidden in velvet. Exclusive consultation. Exclusive derivation rights. Exclusive use of names discovered, devised, or rendered commercially actionable in the course of investigation. There it was. Not just what he would invent. What he would hear.

"You don't even know what it is yet," he said.

"I know what it needs to become."

"And what is that?"

"Askable. Repeatable. Saleable in more than one district. Dull enough to survive success."

Silas laughed despite himself. "Christ, that's a eulogy."

"It is commerce." Gideon folded his hands. "You mistake liveliness for durability, Mr. Vale. A city may gossip in twenty tongues at breakfast. By supper it still needs one label on the packet."

Junie set a dripping pot upside down on the board with more force than the pot deserved.

"My customers need the barrel to come full and the flame to keep steady. The packet can go drown."

Gideon looked at her then. "And when the barrel fails because every corner of the city is pulling the strain in a different direction with nursery rhymes and alley jokes, who pays for that failure? Not the boys at the railing. Not Mrs. Pike. Women like you do."

Junie went still for one beat, hands wet to the wrists. Silas knew that stillness too. It was more dangerous than shouting.

"Do not tell me what women like me pay for," she said. "You're in the room because we already know."

Gideon accepted the hit as if it had only confirmed a premise.

"Then we are spared the sentimental portion of the discussion."

He pushed the envelope forward.

"Sixty crowns on signature. Further payment on stabilization and launch. You clear your pigment debt. You breathe. Miss Vale gets the benefit of a brother not currently one insult away from having his credit shuttered across town. In return, I buy priority and discipline. You do not freelance the matter to the papers. You do not perform fresh names in public for applause. You do the work, privately, until there is something fit to place."

The number was sixty.

The number landed in Silas like a fist to the sternum, not because it was impossible, but because it was exact enough to start spending itself at once. Havelock's forty-three crowns gone. Something toward Junie's paper. Coal for the stove. Boots for Otis when autumn came cold again. A week without inventing nonsense for men who called their own greed innovation.

He covered that first leap with a smirk.

"And if the thing refuses your discipline?"

"Everything refuses at first." Gideon spoke as though patience were simply a cleaner form of force. "That does not make refusal sovereign."

"Suppose the neighborhoods keep their own names."

"They will. In kitchens, on stoops, in the little domestic republics where people insist on being local. I am not at war with private habit. I am concerned with the public channel. Shelves. Posters. Orders shouted across counters. The words repeated by people who need to buy the same thing twice and find it waiting for them." He paused. "Chaos earns fascination. Routine earns revenue. Your gift, I think, lies in helping the first cross over into the second."

That was very nearly flattering. Gideon knew it. Junie knew it too; Silas could feel her attention sharpen behind him.

He said, "Damaris offered a weekly series."

"Of course she did."

"Without exclusivity."

"Because she sells heat. I sell persistence." Gideon's voice stayed even, almost devotional in its civility. "Mrs. Pike wants the city excited. Excitement is brief. I want the city trained. Training lasts."

"That's an ugly word."

"Only to men who profit from improvisation."

Silas leaned back. The chair complained under him. On the bench, Otis sighed in his sleep and rolled one chalk-smeared hand against his cheek. Junie reached to pull the blanket higher over the boy without interrupting the line of her body toward the table. The stove ticked as it cooled. Somewhere below the floorboards, the fuel line gave a hollow glug, a sound like a throat trying not to embarrass itself.

He thought, absurdly and unwillingly, of Inez in the blue lamp light on Lantern Steps, the severe patience in her face when she had held out those damaged trays as if they were evidence from a murder no one else had yet admitted was one. He thought of Junie reading Pell's pretty extortion in a room that smelled of pepper and dishwater. He thought of Otis writing the city into columns he could carry to school.

He said, "That clause there. Names discovered in the course of investigation. That's broad as a wharf."

"It is meant to be broad."

"Meaning if I hear something in a kitchen, it becomes yours."

"Meaning if you intend to use something in the campaign I am financing, I will not pay to be second in line to your conscience."

"There you are," Silas said softly. "That's the part with the knife."

Gideon did not deny it.

"Mr. Vale, if this were a moral matter, I would have sent a parson. It is a market matter. The city is already naming the strain whether you sign or not. The only question is whether the process remains wasteful, dangerous, and available to every fool within earshot, or whether someone competent imposes sequence on it."

"Competent," Junie said. "He means paid."

"Often the two conditions are not enemies," Gideon said.

Silas looked down at the contract again. The ink held steady, black and impeccable, while the room around it breathed stew and soap and poverty. The paper seemed almost indecent on Junie's scarred table. That, more than the number, made the choice feel dirty. Money always looked clean when richer men brought it into poorer rooms.

"You'd have me work in private," he said.

"Until the public act matters."

"And the stable name. You already have one in mind?"

"Several possibilities. I am paying you because none yet has both grace and obedience."

"Obedience," Silas repeated.

"From the market, not the moss."

"I don't think you know the difference."

For the first time, something a shade colder entered Gideon's face.

"I know the only difference that survives distribution."

Junie dried her hands and came to stand beside the stove. She did not tell him yes. She did not tell him no. That would have been mercy, and they had passed the point where mercy came cheap.

"Read it all before you sign," she said. "If you sign."

Silas almost laughed at the if. Not because it was false. Because it was still being offered.

He read. He read the clauses twice. They smelled wrong the way some rooms smelled wrong before you spotted the mildew, all sweetness first and rot underneath. Exclusivity. Retainer. Materials. Public designation. All public-facing applications. Discovery rights. A man's mouth turned into an intake valve.

He could hear Damaris saying leash.

He could hear Junie saying the thing had sat down at the table asking for seconds.

He could hear his own empty purse louder than any of them.

When he took up the pen Gideon offered, the nib scratched on the page because the air had swelled the fibers. His signature looked too decorative for the occasion, all the old flourish still clinging to the S of his name like vanity refusing to die when called.

He signed anyway.

Gideon slid the envelope across at once, efficient as a banker closing a drawer. He rose, replaced his glove, and collected the contract with a care that made it plain he already considered the thing filed inside the future.

"You have done wisely," he said.

"I've done expensively," Silas said.

"Wisdom often presents that way at first."

Junie opened the door for him with a politeness so bare it functioned as insult. Gideon stepped back into the damp lane, his shoes avoiding the worst of the puddled grit without seeming to look down. Then he was gone, a dark clean stroke moving up toward the better-lit streets.

Silas stayed at the table with the envelope in his hand. The paper was thick, warm from Gideon's pocket. Junie did not ask to see inside it. She knew the weight of money without counting by touch. Otis slept on, breathing milk and chalk and child sweat into the cooling room.

"Well," Junie said at last.

Silas thumbed the flap open and looked at the notes. Real notes. Enough to turn several kinds of disaster slightly sideways.

"Yeah," he said.

That was all. Nothing noble fit in the kitchen after a choice like that. The stove ticked. The basin water slowly lost its last heat. Outside, somebody on the landing laughed and said bridelight as if it were only a pretty thing to have in the mouth.

Silas sat with Gideon's money in his hand and knew at least this much clearly: whatever the moss answered to next, his need would be in the word.


# Chapter 4

By the time Silas reached the first plank walk above Sump Ward, his trouser cuffs had taken on the ward's smell: warm mud, lamp slime, old soap, and the sweet-sour breath of digesters working too hard in weather that had not yet learned mercy. The boards under him sweated through their grain. Below, the drainage cut moved like thick tea, carrying peelings, reed scraps, and a pale skim of algae that caught every color the ward gave it and made none of them cleaner.

He had come looking for a useful theft. He would not have called it theft, even to himself. He would have called it a principle, a pattern, one true hinge hidden under all the neighborhood babble, something he could carry back uphill and sell to men who liked their mysteries trimmed flat and lettered in gilt. He wanted one good phrase, perhaps two. He wanted enough to make the runaway moss stop embarrassing everybody by acting like a crowd.

Aunt Vero waited at the landing with a tobacco tin open in one hand, fennel seeds shining against the metal. She crunched one between her back teeth, eyed him with the mild contempt she seemed to reserve for men who still believed dry shoes were a form of virtue, and tipped the tin toward him without generosity. Her thick white braid was looped over one shoulder. One eye drifted a little off him and back again, as if even her face was not prepared to spend both its sights on Silas Vale.

"He'll slip in the first ten yards," she said to Inez. "Let him go under once. The ward might improve him."

Inez had her dark hair wrapped in a work scarf the color of old olives, though the damp had already darkened it nearer black. She stood easy on the planks, weight where the nails still held, one hand on a tray strap cutting across her shoulder. There was nothing theatrical in the way she waited. He had seen women in Patent Row shops perform indifference like it was lace. Hers was laboring indifference. It had things to do after he left.

"If he falls," she said, "you can fish out his boots. That's the expensive part."

Silas put two fingers to the brim of an absent hat. "Nice to see my education has become a public project."

Vero snapped the tin shut. "Only because funerals cost."

He might have answered that with something better if the air had not been so wet it felt poured. Instead he smiled the smile that had sold soap powders, eel bait, and one regrettable line of patent corset hooks, and watched it die against Inez's face without leaving a mark.

"You wanted me to look," he said to her. "I'm looking."

"No," she said. "You wanted a clean answer. Looking is the part before that."

Then she turned and started along the catwalk without checking whether he followed. Of course he followed. Vero gave him a small shove between the shoulder blades for luck or insult. In Sump Ward the two seemed to travel together.

The first glow house sat half below grade, built against brick that stayed damp even in dry weeks. Inside, the heat struck like a body. Fermentation pipes ticked along the walls. Wet burlap hung over racks. Rows of shallow trays gave off different colors the way tired people gave off moods, not one light but several, all related and none obedient. A low green lived close to the floor where the cook pots sat. On the shelves above it ran a colder wash of blue-white, bright enough to find a dropped needle. In the far corner, a patch the size of a dinner plate pulsed whitish gold with an ugly little hunger that made Silas think of irons left too long on cloth.

Three women worked the room without wasting a glance on him until they had decided he was worth it. The first, a heavy-hipped woman with one shoe unlaced because her left ankle had swollen against the damp again, lifted a pot lid with a rag and leaned her face into the steam as if it could tell her whether supper would stretch. Her wedding ring hung on a black cord over her apron instead of on her hand. "That's him?" she asked Inez, not lowering the lid yet. "Patent Row mouth."

"That's him," Inez said.

The woman looked Silas up and down, taking especial note of the yellowed paint caught in the moons of his nails. "Marta Bell," she said. "If you learn something useful, it'll be the first clean miracle I've seen come down from that hill."

Silas spread a hand. "I try not to promise miracles before noon."

Marta snorted and pointed with her chin to the green bed crouched beneath her stove ledge. "Potback," she said. "Here, anyway. We call it that because it sits low and holds. Doesn't spit. Doesn't race. You can leave beans on it and go slap a child or fetch wash and come back to food instead of cinders."

She said the name toward the bed the way some people spoke to a mule: not tenderly, but with a settled agreement. The green answered by evening out along the tray, a dull rich color under the iron, heat more felt in the shins than seen.

Silas crouched. The growth smelled faintly of pond skin and hot copper. "And if somebody called it something else?"

"Then I'd have to cook by whatever foolishness they chose." Marta set the lid down harder than needed. "Last week boys on the upper stairs kept shouting that pink nonsense of yours. By dusk the edge of this tray had gone rosy and skittish. Took twice the mash to keep it under the pot, and the stew still sulked."

Inez crossed her arms. "Patent Row heard color and thought decoration. Here color means your supper keeps or doesn't."

Silas glanced toward the blue-white shelf. "So what do you call that one?"

"Threadshow," said the second woman before Inez could answer. She sat on an upturned crate with a baby pinned to one hip and a needle flashing in her right hand. Her spectacles had lost one arm and were tied in place behind her ear with black thread to match the mourning dress she had cut down twice already. She had a habit of wetting the thread between her lips before every stitch, quick and absent as prayer. "It shows black on black. Good for mending cuffs and finding lice eggs if the school sends them home again. Cool enough that the baby can stare at it without blistering himself stupid."

The baby, offended by being included in the sentence, kicked once and got his foot tangled in her apron tape. She untwisted him without looking down.

"Nessa Quill," she said. "Don't write my name anywhere."

"I wasn't writing," Silas said.

"Yet."

He looked to Inez for rescue and got none. She went to the blue shelf, lifted a tray, checked its underside, and set it back with a touch so practiced he could see the whole weight of the motion before it happened. "Threadshow is bright, not warm," she said. "On Lantern Steps they use a cousin bed for reading slates. Here it stays in kitchens and sewing corners. Different feed. Different trim. Different name because different use."

"But still the same family," Silas said. "Near enough."

Inez's mouth flattened the way other people's might have widened. "Near enough is what men say before they break something that wasn't theirs."

Marta laughed without kindness. Nessa bit the thread off with her teeth.

Silas stood. The room's heat had taken hold under his shirt. "All right," he said. "Say I've been a fool. Not hard to say. But if you're trying to keep the whole city from pulling these beds every which way, doesn't that argue for fewer names, not more? One for heat. One for bright. One for the nasty hot bastard in the corner."

That got him his first full look from Inez since the landing. It was not affection, and it was not even respect. But it was a cleaner attention than contempt, which at that moment felt almost luxurious.

"Come on," she said.

He hated being hauled like a schoolboy and followed anyway.

They went back into the open ward where the afternoon had thickened toward evening without cooling. Wash lines crossed the narrow spaces between houses. Somebody above beat a rug against a railing with the grim concentration of a woman imagining a face in it. Children moved on the planks in bare or near-bare feet, careless only in the way the practiced are careless. Silas had to watch where he set his boots. The boards had whole dialects in them: slick, split, sound, soft, rotten at the edge, safe if you were born to it and a lie if you were not.

Inez took him over a narrow run above the drainage cut. The planks bowed under his height. She did not look back until his sole skidded on a patch of green-black sheen and his shoulder clipped the rail.

"Center nails," she said.

"You might've mentioned that before I started negotiating with the river."

"You knew enough to bring boots. I thought you might know enough to see the nails."

She said it without triumph. That was the part that bit. Mockery was easy company. Her accuracy made him feel large and expensive in all the wrong ways.

At the bend of the walk a warning board had blistered up from the wet. The old paint had bubbled around the words until the letters looked diseased. Below the sign, a ribbon of pale blue moss ran the length of the catwalk edge, thin as knifing and bright where the wood went treacherous. A boy of maybe ten balanced on the far end, fishing bent nails out of a tin with the solemn greed of a magpie. He had one ear patched with a scrap of red flannel and the patient expression of a child used to adults talking over him while he made himself useful.

"Timo," Inez said, "what's that edge called here?"

Without looking up the boy said, "Stepblue. Near the Quinter walk it's shinshow because old Mrs. Quinter kicks every dark thing she sees."

"And why not potback?" Inez asked.

Timo looked at Silas then, pitying him the way only children could. "Because you don't cook on a walkway."

Silas barked a laugh before he could stop it. The boy returned to his nail tin, satisfied that instruction had been accomplished.

Inez touched the warning sign. The board sagged under her fingers. "This one should've been redone three rains ago."

"You've got the paint?"

"No."

"Brush?"

"No."

He gave her a look and got the smallest ghost of weariness in return. "So you've got a rotten sign and no paint," he said. "Very efficient."

"We've got me," she said, and pulled a clasp knife from the pocket of her skirt. "And apparently we've got you."

She handed him the knife handle first. It was not trust. It was work.

There were old skills in his hands that even shame could not chase out. He scraped the blistered paint back to wood while she knelt along the edge and trimmed the overreaching stepblue with short hard cuts, keeping the living strip from swallowing the board. The knife rasped. Her little pruning shears clicked. Below them the cut water knocked bottle glass against stone. It was the kind of labor that made speech optional without making company false.

He found a stub of blue chalk tucked behind the sign post and sketched the letters in before he cut deeper with the blade: KEEP TO CENTER. LOW EDGE ROTS. The wording was not pretty or clever. It was meant to be read by somebody carrying wash with one hand and a sleeping child with the other.

When he straightened, Inez was looking at the board instead of at him. The damp had darkened the scarf at the nape of her neck. He could see the crescent burn scar inside her right wrist when she lifted the shears, a pale curve against brown skin, old hurt gone practical.

"You left room," she said.

"For what?"

"For the blue. Most sign men cram the whole board because empty space offends them."

"Empty space offends landlords. I'm less principled."

Her mouth shifted, not quite a smile. "That might be the first sensible thing you've said."

He should have answered with something easy. Instead he heard himself say, "I know how water eats paint."

"You do?"

"I make my living on walls that sweat."

That made her look at him directly again. There was no flirtation in it. It was something slower, a measuring. She stepped close enough to brace the board with her shoulder while he cut the last groove of the T. Her arm brushed his side once, by necessity. Even through wet cloth he felt the steadiness of her, the way she held pressure without advertising it. Patent Row liked women arranged for looking. This one had been arranged by use, which was not the same thing and did worse things to his concentration.

When the letters were done she ran a thumb through one groove, testing depth. "It'll hold a few weeks."

"If your nail thief doesn't steal the whole post."

Timo looked up, scandalized. "I only steal bent ones."

"A moralist," Silas said.

"He sells them by weight," Inez said. "To buy sugar twists."

The boy shrugged. "My sister likes the red kind."

There it was, the human reason tucked under petty crime. Silas liked him at once and distrusted the liking, because his trade had taught him how quickly affection could turn into harvest.

They moved on.

The next kitchen stood open to the walk, hotter than the first and more crowded. A laundry frame leaned over one wall. Flatirons sat on a brick shelf. The light in here was the whitish gold he had noticed before, but held in smaller dishes, trimmed close and mean. It gave off a dry heat that bit the backs of his fingers when he passed too near.

A young woman was slicing stale bread into thin soldiers for a child at the table, making each piece the same length with a precision that suggested the child would reject any variation as personal insult. Her sleeves were rolled unevenly, and on the crook of one elbow she had drawn a row of tiny blue stars in ink to keep the older boy from using the skin as a slate. The older boy sat with his tongue pressed out the gap in his teeth, pushing the bread soldiers into rank instead of eating them.

"Ruthie Cale," the woman said, glancing once at Silas and then back at the bread. "If you've come to name anything, name my husband home before midnight."

"Can't manage husbands," Silas said. "Too many existing claims."

"Shame. That's the only miracle I'd pay cash for."

Inez nodded toward the dishes of gold-white growth. "Tell him."

Ruthie slapped the older boy's hand away from the nearest tray without turning. She did it by sound. "Fingerbiter," she said. "Because if you hear the word, you don't pet the pretty lamp. It runs hot enough for irons and kettle plates. Good when the baby cough keeps us up and I want hot water fast. Bad for every fool child and every fool man who thinks white means gentle."

The older boy scowled at Silas as if the category might be shared.

Silas held his hand over the tray and felt the bite of it immediately. "And if somebody called it threadshow?"

Ruthie gave him a flat look. "Then I'd scorch the shirts and leave the nits alive."

"And if they called it bridelight?" Inez asked.

Ruthie made a face like she'd bitten soap. "Then it goes vain. Lifts color toward the edges. Wants looking at. Doesn't stay where it's fed."

Silas watched the tray. The growth did have that restless gleam at the rim, a dangerous prettiness like polished metal on a crowded dock. He thought of Patent Row shopgirls leaning in to admire a lamp by color alone and hated them a little for being convenient, which was unfair and therefore familiar.

The boy at the table finally shoved one bread soldier into his mouth and said around it, "Ma says pearlglass is for people who don't do their own shirts."

Ruthie did not hush him. "Ma says lots of true things."

Inez looked at Silas. "Do you want one word now?"

He looked from the irons to the child to the tray and imagined some high-window showroom trying to sell all three rooms at once with one syllable and a ribbon. He could do it. That was the filthy part. He could hear the sentence forming already, smooth as oil: a household radiance, bright for every need. Men had paid him to tell worse lies.

"No," he said, and because the answer surprised him it came out plainer than most things he said. "Not one."

Inez's face did not soften. But the pressure in the room changed, slightly, the way a pipe changed pitch when some obstruction had been cleared.

She took him from there into the back propagation run, where the house walls sweated green and the trays sat in tiers over warm water channels. Here she stopped talking to the residents and began talking to him as if he might, in limited quantities, be able to follow.

"The hill men hear names as labels," she said. "Something to print on a box, something to shout once and own. Here a name tells you how to touch it, where to set it, what not to trust it near, whether it'll cost too much mash for what it gives back. Sometimes it tells you whose hand got burned learning that. Sometimes it tells you what hour to feed it if you don't want it waking the house."

She reached past him to pinch away a pearled clump trying to climb the lip of a tray. Her knuckles brushed his shirt. The motion was unadorned and made his skin pay ridiculous attention.

"So the names are instructions."

"And memory."

"Same thing, half the time."

"Not if you're the one who gets to forget."

That landed. It landed because she said it without heat, which left him no cheap shape to kick against. The people up on Patent Row did get to forget. They forgot what kept them lit. They forgot what happened below the ridge unless the below produced a spectacle worth climbing down for. He made part of his living feeding that forgetfulness better words.

At the last house they climbed to a lofted walk over a line of wash tubs and open barrels where spent mash soured for reuse. The evening was coming on. Across the ward the lights were waking in their various tempers: low cooking greens under stove ledges, sewing blues in windows, the dangerous little whites banked close where irons waited, and stranger mixed colors where somebody's household had argued with itself or the neighborhood had cross-bred too much talk into one poor tray. From up here the place did not look chaotic. It looked annotated.

Silas leaned on the rail and then straightened at once when it gave a wet complaint under his weight.

"You'll go through one of these days," he said.

"Not today."

"That's not a system."

"It is if you know the boards."

The answer irritated him because it was true and because it refused the kind of truth he could bottle. He looked out over the lights again. On the ridge, even from here, Patent Row shone in flatter colors, a few rich signs forcing themselves above the rest. Down here the ward glimmered by need. No one had bothered to make it graceful for distance. It only became beautiful once you understood what each ugly little patch was doing for the hands around it.

"Suppose," he said slowly, "some bastard with wall space decides to press one name hard enough to drag the whole city after it. Signs, handbills, newsboys, drunks, the lot."

"That bastard wouldn't be the first."

"Suppose he does it well."

She turned to him. The river air had found a little cool at last, enough to lift the damp hair loose against her temple. "Then people get hurt before they get obedient. Which is why I asked you to come look instead of standing uphill and talking about elegance."

"Asked," he said.

"All right. Allowed."

"That's kinder."

"No, it isn't."

He laughed once under his breath. It felt rusty. "You really don't miss much."

"I miss plenty," she said. "I just don't enjoy it."

For a moment neither of them spoke. Below, Ruthie Cale's older boy began reciting some schoolroom numbers in a sing-song moan until somebody told him to either mean them or shut his mouth. Farther off, a ferry bell carried over the cut. The ward smelled of soap, hot metal, cabbage, and the thick vegetal sweetness of the grow houses. It should have offended him. Instead it made Patent Row seem oddly bloodless, all those clean gloves and dry ledgers pretending they were not built on wet work.

He put his fingers on the repaired sign groove still ghosted in his skin. "If I said I understood now," he said, "you'd know I was lying."

"Yes."

"If I said I understood more than I did this morning?"

She considered him long enough to make flattery impossible. "That might be true."

He looked at her wrist where the old burn scar curved pale as moonlit bone. He looked away before the look turned stupid. "Then tell me what the useful next step is."

"For whom?"

There it was again. The clean knife. He had half a dozen answers prepared by habit and none he wanted to hear himself say. He could have said that the next step was for him, for the contract in his pocket notebook, for the advance already walking around inside his debts wearing respectable shoes, for the part of him that still believed there had to be a single key because single keys got you paid.

He let most of that rot where it stood.

"For the moss," he said at last. "For the people cooking by it. For not making things worse than I've already made them."

Something in her expression changed then, though not into trust. Trust would have been childish and unearned. What came instead was narrower and more useful.

"You come back tomorrow without trying to coin anything," she said. "You listen when people answer instead of shopping their mouths for what will look good on a wall. You carry trays if I tell you to carry trays. You mend boards if they're failing. You don't call any bed by a name you haven't heard used in the room where it's living. And if you get a clever sentence in your head, you let it die there."

"That's a punishing apprenticeship."

"Then don't take it."

He watched the ward lights settle deeper into themselves as dusk took the sky apart above them. Potback sat under one stove, threadshow glowed in a window, stepblue found the wet edge of a walk, and fingerbiter banked mean under an iron plate. None of them sounded elegant. That was not their failure. That was their virtue. They did not ask to be admired first.

Up on Patent Row, a man could rename a thing until it forgot its mother. Down here the words had mud on them and instructions in their teeth.

"I'll come back," he said.

Inez nodded once, as if accepting delivery of a bundle she had not ordered and might still return. "Early. Before the heat turns everything stupid."

"That includes me, I assume."

"Especially you."

He should have reached for swagger then, some filthy little bow to leave the exchange on familiar ground. Instead he only stood there with her a moment longer, shoulder to shoulder above the ward, looking at the different lights doing their different work. The attraction he felt was not clean and not especially comfortable. It had very little to do with her mouth and a great deal to do with the fact that she could walk him through a whole city of living argument and never once confuse knowledge with performance.

When she went back along the planks, he followed her center-nails careful this time, watching where she set her feet and hating how much he liked being taught. Behind them the repaired warning board caught the first full wash of evening and held its letters plain against the damp.


# Chapter 5

Gideon had dressed Patent Row for a wedding between commerce and obedience.

By noon the old cooperage wall had been skinned in fresh lime and damp plaster, smooth as a rich man's lie. Verdancy House bunting drooped from the scaffold in strips of pale green silk that looked refined from the middle of the street and fungus-sick when a person got close enough to see the water marks. Two hired boys in white gloves stood guard over trays of handbills and paper crowns. A string quartet, paid well enough to hate itself, kept sawing out something solemn while the whole block sweated under them. Even the gutters had been sluiced. They still smelled of eel rot, brewer's mash, and the sweet dead breath of warm pond water, but now they smelled of those things in public clothes.

Silas stood on the scaffold plank with a mahlstick under one arm and his brush roll at his hip, staring at the wall that was supposed to save him.

Celadon Crown had sounded expensive the first time Silas said it across Gideon's desk and watched the man smile like a till springing open. It sounded more expensive now, printed in soft green on the handbills, set in looping Roman letters above the promise of regular color, regular heat, regular supply. It was the sort of name that knew which fork to use. It belonged in Gilt Crescent under cut glass and folded napkins. It did not belong in a cellar where a woman needed to know if her soup would scorch or if the lamp by the stairs would stay cool enough for a child not to blister two fingers on the cage. Inez had shown him too many kitchens, lamp cages, and wet steps for the word to sit clean in his mouth anymore. Silas knew that now. He knew too much now. That had not stopped him from wearing his best coat and turning up early with his brushes cleaned and his hair tied back.

He needed the launch to work. Need sat in him with the vulgar simplicity of rent. Gideon's advance had already gone into meals, varnish, and the sort of immediate necessities that vanished so fast a man looked down and wondered whether money had ever been a material thing at all. There was more to be had if the campaign took. More work, more visibility, more proof that his mouth and hand were still worth a crowd stopping for. A failed private job stank for a week. A failed public christening got into the brick.

"You look like you're waiting to be hanged," Damaris Pike said.

She had come in widow's black gone shiny at the elbows, with ink smudged into the lines beside her thumb and a peppermint turning slowly between her teeth. The chipped edge of her front tooth showed when she smiled. She had pinned her hat with two ordinary steel pins and one little brass fishhook, because she never wore anything without making it answer two jobs.

Silas glanced down at her. "Only if the rope's paying."

"If the rope's paying, you'll flirt with it first."

"You're here early for a hanging."

"I'm here for a christening. A good hanging comes free if the Almighty is kind."

She looked up at the wall, the silk, the quartet, the trays of crowns, and her mouth curled around the peppermint.

"He's laid this on thick," she said.

"That's because he's selling thin goods."

"Don't be vain. You're thin goods too."

She said it pleasantly, as if remarking on the weather. That was one of the reasons Silas preferred her to nicer people. Nicer people put a napkin over the knife before they set it in.

Across the street, buyers from Gilt Crescent were arriving under parasols the color of milk and old pistachio. One man wore a waistcoat bright enough to announce an inheritance. Two sisters from a ribbon house picked their way between puddles with the furious precision of women who had never once in life been forgiven for dirty hems. Behind them came the real body of Patent Row: apprentices with size on their cuffs, newsboys, clerks, laundry women on dinner break, ferry men in shirts gone dark between the shoulder blades, errand girls, and children who had followed the handbills because a printed crown still looked like a prize if you were eight and barefoot.

And beyond them, where Patent Row tilted down toward the drain cut, Silas saw Inez.

He picked her out because she was not trying to be seen. Everybody else on the street shifted, called, bargained, displayed. Inez stood with one boot on the curb and one on the slick plank thrown over the gutter, her weight easy as if the city had been built for her balance alone. Her dark hair was wrapped in a work scarf the color of old brick. A strip had come loose at the wrist and showed the pale crescent burn scar he had noticed in the grow houses when she reached past him for a hot pipe without flinching. She was not dressed for spectacle. She was dressed for weather and bad surfaces and something that might need lifting.

Two women stood with her. One had red forearms from lye work and a green comb stabbed through her hair like a threat. The other had a basket hook looped over one elbow and kept scratching the side of her neck with it, slow and absent, as if crowds always made her itch. They watched the wall the way practical people watched a man who had promised too much.

Silas wished, suddenly and stupidly, that the whole thing looked less foolish from where she stood.

Gideon emerged from under the awning at exactly the moment a lesser man would have hurried. He never hurried. He moved as if time had been instructed in advance. His coat was pearl gray, his gloves cream, his silvering hair so neat that the damp seemed to refuse him on principle. A little mist of river humidity hung on everybody else. Gideon appeared to have negotiated separate terms.

"Mr. Vale." He tipped his head up toward the scaffold. "When I begin, I want the first line in place before the second refrain."

"You want miracles to count bars now?"

"I want timing. Miracles are a language for people who dislike invoices."

His eyes moved once over the crowd. He had the habit of looking at human beings the way other men looked at shelving.

"There is appetite here," he said quietly. "That is useful. We will give it shape."

Silas almost laughed. Appetite here was sweat, debt, boredom, curiosity, and the city's incurable taste for anything that might embarrass its betters before supper. But he nodded. It was easier than arguing, and harder to explain why he had begun to feel, after Sump Ward, that a shaped appetite could still bite the hand that arranged it.

Gideon stepped onto the little platform built before the wall. One of the white-gloved boys struck a brass triangle for silence. The quartet stopped on a held note and the street, by some miracle of appetite and heat and curiosity, actually quieted.

Gideon did not raise his voice. He never needed to. He spoke like a man who expected the room, the ledger, and the weather to meet him halfway.

"Neighbors, patrons, householders," he said. "Our city has lately enjoyed more novelty than discipline. Novelty has its amusements. Discipline keeps a stove steady, a lamp dependable, a purchase respectable. Verdancy House is pleased today to present a strain under a name equal to its quality and fit for repeated trade."

That was Gideon at his most dangerous. He made greed sound like civic housekeeping.

The handbills lifted across the crowd. Buyers leaned. Children put on the paper crowns and instantly began ruining them. One little boy with dock dirt ground black into one trouser knee turned his crown into a telescope before the speech was halfway done. His mother smacked his shoulder without looking at him.

Gideon went on.

"A city may chatter. Commerce must answer clearly. The name is Celadon Crown."

On cue, the quartet shifted into something lighter. The boys with crowns began the refrain first, trained and shrill and a little frightened.

"Celadon Crown," they sang.

The hired callers by the curb repeated it. Then the crowd did, because crowds would repeat almost anything once if enough bodies said it first.

"Celadon Crown."

The handbills shook. The paper crowns bobbed. A line of sample trays on the platform, each holding a fed patch of the runaway moss under glass, went from unsettled green to a pale, courtly shade that could have sat nicely beside silk ribbon and expensive soap.

The cooperage wall answered too.

The seeded wash beneath the damp lime breathed out a soft glazed green, almost blue at the edges, the color of cool porcelain in a rich dining room. A little murmur moved through the block. One of the ribbon sisters actually smiled. The man in the inheritance waistcoat said, "There now," to nobody in particular, with the relieved vanity of a person whose taste had just been endorsed by biology.

Silas felt it in his own body, that terrible sweet lift when a thing might work after all.

For one minute the wall did look obedient. The green held. The handbills made sense in the light. Even Patent Row seemed briefly willing to pretend it had not been built on scams, spills, and people shouting from one bad floor to another. Silas hated how badly he wanted that minute to lengthen. He wanted Inez to see that he had not learned nothing from her. He wanted Gideon to be right just long enough to pay out. He wanted the city to sit for him like wet paint under a good brush.

Gideon turned without looking up. "Now, Mr. Vale."

***

Silas set his mahlstick, dipped the long lettering brush into the celadon pot, and touched the wall.

The first stroke took beautifully.

The brush slid through damp heat and lime the way a skiff knife slid through river skin, easy and sure. He pulled the high curve of the C with his whole shoulder, then thinned the tail with a practiced turn of the fingers. The green against the brighter green beneath had just enough shadow to lift. He heard the little public sigh a crowd made when labor looked effortless. That sound had paid his supper more than once.

Below him, the trained children sang again.

"Celadon Crown."

He began the E.

That was when a woman above the cooper's balcony rail barked, "Cooklow."

The voice had lye in it. Silas looked up without meaning to and saw the laundry woman with the green comb in her hair. She had climbed the stairs with her wet arms bare and her apron still on, and she leaned out as if the whole performance had been staged for her correction alone.

"Cooklow," she said again, louder. "That's what it is if you've got a pot to mind."

Her little girl, who had soap flakes stuck to one shin, echoed her at once because children trusted repetition more than prestige.

"Cooklow!"

Three women near Inez laughed and took it up. Not mockery exactly. Not at first. More the reflex of people hearing a false instruction and answering with the right one before it got somebody burned.

"Cooklow."

The lower third of the wall cooled toward a denser kitchen green, darker and steadier than the elegant wash Gideon had bought. The tail of Silas's E blurred.

He heard it happen before he fully saw it: a moist little hiss under the paint, as if the plaster had put its tongue through the letter.

"Keep going," Gideon said.

Silas kept going. Of course he did.

He laid in the L fast, then the A. The crowd's official refrain tried to regain the street.

"Celadon Crown."

But a ferry hauler by the curb, broad as a packing crate and still wearing one leather shoulder pad from the morning's loads, cupped his hands and shouted, "Ropegreen."

"Ropegreen," somebody from the docks answered at once.

"Ropegreen!"

That one moved through the middle distance of the crowd with the rough comfort of a thing said a hundred dawns running. Silas had heard something like it at Ferrymouth without marking it. That felt worse than hearing it fresh.

The center of the wall darkened to a dockside green with black in it, the color of wet rope and old pilings. His pale lettering rose against it for half a breath and then began to sink, as if the wall were swallowing his hand a fraction behind his hand.

The G from Celadon puckered at the top. A bubble swelled beneath the fresh stroke.

Silas flattened it with the brush.

The bubble spat.

A fan of celadon slurry shot out from the brick and peppered the nearest buyers. One of the ribbon sisters took three wet flecks across her bodice. They did not sit there politely. They brightened. One turned a cheerful bridal pink against the cream silk, another darkened to rope green, and the third went a flat kitchen white that made the stain look at once expensive and diseased.

The sister looked down in horror.

"Oh, for Christ's sake."

The street laughed. Not the whole street. Enough.

Silas heard Damaris laugh with her whole chest. He did not look at her. Looking at Damaris when she had just found money was never wise.

"Silence, please," Gideon said.

He did not shout. That made it funnier.

Silas wiped the brush on the pot rim and reached for more pigment. His fingers had started to slip in the damp. Heat came off the wall in pulses now, cool and warm by turns, as different names struck it from different mouths. Sound was painting faster than his hand.

A thin old man under the awning, the sort of clerk who had kept his cuffs clean by despising everybody, called up in a nasal fury, "Use the proper term."

From the other balcony a woman Silas had not noticed before leaned out with a mourning ribbon tied around one wrist and said, very clear, "Wakeblue."

That did it.

Not because she shouted. Because three people on the landing below her repeated it with the grave immediate obedience of people who knew exactly what room that color belonged in.

"Wakeblue."

The upper edge of the wall slid toward a deep funeral blue that made the pretty green beneath look cheap and frightened. It was beautiful. That was part of the humiliation. The wall was not merely failing him. It was showing off.

Somebody near the drain cut yelled "Bridelight," half as a joke, half from old habit, and pink lightning veined through the O he had just started in Crown. The crowd answered that one with scandalized delight, because they all remembered where it had begun and because people preferred the filthy accidental name to the decent purchased one.

"Bridelight!"

"Ropegreen!"

"Cooklow!"

"Wakeblue!"

The trained boys kept trying to hold their line.

"Celadon Crown," they sang, and now they sounded as if the song were happening to them against their will.

Inez had not moved. Silas looked down and saw her watching the wall, not him. He hated that more than if she had laughed. Her face had gone harder, cleaner. When pressure rose, she lost decoration from expression the way some tools lost rust under a wire brush.

"Mr. Vale," Gideon said, still quiet. "Finish it."

"You hearing the same city I'm hearing?"

"Finish it."

That made Silas mean-funny, because shame had begun nibbling and he knew no better first defense.

He leaned down off the plank. "You lot want to pick one before I grow old up here?"

"Pick your own stove, darling," the lye-armed woman called back, and the street barked.

"You paint what the wall says," the ferry hauler added. "That's your trade, isn't it?"

The worst part was that Silas could not say no with a clean conscience.

He turned back to the wall and painted harder, as if force had ever once in history improved lettering.

Celadon Crown came out across the cooperage bricks in immense capitals, but each letter lived in different weather by the time it was done. CELA lay over mottled green with pink veins running through the counters. DON sat in a patch gone almost white at the edges, fever-pale and wrong. CROWN broke under bands of rope green and wake blue. The wet lime kept sweating through the pigment, lifting it in translucent blisters. Every fresh correction birthed a new leak. By the time he outlined the final N, the first C had already begun to fray at the shoulders.

The paper crowns in the crowd started changing color to match whatever name their owners shouted. Children discovered this at once and turned it into sport.

"Wakeblue!" one boy hollered, and his crown darkened.

"Bridelight!" screamed another, and three crowns blushed pink.

"Cooklow!" cried the girl with the soap on her shin, and the band of her paper crown turned a domestic dull green that looked ridiculous above her solemn face.

Laughter rolled outward in hot, bright waves.

Gideon's hired callers tried to restore order by giving the official name more volume. That only widened the channels. More mouths, more answer. Patent Row took the opportunity the way a hungry man took dropped fruit.

Now the names were not only correction. They were wager, territory, class irritation, neighborhood pride, old joke, small revenge. People from Lantern Steps wanted the upper houses to hear they still named their own light. Dock men wanted the ridge buyers to know a rope had touched the thing before silk had. Women who cooked by it refused to have upholstery vocabulary dropped into their pots. Boys shouted whatever got the prettiest reaction. Old men muttered the name of a dead wife's lamp habit just to see if the wall remembered grief.

The wall remembered everything at once.

It brightened until the letters seemed backlit from inside the brick. Then it shifted in islands and bands so fast Silas's eyes watered trying to follow it. A violetless white flashed under the middle of Crown. A pink run burst through the R and crawled down like a tongue. Blue filmed over the top line. Green thickened at the base. The effect was not elegant, not stable, not sellable.

It was magnificent.

That made him furious.

He slapped one palm to the brick to steady himself and felt the living temperature of the thing kick between cool and hot under his skin. Not metaphor. Actual heat, then no heat, as if a dozen households were arguing through the wall about what they needed from it.

"Fuck," he said.

Not under his breath. Not to himself. To the wall, the crowd, the contract, the silk bunting, and the exact shape of his own stupidity.

The wall answered with another spit.

This time the slurry caught the man in the inheritance waistcoat full across the front. It hit him in a broad starburst that turned first celadon, then bruised blue at the edges. He made a small shocked sound and took a blind step backward into one of the tray boys, who dropped a stack of sample cards into the gutter. The cards stuck, drank street water, and came up on the soles of passing boots like a flock of drowned leaves.

Now even the ribbon sisters laughed, because once expensive people were visibly sticky the moral universe felt briefly restored.

***

Silas climbed down before he broke his neck. The ladder rungs were slick with mist and paint, and for one hard second he imagined exactly how the street would sound if he slipped and came down backward into his own branding launch. That image got him to the ground more carefully than dignity did.

Heat closed over him at street level. Perfume, pond rot, lamp smoke, wet brick, human armpit, and the mineral stink of torn plaster all crowded the same breath. Somebody's elbow hit his ribs. Somebody else pushed a handbill against his chest like a joke gift. The crowd had lost its shape. That was always the danger point. A crowd with a program could be steered. A crowd that had smelled blood wanted participation.

"Ask the wall!" a boy yelled.

"Ask the cook!" somebody else answered.

"Ask your damned laundry!"

"Wakeblue!"

"Bridelight!"

"Celadon Clown," said a voice from somewhere he could not place, and that one nearly took the block apart.

Even Silas laughed once, a sharp involuntary bark, because there were moments when the city's vulgarity was too exact not to admire.

Gideon did not laugh.

He had stepped down from the platform and removed one glove finger by finger, as if bare skin might somehow negotiate better terms with disaster. There was a wet fleck of pale green on his cuff. It was the most naked Silas had ever seen him.

"Mr. Vale," Gideon said.

That was all. No volume. No scene. Which meant the scene was being stored for later use.

"It was holding," Silas said. "You heard it. Another half minute and we'd have had the line fixed."

"We had the line fixed. We did not have the street."

"That's because the street lives here."

Gideon's gaze settled on him, cool as ledger metal.

"That is not news."

"Then next time don't stage a coronation on a drain."

Silas knew at once he had gone too far. Gideon's expression barely changed, but the little remaining politeness in it folded up like a paper card.

"There will be a next time," Gideon said. "You will be less theatrical in it."

Before Silas could answer, Damaris was beside them, notebook already open. She wrote in a fast cramped hand with the pencil braced against her chipped tooth between bursts, as if she kept the point sharp by spite alone.

"Was that 'less theatrical' before or after the gentleman in the blue waistcoat got christened in public?" she asked.

"Mrs. Pike," Gideon said, and if contempt could have been notarized it would have sounded like that.

"Mr. Wren. Marvelous event. Strong colors. Fine disorder."

"You are obstructing business."

"No, sir. Business obstructed business. I only arrived with paper."

She tipped her chin toward the wall where Celadon Crown still stood in huge letters, every capital now crosshatched by the city's disobedience.

"I wouldn't rush to call it a failure," she said. "You've got argument. Argument sells better than agreement unless the agreement is attached to funerals or sugar."

"I am not selling newspapers."

"That's why I'm."

She snapped the notebook shut and turned to the crowd like an actress hitting her mark.

"What do you call it in Lantern Steps?" she shouted.

The answer came at once, overlapping and delighted now that somebody respectable had finally asked the useful question.

"Cooklow!"

"Wakeblue!"

"Ropegreen!" from the dock men, louder now because nothing encouraged a ferry crew like seeing upper-street commerce lose its hat.

Damaris pointed her pencil without choosing.

"And in Sump?"

That produced a beat of silence, not because no one knew, but because some names were not for her column and some people on that street still understood the difference.

Then Inez said, "Depends what you're doing."

She had stepped forward just enough for Silas to hear her without strain. She did not raise her voice to a public pitch. She never gave more air than a sentence needed. The crowd near her quieted around the fact of being answered by somebody who worked for a living with the thing under dispute.

"If you're cooking, one name," she said. "If you're crossing stairs with a baby and no daylight, another. If you're sitting up with the dead, another. That's the trouble with your pretty crown. It doesn't tell anybody what their hands are in."

Damaris's eyes lit like press lamps.

"There," she said softly, mostly to herself. "There you are."

Silas saw the exact second the story left Gideon's platform and entered Damaris's hands. It was not mystical. It was trade. She had the line that carried class, joke, injury, and consequence all at once. By breakfast it would be on half the ward tables with grease on the margins.

Gideon understood it too. His jaw tightened once.

"Mrs. Pike," he said, "if you print household misinformation, you will answer to counsel."

"If I print what your own wall did, counsel can sue the brick."

She whistled to a runner boy in a cap too large for him. He came skidding through the gutter water, one shoe half unlaced and a smear of printer's black already along his cuff.

"Tell Nall to hold the second side for this," she said. "Big head. Not elegant. I want verbs that make a racket."

"What's the head?"

She looked at the wall, at Silas striped in accidental color, at the ruined waistcoat, at the paper crowns going pink and blue over a crowd now cheerfully arguing itself into neighborhoods.

"Patent Row Crowns a Moss and Loses the Street," she said. "No. Better. Crown Refused by Kitchens, Docks, and Dead. Run both if there's room."

The boy grinned and bolted.

Silas felt the blood drain through him in a slow ugly pull. It was not only embarrassment. Embarrassment he knew. This was the larger, colder thing underneath it: the sense of a door swinging open behind him onto consequences he could not charm shut again. Damaris would make him comic by morning. Gideon would make him useful or punishable by afternoon. And Inez, who had walked him through rooms where names kept people safe, had now watched him stand on a public scaffold and try to lay one expensive word over all of it like wallpaper.

He turned to her.

"I didn't think it would go like this."

It was a stupid sentence. He knew it while it was still warm in his mouth.

Inez looked at him at last. Up close the air had freckled damp along her temple. There was a bit of plaster dust on her scarf where somebody in the crowd had brushed past. Her expression had not gone soft, but it had shifted out of pure anger into something worse for him: exact appraisal.

"No," she said. "You thought the wall would mind manners if the paper was nice enough."

The lye-armed woman behind her snorted. The basket-hook woman scratched her neck again and said, not unkindly, "That's Patent Row sickness."

Silas could have lied. He could have told Inez he had tried to split the difference. He could have said he meant to get money out of Gideon and then steer the rest better later. All of that had some truth in it. None of it improved the scene in front of them.

Instead he said, "I needed it to take."

"Of course you did."

That hurt because she did not say it like a moral victory. She said it like a maintenance fact. Damp rose. Pipes burst. Men needed money and called it judgment.

She nodded once toward the gutter where celadon run-off was already threading downhill through the brick channels in bright diluted ribbons.

"That slime's heading south," she said. "If it gets into cook stoves or lamp trays, you'll hear about it from people who don't give speeches first."

Then she stepped back into the crowd and was gone, not vanished, just absorbed by bodies that had better reasons than Silas to be in the street.

The quartet had given up. One of the violinists sat on the platform edge wiping his strings with a handkerchief that would never come clean. The ribbon sisters were leaving with their damaged hems held up. The inheritance waistcoat man kept touching his chest as if hoping the stain would prove imaginary. Children were still testing their paper crowns for color, turning in circles and shouting names at one another with the shrill ecstatic seriousness of a game they would carry into schoolyards, kitchens, ferries, and alleys before the day was done.

"Wakeblue!"

"Bridelight!"

"Cooklow!"

"Celadon Clown!"

That last one came back again and again because mockery had the best legs in Nacre Bend.

Silas looked up at the wall.

Celadon Crown was still there, huge and undeniable. He had painted it well. That was the insult. The capitals had weight, curve, proportion, a professional confidence no crowd could deny him. But the city had written through him. Pink burned in the counters. Rope green thickened at the bases. Blue held the upper brick like evening in a mourning room. White flashed where the heat had gone wrong. The letters looked less like a decree than like something caught in crossfire.

Gideon followed his gaze.

"This can still be placed," he said.

Silas turned. "Placed where?"

"Everywhere repetition is better managed."

The answer chilled him more than rage would have. Gideon was not surrendering. He was taking notes.

The lone wet fleck on Gideon's cuff had turned, during all this, from pale green to a sharp accusing pink.

Silas almost told him. Instead he watched Gideon notice it, and watched the man's face harden by one careful degree.

Down the slope, Damaris's runner vanished into press alley. The crowd was already carrying the argument outward in clumps, each cluster with its own preferred word. Handbills stuck to boots. Paper crowns drifted in the gutter. Celadon wash ran through the channels toward Ferrymouth and the lower wards in bright infected threads.

Silas stood in the middle of the emptied ceremony with paint drying on his knuckles and other people's names drying on his work, and understood that what had broken was not only the launch.

Patent Row had seen him lose the street.


# Chapter 6

Silas woke on two flour sacks in Junie's back room with his cheek stuck to rough canvas and a smell in his nose that did not belong in any honest kitchen. Broth, yes. Old onions, yes. Lamp smoke, river damp, vinegar, yeast, all the ordinary Ferrymouth perfumes that got into your clothes and stayed there until rain beat them out. Under it sat the sweeter note, the one he had learned to distrust the day before: warm pond rot with a sugared edge, as if somebody had poured syrup over ditch water and called it progress.

Pink light quivered under the prep table.

He rolled onto an elbow too fast, banged his head against the shelf above him, and heard Junie say, from somewhere near the sink, "Good. You're alive enough to scrub."

The back kitchen looked as if Patent Row had vomited through it in the night. Three lamp trays had been hauled off the range and laid upside down on old towels. Their rims wore a fur of pale slime, not one color but several failing to agree: bridal pink at the edges, a sickly milky white where the tin had held heat, a ribbon of mourning blue in the seams. A handbill with half of GIDEON WREN'S VERDANCY HOUSE gone to pulp clung to the leg of the wash table. The word CROWN still showed in a curl of green ink before the paper dissolved into jelly.

Junie stood at the sink in shirtsleeves, broad shoulders shining with sweat, her hair pinned hard and crooked because one side had already worked loose. The small healed split in her left eyebrow had gone white with heat. She scraped at a ladle with a fish knife and counted under her breath as if she were keeping score against God. "One good kettle. Two trays maybe. One ladle gone to hell. One brother too stupid to die when it'd be useful."

Silas pushed himself up. His coat, stiff with dried splash from the launch, hung over the lamp rack. The cuff nearest the floor had gone tender pink and was putting out a faint expensive glow, the kind Gideon would have liked under glass and Junie would have liked nowhere near supper.

"How bad?" Silas asked. His mouth tasted like old metal. The back of his neck still remembered the crowd laughing when the wall had outperformed his brush.

Junie did not waste time giving the answer shape. "Bad enough that if I had let the first ferry crew in an hour ago, I'd have served stew by diseased moonlight. Bad enough that your lovely coat spent the night teaching my trays bad habits. Bad enough that you can stop looking ashamed and start carrying water."

"I didn't mean to bring it here."

"You brought it here, laid that coat on my rack, and slept like a saint in a painting while my stove learned new tricks. Don't hand me remorse when I need your hands."

He got to his feet. His shirt had twisted around him in the night. The room was close already though the day had barely started, and outside the half-open door Ferrymouth was waking in knocks and gull shrieks and a ferry bell struck flat by damp. He picked the handbill off the table leg. It stretched before it tore. A clot of white slime dropped onto his boot and crept, deliberate as gossip, toward the leather seam.

Junie pointed with the fish knife. "Cold bucket first. Pump's still clean. Hot water makes it throw spores."

"Since when do you know that?"

"Since sunrise. Move."

By the time he came back with two sloshing buckets cutting his shoulders raw, Junie had stripped the front range down to iron and hauled the bread board onto a flour barrel. She had not once, he could tell, permitted herself the luxury of standing still to be miserable. Ruined things had been sorted into piles: salvage, doubtful, gone. Bones for stock. Onions worth cutting. Aprons clean enough to lie with. The kitchen had the grim order of a battlefield if a battlefield also needed to serve eel pie by noon.

She jerked her chin at the buckets. "There. Now strip off that coat before it courts my dish line."

"You say the sweetest things to me."

"I say them because your father is dead and nobody else did the job well enough."

That landed where she meant it to. Silas peeled the coat off and held it away from himself. Wet light clung to the nap in streaks. Yesterday, under the buyers' parasols and the shouting, it had seemed almost handsome for a minute, the wall running faster than paint, color leaping from one insult to the next. Here it looked diseased. Worse than diseased. Ridiculous. A clown's skin after the clown had drowned.

Junie tossed him an apron. "Wear that. If the coat still answers to those fools, I don't want it within spitting distance of the soup."

He caught the apron and tied it on. "What did you do with the customers?"

"Told breakfast they could eat bread and cold herrings or starve with dignity elsewhere. Most of them chose bread. Two chose to complain. They're still alive, so perhaps I was merciful."

She set one of the trays in the cold basin and the water answered with a nasty little hiss. The slime on the rim tightened to pearled beads, then loosened again as if reconsidering. Junie looked at it with flat dislike.

"It started in the rack," she said. "You left your coat there. One of the launch cards was stuck in the cuff. By dawn the left side of the stove was growing lace. Then the lace got ideas."

"Shit."

"Yes." She scraped with the fish knife and a flap of pinkish mucus lifted, then snapped back and slapped her wrist. "And if you call it beautiful, I'll break your nose."

Silas took a rag and reached for the basin. The tray was cold at the center and meanly hot at the handles, as if it had opinions about where a man ought to touch it. He nearly dropped it.

Junie barked a laugh without any amusement in it. "There. You finally named something true with your face."

He wanted, stupidly, to explain the launch. He wanted to say the wall had gone bad before the chant was half set, that children on the balconies had yelled other names and the moss had flashed through colors quicker than he could mix them, that Damaris Pike had looked delighted enough to kiss him and Gideon grim enough to invoice the weather. He wanted to say he had walked down from Patent Row wearing half the city's laughter like a second shirt. Junie would have heard none of it. The ruined ladle at the sink made that plain.

Instead he said, "Can it be cleaned?"

"I sent for Miss Cordero."

He looked up too fast. "You sent for her."

"I sent for the person who knows how not to poison a kitchen. Your vanity wasn't in the neighborhood."

He bent over the basin before Junie could see the heat that answer kicked through him. It was not good heat. Or not only that. Inez arriving to find him asleep in the wreckage of his own public performance felt less like rescue than sentence. He ran the rag along the tray rim. The slime bunched under the cloth and let out a smell like old flowers left in bilge water.

Junie said, not unkindly and not kind either, "Save the face for later, Sile. She has seen worse than you."

"That's a comfort."

"It ought to be."

He scrubbed. The tray fought him with small obscene persistence, as if each stringy edge preferred the kitchen it was ruining. Twice he thought he had the seam clean and twice the blue came back up from the metal like a bruise surfacing. Junie worked beside him in hard efficient strokes. They spoke only when something needed moving. The hush between them was not peace. It was labor.

When the back door opened again, river noise came in with a cooler strip of air and Inez Cordero behind it.

She had tied a dark work scarf over her hair and looped a length of copper wire through the knot, so it rode against her shoulder like something she might need before noon. Her skirt was hitched for walking on planks. Mud striped the hem in careful dried fans. She carried a canvas tool roll in one hand and a sack under the other arm that left wet salt dust on her sleeve. The crescent scar on the inside of her right wrist showed when she set the sack down.

Her gaze went first to Junie, then to the trays, then to Silas in the apron. The order of that stung more than if she had started with him.

"How many?" she asked.

Junie held up three fingers. "Three trays touched. One ladle gone soft. Kettle skirt only, I think."

Inez crossed to the basin and put her knuckles against the tray rim instead of grabbing it. Then she bent and listened, as if the tin might answer through sound before it answered through color. "You were right to keep it cold." She glanced at the handbill pulp near Silas's boot. "And wrong to let that paper stay in the room."

Junie snorted. "By the time I found it, it had married the floorboards."

"It'll come up."

"So will rent," Junie said. "That doesn't make me cheerful."

Inez's mouth moved at one corner. Not a smile. Something drier. "No. It doesn't."

Silas said, "If you're here to enjoy yourself, you've chosen a poor house for it."

She looked at him then, full on. Her face gave him nothing cheap. The room, already close, seemed to take one slow breath and hold it. "If I wanted enjoyment, Mr. Vale, I'd have stayed where people know better than to sleep in a kitchen wearing active moss."

Junie pointed the fish knife between them. "No public quarrel in my back room. If either of you starts performing, I'll put your heads in separate buckets. Miss Cordero, tell me what can be saved. Sile, stop bleeding pride on the floor and fetch the coarse salt."

Inez untied the sack and turned it out: salt, two strips of old burlap, a narrow scraper with a bone handle worn smooth by years of thumb pressure, a stoppered bottle of sour whey, and a bundle of dried reeds cut to brush size. She laid them out in a straight line on the table, each tool placed as precisely as a syllable.

Silas went for the salt crock. When he came back, Inez had already shaved a thumbnail of white growth off the tray and pinned it under the scraper blade. It twitched once. He would have sworn it twitched.

"Don't use the fish knife," she told Junie. "It'll nick the tin and give it somewhere to start again."

"Then your man can have the fish knife and his own hand if he wants excitement."

"He's had enough of that," Inez said.

Junie barked a laugh this time and meant it.

Silas set down the crock harder than necessary. "Am I in the room?"

"Unfortunately," Junie said.

Inez did not bother with him. She tipped sour whey onto the tray seam. The slime recoiled from the acid smell and thinned from milky white to a washed-out green. "Hold it there," she said.

He took the tray, careful now. Her fingers brushed his for less than a second. Hers were cool from the walk outside, callused at the pads, the skin damp with mist or sweat. The contact was practical and it ran through him like a lie.

"There," she said, and scraped. A wet ribbon came free with a soft tearing sound that made his shoulders climb. "Good. Keep the rim level."

"I'm keeping it level."

Junie looked over. "You aren't."

"I'm close."

"Close burns soup."

Inez glanced once at his hands and adjusted the tray with two quick pushes of her thumbs. "Now you're close."

They fell into an ugly little rhythm. Sour the seam. Salt the scrape. Wipe. Into the second basin. Back to the table. The slime changed manners every few minutes. Sometimes it clung stubborn and gelatinous. Sometimes it broke into pearls that rolled for the cracks in the boards. Once a line of pink shot up the handle toward Silas's thumb and Inez slapped salt onto it so fast he felt more startled than burned.

"Christ," he said.

"If you were going to pray," Junie said, "you had all yesterday."

The handle cooled under his hand. Inez had not removed her palm from the metal yet. He could feel the shape of her strength through the tray, not soft, not theatrical, just there. She let go only when she was certain the heat had settled.

"What did you call it?" he asked.

She wiped the scraper on burlap. "Nothing out loud."

"You said something."

"I told it to hold."

"That worked."
