"Three voices in a hot room with live metal under them can nudge things. It won't remake a district. It can keep a kitchen from biting you." She reached for more salt. "That's different from a crowd teaching a wall to blush for money."

Junie pointed her chin at Silas without looking up from the kettle skirt she was scouring. "Hear that? She's trying very hard not to say I told you so."

"I don't waste effort on the obvious," Inez said.

That should have annoyed him. It did, a little. Mostly it made him want to laugh, and because there was nowhere to hide in the room and no crowd to feed, the laugh came out small and true.

"Fine," he said. "What do I say, then?"

Inez looked at him as if assessing whether he was capable of carrying a bucket without naming it after a duchess. "Say what the thing is for. Not how it looks. Not how you want it sold. What it has to do."

"For example."

She pointed at the tray in his hands. "Low hold."

"That sounds like an order, not a name."

"In a kitchen those are often the same."

Junie said, "Low hold," in the tone she usually used for getting dockhands to sit before they fainted.

Inez repeated it once, quieter.

Silas, feeling foolish enough to be obedient, said it too. "Low hold."

The tray's rim went from uncertain blue to a steady workman's green no brighter than old bottle glass. It did not transform. It settled. The difference was almost insulting in its modesty. No flourish. No gasp from a crowd. Just the metal cooling to something a woman could use without losing skin.

He stared at it.

Inez had already moved on to the next tray. "Again."

"That was all?"

"No," Junie said. "There was also labor. Keep up."

The second tray proved meaner. Its seams had held more of the launch muck and some of the white had gone inside the lip where the tin folded. Inez had him hold the tray so close his forearms ached. Junie scraped at the opposite side with a reed brush, cursing under her breath in the same clipped rhythm she used for chopping onions. By the time they won it back to serviceable green, Silas's apron was smeared with slime, whey, dishwater, and his own sweat. A clot of something pale and stringy hit the floor near his shoe and made a tiny indecent wobble before Junie stamped it with the heel of her boot.

"There," she said. "Now it's as dead as your sense."

"You don't get to be pleased about this."

"Who's pleased? I'm still down one ladle and a morning."

Inez stood and flexed her right wrist. The scar there paled and darkened as the tendons moved under it. "You may lose the ladle entirely. Don't let anyone eat off the small spoons from that rack until they've had ash water and a second rinse. The big kettle can stay if you bank the heat low through service."

Junie said, "Can I get through supper?"

"Yes."

Junie's shoulders dropped a fraction. It was the closest she came to relief. "Then I can insult my brother in a less hurried spirit."

Silas had been looking at Inez's wrist too long. She caught him at it, not with vanity but with alertness, as if he had reached toward a tool that was not his. He looked away first and hated that she had seen him do it.

"I owe you," Junie said.

"You owe me two bowls and those three trays back tomorrow so I can reseat the rims."

"Done."

"I can bring them," Silas said, before he knew he meant to.

Inez turned the bone-handled scraper in her hand. "Can you arrive without carrying a parade on your coat?"

"If I strip first."

Junie made a low sound that might have been a laugh or a warning. "Behave, both of you. I still have pies to warm."

The third tray took longer because fatigue had set into all three of them and because the slime, now sensing defeat or maybe only the kitchen settling into one practical mood, got stubborn in a petty domestic way. It clung under Junie's nails. It webbed between the tines of a fork. It made a wet kissing sound when Silas pulled the burlap away too fast and Junie, without pausing, said, "If that thing starts flirting with you, charge it rent."

He said, "I would, but I hear it pays in adjectives."

That got the laugh from Inez at last. It came out short and unwilling, like a knot breaking loose in rope. He looked up before he could stop himself. Her head had tipped back a little. The scarf had slipped enough at the temple to show damp hair flattened there by work. The laugh did not make her sweet. It made her more exact, because it let him see what she looked like when control relaxed and returned.

Junie, hearing it too, said, "Good. Now that the room has witnessed a miracle, somebody hand me the onion pot."

By the time the first dinner bell from the ferry slip rang through the lane, they had three trays back on the range, green and low and ugly as they ought to be. Junie slid the soup kettle into place over them and watched the first slow bubbles gather at the edge. No one spoke until the boil held steady.

Then she shoved a clean towel into Silas's chest. "Front room. If anybody asks why the kitchen smells like a swamp saint's drawers, tell them it's fashion."

"You don't want the truth?"

"The truth is not paying tab two and wants more bread. Move."

He moved. Behind him, Inez was rinsing the last of the tools, Junie was already ladling broth, and the kitchen that had nearly gone strange under his coat answered them now with the plain useful noises of service: tin on iron, knives on board, bowls set hard on wood, the range breathing its banked green breath.

***

The rush ran long because Ferrymouth was full of men who had spent the day hearing about Patent Row and wanted supper with a side of scandal. Junie gave them stew, bread, and no gossip worth carrying. Silas worked the room in his shirtsleeves and towel, fetching bowls, slicing pie, taking coins greasy from rope hands and dock tar. Twice somebody tried to tease him about the launch. Twice Junie called for more broth before he had to answer. By the time the last regular limped out into the blue-green dusk, the kitchen lamps had burned low and steady, his feet felt hammered flat, and his shoulders had found muscles he usually let brushes do without.

Junie dropped the bar into place across the front door and leaned her forehead against it for one brief second before turning back toward the kitchen. That one second was all the self-pity she allowed herself, and even then it looked more like calculation than grief.

"Sit," she said.

Silas and Inez were both still standing with the foolish endurance of tired people when somebody finally grants them rest. Junie pointed two fingers at the table. "I wasn't speaking in metaphor."

The meal she put before them was what the day had left: eel ends pulled into bean mash with browned onions, hard bread fried in drippings till it almost pretended to be fresh, pickled green tomatoes chopped rough, and the heel of a custard pie that had collapsed on one side and therefore could not be sold to anyone proud. She set down three bowls and then her tally book, because Junie never ate without her numbers close enough to touch.

Silas looked at the book. "You aren't adding this to my debt."

"I add everything to your debt. The question is only which column."

Inez sat with a small sound at the back of her throat as her knees bent, not complaint exactly but the body reporting in. She had loosened the scarf and retied it lower, enough that a dark wave of hair had escaped at the nape of her neck and stuck there with sweat. The copper wire was gone from the knot now, laid with the tools to dry. Her forearms, scrubbed clean, still carried faint green shadows in the fine lines around the wrists.

Junie caught Silas looking and slid him his bowl hard enough to jar the spoon. "Eat before you invent another disaster."

He ate. The first mouthful hurt in the way good food hurts when a man has spent the day smelling it and not earning any of it. Salt, eel fat, onions, vinegar from the tomatoes. The recovered trays held their low green under the pot with no sign of the day's mischief. That almost undid him more than if one had failed.

Junie took up her spoon. "We lost the ladle. One pie tin's suspect. Two small spoons are in disgrace till morning. I'm charging Patent Row in spirit if not in coin."

Silas swallowed. "Charge me in coin. You've earned the pleasure."

"I already do."

Inez said, "The spoons will come back if you ash them twice."

"Good," Junie said. "I hate buying spoons. They vanish faster than husbands."

That got another short laugh out of Inez. Silas smiled into his bowl before he could help it.

Junie noticed and stabbed a piece of fried bread. "Don't look so blessed. One clean-up and I still remember you dragged the trouble in here."

"I know."

"Do you?" She was not sharp about it. She was tired past sharpness. "Because men like you often know a thing very loudly for an hour and then forget it by market dawn."

He could have defended himself. Once he would have. Instead he said, "I know enough to smell it in my own coat."

Junie nodded once, accepting that much and no more. "Good."

Silas ate another spoonful. Outside, the river knocked at hulls in the slip. Somewhere down the lane a child tried out one of yesterday's launch names in a sing-song dare and got shushed by an older voice before the second repetition. The city was full of mouths. That was the whole damned problem.

He looked at the trays under the stove. "When we said low hold. Why did that work when all the pretty names yesterday did nothing but turn the wall stupid?"

Junie said, "There he is. He's discovered questions."

Inez wiped her spoon on bread before answering. In the front room she had been all economy and correction. Fatigue altered her without softening her. Her sentences did not get careless. They lengthened, made room for example. "Yesterday was a market crowd trying to make one thing answer everybody at once. Men wanted show. Buyers wanted certainty. Children wanted to heckle. Street women wanted to spoil the buyers' afternoon. The moss took all of it and showed off." She tipped her spoon toward the range. "This was a tray in a hot kitchen with three people who all needed the same thing from it."

"So a kitchen beats a crowd."

"No. A crowd beats anything if it's big enough and keeps at it. But a house can hold its own shape if the people in it keep speaking from use instead of appetite."

Junie said, "In plain English."

Inez looked at her bowl as if the explanation were down there with the beans. "A word learns the body it lives in. On Lantern Steps, there are names people use only for crossing after rain. Not because the names sound noble. Because an old man with bad knees can remember them in the dark. In the wash houses there are words for what stays cool enough to wring beside and what needs the children kept off. In a kitchen, if you have a tray that must hold a slow pot through the second ferry, the useful word is the one that gets repeated with burnt fingers and empty plates in mind. That kind of saying sticks different. It sticks to work."

Silas turned that over. Her voice had gone lower as she talked, not secretive, just tired into honesty. The words were clean. No sermon. He could see the Steps while she named them: wet boards, blue lamps, somebody's grandmother choosing a safe path by habit older than posters.

"And I've been giving people words to admire," he said.

Junie snorted. "You've been giving rich men words to invoice."

He took that. It belonged where it landed.

Inez did not rescue him. She never had. "Admiration is easy. Maintenance isn't. A pretty word can spread fast because it asks nothing except a mouth. A useful word has to be proved over and over with pots, stairs, burns, children, weather. That makes it slower. It also makes it harder to kill."

He thought of Gideon's launch cards, thick paper, elegant letters, the crowd chanting Celadon Crown while the wall flashed funeral blue and bridal pink and one ugly diseased white that made the silk buyers recoil. He thought of the retainer folded in his room, the advance already warming one corner of his future, Havelock Pigments waiting on payment, Junie's fuel notes hanging over her like a wet ceiling. He did not say any of that. The omission sat between his ribs like swallowed tin.

Junie was watching him with the kitchen's low lamp turned in her eyes: enough light to work by, not enough to flatter. "Don't go noble on me now. It doesn't suit you."

"I wasn't trying for noble."

"Good. Because I need you housebroken, not transformed."

That startled a laugh out of him, and when he looked over Inez was smiling into her bread with the expression of a woman who would deny it in court.

"What do you call this stuff in Sump Ward," he asked her, nodding toward the bucket by the sink where the day's scrapings sat under salt, "when it gets in everything and won't die with dignity?"

"Depends where it starts."

"That's infuriating."

"It's accurate." She counted on damp fingertips. "If it climbs a lamp rack and leaves the tray too hot at the lip, one house calls it widow's bite. If it webs a wash line and starts dropping cold light on the sheets, another house calls it nun silk. In the propagation sheds, Aunt Vero calls the same thing bastard lace when it thinks it's being clever."

Junie nearly choked on a bean. "Bastard lace. That's better than mine."

"What's yours?" Silas asked.

"Dock scurf. Because I don't have time to poetry every nuisance."

"You have time to improve everybody else's."

"That's different." Junie scraped her bowl clean and set the spoon across it with decision. "And before you start collecting those, understand this: names like that belong to the rooms that earned them."

Silas looked from his sister to Inez. He could feel the correction in it before either woman sharpened it further. Not a speech. Just a boundary laid on the table beside the bread.

"I heard you," he said.

Inez met his eyes. Hers were dark in the low light, steady enough to make lying feel clumsy. "Did you."

It was not a question he could answer honestly without dragging Gideon's contract into Junie's kitchen and letting it foul the room all over again. He looked down at his bowl. "I heard you."

Junie clicked her tongue, not fooled, not pressing. That was worse. She rose and took up the bowls. "Then hear this too. If either of you turns my supper into a tribunal, you'll wash every pan in the place before sunrise."

"There are not enough pans left for that threat," Silas said.

"Then I'll borrow some."

She carried the dishes to the sink and shoved her tally book aside with the heel of her hand. Silas half rose to help, but she cut him off. "Sit. If you stand up out of guilt one more time tonight, I'll nail you to the bench. Miss Cordero, you can help me in a minute. He still owes me the use of his ears."

Inez stayed seated. That in itself felt like a choice.

From the sink came water, crockery, Junie's muttered arithmetic. Silas and Inez had the table between them and very little else. Without Junie's voice filling the room, Ferrymouth came back in: rope bumping posts, a late shout from the slip, the soft hungry insect whine around the back door screen. The lamp over the table showed Inez's face differently than daylight had. Fatigue had taken the public edge off it. Not the caution. Never that. But something in her had unclenched enough that he could imagine, for one dangerous instant, what warmth from her might feel like when it was not handed out in the form of instructions.

He said, because the honest thing was suddenly easier than the polished one, "You didn't have to come."

"Junie asked."

"Still."

She dragged a fingertip through a smear of bean left in her bowl, considering. "A kitchen losing light is everybody's business in weather like this."

"That's a very civic answer."

"Do you want the uncivic one?"

He did not trust his voice for a second, which annoyed him. "Yes."

She looked at him directly, and when she spoke her tone had the same precision it had used all day, only warmer under it, as if heat had moved closer to the surface of the metal. "I wanted to see whether you were all mouth when there wasn't a crowd to feed."

He sat back. The room felt smaller. "And?"

Inez broke off a piece of fried bread and held it between finger and thumb while she decided how much truth he could carry. "You're worse with a crowd."

He laughed once through his nose. "That bad."

"That bad."

"But not all mouth."

"No." She ate the bread. "Not today."

The compliment, because it came from her and because it had cost labor to earn, hit deeper than applause ever had. He wished, absurdly, that he were cleaner. That the day had not left salt under his nails and dishwater in his sleeves. Then he thought of her hands in the basin, the burn scar at her wrist, the damp hair at her neck, and understood cleanliness had nothing to do with it.

Junie came back to the table carrying a wrapped parcel of bread ends and pie scraps. "For the walk," she told Inez. "I know better than to send a woman home on principles alone."

"I brought salt, not holiness."

"Same difference to some men." Junie set the parcel down and pointed at Silas. "You. Carry the trays to the back landing in the morning. Not tonight. Tonight you're too tired and therefore liable to become poetic on the stairs."

"You make poetry sound catching."

"In your case it's."

Inez stood. When she reached for the parcel, her sleeve slipped back and the crescent scar on her right wrist showed again, pale against damp skin. He had noticed it before. Now he wanted to ask about it and knew that wanting was already too intimate for the day they had had. He stayed where he was.

Junie saw everything because Junie always did. "First bell," she said to Inez. "If he doesn't appear, I'll sell him for lamp mash."

Inez adjusted the parcel under her arm. "If he appears dressed for Patent Row, send him home."

"Cruel woman," Silas said.

"Practical woman," Junie corrected.

Inez looked at him over the table. "Come in clothes you can ruin. There are rims to reseat and two lamp lines in Sump Ward still acting foolish after yesterday. If you want to understand what you touched, use your hands."

There it was: not forgiveness, not invitation in any gentle form, but a place. Work. Morning. The thing he had been asking her for since Lantern Steps, though he had not always known enough to ask it right.

"I'll come," he said.

Inez's gaze moved over him once, taking inventory the way she had taken inventory of the trays: what could be saved, what still wanted scraping, where the weak seam lay. "Good," she said. "Try not to arrive glowing."

Junie laughed outright then, tired and mean and fond. "Out with you before I start charging rent for courtship."

"This isn't courtship," Inez said, already at the door.

"No," Junie said. "That's why it has some chance."

Silas followed them to the back landing because the room had become too full to stay in. The night outside pressed warm and damp against his face. Ferrymouth lay under its ordinary working lights now, patched green along the slips, a safer blue on the farther stairs, no trace from here of the overbright public idiocy of Patent Row except what lived in his memory and on the coat Junie had hung as far from the stove as possible.

Inez paused on the top step and looked out over the lane before setting her foot down, steady on the wet wood the way only locals and fools ever were. The parcel was tucked against her ribs. The tool roll knocked lightly against her leg.

"Miss Cordero," he said.

She half turned.

The apology crowded up, wanting shape. So did the contract. So did desire, raw and badly timed. He chose the only part he could say without fouling the night. "Thank you."

Her face gave him the same dry composure it had worn at the door, but the lamp from Junie's kitchen touched one cheek and made the restraint legible rather than cold. "You worked," she said. "That helped."

It was not pardon. It was better than pity.

She went down the steps. At the second landing she glanced back once, not coyly, not by accident either. Then she kept going toward the lower streets where the practical lamps held and the names he had not earned yet lived in people's mouths without asking permission from men like him.

Behind him Junie said, from the doorway, "Shut your mouth, Sile. Mosquitoes will think you're courting them too."

He shut it. He stood one moment longer in the damp dark with his hands still smelling of sour whey and tin and the faint impossible trace of Inez's skin where practical work had put them briefly together. Then he turned back into the kitchen, where tomorrow had already begun in stacked bowls, drying burlap, and three rescued trays cooling to a low unglamorous green.


# Chapter 7

The room Gideon kept above Verdancy House sat high enough over Patent Row to miss the gutter stink for three breaths and no more. After that the city got in anyway: yeast, wet paper, lamp mash warming in cellar pipes, the sweet edge of rot that all rich men in Nacre Bend pretended was someone else's climate. Gideon had arranged his office as a rebuttal. The curtains were cream instead of greened white. The desk was walnut instead of pine. Even the ink smelled dearer, iron and clove instead of the ordinary sour black used by printers and creditors.

The clerk who admitted Silas wore a mourning brooch shaped like a tiny anchor and kept cracking fennel seeds between her back teeth as if the office itself gave her heartburn. She set a folded paper beside Gideon's hand and said, "Mr. Vale," with a look that suggested she had been told what kind of man he was and had improved the picture privately.

Gideon waited until the door shut before he touched the paper. He was gloved as usual, pearl gray kid, clean enough to insult half the city on sight.

"You look tired," he said.

"I got laughed at in three wards on my way here. It takes a certain energy."

"Public amusement is not fatal. It is often preparatory."

Silas dropped into the chair opposite him without being asked. The leather gave a little squeak under his damp coat. "If that's your sermon on the launch, save it. I was there for the collection."

Gideon slid the folded paper across the desk. It was a receipted statement from Havelock Pigments. Forty-three crowns, settled in full. The owner's greasy thumbprint sat at the bottom like a bruise.

Relief hit first, clean and ugly. Havelock would stop sending boys past his shop to stare in at the empty shelf space. Havelock would stop saying Mr. Vale in the tone usually reserved for men found asleep in drains. Silas ran his thumb over the number once, then hated himself for letting the pleasure show anywhere near his face.

"You asked for time," Gideon said. "I have purchased it."

"For me."

"For the work."

Silas folded the receipt and tucked it into his waistcoat. "And what work is that now? Celadon Crown got itself chewed to gristle."

"No." Gideon sat back. "Celadon Crown remains useful. What failed on the wall was exclusivity delivered too early and too theatrically. That is an error of sequence, not of principle."

There were sample labels laid out in neat fans across the desk, cream stock and dark green borders. Silas had noticed them when he came in and pretended not to. Gideon drew one closer with a fingertip.

"People do not adopt a name because it is true," he said. "Most products have no truth to speak of. People adopt a name because it feels already worn by someone they trust. A wife at a wash line. A mother on a stair. A ferryman with a bad knee who has needed the thing in winter. Habit, Mr. Vale. The market rewards habit."

Silas laughed once, short through the nose. "You want home words."

"I want tested words."

"From where?"

Gideon looked at him as if the answer ought to have been obvious enough to shame him. "From the people who have already done the testing for free."

The clerk's fennel crack came faintly through the door. Somewhere below, a press thudded three times and stopped. Silas stared at the labels. The blanks had headings penciled at the top in Gideon's elegant square hand: kitchen, stairs, nursery, sickroom, yard.

"Those are neighborhood names," Silas said. "Use names."

"Yes."

"You want to strip them."

Gideon lifted one shoulder. "I want to place them. Strip is a word for men who break doors at midnight. I am speaking of circulation."

"Circulation that gets billed."

"Naturally."

Silas rubbed his forefinger against the nicotine stain on his thumb. He could feel the little ridge of dried paint under the nail. He thought of Junie's coal bin. He thought of the Havelock receipt warm already against his chest. He thought, unwillingly, of Inez in Junie's kitchen two nights before, sleeves shoved up, explaining in that flat steady voice why a name that cooked too hot could blister a child's hand before the rice softened.

"You said we were looking for the stable public name," he said.

"We are looking for whatever will make the public ask twice. One banner name, yes. Several household shades beneath it. Shades breed loyalty. People like to think their purchase has intimacy."

"So truth can go to hell."

Gideon's mouth changed by half an inch, which in him counted as candor. "Truth is a scholar's hobby. I am trying to keep hold of a city."

Silas leaned back and let the chair take his weight. "You can't get those names from me by asking pretty. Most of them won't tell me where they piss, let alone what they call the glow in the nursery."

"Then do not ask pretty."

"You make theft sound like a table manner."

"You make too much of ownership." Gideon folded his hands. "A phrase spoken in a kitchen does not become sacred simply because the ceiling is low. If a useful name exists, leaving it buried is waste. We will improve distribution. Households benefit. We benefit. You are paid."

There it was, the whole creed in one dry mouthful. Folk knowledge as ore in wet ground. Somebody else's labor, already proven under heat and hunger, waiting for a man with better stationery.

Silas ought to have walked out then. He knew that. The knowledge did not move his body an inch.

Part of the trouble was that Gideon was not wholly wrong about the names themselves. Silas had heard enough now, in wash yards and stairwells and over Junie's sink, to know the best ones came out of use the way color came out of a bruised plum. Nobody invented them from nowhere. They arrived with burnt fingers and dark landings and old women refusing to break their hips in the privy dark. They had work on them. They clung.

And he loved that. He loved it even while he hated the hand reaching for it.

"Suppose I bring you trash," he said. "Jokes. Decoys. Half the city has decided it's funnier to lie to me than to pay their tabs."

"Then bring me enough of it to sort." Gideon reached for a small morocco notebook and pushed it across the desk. "You know how words wear. You know when a mouth is trying on a hat and when it is naming the pan it uses every morning. That is why I retained you and not a stenographer."

The notebook was better than anything Silas carried for himself, soft leather, thick paper that would take ink without feathering. He opened it. Gideon had ruled faint columns on the first page. Sound, use, district, repeatability.

Silas barked a laugh in spite of himself. "Repeatability."

"That is the only category that matters."

"That is the only category that matters to you."

"Which is why I am solvent."

Silas flipped the notebook shut. He could feel the trap with both hands. He could also feel the craft in it, and that was worse. There was a game inside the theft, a difficult and delicate one: separating live language from the city's deliberate nonsense, hearing what had been worn smooth by need, choosing what would travel without dying in transit. Gideon knew it. Gideon had built a ledger around that knowledge before Silas had admitted it to himself.

"If I do this," Silas said, "I'm not promising you the final name."

"You are promising me useful raw stock."

"You have a hell of a gift for making ugly work sound like a refinery."

"And you have a gift for making ugly work sound like art. This is why we suit each other, Mr. Vale."

Silas stood because if he stayed seated he might throw the notebook back across the desk and watch whether Gideon flinched. Gideon would not. That was part of what made him intolerable.

"You will want quick returns," Silas said.

"I always do."

"And if I decide some of it's too private?"

Gideon looked up at him. The gray gloves rested motionless on the blotter. "You will decide according to your need for money and according to the very evident standard by which you have lived so far."

Silas felt the line land. It was not loud. Gideon never wasted volume where humiliation would do better.

"That's a cheap shot from a man whose cuffs cost my rent."

"Cheap shots are for crowds. I am only taking inventory."

Silas tucked the notebook under his arm and headed for the door.

"Mr. Vale," Gideon said behind him.

Silas stopped.

"Do not waste time looking for authenticity as if it were a saint's relic. I do not need the truest word. I need the word a stranger will believe has already been used by someone poorer and wiser than himself."

Silas turned enough to show half his face. "That may be the foulest sentence I hear this week."

"Then let us hope the week is professionally fruitful."

On the stair down, the air got hotter with each landing. By the time he reached the street the Havelock receipt was sticking to his chest, damp through the cloth, and the little notebook in his hand felt less like paper than a fresh-made tool somebody had put there for burglary.

* * *

Junie did not thank him for the sack of potatoes or the coil of smoked eel or the jar of lamp mash he set near the stove. Gratitude was not the house currency. She lifted the mash, judged the weight with one hand, and called to the pot boy to keep his filthy thumb out of it. Then she squinted at Silas across the steam.

Flour had pasted itself to the split in her left eyebrow and gone gray with sweat. "Havelock's runner came by smiling this morning," she said. "I liked him better mean."

"You'll live."

"That's usually the plan."

She went back to skimming eel fat. "Whatever your patent man bought, he had better not have bought your last clean thought with it."

Silas took a heel of bread from the board and tore it apart with both hands. "What makes you think I had one."

"Because you're still vain enough to keep one in reserve."

That was all she said. Lunch was coming on. Ferry bells knocked against the damp air outside, and the room had already shifted from family to trade. Men stamped in with rope burn on their palms and river mud drying high on their boots. Otis darted through once with a stack of spoons and a trouser knee gone black from the dock, then vanished again when Junie snapped his name. Nobody in the room had time for a confession, which suited Silas better than mercy would have.

He left by the side door with Gideon's notebook in his pocket and the city already talking around him.

At Ferrymouth the first lie came from a ferryman with two wedding rings on one hand and none on the other, a broad pink man who hummed hymn tunes while he patched net floats between runs.

"What are folks calling that glow now?" Silas asked, leaning on the rail.

The ferryman did not even look up. "Taxman's Mercy."

"That sounds invented."

"Everything sounds invented till the third week."

The next came from a woman gutting silver eel behind a shed, her sleeves rolled to the shoulder and one ear stuffed with wool against the gull noise. She said the proper ward name was "Bishop's Spit" and smiled without teeth. Three boys on overturned crates snorted so hard one of them nearly fell backward into the fish scales.

By the fourth and fifth Silas had the measure of it. The lies came too fast, too polished, or with too much waiting after them. A real use name had weight in the mouth. It carried a task with it. These came dressed for the stage: dock bride, saint-piss, bellglass milk, widow wink, prayer mold, green fever lace. They were excellent, some of them. That was the infuriating part. The city could lie beautifully when it had a target.

And the target was him.

Word had gone ahead. He could tell by the way people sharpened when he drifted near, by the little nudges and glances before somebody offered up a phrase. A teamster with a cauliflower ear said "wharf virgin" and got a cuff on the arm from his partner for trying too hard. A girl selling ribbon scraps from a tray at Patent Row, no more than fourteen and already keeping her own accounts in blue pencil on the inside of her cuff, told him it was called "Mother's Lie" in three districts and held his gaze until he wrote it down. Then she burst out laughing and said, "Now he has it in his book."

Silas laughed too. He could not help it. The whole thing had the shape of a card hustle run against him by a city that had finally decided to learn his favorite trick and improve it. He spent an hour following the lies anyway, partly because sometimes a false trail crossed a true one, partly because he was good at hearing how people chose their nonsense, and partly because the chase still pleased him. It pleased the part of him that liked pigments lined in jars and letters balanced on a brush point and the first hot second before a crowd decided whether to hate him or cheer.

He sat on an upturned cask outside a chandlery and sorted the notebook pages with his thumb. Gideon's columns were beginning to fill.

Taxman's Mercy: repeated by men who had been watching each other too closely. Joke.

Mother's Lie: better. Bitter enough to feel used. Could be decoy. Could be old. Worth another listen.

Bellglass Milk: nonsense, but elegant nonsense.

Dock Bride: spread by children too quickly to trust.

He hated himself for enjoying the discrimination. Good lies had their own brushwork. He could hear where a word had been fattened to tempt him, where a little smear of obscenity had been laid on for his benefit because everyone in Nacre Bend knew he liked his language with a little gutter in it. The neighborhoods were not merely refusing him. They were feeding him counterfeit according to his taste.

By midafternoon the notebook looked busy and told him almost nothing. He crossed Patent Row under walls still ghosted from the failed Celadon launch and watched two newsboys arguing over whether bride-rot would make a better headline than crown-slime. Even the children were jamming the line on purpose now. A grocer's wife with violets pinned to a hat gone shiny at the brim leaned out to call after him, "Ask Sump Ward for angel sweat, Mr. Vale. They keep barrels of it."

Her friend inside the shop laughed so hard she had to brace a hand against the flour bin.

Self-defense, Silas thought. Not just mockery. The city had understood the exchange. If names had value, then value could be spoiled at the mouth before it reached the buyer. Feed the patent man noise. Make him pay to sort sand from sugar. Make him work for what he meant to take for free.

He admired that too.

By the time the light started bending toward evening, the honest part of the day had made itself plain. Nobody with sense was going to hand him anything useful. Not in Ferrymouth, not on Patent Row, not while his mouth still had Gideon's money on it.

Which meant the asking part was over.

He stood under a poster half scraped from brick and looked south where the street fell into the wetter districts. Sump Ward lay there in its own haze, all plank cuts and low roofs and lamp vapor. He told himself he was going because their names would be easier to recognize against the trash. He told himself he would only listen. He told himself half a dozen things a man told himself when he had already chosen the uglier road and wanted it paved before he walked it.

Then he put the notebook back in his pocket and went down toward the water.

* * *

Sump Ward always felt lower than it was, as if the whole district had learned over years of flood to tuck its shoulders against the sky. The heat stayed trapped between the houses. Wet shirts on lines dragged cool against Silas's cheek when the breeze shifted. Planks flexed over black runoff ditches that smelled of soap, algae mash, and old river weed. Somewhere close, fermentation pipes gave their slow underwater bubbling like a giant stomach trying not to complain.

Inez was on the catwalk outside one of the grow houses, trimming pale frill from the edge of a mother tray with a thin hooked knife. She had her hair wrapped in the dark work scarf again. The burn scar inside her right wrist flashed once when she turned the tray. She saw him before he had both boots on the landing and her face went still in the precise way she had when she was angry enough not to spend extra movement.

"What do you want?" she said.

"Brought back the warning tin from Ferrymouth. The one I repainted."

He lifted it. The sign still smelled of fresh size and lampblack. LOW HEAT ONLY in hard clear letters, arrow beneath, no flourish. He had done it plain on purpose.

Inez wiped the knife on a rag. "Leave it by the door."

"That's all?"

"What else were you hoping for."

Before he could answer, Aunt Vero called from inside, "If he's hoping for praise, tell him to take off his boots first. I don't want gratitude tracked over the clean boards."

She emerged carrying a basket of cuttings lined with damp burlap. Her thick white braid had come loose at the nape, and one eye drifted a little as she sized him up, which only made the look meaner because it felt like being judged twice from slightly different angles.

"Afternoon, Vero," Silas said.

"That depends who is having it."

"I fixed the sign."

"Then you know how nails work. Hang it and be useful for one full minute before you turn back into yourself."

Inez stepped aside just far enough to let him pass, which was not welcome so much as the absence of a barricade. He took the hammer from the hook by the door, set the tin where she pointed, and drove the nails in while three women inside the grow house pretended not to watch him. The boards sweated under his palm. The last nail bent crooked, and he had to pull it and do it again.

When he stepped down, Inez was already carrying trays toward the back room. "Since you're here," she said without looking at him, "take that stack to the wash bench. Not the blue-tagged ones."

There was the discipline in her: if a man insisted on occupying space, turn him into labor.

Silas lifted the stack. Wet heat came through the wood into his hands. The moss on top glowed a queasy green under the burlap, sulking after a trim. He followed the narrow passage between hanging sacks and shelves of jars, shoulders turned to avoid brushing the live edges.

Voices carried more cleanly inside than out. He heard Aunt Vero muttering at somebody to stop breathing directly over the starter beds as if youth itself were a contaminant. He heard a younger woman's answer, quick and tired.

"I'm not breathing on them, I'm asking which trays go to Belen first."

The young woman came into view only by slivers between hanging cloth. She had spectacles mended with wire at one temple and little fish drawn in chalk all over the hem of her apron, as if she had been amusing a child while the starch dried. She was balancing two trays against her hip and waiting for instruction.

Inez answered from the room beyond, all business. "Hushgreen to her bed. Potback to the broth shelf. And put step-blue by the privy door before dusk, or she will miss the plank again."

Silas stopped so abruptly the tray edges bit his palm.

Nobody had said the words to him. Nobody had even lowered a voice because of him; that was the point. The names were moving inside a task the way they had always moved, fast and useful and attached to a body that needed something before night. Sickroom. Cooking shelf. Safe footing in the dark. Three names and what they did, all at once.

He could have made noise then. Shifted his boot. Let the stack thump. Given the room a chance to clot around the words and keep them.

Instead he stood still.

Hushgreen. Potback. Step-blue.

He set them in order in his mind the way he set letters before brush met board. Not by sound first but by placement: bed, broth, plank. Then by sound, because he could not help that either. Hushgreen with the breath buried in it. Potback blunt and work-worn. Step-blue quick on the tongue and impossible to misunderstand.

The tray in his hands felt suddenly heavier, not because it weighed more but because he knew what he was doing and kept doing it.

When he moved again he did it carefully, as if gentleness altered the nature of the act. He laid the stack on the wash bench. His fingers had left wet crescents on the wood.

Inez came through the hanging cloth a moment later with her own arms full of cuttings. She looked at him once. Maybe she saw something in his face, maybe only sweat. "You can go," she said.

"That's the warmest dismissal I've had all day."

"Then your day is earning its character."

Aunt Vero barked a laugh from the next room. "Don't flirt at my tray beds unless you're planning to fertilize them with gold."

Silas gave them the grin his body knew how to produce without help. "If I ever shit coins, Vero, I will start with your doorstep."

"No, you won't. You'd spend them showing off halfway down the lane."

The exchange landed the way such exchanges always did, half comic, half blade. Inez did not smile. That was what finally made him leave.

Outside, dusk had thickened the ward into blue-green layers. He crossed two plank bridges and ducked under the eave of an abandoned wash shed when the first warm spit of rain came on. Mosquitoes combed the air over the ditch. From somewhere behind him, Belen or some other old woman started coughing, the wet chest-deep kind that made a whole house sound short of air.

Silas pulled the Havelock receipt from his waistcoat because it was the first paper his hand found. Forty-three crowns, settled in full. On the blank back he wrote:

hushgreen - bed/sickroom

potback - broth, kitchen

step-blue - privy/stairs

The nib scratched harder than he meant it to. He stared at the words until the ink took hold and lost the wobble of his hand.

There. That was the moment. Not the overhearing. Men overheard things every day. Not even the remembering, because memory could still fail if a man was decent enough to let it. This. The paper, the pinning down, the choice to make somebody else's working speech into stock.

Rain drummed harder on the shed roof. Silas folded the receipt along the old crease so the paid debt sat inside the stolen names and put it back over his heart.

Then he walked uphill to deliver it.

* * *

Verdancy House never truly slept. It only changed machinery. By day the showroom windows glittered and clerks moved about with samples on velvet pads. By evening the back floors woke: paste heated in kettles, proofs hung from lines, and men with ink-black cuffs argued over type sizes as if salvation depended on twelve-point versus fourteen.

Gideon had moved downstairs to the proofing room by the time Silas arrived. He stood at a long table under three shaded lamps, gloves off at last, sleeves unmarked. Sheets lay all around him, some handbills for patent tonics, some bills for a summer recital on Gilt Crescent, some blank stock waiting for whatever fresh appetite the city could be taught by dawn.

A pressman with a flattened left ear was setting type in a composing stick nearby and singing to himself under his breath. Every few lines he paused to tuck a peppermint clove into one cheek, which gave the whole room a medicinal sweetness over the ink.

Gideon looked up. "You have something."

Silas laid the folded receipt on the table. "I've got possibilities."

Gideon opened it and read in silence. His eyes moved once, then back, slower. The pressman stopped singing without being told to.

"And the rest?" Gideon said.

"Mostly jokes. Waste. People have grown strategic."

"Good. Resistance confirms value."

Silas pulled Gideon's notebook from his pocket and flipped it open. "You wanted enough to sort. Here. If you like Bellglass Milk for a nursery line, by all means damn yourself with it."

Gideon took the notebook, skimmed a page, and almost immediately set his finger on three entries. Not the funniest ones. Not the flashiest. The useful ones.

"These are false," he said, tapping dock bride and taxman's mercy away. "These are vulgar decoys. These" - his finger came back to the receipt - "have wear."

Silas's jaw set. "You can hear wear, can you."

"So can you."

That was the worst part again: no need for pretense between them. Gideon understood the theft exactly and admired it more for that.

He handed the notebook to the pressman. "Mr. Herron. Pull me a household series under the Celadon umbrella. Half-sheet first, wall bills by district after."

Herron wiped his fingers on his apron and took the book. The flattened ear made his head sit slightly askew, and when he read he moved his lips, a habit from childhood or injury or both. "Text, sir?"

Gideon was already composing aloud. "Celadon Crown Household Shades. Hushgreen for nurseries and convalescent rooms. Potback for kettle, galley, and cooking shelf. Step-Blue for stairs, privies, and safe night crossings."

Silas heard the transformation happen in real time: not only the theft of the names but the theft of the labor inside them. Broth shelf became kettle, galley, and cooking shelf because richer buyers would not think of broth first. Privy door became safe night crossings because embarrassment sold worse than reassurance. Gideon was not merely taking the words. He was polishing the fingerprints off them while keeping enough grime to look trustworthy.

"You can't print those by supper," Silas said.

"Why not."

"Because they're not yours."

Gideon glanced at him. "They are not anyone's in the only sense that matters."

"That's not a sentence, that's a burglary tool."

Herron coughed into his fist and kept setting type. The tiny metal clicks sounded like teeth.

Silas leaned both hands on the table. "I brought them for assessment. Not immediate issue."

"Do not be childish. If they work, delay helps your opponents." Gideon sorted through paper weights until he found the green border he preferred. "You have done your part. Let me do mine."

"My opponents."

"Do not go sentimental now, Mr. Vale. It makes you vague."

Silas wanted to snatch the receipt back. He wanted to grind the wet ink under his palm and leave the whole room smeared. But he had brought the words here himself, folded in with the proof of his paid debt like a joke too exact to laugh at. Gideon had only unfolded what Silas delivered.

Herron set the first proof on the roller bed and looked to Gideon. "Caps on the shades, sir?"

"Yes. Let them read at distance."

The press took the sheet with a sucking kiss and spit it out wet and sharp. HUSHGREEN sat there in black block letters, clean as a magistrate. The paper shone where the ink had not yet dried.

Silas stared at it long enough for the letters to stop being letters and become what they would do once boys shouted them at corners and wives asked for them over counters and some idiot on Patent Row explained them to a room he had never cooked in.

"You could still choose not to," he said, though now it sounded weak even to him.

Gideon signed the order slip. "No. You could have chosen not to."

The bill-stickers went out before midnight with buckets of paste and rolled sheets under their arms. Silas did not mean to follow them. He found himself doing it anyway, trailing a half block back through streets gone slick under a thin rain. Patent Row first, then the turn toward Ferrymouth, then the lower lanes where people woke early enough to catch the first boat and would read with their mouths still tasting sleep.

Paste slapped brick. Brushes rasped. The men worked quickly, covering old advertisements and hand-painted warnings alike. On a warehouse wall near the slips, a fresh half-sheet went up over a faded notice for tide levels. On the side of a coal yard, another covered a chalked menu. By the time the first bakery fire was being coaxed alive, CELADON CROWN HOUSEHOLD SHADES had begun marching down the city in even green borders.

At the corner by Junie's lane, one of the bill-stickers papered over a hand-lettered board Silas had done years back for a widow who sold boiled coffee before dawn. He watched HUSHGREEN go across her old price list like a coffin lid.

A woman with her hair tied in a rag came out carrying a chamber pail and stopped to squint at the new bill. She had a cut on her knuckle wrapped in blue thread and sleep still stamped along one cheek.

"Step-Blue," she read aloud, tasting it. "For stairs."

From inside the house behind her, somebody answered, "That sounds useful."

Useful. That was how theft crossed the threshold. Not with a scream. With recognition. With a tired stranger hearing a true thing stolen into a new uniform and reaching for it because dawn was cold and the steps were slick and usefulness had never had the luxury of purity.

Silas stood in the rain and watched the paper shine.

By six bells, three ward names were already in mouths that had never earned them, and the paste on the walls was still wet.


# Chapter 8

Silas reached Sump Ward with his clean shirt already pasted to his spine and the mud named for the feast sucking at his boots as if the ward itself wanted an answer before he got a step farther. Mid-summer heat had kept the digesters lively all day. Even at dusk the air above the drainage cuts smelled of yeast, sweet rot, lamp smoke, and hot river weeds. Lanterns were going up on clotheslines between the grow houses, green and blue and one sudden vulgar blush-pink where some patch had answered to city gossip again. He had told himself the Feast of Mud Names was the one night a man might disappear inside other people's noise. He could carry tables. He could keep his mouth shut. If fortune grew unexpectedly charitable, he could find Inez at the edge of the crowd and say one honest thing before she told him to go to hell.

The feast always came after the first hard weeks of summer, when the ward mud stopped pretending it might dry and settled into its true profession of swallowing shoes, stools, pails, tempers, and sometimes whole hens. Sump Ward answered by feeding itself on top of the mess and naming every fresh indignity something memorable enough to laugh at. By the time Silas ducked under the first wash line, doors had already been laid across casks for tables, planks had been bridged over the worst soft patches, and somebody had chalked names onto slates tied to the serving bowls. A tureen of crab stew wore the title *Bridegroom's Shame.* A crock of pickled okra had become *Saint Judas Fingers.* A pan of corn cakes steamed under the pious lie *Mudfast Virtue.*

Aunt Vero stood at the middle of it with her thick white braid looped down her back like mooring rope and her broad hands deep in a bowl of pepper mash. Heat and algae stain had browned her wrists nearly to walnut. One eye drifted a little when she was amused, which it was now, because a knot-kneed fiddler on an upturned crate had just missed three notes in a row and was trying to bluff the fourth. She saw Silas before anyone else announced him.

"If you came to stare, charge admission," she said. "If you came to work, take that end."

She jerked her chin at a door table leaning crooked against a wall. Silas obeyed before she could reconsider. The opposite end lifted in the hands of a girl with cropped hair and a split thumbnail stained purple from berry dye. She didn't offer her name and he didn't ask. Together they hauled the door through a lane of children who were trying to rename a puddle beside the privy by democratic insult.

"It's Bishop's Bath," one boy declared.

"It's not," said another, missing a front tooth. He held a spoon like a gavel. "It's Courting Soup."

Silas set down his end of the table, wiped his palms on his trousers, and heard himself say, "It's Tax Collector's Kiss. It sticks to you and leaves less than it took."

That bought him a burst of laughter so quick and easy it hurt. Aunt Vero barked once through her nose. The gap-toothed boy slapped the mud with the spoon as if ratifying law. Somebody down the line shouted that Patent Row Silas could stay if he kept that sort of sense and none of the expensive kind. The remark came with no poison in it yet.

He had not understood until then how badly he had wanted that lack of poison.

The ward moved around him with the loose efficiency of people who had done this every year and expected to do it in old age if the city let them. Women spread oilcloths with one hand and kept babies off the trough rims with the other. Men came in from the catwalks carrying jugs, lamp cages, and a whole skinned carp on a board. The knot-kneed fiddler found his tune and stayed with it, sawing through a reel while a woman with her sleeves pinned above strong freckled elbows kept time on a spoon against a crock. The planks sweated underfoot. Mosquitoes hovered at calf height. Every few minutes the Sallow River announced itself with a ferry bell or the hollow knock of a hull against pilings.

Silas took a stack of bowls where someone pointed, then a basket of crusts, then a barrel hoop that had come loose from a beer cask. Working was easier than arriving. Work gave his hands somewhere to live while his mind tried not to circle the week behind him. His mind kept circling Gideon's office, the good paper, and the little kick in his gut when the advance cleared Havelock Pigments and made his worst creditor suddenly courteous. The nastier memory under all the rest was of himself on a Sump Ward catwalk, still as a rat in the reeds, listening to women use a language built for keeping light from hurting people, and storing it in his head like lifted silver.

He was carrying a crate of cups when he saw Inez.

She came out of the side grow house with a tin basin on one hip and a length of pruning twine still looped around her left wrist, as if she had changed for the feast only as far as the door and no farther. She had traded her work apron for a dark skirt and a clean blouse with the sleeves rolled anyway. Her scarf was fresh tied, indigo with a small tear near one edge where it had been caught on a nail and mended badly. The crescent burn on the inside of her right wrist flashed pale when she shifted the basin higher. She moved across slick plank with the same steady placement she would have used in a flooded cellar. One of the little girls trailing her kept trying to match steps on the narrow boards and failing.

Inez caught the child by the shoulder without looking down. "Heel first on the dry stripe," she said. "Not in the shine. You want supper or a mouthful of trough?"

The girl corrected. Inez let go. Then she saw Silas with the cups.

Nothing softened in her face. Nothing hardened either. She looked at the cups, at the line of people waiting for them, and made a practical choice.

"Those go by the cider cask," she said. "Not there. Unless you want everybody drinking crab grease."

He set them by the cask. "I can avoid that much ruin."

"We'll see."

It was not forgiveness. It was not even kindness. Still, she had spoken to him as if he existed inside the work instead of outside it. He took the scrap as greedily as a beggar takes crust.

For half an hour the feast let him keep it. He fetched more jugs. He lettered two fresh slates with a carpenter's pencil because the rain of last week had blurred the first batch into gray ghosts. He kept the lettering broad and plain, without Patent Row flourishes, and when Aunt Vero looked over his shoulder at *Last Ferry Eel* and *Cold Pot Green* she grunted as if he had at least not made an ass of the nouns. A woman with a cracked alto voice demanded a better name for the beer that had soured in one cask and turned sweet in the next. Silas said *Widow's Surprise,* and the knot-kneed fiddler nearly dropped his bow laughing.

The warmth of it was the dangerous part. It was not dangerous because they laughed at him. That happened everywhere. It was dangerous because they laughed with him and then went back to their own talk as if he might, if he behaved, be one body among others instead of the sort of man whose mouth had to stand on a crate and collect coin. The Feast of Mud Names turned naming into common property for an evening. A child could improve a grandmother's joke. A grandmother could spit on a man's contribution and replace it with something better. Nothing stayed owned long enough to go stale. Silas had spent years making a trade out of sticking a phrase to a surface until nobody could imagine the surface bare. Here the pleasure ran the other way. Names were thrown, used, revised, laughed off, and born again two bowls later.

It made the thing he had done sit in him worse.

By full dark the lanterns were lit. Blue light lay cool along the plank edges. Green lamps under the tables made bare ankles strange. Somebody had hung strings of polished mussel shells that ticked together whenever a warm draft came off the ferment rooms. Silas had a tin cup of sour cider in one hand and a heel of cornbread in the other when he noticed the paper packets.

At first he took them for favors or betting slips. Two boys had strung them through a cord and were wearing them like medals across their chests. A woman used one to fan smoke away from a grill. Another had folded a packet into a little scoop for salt. The paper was cheap and stiff, but the corners carried a gilt crown stamped so thin it rubbed off on fingers. The mark was Verdancy House, and his stomach went hollow before he saw the words.

The printer had set the letters, not his hand. But the block capitals had come from the sample alphabet he had sketched for Gideon's label man, broad at the shoulder and mean little spurs on the R and K. He knew them the way he knew the notch in his left thumbnail. And on the front of the nearest packet, in green ink already smudged by grease, sat a name he had heard behind Aunt Vero's south wall three nights earlier.

*RAIL-BLUE.*

Below it, in smaller print: For steady crossing light.

He might still have walked out then if his body had obeyed the thought. Instead he stood with cider halfway to his mouth and watched the rest of the ward see what he had already seen.

Inez was the first to stop moving.

She had been reaching past one of the boys to take a ladle from the table. The packet string caught on her knuckles. She glanced down with irritation, then took one between finger and thumb. Her gaze moved once over the lettering. Then she read it again. The air seemed to narrow around her.

"Where'd you get this?" she asked the boy.

He grinned, pleased to have produced an object worth adult attention. "Man at Lantern Steps. Three for a farthing if you said the names out loud."

His friend chimed in, "He gave us six because Nilo said *rail-blue* faster than me."

The knot-kneed fiddler was still playing, though softer now because people close to him had turned their heads. Inez plucked the next packet off the string. Then the next. Her hands were steady. That was worse than if she had torn them.

*PAN-COOL.* For kitchen lamps.
*WASH-LINE GREEN.* For damp rooms.
Then she reached the one that made the color leave her face without making her pale.

*MOTHER-HUSH.* For nursery calm.

Silas knew that one too. He had heard it through slatted wood, Aunt Vero saying it low to a young mother whose baby would not sleep near hot light. He had remembered it because the phrase sat oddly in the mouth, soft first, then shut. He had sold it because Gideon had wanted everything useful and because Silas, in the moment that mattered, had wanted to be the kind of man who got to keep the advance more than he had wanted to deserve Inez's trust.

Inez lifted her head and looked straight at him. There was anger in it, but it was not the bright clean anger he had earned from her on Lantern Steps. This was flatter and worse. The look of a woman seeing her own wash hung out in public with strangers' hands on it.

"Who gave you that word?" she asked.

Noise still moved at the edges of the feast. A baby cried once. Somebody set down a bowl too hard. But the middle of the ward had gone quiet enough that he heard a shell string tap against itself in the warm air.

Silas's first instinct was the old one: talk, rename, make the thing a little sideways so he could get his foot out from under it.

"Inez, those are Gideon's packets," he said. "He'll print saint names, dog names, a drowned man's last cough if he thinks it sells mash. I didn't know he'd scatter them down here tonight."

Her voice stayed level. "That wasn't what I asked."

He felt the crowd turn toward him the way water turns when a bigger hull cuts through it. The boy with the packet string stopped smiling and looked from one adult to another, trying to find the rule he'd broken.

Aunt Vero was beside Inez now. She held out her hand. Inez gave her the *MOTHER-HUSH* packet. The older woman read slowly, lips moving over the printed phrase, not because she could not read it but because she meant to make each part answer for itself. The drifting eye settled hard enough to seem straight.

There was no use lying. He had already stepped past any lie that would save him. "I heard things," he said. "That's the truth of it."

Aunt Vero looked up. "Heard."

"Yes."

"Not told."

There was no use denying that either. "Not told."

The fiddler let the tune die mid-bar.

Aunt Vero turned a little so the people beyond her could hear. When she spoke again, her voice had gone oddly formal, the way some old women prayed before they cursed. "This fool listened where he was not invited. He took words off working mouths. Then he sold them uphill so they could come back wrapped in trash paper."

That broke the stillness because the feast had dozens of people inside it who knew at once what they were hearing. A woman by the eel grill snatched a packet from her salt scoop and squinted at it. A man near the cider cask dug two more out of his pocket with the baffled disgust of a person finding lice in a cap. Voices began to rise, not yet in one shout, but in separate recognitions.

"Pan-cool is ours."

"My sister says wash-line green in the damp room."

"Who the hell printed rail-blue?"

"They're selling crossing words now?"

One of the older men laughed once, ugly and unbelieving. "For a farthing apiece, by the look of it."

Silas saw the movement travel through them as memory. Each packet called back a room, a hand, an instruction passed between women over steam or between neighbors on wet steps after dark. The names were not ornaments here. They were habits for keeping light useful, for keeping skin unburned, for finding the rail before a drunk uncle found the canal instead. He had known that. That was what made it theft instead of cleverness. He had known and done it anyway.

Inez had not taken her eyes off him. "Who gave you *mother-hush*?" she asked again.

He could still have spared himself the full shame by refusing the question, by letting the crowd decide the rest from his silence. Instead he answered because the word itself had become too filthy in his mouth to hide behind.

"No one gave it," he said. "I heard your aunt say it in the south house. Through the slats."

For a second he thought Inez might strike him. It was not because she stepped forward; she did not. It was because something in her face finally moved, and the movement was smaller than rage. It was hurt stripped of surprise, a clean cut. He had not been prepared for that. His own shame had kept him imagining her anger as heat. It turned out to be cold workmanship instead, the precise knowledge of where a blade went.

"You listened at a nursery wall," she said.

He opened his mouth and nothing useful came out. "I..."

"You listened," she said, "at a nursery wall."

That landed harder than any public insult he had ever collected. The crowd heard it land. So did he. In the silence behind it he could picture the place exactly: damp boards, one lamp trimmed low, a fretful child, Aunt Vero teaching a new mother which light ran coolest. He had made it sound to himself like research. Standing there with a hundred bodies and supper smells around him, he saw it plain. A grown man crouched by slats, stealing household caution because a rich bastard wanted new copy.

He said the worst honest thing before he could dress it up. "Yes."

The uglier voices came then.

"Thief."

"Patent Row rat."

"He took nursery words."

A woman Silas knew only as the one who always wore a brass thimble on her necklace because her hands shook too much to sew without losing it said, "My niece blistered two fingers when the wrong name went around last month. You selling the right ones too now? Is that the trick?"

He had no answer she could use.

Aunt Vero did. She held up the packet between finger and thumb as if it were damp laundry. "This one told women which lamp stayed cool in a fever room," she said. "This one told boys which blue would hold to a handrail in flood dark. This one" - she shook *MOTHER-HUSH* once - "never belonged to a market at all. He did not take poetry. He took instructions. He took the names that keep people from getting hurt and sent them to a man who'd print them by the hundred with a crown in the corner."

The cheap gold flashed under the lantern and seemed suddenly obscene.

Silas heard himself start up again, the old reflex dying hard. "I told Gideon not to strip the use off them. I told him if he printed them, he had to keep the point..."

Inez laughed, once. It was not pleasant. "You told him."

He shut his mouth.

"Did he pay extra for your concern?" she asked.

"No."

"Did he pay enough for you to stop?"

He thought of Havelock Pigments wiping his slate, of Junie's thin margin, of the way need had looked respectable while he was standing in Gideon's office. Those things were true. None of them helped him here. None of them should.

"No," he said.

Aunt Vero snorted. "Look at that. He can answer plain when the whole ward hears him."

A pressure had built in the bodies around him. It was not a theatrical circle or the neat spacing of a show crowd on Patent Row. This was worse because it was household close: elbows, bowls, lamp handles, damp skirts, working boots, the ordinary distance at which people fed one another and argued about salt. Silas could feel heat off them. Somebody behind him set down a jug with deliberate care, freeing both hands. The knot-kneed fiddler was no longer holding his bow at all. He had laid it across his lap and was looking at Silas with the pinched pity a man gives a dog before somebody else kicks it.

The shame turned physical in stupid little ways. The cider in Silas's stomach went sour. Sweat trickled down his ribs under the shirt. His right hand wanted a brush or a railing or anything to occupy it. He hated that some part of him still looked for the shape of escape, some final line that might win the room back. There was none. A room that had been bought from him before could be bought again. This one had to live by what he had stolen.

A man stepped out from the cider cask. Silas had seen him before on the ward walks, a lamp trimmer maybe, narrow in the hips and broad in the chest, with lye whitening the hairs of his forearms and a baby's knitted sock tied absurdly to one vest button for luck. He did not say anything. He only planted both palms in the middle of Silas's chest and drove.

Silas hit the plank edge with one heel, skidded, pinwheeled once, and went over backward into the fermentation trough.

Warm mash closed over his shoulders with a sound like a crowd inhaling. It was thinner than mud and thicker than soup, alive with bubbles and stray skins of algae. It forced itself up his nose, into his ears, between his collar and neck. The trough had taken a spill-born chorus strain earlier in the week, and the mash answered the lamp light in an oily green shot through with blush-pink. When Silas came up choking, hair plastered flat and one boot gone, the stuff made him glow like a cheap saint painted by a drunken apprentice.

The ward laughed.

They could not help it. Neither could he, if he had been standing dry and somebody else had come snorting out of that trough with cabbage strings on his shoulder and luminous scum hanging off his lashes. The laugh cracked out of fury and sat inside it, ugly and genuine both. Someone shouted, "Name that, then," and somebody else said, "Call it Patent Baptism," and even Aunt Vero's mouth twitched before it flattened again.

Silas planted his palms on the trough rim to haul himself out.

His left hand held. His right slid on mash, caught against the iron hoop banding the trough, and folded under him. Pain shot from wrist to elbow in a white hard line so bright it cleared every thought from his head. He made a sound that was too raw to count as a word and dropped half back into the slurry. The laughter snapped short in the people nearest him. He clawed again with the left, dragging his useless right arm clear. The wrist had already started swelling under the smear of luminous mash.

"Don't grab him," Inez said sharply.

That stopped the two men who had moved forward, whether to help or to shove again Silas could not tell. She was still standing where the crowd had first opened around her, packets crushed in one hand now, green ink bleeding onto her palm. The precision had returned to her voice. It did not sound merciful. It sounded like a woman refusing to let the scene turn sloppy.

"He can get out," she said. "Let him feel where the edge is."

So he did. He hooked his left elbow over the trough rim, got a knee under him, and slid out onto the planks in a shining ruin. Mash ran off his sleeves and dripped from his nose. His stocking foot hit wood with a slap because the trough had eaten one boot. The missing boot bobbed once in the luminous slurry like a dead animal refusing burial.

The crowd had gone from laughter to something meaner and quieter. People could see the injury now. They could also see that it was not enough.

Silas cradled his right wrist against his chest and tried to stand straight. He was aware of every detail with humiliating clarity: the way the mash stank sweeter close up, the way wet shirt cloth sucked at the scar under his ribs, the way a thread of pink-lit slime crawled down from his ear to his jaw. He spat on the plank. Bright green spit joined the cracks between boards.

Aunt Vero came close enough to inspect him as if he were a spoiled batch. The drifting eye had started to wander again, this time from fatigue or disgust. When she spoke, the ceremonial note was gone. Only the ward remained.

"Now you look priced proper," she said.

No one laughed.

She lifted the packet string from the boy's neck and dropped it on Silas's shoulder. The little rectangles slapped wetly against his mash-coated shirt.

"You want labels, wear them."

The boy started to object, then thought better of it.

Inez stepped forward at last. She did not come all the way to him. She came just far enough that he could see the green ink ground into the lines of her hand from the crushed packets. He noticed absurd things even now: a loose thread at the edge of her cuff, the small damp crescent where sweat had darkened the scarf knot at her neck, the fleck of cornmeal on one knuckle. She looked tired more than enraged. That, more than anything, made him understand the size of what he had done. He had taken work from people already carrying too much of it.

"You don't get to stand in this ward and act surprised," she said. "You saw what the names were for. You saw the kitchens. You saw the crossings. Then you took what wasn't offered and let it come back as a trick."

"I know."

It was the only honest answer. It was too small. It was worthless. It was true.

Her mouth tightened. "No. You know now because we're making you hear it with witnesses. Before now you just wanted the money and the room."

The line found him exactly. He did not bother denying it.

Aunt Vero tipped her chin toward the north path, the one that climbed out of Sump Ward toward Lantern Steps. "Out," she said. "Eat your supper uphill. If you come down here tomorrow, you'll go in deeper. If you come with apologies, save them till they can carry water, cut rot, or mend a lamp. Your mouth is shut to us."

A murmur of agreement traveled the planks. It was not a cheer or the blood sound a mob makes when it smells a better injury ahead. This was household judgment. It was worse because it would last.

Silas looked once at Inez because some stupid part of him still asked for a smaller sentence from her than from the others. She gave him none.

"You listened where children sleep," she said. "Don't come back asking me to understand why."

There was room in that line for nothing but distance, not hatred. Hate at least admitted appetite. This was denial of claim.

He bent for his fallen hat and almost blacked out when instinct brought his right hand down. Pain burst hot and nauseating. He swore, low and useless. The thimble-necklace woman kicked the hat toward his left foot so he would not have to stoop so far. It was the only kindness left in the place and it shamed him nearly as much as the trough had. He picked it up with his left hand, jammed it against his mash-slick thigh, and started toward the north path with the packet string stuck to his chest and one wet stocking squelching on the boards.

No one touched him on the way out. They did not need to. The crowd held open just enough space to make plain that it was not company. Behind him he heard bowls lifted again, benches dragged, somebody telling the children to keep back from the trough. The fiddler did not resume at once. Then, after ten paces, he did, tentatively, like a man testing whether a broken thing could still carry tune.

Silas reached the last plank before the slope and turned despite himself.

The feast had already begun the work of closing without him. Aunt Vero was back at the tables, barking for more pepper mash. The knot-kneed fiddler found his reel again. The boy with the missing tooth leaned over the trough with two other children, pointing at Silas's lost boot and arguing some new ridiculous name for the shining slop around it. Inez had her back to him, one hand braced on the cider cask while she spoke to a young mother holding a drowsy child. She did not look up.

The ward had made its decision and gone on feeding itself. That was the shape of the truth. Patent Row shame had always required an audience that stayed with you long enough to enjoy itself. This was different. These people knew exactly how much of him they needed to waste: a shove, a trough, a closed path, then back to supper because kitchens still wanted tending and lamps still wanted the right words. He was not important enough for grand violence. He was only dangerous enough to bar.

By the time he climbed into Lantern Steps, the mash on his clothes had started to cool and tighten. His right wrist throbbed with each heartbeat, thickening under the skin. The packet string slid down and tangled at his elbow. He tore it off with his left hand and almost flung it into the drainage cut. Instead he looked at the wet paper stuck together there, his own borrowed alphabet blurring green into gold, the ward's working names turned to trash in a fist.

He shoved the whole mess into his pocket.

From below, past the plank walks and grow-house roofs, the Feast of Mud Names rose back into full sound: fiddle scrape, crockery knock, a woman's laugh sharp as a spoon on tin, children yelling over whatever name they had found for the trough that had baptized him and spat him out. The night kept moving. Sump Ward had its feast. Silas had a ruined shirt, one boot full of mash, a wrist already failing him, and no place left in that light.


# Chapter 9

By morning his right hand had the color and shape of a bad pear. Junie had bound it the night before with a strip torn off an old apron and two thin laths from a fish crate, and the whole thing had gone damp in his sleep. When Silas loosened the knots in the gray light of the shop, the wrist looked almost ordinary until he tried to turn it. Then pain ran from the heel of his hand to the elbow, bright and private as a nail pushed under the skin.

He sat on the stool by the front window and breathed through it until the room stopped narrowing. The shop smelled as it always did: oil, river damp, chalk dust, old varnish, the sweet rot of rags that had cleaned too many colors. Usually the smell steadied him. This morning it only made him aware of every place his body had been handled yesterday. Mud had dried in the seam of one boot. There was a faint green line under his thumbnail where the trough scum had lived through soap and scrubbing and another round of Junie calling him filthy as she tied up his hand.

He told himself he needed one clean board, one paid board, something square and ordinary enough to put the whole feast behind him. Men got laughed at in this city every day and still had to eat. A trough dunking was only a trough dunking if he could get paint to obey him by noon.

He had promised a fresh hanging sign to a collar-and-notions stall off Patent Row, a narrow place run by Mrs. Valey, who wore mourning for a husband dead six years and still pressed the black cuffs of her sleeves flat whenever she had to discuss money. She arrived ten minutes after opening with the old board under one arm and a canary-yellow tape measure looped around her neck like church trim.

"You look like hell," she said, which in her mouth counted as concern.

"That's kind of you, Vera."

"Don't flirt with me while you've got pond slime in your ear."

He put a hand to it at once. She snorted and set the board on the trestles.

"Just the same as before," she said. "Cream ground, blue letters. I don't want any of your patent flourishes. Women buying hook closures don't need to feel courted by a sign."

"You wound my trade."

"Your trade can survive it. Can your hand?"

"Course it can."

She looked at the splint, then at his face, and did him the favor of pretending not to see the lie settle there. Mrs. Valey worried the edge of one cuff between finger and thumb while he marked out the lines with charcoal in his left hand. The left could measure, no trouble. The left could even draw a passable guide. It was only when he picked up the sable liner with the right and tried to roll the stroke down into the first V that the morning went bad for real.

The brush should have turned under his fingers like a thing bred to it. Instead the wrist gave a tiny dead hitch. The line thickened where it ought to have narrowed, then dragged crooked across the cream field and finished in a little black hook that looked less like a letter than a fishbone.

Silas stood very still.

Outside on the lane a wagon hit a rut and somebody swore at a mule. From farther up Patent Row came the sing-song cry of a broadside boy selling midday scandal before midday existed. The city kept its own rhythm. The bad stroke sat on the board and did not improve.

"Well," Mrs. Valey said after a moment, quiet enough to save his pride if he wanted the saving. "That's not my V."

He wiped the brush. "No. It isn't."
