He took a rag dipped in turpentine and scrubbed the wet line off before it could bite into the ground. Black smeared gray. The smell rose sharp enough to sting his eyes. He waited for his hand to stop trembling, reset the brush, and tried again.

The second V looked like a man trying not to limp.

Mrs. Valey did not sigh. That made it worse. She only reached into her sleeve pocket, brought out the little purse she carried pinned inside her bodice, and counted the deposit coins back into her palm one by one. She always counted with her lips moving. Her dead husband had kept the books before his lungs gave out; now she moved her mouth for both of them.

"I'll take it to the stencil man near Gallow Court," she said. "I hate his work. He makes every shop look like a pharmacy. But he doesn't wobble."

"Give me two days."

"I need it before the excursion ferry comes through tomorrow."

"By tomorrow, then."

She folded the coins back into the purse. "Silas."

He hated pity from people without imagination. It felt too much like being wrapped for storage. "I'm fine."

"You're many things. Fine isn't the one that concerns me." She took the board back under her arm, the tape measure knocking lightly against her breastbone as she lifted. "I liked your old lettering because it looked as if a hand had done it. That was the virtue. But a hand still has to function."

After she went, he shut the shop door though the room was already hot. He tried two more boards from smaller jobs, one for a boat chandlery and one for a washhouse stair arrow, and ruined both in different ways. On the block letters the wrist failed at the end of each downstroke so the line bellied out as if the wood itself had softened. On the arrow he could not keep the point true. It bent drunkenly toward the gutter. He scraped, sanded, started over. By eleven the floor around the easel was furred with curled paint skins and his shirt stuck to his back.

The worst part was not the pain. The pain at least made sense. The worst part was how much of the work lived in a turn too small for anybody else to notice, in a fraction of pressure, a slight roll of finger and wrist, the tiny private grace that made an O sit proud instead of stupid. He had spent years teaching his hand to lie beautifully for money. Now the lie kept breaking open halfway through the sentence.

He wrapped the wrist again and went to the window for air. Across the street a Verdancy House runner was pasting fresh placards over last week's mess. The man had ears that stuck out through his wet hair and a habit of smoothing every corner with the side of his thumb before he moved on, as if he could not bear a wrinkle left alive behind him. CELADON CROWN, the posters said in pale green capitals bordered with gilt. ONE CLEAN NAME FOR A STEADY FLAME.

Silas had written that line three nights ago by Gideon's lamp, half drunk on relief because the advance had cleared Havelock Pigments and bought him one evening without creditors in his throat. He had thought it a neat piece of trade, with a bit of internal chiming. It was easy to remember, easy to mouth, the kind of phrase that slipped under a door before a man could set his boot against it.

Now it looked uglier in public than any insult painted on a privy wall.

The runner finished papering the post and stepped back to admire his rank little harvest. He noticed Silas in the window then, blinked once, and looked away too quickly. That was the new thing. Yesterday strangers had laughed in his face while he came up shining from the trough. Today they did not laugh. They refused him the common courtesy of pretending not to know.

Silas turned from the glass, counted what money remained in the till, and found less than he had been bracing for. There were a few loose crowns, some coppers, a half-used block of leaf, and the bent nib of a ruling pen. Paint he could not spread was only colored debt. He lowered the shade, locked the shop, and carried his bad hand down toward Ferrymouth because if he could not earn, he could at least lift things.

The river announced itself before he saw it. Ferrymouth always did: rope slap, gull noise, paddle churn, men calling distances they did not trust each other to judge, the steady iron complaint of docking hardware taking another blow. Junie's kitchen sat in the middle of it with both front shutters propped open, leaking steam and onion and eel broth into the dock stink. The sign over her door still read VALE'S BROTH & BREAD in his own hand, a bit faded on the downwind edge. He stood under it a second too long, looking at the clean certainty of the letters, then ducked inside.

Junie had three pots going and six lies arriving at once. She was broad across the shoulders and moving so fast the steam seemed to follow her rather than the other way round. A streak of flour ran from one thumb knuckle to her wrist where she'd swatted at sweat without washing first. She looked at the wrapped hand, then at his face, and pointed with the ladle toward a crate of onions.

"Sit there and peel till I tell you different."

"Good morning to you too."

"Morning was an hour ago. Peel."

He sat. The bench rocked on an uneven leg. He braced the onion against his stomach and worked the skin loose one-handed while ferry crews came in hungry and left louder. Junie moved among them with her usual brisk blasphemy, sliding bowls down the counter, keeping tabs in her head, correcting a boatman's arithmetic without looking at him. She had not asked for the story of the feast. She had seen enough when he came in last night shining green and humiliated from eyebrow to boot sole. Her restraint had felt almost tender then. In daylight it felt like a bill being held for later.

By the time he had a basin full of onion skins and a watering left eye, the lunch rush was packed tight enough that bodies warmed the windows. Dock hands in algae-stiff aprons, a crate porter who always took mustard with a spoon as if he were daring it to fight him, two ferry clerks from the up-river line, and one schoolteacher with wet hems and no patience for men who lingered over change jammed the room. The room sounded like cutlery on crockery and water slapping pilings and the quick repetitive thud of Junie's knife through stale bread.

What he had not expected was to hear his own trade come in with the customers.

"Saw a new board up by Lantern Steps," one of the ferry clerks said, wiping broth off his mustache. "Pretty pale thing. Crown on the top. They say the bed burns cooler under the proper name."

"Proper for who?" Junie said.

"For whoever can pay, I suppose."

The crate porter laughed through his spoonful of mustard. "My wife says if it answers to one name it'll probably stop listening to ours."

"Your wife says that about dogs, priests, and engines alike," said the schoolteacher, and the whole counter snorted.

Then the outer door banged and Otis came in at a run from the slip, narrow shoulders damp with fog-sweat, one trouser knee dark with the same old dock dirt that never fully left it, slate tucked under his arm. He had lost the front tooth and could not stop tonguing the gap between words. He went first to the bread stack because he always checked whether the crust heels had been saved for him.

"Wash," Junie said without turning.

"I did at school."

"Then it'll only improve you to do it twice."

He gave Silas a quick grin on the way to the basin, the kind children used when they had clocked adult damage and meant to be kind without quite knowing how. "You still look swampy, Uncle Sile."

"You look undereducated."

"Miss Bell says that's not a proper word."

"Miss Bell can write me a letter."

Otis giggled, splashed his hands, and came back with his slate and a folded scrap of paper gone soft in his pocket. He climbed onto the end stool by the stove and watched Junie plate bread and eel. His eyes moved greedily over everything in the room: bowls, steam, strangers' boots, the ferry bells beyond the shutters. He lived as if the city were a toy set always one turn from revealing a new hidden door.

"We had copy practice," he announced.

"Did you," Junie said.

"Miss Bell had those free sheets from the supply man with the nice loops. We had to write the line six times."

He unfolded the paper and smoothed it flat on the counter with both hands. At the top, in pale green print gone fuzzy from pocket sweat, stood a tiny gilt crown and the same phrase Silas had watched pasted on the post outside his shop.

ONE CLEAN NAME FOR A STEADY FLAME.

Below it, in schoolroom pencil and a child's fierce concentration, Otis had copied the line again and again. The first version leaned uphill. The second broke the S in steady into two snakes. By the sixth he had the rhythm of it.

"Look," he said, pleased with himself. "I can do the C proper now."

Junie's ladle stopped in the air.

The room did not go silent. Ferrymouth never granted anybody that sort of stage. Someone at the back wanted more bread. A ferry bell rang twice. Steam kept lifting off the pots. But within the space directly around the counter something changed so hard Silas felt it like a chair leg striking his shin.

Junie set the ladle down with great care.

"Where'd she get that?"

Otis blinked. "The supply man. He had a whole stack. He said the letters were good practice."

"What supply man?"

"The nice one with the green cuffs. He gives boiled sweets if you say the whole line first."

One of the ferry clerks snorted. "Efficient fellow."

Junie turned her head and looked at him. She did not raise her voice. She did not have to. The clerk bent over his bowl at once as if it had become a matter of religious study.

She looked back at Otis. "And what did Miss Bell say about it?"

"That it's important to ask for goods by their proper trade names or shops cheat you." He frowned, feeling trouble now without knowing its edges. "I only copied it. Everybody did."

Silas knew the exact place in the sentence where the hook had been set. Proper trade names. That was not his line, but it lay snug against it, both of them built to do the same work. Make one word feel clean and everyone else's feel grubby, backward, uncertain. He could hear Gideon praising the usefulness of the turn. He could hear himself accepting the praise because praise from men with money always came wrapped around something he needed.

Junie took the paper between two fingers as if it had grease on it.

"Listen to me, Oat. Goods don't become honest because some bastard paid to print a crown over them. A lamp burns how it burns. Broth feeds you or it doesn't. A thing's use comes first."

"I know," Otis said, but too quickly.

"Do you? Then say it back."

He looked from her to Silas and back. He had inherited Junie's fast little weather sense. He knew when the room around him had changed, even if he did not yet know why. "A thing's use comes first."

"Again."

"A thing's use comes first."

"Good. Now take that sheet and feed it to the stove."

Otis flinched. "But my writing."

"You can write it better on clean paper that isn't selling you while you learn."

That was when Junie got colder than she had been over the trough, colder than she had been when he staggered into her back room stinking like a cut ditch and trying to make a joke before he fell onto the chair. Silas had expected anger at his disgrace. Anger he understood. This was something flatter and worse. This was the look of a woman seeing a stranger's hand inside her cupboard.

Otis slid off the stool and hesitated by the stove, paper in hand.

"Ma?"

"Do it."

He fed one corner of the sheet to the open grate. The fire licked it, then took the rest. The gilt crown curled black first. The slogan puckered, flashed, and was gone. Otis watched it with a sorrow out of all proportion to the scrap itself, because children hated wasting effort and could not yet distinguish between labor and obedience.

Junie saw that too. Her face changed, though not much.

"After service I'll help you make a fresh copy line," she said. "Something worth practicing."

"What line?"

She picked up the ladle again. "We'll think of one that doesn't belong to a patent office."

Otis nodded, subdued, and went to collect bowls.

Silas said, low, "Junie."

"Don't," she said.

"I know."

"You don't know enough yet. That's the issue."

She moved past him with the broth pot, using her hip to shove his knee aside because her hands were full. "I can live through you making a public ass of yourself. God knows I've done it. Men get hauled through their own nonsense every week on this dock and the tide still comes in. What I can't stomach is them teaching my boy to ask permission before he asks for light."

He looked at Otis carrying bowls toward the wash tub, careful despite the missing tooth, careful despite the way he'd been wounded by the burning of his own copy work. "It was a line. Trade trash."

"Yes," she said. "And now it's in the schoolroom. That's how trash learns table manners."

The lunch crowd kept arriving. A deckhand came in humming the slogan without knowing he was humming it. Two boys on the slip took turns shouting the words at the ferry windows to hear the echo off the glass. A woman buying bread to carry home asked if Junie had thought of repainting her sign in a lighter color because the excursion people trusted pale goods. She meant it kindly. That was part of the obscenity. The city was absorbing the sales pitch as advice.

Silas peeled potatoes after that, because onions were done and apology was useless. The bad hand rested useless in his lap, heavy as borrowed meat. Every few minutes he caught the phrase returning from some other mouth outside, thinner or louder or half mangled, and each version did its work anyway. Words crossed water fast in Nacre Bend. A joke on Patent Row could be a school exercise by noon and kitchen doctrine by supper. Ferries carried more than bodies. They carried the laziness by which a city decided what counted as proper.

When the rush finally thinned and the room could breathe again, Damaris Pike appeared in the doorway with a parcel of damp papers hugged to her ribs. Her widow's black had gone shiny at the seams from years of elbows and worktables, and the chipped front tooth flashed when she smiled. She always looked as if she had slept three hours in a chair and spent two of them arguing with the chair.

"You print vultures quick," Junie said.

"Only on days ending in y." Damaris thumped the bundle onto the counter and fanned herself with a folded proof. "You look murderous, which improves the room. Silas, you look like a saint dragged through algae and denied the promotion."

"If that's this week's headline, spare me."

"The headline's better than that. Unfortunately for you, the city is already getting bored with your suffering." She took a bowl without asking and sniffed it approvingly. "Which means the interesting harm is elsewhere."

Junie snorted. "Take him, then. He's been sitting here like a damp curtain when he isn't peeling things badly."

"I heard that," Silas said.

"I aimed it so you would."

Damaris tore bread with inked fingers. "Come ride the three-forty crossing with me."

"For what?"

"Because you're still reading this as a scandal. It isn't one anymore. It's habit. Habit pays better and lasts longer."

He should have refused. He was tired, his hand throbbed, and he had no appetite for being instructed by the woman who'd made a serial amusement out of his every wrong turn since spring. But Junie had already turned away to count coins into stacks by the till, lips moving quick as knife work, and he knew the difference between being dismissed and being sent.

"Back by dark," she said without looking up. "Otis still needs that copy line."

"What's it going to be?" Otis asked from the wash tub.

Junie glanced at the river, then at the boy. "We'll start with this. Ask what a thing does."

Otis repeated it softly to see how it sat in his mouth. "Ask what a thing does."

Silas followed Damaris out to the slip.

***

The ferry deck was slick with old mist and shoe polish and the greenish paste that grew wherever rope stayed wet too long. Damaris knew everybody who worked the line, though not always by their right names. She flicked a paper at the ticket girl, who wore spectacles tied behind her head with blue thread and kept scratching the same ink stain on her cuff as if she could erase it by irritation alone.

"You still owe me two fares from last month," the girl said.

"Then carry me in debt, my dear. It becomes me."

"Nothing becomes you."

"Cruel and accurate."

They boarded with the laborers and errand boys and two women carrying lamp trays wrapped in sacking. Silas kept to the rail because his stomach wanted air and his wrist wanted a place to rest. The ferry shuddered free of the slip. Water opened under them in a chop the color of spoiled bottles. Nacre Bend rose on either side, terraces and plank runs and damp brick faces layered with signs like scales on a fish no one had ever finished gutting.

Damaris handed him a paper. He did not open it.

"Cowardly," she said.

"Practical."

"Fair."

She leaned her elbows on the rail beside him. Up close she smelled of printer's ink and some cheap violet water trying bravely to cover it. Her hat was pinned at an angle that would have looked rakish on a man and looked merely overworked on her.

"Do you know why I like ferries better than presses?" she asked.

"Because the ferries float."

"Because presses only print what I put in them. Ferries print whatever anybody repeats by the time they dock."

She nodded toward the cabin, where a pair of office clerks were examining a handbill together.

"Morning headline, noon joke, evening purchase. That's the whole sermon. A paper starts it if it's lucky. A ferry makes it common."

He looked where she was looking. The handbill in the clerks' hands bore the same pale border he'd seen on the school copy sheet. One clerk mouthed the slogan while reading and the other answered the rhythm under his breath without seeming to know he had done it.

"You're pleased with yourself," Silas said.

"I'm never pleased with myself. At best, I'm professionally interested." She bit into the bread she'd stolen from Junie's and chewed. "You've been very good copy, yes. Man of words hoist by wet neighborhood. Public loves that. But while everyone was laughing at you, somebody figured out how to move the campaign out of the papers and into the useful places."

"Schools."

"Schools, ferries, supply invoices, chalk boards in lodging houses, little cards tucked into lamp shipments, copy slips, prayer raffle prizes, all the humble holes where habit nests." She swallowed. "Look."

They were passing the lower edge of Lantern Steps. From the water he could see the landings stacked up the hill, little platforms of life between stair runs. Shirts hung on lines and buckets sat by doors. A woman shook a rug hard enough to mean it. Along the retaining wall below, three men in paint aprons were working their way over a row of older signs.

One board had advertised repairs for blue-burn lamp cages in lettering so weathered only the pressure of the strokes remained. Another, nailed crooked over a cellar door, still carried half a warning about hot trays and children. The crew had already sanded the faces pale. Now they rolled a single soft green over all of it, methodical as undertakers washing a body before display.

Above the wet ground color came gilt stencils: a crown, a flourish, the neat certified capital letters of Verdancy House.

"Those weren't even shop signs," Silas said.

"No. They were instructions." Damaris tapped ash from an unlit cigarette she kept mostly for gesture. "Which is inconvenient if your business model requires the city to stop speaking in instructions and start speaking in purchases."

The ferry moved on. He twisted for another look and his wrist punished him for it. One of the paint men reached the old warning board's lower edge and covered the word HOT last. For a second the letters showed through the celadon skin, dark and ghostly. Then the roller went back over them and the wall became decor.

Silas knew that pressure. He knew exactly how many coats it took to kill a stubborn old letter on cheap wood. He knew the tricks for making a replacement feel inevitable: soft border, centered wording, enough breathing room around the phrase that people mistook expense for truth. Gideon had not bought only wall space. He had bought legibility. He was whitening the city's teeth while the mouth was still in it.

"Keep looking," Damaris said.

At the next bend the ferry gave them Patent Row's flank and then the cheaper stretch below Gilt Crescent where aspiring shopkeepers copied upper-district taste badly and charged extra for the attempt. Here too fresh celadon had begun to bloom. A boarding house he'd once lettered BRACKEN ROOMS in russet and cream was now half hidden beneath a new panel: CROWN VIEW LODGING. The old name still showed at the bottom where the carpenter had measured wrong, the K peeking out like a broken nail.

On the levee below it, a fruit seller's awning had been repainted the same pale green. Even the ferrymen's directional arrows were being redone, not to point toward people or functions but toward approved sellers. AUTHORIZED FUEL THIS WAY. CERTIFIED CUTTINGS AHEAD.

"It's everywhere," he said.

"That's what campaigns are for."

"No. I mean the kind of it."

Damaris glanced sideways at him but let him take his time. That was one of the reasons he trusted her more than was wise. For all her appetite for spectacle, she understood that a person sometimes required a few beats to become smarter than his own vanity.

Silas watched a woman on the quay below bend to pick up the old plank sign somebody had thrown down after replacing it. She was middle-aged, red-armed, wearing a man's cap with the brim cracked through. She tested the board's weight, rubbed the surviving paint with her thumb, and then tucked it under her basket rather than leave it for scrap. He did not know her. He only knew the motion. It was the motion of somebody salvaging a name before strangers could decide it had never mattered.

"He's not only selling fuel," Silas said.

"Congratulations."

"Shut up."

She actually did, which was rarer than kindness from her and therefore counted more.

He kept his eyes on the city. From the water the changes lined up in a way he had not seen from the street. He saw the school copy sheet, the ferry placard, and the pale respectable color washing over a hand-lettered warning, a market arrow, and a rooming-house board with someone's older bargain still faint beneath it. Separate, each thing was trivial enough to laugh at. Together they made a pattern as plain as a net.

The net did not have to drag a man into the river to take his place from him. It only had to rename the block until the people already living there sounded incorrect.

He thought of Junie being advised to repaint lighter for better customers, as if broth had a class so long as the sign did. He thought of Otis copying the line because the loops were pretty and the teacher said proper names prevented cheating. He thought of the women in Sump Ward speaking to the moss by use and risk and heat while he and Gideon had trimmed those living rough instructions into something purchasable. It was not a product, not really. It was a border, a way of telling the city which mouths counted as civilized enough to define a thing and which ones would henceforth sound provincial, suspect, low.

It was eviction without marshals, without even the vulgar honesty of a locked door.

You washed the wall. You called the old name confusion. You taught the children a cleaner sentence. By the time rent rose or a supplier changed terms or a woman stopped asking for the local tray by its right rough word because the clerk made a face, the work had already been done. The room had been taken before anyone admitted a taking.

The ferry engine thumped under his boots. Across from them, one of the women with the wrapped lamp trays had opened her bundle to check the stock. The moss inside gave off a muted practical green, the color of work left alone. She covered it again the instant a passing clerk leaned too close, not out of melodrama but out of habit. Guard the useful thing. Guard the name attached to it. Guard whatever a richer man might decide looked better after translation.

"I thought the shame was the point," Silas said at last.

"Your shame is always a point," Damaris said. "Just rarely the point."

He let that pass.

"I helped him build the rhythm for it," he said. "Not just the names. The way the lines close. The way they stick."

"Yes."

He turned to her. "You don't have to enjoy that quite so much."

"I'm not enjoying it." The wind flattened her veil against one cheek and showed the tired skin under her eye. "I'm telling you not to make a religion out of feeling rotten. It's boring and it wastes daylight. If you have finally noticed the thing is bigger than your humiliation, then do something useful with the notice."

They were nearing the far slip. Bell metal rang. Deckhands shouted. The city pressed in again with its wet planks and stacked goods and impatient bodies. Damaris folded the rest of her bread into her paper, tucked it into her pocket, and straightened her hat.

"I've got evening editions to lie in," she said. "You can stay on for the return if you're thinking."

"I'm thinking."

"Try not to sound so injured about it."

She stepped off with the crowd, black skirts brushing fish scales and coal dust alike, already calling after a runner about column space before both feet had found the dock. Silas stayed where he was as the ferry took on new passengers and shoved back into the channel.

The return crossing gave him the same city with less excuse not to understand it. A pair of boys on the upper bench sang the slogan to the tune of a hymn and then dissolved into laughter when they forgot the middle. A drayman asked another where to find certified cuttings. An old woman corrected him with a local name anyway, then muttered the official one after it because experience had taught her which word would get the sack loaded. Near Ferrymouth two workmen were taking down a sign for a cheap lodging house and leaning a fresh celadon panel against the wall to replace it. The new board had been centered beautifully. He knew the hand that had spaced it, if not the man. It was decent work. That was the filthiest part, good work in service of a smaller mouth.

His wrist ached in time with the ferry engine. He rested it on the rail and looked until the ache stopped being the chapter heading of his own disgrace and became only one hurt among others. He thought of Junie's kitchen, Otis's copy sheet, the warning board painted over, the woman rescuing a discarded plank as if it had family in it, and Inez's people hearing their rough practical speech come back to them in clean purchased form and being told the cleaned version was proper.

He had thought theft was the moment a man pocketed a word that was not his.

It was also the slower labor after: the sanding down, the whitening, the teaching, the part where a city learned to repeat the stranger's phrase until the people already living there sounded wrong in their own mouths.

When the ferry slid back against Ferrymouth and the ropes flew, he got off without hurrying. Junie's sign still hung over the kitchen door, dark letters on honest board, broth and bread promised plain. Evening damp had deepened the blue strokes he had laid there years ago. For the first time all day he did not look at it as proof of what his hand used to do.

He looked at it as something still alive enough to be taken. He had spent years teaching walls to flatter strangers. He had helped give eviction a prettier mouth.


# Chapter 10

By the time Silas reached the last plank over the drainage cut, the night had gone so hot the boards sweated under his boots. Sump Ward lay low and shining under a skin of damp, every catwalk plank dark with old overflow, every wash line hanging slack as dead eels. The grow houses gave off their own weather. Warm green light leaked through the slats. Ferment steam rolled out sweet and rotten, apples left too long in a barrel, yeast, mud, the faint metallic bite of lamp chimneys cooling. He had come without a speech. He had left his hat at home, his mouth shut, and his good hand empty.

Aunt Vero waited at the west gate with a paper twist of fennel seed in one broad brown hand. She chewed it on long shifts because the digester steam turned her stomach mean, and the sweet anise smell sat oddly against the ward stink. Her white braid was looped up and pinned with a bent brass spoon. One eye tracked him cleanly. The other wandered toward the cut as if it had a better opinion of the evening.

"If you came to kneel," she said, "kneel where somebody can mop around you. If you came to lift, the west house is going pretty, and nobody here needs that shit."

Silas nodded once. "I came to lift."

"We'll see."

She let him through.

Inside, the heat thickened at once. The west house had two rows of shelf beds, a central table slick with cuttings, and a long ferment wall breathing through iron coils. The practical trays along the outer shelves should have been a workman's green, sturdy and ugly, good for kitchen flame and stair landings. Half of them had thrown a pale frill over the surface instead, a delicate gold-white lace that looked expensive from a distance and useless up close. The stuff crisped at the edges when it dried and spat too hot when burned. Fashion had gotten into the beds again.

Inez stood on the far side of the table with her skirts pinned up to mid-calf and her sleeves rolled hard over the forearms. Her scarf had slipped back enough to show damp dark hair at the nape. Mud striped one shin. She had a hooked trimming blade in her right hand and a crate braced against one knee. She looked at Silas once, from face to boots and back, took in the lack of hat and excuses, and pointed with the blade.

"Take that stack to the drain bench," she said. "Not the pale top growth. The whole trays. Hold them level or you'll shake spores loose."

He moved before she finished talking. The trays were heavier than they looked. Wet root mats dragged against the wood, and his right wrist complained at the first lift, a hot twist under the old sprain that traveled halfway to the elbow. He shifted the weight into his left hand and his shoulder and kept walking. The drain bench stood under the outer shutters where the night air could get at the bad growth. He set the stack down, came back, lifted another.

Nobody thanked him. Good. Thanks would have made the whole thing theatrical.

Women he did not know worked the opposite shelves with their mouths set tight. One of them hummed under her breath, not a tune, just enough sound to keep time with her knife. Another had pinned her hem with fishbone clips and kept blowing curls out of her eyes with small irritated bursts. Silas had never seen either woman before. Tonight he noticed the fishbone clips because one was painted blue. He noticed the humming because it stopped whenever he passed too near. The ward had not forgiven him. It had merely run short on hands.

Aunt Vero shoved a pump handle down with both palms and spat husks neatly into the gutter. "Lantern Steps boys stood on the outer walk all afternoon saying Celadon this, Celadon that, like it was a prayer and their own mothers weren't cooking by these beds. Now the shelf thinks it's a chandelier. Cut under the lace. Save the root. Throw the vanity in the drain."

"Yes, ma'am," Silas said.

She snorted. "Don't sweet-mouth me, painter."

Inez dragged a tray toward herself and slit the pale crust away in long practiced strokes. Underneath, the real moss showed wet and dense, darker green, still alive. "If the root smells sweet, it's gone," she said without looking at him. "If it smells like wet iron, keep it. And don't mash the runners. We need them by morning."

He took the next tray to her table instead of the bench. "Show me the first one."

That brought her eyes up again. Suspicion first. Then calculation. She angled the tray so he could see the seam where useful growth ended and the fashionable lace began.

"Here," she said. "Not here. The white edge lies. It looks shallow. It isn't."

Her hand came over his for a moment to set the knife angle. The inside of her right wrist brushed his knuckles. He felt the ridge of the old burn scar, smooth and abrupt as fired clay. Then she stepped back and he cut where she'd shown him, slow because of the wrist, careful because if he ruined even one tray in front of her she would never let him touch another.

The work settled them into a rhythm that was not peace but could pass for it in low light. Lift, brace, cut, sort, carry. Mud made a sucking sound under every boot turn. The ferment wall knocked softly behind the iron coils, a deep wet glug as if the house had its own patient stomach. Now and then a tray flashed a sicker pale under the lantern and had to go whole into the drain cart. More often the ugly useful green held under the branded skin. Silas cut and saved what he could. Sweat ran down his ribs and soaked his waistband. His shirt stuck to the river scar under his left side. The room smelled of boiled pennies, old leaves, lamp oil, wet rope.

He lasted near twenty minutes before the words rose hard enough to need letting out.

"I signed Wren's paper before Lantern Steps," he said.

Inez did not stop trimming. "Lift that corner."

He did. Together they shifted a sagging tray onto the table.

"Before you first dragged me through a working house," he said. "Before Junie cleaned slime off her soup pots. Before I heard one name in here that meant more than copy to me."

"I heard you the first time."

"Then hear the rest."

Her blade kept moving. "Talk and work, then. If you slow down, I won't listen."

So he talked and worked. It was the only decent arrangement he could think of. He told her the plain frame first because ornament would have made him sick. Gideon had put a retainer in front of him. Exclusivity. Stable name rights, campaign rights, first use on any public surfaces Silas lettered for the strain. Enough money to clear Havelock Pigments and keep collectors off his door for a while. Enough to buy Junie a few quieter weeks if he stayed careful. Enough to make the paper feel like relief when it should have felt like a hand at his throat.

He expected her to cut him off. She did not. That was worse.

"How long did you mean to keep it from me?" she asked.

He shaved pale lace away from a runner root and dropped it into the refuse pail. "I told myself I'd say it when I had something useful to bring with it."

"That's not a time."

"No." He did not look up. "It isn't."

She waited.

He hauled a full refuse pail to the drain bench, dumped the glittering waste into the black water where it hissed and curled, came back empty. "I meant to keep it from you until the contract stopped shaming me," he said. "That's the ugly truth. I kept thinking if I solved the thing fast enough, the paper under it would matter less."

"To who?"

"Mostly me."

The answer sat between them a moment, ugly and unadorned. He had never heard a confession sound less noble.

Inez hooked two fingers under a sagging runner and held it up for him. "Cut there."

He cut.

"And after you knew what the names did here?" she asked.

The question hit deeper because it did not let him hide inside the first bad choice. It forced him to account for every choice after it.

"After that, I kept taking his money because I liked not being poor for five damned minutes," he said. "I liked walking into Havelock's and watching him go polite when I laid the crowns down. I liked not owing Junie every spoon of stew I swallowed. I liked being the man they paid first when a room needed a mouth."

The knife slipped a little. He nicked his thumb. Not deep. Enough to sting. He sucked the blood off without thinking and tasted salt, iron, fennel from the air.

"I wasn't starving," he said. "That's the part I don't get to dress up. I was vain and scared at the same time, and I let those two things climb into bed together. Then I stole from you because it was easier to call theft research than to say I had sold the listening part of myself."

At that, Inez finally stopped moving.

The lantern threw a low green shine under her cheekbones. Sweat had loosened a curl against her temple. He could not read forgiveness anywhere near her face. He had not expected to. What he saw instead was attention, sharp and cold and more dangerous because it was being spent on him rather than withheld.

"How much did he tell you?" she asked.

"About what he wanted?"

"About what he meant to do with the names once he had them."

He put the knife down before answering. "Enough. Not all in one sentence. Enough to know he wanted the city to forget where it learned anything useful. Enough to know pretty sold faster than safe."

"And you still helped."

"Yes."

She looked back to the tray, as if the tray at least had the decency to be specific. "Don't ask me to call that weakness."

"I wasn't going to."

"Good."

He lifted another tray. His wrist barked again. He made the sound one makes when a nail catches in rotten wood, brief and involuntary. Inez's eyes flicked to the wrapped joint.

"Give me your hand," she said.

He did. Her fingers were muddy and cool at the tips, hot in the palm. She untied the rag around his wrist, inspected the puffed bone with one quick frown, then retied it tighter and flatter, using the strip so it sat firm under the heel of his hand instead of cutting the swelling.

"You came at night because you thought darkness would help?" she asked while she worked.

"No. I came at night because Aunt Vero's boy said the west house was in trouble and I knew you wouldn't throw out a body while the trays were going."

"That's not the same as being wanted."

"I know."

She tied the knot with her teeth, spat the loose thread, and dropped his hand. "Hold the heavy end from now on. No fine cuts unless I hand them to you. If you go stupid and tear something living, I'll put you out myself."

It was not pardon. It was work. It felt, in that room, larger.

They kept going. Once the outer shelves were stripped, they carried the salvageable trays into a cooler aisle and re-racked them by use: low-heat kitchen stock, stair lamps, wash-yard light, nursery jars. Inez named each class without ornament. Pot-green. Step-cool. Soaplight. Silas repeated them only when she asked him to mark a crate. He heard how each phrase sat differently in the mouth, not built to charm a crowd but to keep someone's hand from blistering over a stove.

Aunt Vero came through twice more, once with a bucket of sand and once with two boiled eggs in her apron pocket that she ate standing up, tapping shell bits into her palm and muttering about men from the ridge who wanted their lamps to look delicate. "Delicate," she said, crushing the word as if it had bones in it. "You can't fry smelt on delicate." The second time she passed Silas a tin cup of ditch-cooled water without comment. He drank. The water tasted of zinc and algae. It was the best thing he had put in his mouth all day.

Near midnight the women from the far shelves began peeling away to other houses. Fishbone clips. Humming. One by one gone into the dark. The worst of the outer rot had been cut back. The drain cart stank with discarded lace. The saved trays glowed a steadier workman's green. Silas was carrying the last stack toward the propagation shed when Aunt Vero blocked the doorway with her broad shoulder.

"Inner bed next," she said to Inez. "The mother shelf threw a fever stripe by the flue."

Silas looked up.

Vero caught it and showed him her teeth, yellowed and wicked with fennel dust. "Don't flatter yourself. You're not touching the mother tray. You're lifting what she tells you and keeping your damned shadow off the roots."

"Fine by me," he said.

"No, it isn't. But it'll do."

She leaned close enough for him to smell the egg salt and anise on her breath. "If you sell one more home-name out of this ward, boy, I won't trouble the saints with the matter. I'll handle it myself."

Then, because she was Aunt Vero and dignity had never once slowed her down, she patted his cheek with the same hand she'd been using on the sand bucket and left a cool muddy print there before shoving past him toward the outer walk.

Silas wiped it off with the back of his sleeve and heard Inez make a sound that might have been the first nearly-laugh she had given him in weeks.

It did more to his balance than the shifting plank under his boots.

* * *

The propagation shed sat lower than the rest of the house, half sunk into packed river mud, its walls lined with warm brick channels that carried digester heat past the shelves. The room was narrow enough that when Silas turned with a tray he had to do it on the balls of his feet or crack the corners against the posts. Condensation beaded on the inner glass and ran down in crooked silver threads. A single hooded lamp burned over the bench, throwing light on rows of starter jars and clay labels stacked in chipped blue saucers. The heat in there was different from the workroom heat. Less air, more skin. It lay on him like a wet hand.

Inez slid the bar home behind them, not to keep him in, only to keep the outer draft from shocking the bed. Still, the sound of the wood settling into place changed the room.

"Set the salvage on the lower rack," she said. Her voice had gone quieter with the hour, but not softer. "These are the runners that matter. If the fever stripe gets into the root shelf, tomorrow turns ugly."

He did as she told him. The lower rack was crowded with shallow trays and one long narrow shelf under muslin, the cloth damp and breathing faint green through the weave. He kept his shadow off it as ordered. When he straightened, the top of his head nearly brushed a hanging line of drying tags.

For a while there was only the work again. Inez loosened the hot striped section with two fingers and a blade thin as a fillet knife. Silas steadied the tray, moved jars where she needed space, packed damp grit around healthy runners, carried bad growth to the cull bucket. The room forced them close. Her hip brushed his when he reached for the grit bowl. Once she said "Behind you" and laid both hands on his ribs to shift past him in the narrow aisle, and the press of her palms went through the wet shirt straight into his back like a brand.

He thought about nothing. That was the only way to keep his hands useful.

It almost worked.

"You should have told me before the feast," she said.

He kept his eyes on the tray. "Yes."

"You should have told me before I took you through the lower houses."

"Yes."

"You should have told me before you let me think the worst thing about you was vanity."

That one took him a second. He let the grit bowl down too hard. A little spray of wet sand hit the bench.

"That wasn't the worst thing?" he asked.

Inez's mouth moved, not quite a smile. "No. The worst thing was that you listened well enough to learn what hurt people and then used that gift to make money from the hurt."

He stood very still in the damp heat, one hand on the bowl. "That's fair."

"I know."

Silas nodded. The motion made sweat break free from his jaw and drop to the bench. There was nothing to defend. That, more than the accusation itself, left him strangely empty, all the old frantic words gone out of him. He had spent weeks inventing speeches for this room and could not remember one. Good.

Inez touched the cloth over the mother shelf with the backs of her fingers, checking warmth the way another woman might have checked a child's forehead. "Why did you come tonight, really?"

He could have lied prettier. The room was small enough that any lie would have fouled the air.

"Because I couldn't stand carrying it alone anymore," he said. "Because I kept hearing Otis say those polished bastard words from school like they belonged in his mouth. Because I knew you had every right to shut me out and I still came hoping work might get me through the door your pity shouldn't."

She considered that while she peeled a bad stripe from the tray and laid the living part on fresh grit. "You didn't come for absolution, then."

"No." He paused. "If I'd gotten that, I'd mistrust it."

That did it. She looked at him fully then, and the attention in her face changed. Not gentled. Not made safe. It deepened, the way river water darkened when a cloud passed over it and made the depth easier to guess.

"Hand me the muslin," she said.

He did. As she spread it over the shelf, the scarf at her neck finally came loose and slid down between her shoulder blades. She pushed it off impatiently and set it aside on a hook. Her hair, flattened and damp from hours under cloth, showed its own life at once, heavy and black and waved where the tie had held it. Silas watched one bead of sweat travel from behind her ear to the hollow above her collarbone. He looked away so fast he almost laughed at himself.

"Hold this," she said.

It took him an idiot second to realize she meant the lamp, not the room between them. He took the lamp and lifted it while she checked the underside of the shelf. The light caught the inner line of her right wrist again, that crescent pale against brown skin, and the curve of her thumb wet with grit. The burn scar, the work scar, the same way his own rib pulled white under damp cloth when the light hit from the side. Bodies kept their ledgers whether anyone wrote them down or not.

"Silas."

He had to drag his attention back to her face. It was the first time she had used his name that night. Maybe the first time in weeks. Maybe longer.

"What?"

"You're shaking."

He was. Not from nerves alone. The room had cooked every clean reserve out of him. His arm holding the lamp had begun to tremble with tiredness.

"Set it down before you drop it," she said, and something in the way she said it had more care in it than the words themselves admitted.

He set the lamp on the bench. When he straightened, they were close enough that if either of them breathed deep their shirts touched.

"I'm all right," he said.

"You're not all right," she said. "You're standing. That isn't the same thing."

Her hand rose, stopped once, then went on and touched the muddy print Aunt Vero had half left on his cheek. She rubbed the last dried edge of it away with her thumb. The gesture was so small it split him worse than the earlier accusations had. He looked down at her hand because it was safer than looking at her mouth. The burn scar on her wrist sat under his eye. Without thinking too hard, because thinking too hard had ruined enough already, he turned his head and pressed his mouth to that pale crescent.

She drew in breath.

Not a flinch. Not a recoil. Breath.

"That scar came from a pipe burst," she said, her voice lower now. "I was seventeen. It peeled half the skin off. Vero said I had been trying to work faster than heat does."

Silas looked up. "What did you say?"

Her thumb moved once along his jaw, testing the rough there. "I said the pipe could go to hell."

He laughed, quiet and wrecked with tiredness. It came out more honest than anything else he had managed that night. She heard the break in it. Her eyes changed again.

"Don't you dare turn gentle on me because you've told the truth once," she said.

"I wasn't planning to."

"Good."

Then she kissed him.

There was nothing polished in it. No slow permission seeking, no pretty surprise. Her mouth landed hot and direct on his, tasting of ditch water, salt, fennel drifting in from the workroom, and the long shift they had both just done. He set one hand on the bench behind her because his wrist would not bear a grab, and she caught his shirt front in both fists and pulled him close enough to knock the clay labels together. They clicked in their saucer like little teeth.

He kissed her back hard enough to tell the truth with it. She opened for him at once, not soft but hungry, and the whole room seemed to narrow further around the sound of their mouths and the slow wet plunk of condensation from the glass. When she shoved his shirt up, her palm found the scar under his left ribs and paused there, mapping it. He made a rough sound into her mouth. She kissed the scar through the cloth first, then lifted the shirt higher and kissed bare skin. The tenderness of that nearly undid him. Then she bit just above it, sharp enough to make his knees loosen, and he understood what kind of mercy she meant to offer and what kind she did not.

He got her to the bench in two awkward steps because the floor was too slick and the room too narrow for grace. She sat back against the wood with jars rattling behind her, skirts bunched in both fists as she yanked them clear of her knees. "If your wrist gives, say so," she said, breathing hard. "Don't prove anything stupid on me."

He nodded. "Help me with the buttons."

She did, quick and practical, working open his damp trousers while he pushed fabric up her calves and found the warm inside of her thigh with his left hand. Mud had dried in a thin map over one knee. He kissed it because it was there. She made a sound low in her throat that might have been his name or might have been only relief that he had finally learned where to put his mouth and where not to put words. Her drawers were already wet where his fingers slid under, heat and slick and her body admitting what the rest of her still held at blade point. When he touched her properly she shut her eyes and leaned her forehead to his shoulder for one breath, just one, then caught his wrist and guided him harder.

"Like that," she said. "Don't make a ceremony of it."

He obeyed. Ceremony was for showrooms. This was work of another kind, no less exact for being desperate. He rubbed her until her breath went ragged and her heel thudded once against the bench leg. Then she dragged him between her knees, took him in hand, and the room tipped. The hooded lamp threw their shadows huge and warped across the sweating glass. Outside, somebody crossed the outer walk and the boards complained, a brief groan of wood over water. Neither of them stopped. If anything, the danger of being overheard stripped the last of the distance from the air.

She pulled him into her on the first try, muttering "There" like she was seating a stubborn tray. The bench edge bit the backs of his thighs. He braced more on his forearm than his bad hand and moved carefully once, then not carefully at all because she wrapped her legs around him and bit down on the meat of his shoulder through the shirt and said, against his neck, "Again." So he did. The propagation shed answered in little noises: glass ticking, bench scraping, the wet slap of his thigh against her skirt, the breath torn out of both of them in turns. Her hair had come wholly loose now and kept sticking to her mouth; he pushed it back with clumsy fingers and she laughed once, breathless and mean in the best way, before she grabbed his face and kissed him until he lost count of everything but the motion.

His wrist flared once when the bench shifted. He hissed. She felt it at once and turned them with more strength than looked possible, dragging him down onto the stack of empty jute sacks by the wall and coming over him without breaking the rhythm. The sacks smelled of damp grain and old moss dust. Brick heat pressed through them into his back. Her skirt spread over his hips. She rode him with one hand braced on his chest, the other clamped over her own mouth when a sound got too close to carrying. He reached up and caught that hand away, kissed the center of her palm, then held her by the waist while she moved, hard and exact, until the careful part of him went white and useless. When she came, it was with her forehead driven into his shoulder and the muscles of her stomach knocking against him in short fierce pulls. He followed a moment later, half choking on her name because the room had left no extra air for grace.

Afterward they stayed where they had landed, tangled on the hot sacks, the lamp still burning, both of them listening to see if the house had heard. Nothing came but the ferment wall's patient knock and the far water in the cut. Silas laughed once under his breath, not from comedy exactly, more from the fact that his whole body felt beaten and newly built at the same time.

Inez lifted her head from his shoulder. Her mouth was red where he'd kissed it. There was a smear of green grit along one cheek. "Don't mistake this," she said.

He looked at her. "I won't."

"Good." She drew two fingers down his sternum, slow, almost absent, then pinched him hard enough to keep the point in it. "Because I wanted you. That's true. I also wanted the truth out of your mouth and your body tired enough not to dress itself in silk while it gave it."

"That worked," he said.

"It did."

She rolled off him and sat up, dragging her skirt down with brisk hands. For a moment he watched the line of her back as she reached for her scarf and then stopped, leaving it where it hung. The room smelled like sex now under the moss and heat, unmistakable and dangerous. He buttoned himself slowly one-handed. His skin felt flayed open to the air.

When she spoke again, her voice had changed once more. Warmer, yes. Also more exact, which he would not have thought possible.

"Wash your hands," she said. "Both of them. Then sit up."

He did not ask why. There was a basin by the inner wall with a trickle pipe above it. The water came down warm from the house cistern. He scrubbed grit and her slick from his fingers, watched muddy swirls gather and go, came back wiping his hands on a clean rag from the shelf. Inez had moved to the bench. She knelt, reached behind a stack of cracked lamp chimneys, and pulled out an oilskin packet tied with faded blue cord.

Even before she untied it, he knew it was not some casual workbook. The oilskin had the shine that came from years of palms, not months. One corner had been stitched by hand in two different threads, one old brown, one newer black. The whole thing smelled faintly of lamp oil and basil and the locked-warm scent papers took when they lived near ferment heat.

Silas stayed standing. He had just had his mouth on her throat. The packet still felt more intimate.

Inez glanced up at him. "Sit."

He sat on the bench opposite her.

She laid the packet between them and rested one hand over it for a second, not ceremonious, just steadying herself. "My mother started this," she said. "I kept it after she died. Vero added to it a little when my hand was still ugly. Most of the newer pages are mine. It's names, uses, warnings, where a strain was first taken, which households say it different, which names travel safe and which don't. Not every house. Not every ward. Enough to keep people lit."

Silas looked at the blue cord. "Why are you showing me?"

Her mouth tightened, then eased. "Because if you're in this with me, I need you dangerous in the right direction. Because I'm not going to let you put your hands on me and then leave you ignorant of what you've been stealing from. Because next time you choose, I want you to know the full weight of what you are choosing against."

She untied the cord and opened the ledger.

The pages were thick and gone soft at the edges from years of damp fingers. Some entries ran in a compact older hand, straighter and more school-taught than Inez's. Others broke into her own practical slant, English braided with Spanish abbreviations, small sketches of leaf edges and flame heights in the margins, grease fingerprints, dates keyed to flood years instead of calendars. Names filled the pages, but never alone. Every one had company. Pot-green: low blue edge, safe under iron, do not use in sleeping rooms. Step-cool: holds under fog, weak for boiling, good near children. Soaplight: bright, bitter smoke if overfed. A page farther in carried crossed arrows between three names for the same strain, each tied to district, weather, and burner type. Another had a note in the older hand: never sell cuttings on a dry week, they lie about heat.

He let out a breath he had not known he'd been holding.

"This isn't flavor," he said.

"No," Inez said. "It never was."

He touched the page edge with one clean finger, careful as if the paper had a pulse. "And I thought I understood names."

"You understood how to make strangers repeat them." Her tone was not cruel now. That made it cut cleaner. "That's different."

He turned another page. More lineages. More notes. A page marked with two hands, one older, one younger, arguing in the margin over whether a certain riverbank cutting should be called shade-milk or ferrygreen after the flood. A later correction, practical and exasperated: both, depending on the burner. Then, near the back of that section, a phrase in the older hand circled twice, underlined once in Inez's darker ink years later: mothers in drain, do not carry to market. His eyes stopped there.

Inez watched him notice it. "Not tonight," she said.

He nodded. He understood enough to obey the edge of that no.

She folded her hands on the table. The burn scar showed pale in the lamplight. "You read here. You don't take it out of this ward. You don't copy a line. You don't give Gideon one word from these pages, not directly, not dressed up, not by accident because your vanity gets hungry again. If one line from my mother's hand reaches him through you, I'll break the left hand too. I need you to hear that plain."

Silas looked from the page to her face. There was no heat left in the threat, which was how he knew it was real.

"I hear it," he said.

"Say it back."

"I don't take it out. I don't copy it. I don't repeat from it without you. Nothing from here goes to him. Not one word."

She held his eyes another moment, checking not romance but comprehension. Then she pushed the ledger a little farther toward him.

"Good," she said.

He read until the words blurred from tiredness. Not long. Long enough to feel his own ignorance rearrange itself. Long enough to see that every useful name in the book lived inside a web of bodies, rooms, weather, tools, and memory. Long enough to know he had spent years being praised for trimming language down to the one bright syllable that sold, and that here the truth of a thing thickened the more hands it passed through.

When he finally looked up, Inez had not moved away. She sat with one bare foot tucked under her skirt, watching him with the same wary steadiness she had watched him lift trays, fuck her, confess. There was no soft afterglow to hide in. The room still smelled of labor. Dawn had not yet broken. The city outside still wanted one name clean enough to own.

"Why me?" he asked quietly. "For this, I mean. Not the other part."

Inez glanced at the ledger, then back at him. "The other part is easier."

That landed between his ribs and stayed there.

She went on. "You know how words travel. Better than anybody I trust less. You also know now what they cost when they travel wrong. That may make you useful. Or it may make you a worse disaster than before. I haven't decided which. I decided only that I would rather choose my risk awake."

Silas closed the ledger with both hands and immediately gave it back.

"That's fair," he said.

"Yes." She took it, retied the blue cord, and slid the packet back behind the chimney stack. "Don't make me stupid for having wanted you."

He stood, every muscle sore, every thought changed and not yet sorted. "I won't."

Inez gave him a look that said promises were cheap even now, maybe especially now. Then she rose, gathered her fallen scarf, and moved to unbar the door.

When she lifted the wood free, the first thin gray of morning was leaking into the outer walk. Somewhere beyond the grow houses a ferry horn sounded over the cut, tired and long. Silas followed her out carrying nothing in his hands and more weight than he had brought in. His fingers still smelled of moss, lamp oil, and her skin. Behind him, hidden in the warm dark of the propagation shed, the ledger waited like a second pulse in the wall. He had been inside her body. He had been inside the book. The book was the riskier gift.


# Chapter 11

By noon the propagation shed had turned into a wet lung. Pipe heat breathed through the plank walls, burlap sweated on the tray racks, and Inez's ledger kept sticking to Silas's forearm where the oilskin had gone tacky with old lamp grease. He had never seen a book that felt so much like a tool. It had corners blunted by years of wet hands and one scorched patch near the spine where somebody had set it too close to a burner. When he lifted a page, it gave with the little kiss of paper peeling off itself.

Inez weighed the ledger flat with two stone sinkers and bent over it in her work shirt, sleeves rolled, hair wrapped up and damp at the nape. The burn scar inside her right wrist shone pearly when the shed light hit it. She had been warmer with him before dawn, warmer and lazier and willing to laugh into his throat when he said something stupid with his face in her shoulder. By daylight she had gone back to precision. It was not coldness. It was the kind of trust that expected him to keep up.

"Stop reading the names first," she said.

He looked up from a column that ran mudglow, widow-step, pan-blue, drain-hush. "What else did your mother put in here worth my humiliation?"

"The part that keeps people from getting burned."

He made a face, but he obeyed. The names stood at the edge of each entry, small and practical, as if embarrassed by how much men liked them. The real body of the page was measurement, heat behavior, smoke quality, where a cutting would hold steady, which houses used it, what not to do if children were in reach. Some entries had a little hooked mark in the margin. Some had three pin dots, close together like nail holes. Beside several of those, in different hands and different years, ran the same notes in different wording: answers hard in chorus; unstable if street-sung; keep inside household speech when possible.

Silas touched the three dots with his left index finger. His right wrist still had the ache of bad weather in it, and the old trough sprain woke mean whenever he tried to grip too hard. "These are all the strains that turn when a crowd mouths at them."

"Not all. The ones we knew enough to fear."

"And you wrote them down anyway."

"We wrote them down because fear isn't useful if it dies with the person holding it."

He looked at her. She had not raised her voice. She almost never needed to. He went back to the page. Once he quit admiring the names and started reading like a man trying not to make another ruin, patterns rose out of the ledger the way old lettering rose from a wall after fresh rain. The hooked mark showed up beside half a dozen entries scattered over years. One note would say split from old M.D. stock. Another said mother line still steadier in wash cellars. Another, in a hand he guessed was Inez's mother's, said ridge buyers sniffing around, keep the better trays off the main walk.

"M.D.," he said. "That isn't market drain."

"No."

"Mother digester?"

That got him half a glance. "You're guessing like a man who used to get paid for it."

"Used to and still would, if I had less character."

"You don't have enough character to brag that way."

He laughed despite himself, then frowned again at the page. The same initial sat beside too many useful things to be an accident. Low smoke. Even heat. Takes to plank shade. Holds brightness after splitting. Answers more than one local name without going white. One entry had a slash through the name column, as if the writer had changed her mind about what to call it and decided the use mattered more. Under that slash: from M.D. mother, fourth split, keep from men with sample jars.

Silas read it twice.

Then he turned three pages and found a later entry: fourth split lost, likely carried upslope. Another hand had added, years afterward, if the bastard comes back pretty, do not trust it.

The shed seemed to tighten around him. He could hear bubbling from the ferment pots in the back room and the soft wet nips of trimmed moss dropping into a pail somewhere behind the racks. He had spent half his adult life pretending invention began in the mouth of the man quickest to speak. The ledger made that vanity look cheap. Names had parents. Strains had grandparents. Labor sat behind every bright public surprise with its sleeves rolled and its shoes ruined.

"This isn't about one runaway patch," he said.

"No."

"This thing already had a line. A real one. It didn't crawl out of a gutter last month and ask the city to adopt it."

"No."

He tapped the note about the lost split. "And somebody walked one off."

Inez took a slow breath, not weary, not patient either. "Somebody walked something off. Or sold it. Or let a buyer look too long. The old notes don't tell stories because stories get improved when people are ashamed."

"You know what M.D. means."

She set a finger on the page, just below the line, on a grease stain that had gone almost black with age. "I know enough to know I don't know all of it. My mother wrote what she needed for work. When something belonged to grief she left it to older mouths."

"Vero."

"Yes."

He shouldered back from the table. The bench bit damp through his shirt. "Vero's been waiting for this, hasn't she."

"Vero's been waiting for men like you to learn how often they arrive late and call it discovery."

"That was elegant. Spend the whole night making a sinner honest and by noon you're carving him up with file work."

"You were not honest the whole night."

"No," he said, and the word came out rougher than he meant. "Not the whole night."

Something moved in her face then, not softness exactly, something nearer recognition. She closed the ledger, tied the strap, and slid it under her arm. "Come on. If we ask Aunt Vero before she's eaten, she'll insult us faster and talk longer. After she eats, she only insults."

He followed her out into the glare. The catwalk planks outside the shed had gone slick under the high summer damp. Down below, wash lines sagged between houses, and the drainage cut carried a skin of green where the light missed it. A woman on the far landing shouted for more lamp cloth. Two boys argued over a game and called the same patch of stair moss by two different names without stopping to notice they had done it. The moss at the stair edge gave a faint thread of blue, then settled back into workaday green.

Silas looked after it. The ledger had opened something uglier and larger than fraud. It had opened lineage.

***

Aunt Vero was in the hottest house of the row, knee-deep in trays and cursing at a burner valve with the kind of concentration some people saved for prayer. She had hitched her skirt with a strip of old ferry rope and woven a sprig of rue into the end of her thick white braid because, as she once told a room full of doubters, bad bargains liked a clean scalp. One eye drifted a little when she was tired or amused. At the moment it gave her the look of judging them from two directions at once.

"If you came to ask blessing on whatever happened in that shed last night," she said without turning, "take your damp shame outside. I am busy with work that feeds people."

Inez stepped over a trough rim and shut the door behind them. "We came about the ledger."

"That is almost better. Less vulgar."

"Not by much," Silas said.

Vero looked at him then, broad brown hands on her hips, algae stain sunk permanent into the creases. "You still talk like a brass band in a pantry."

"I can leave."

"You could. It wouldn't improve your understanding."

She went back to the valve. Silas watched her crack it loose with a strip of cloth and the heel of her palm, then listen with her head bent until the hiss evened out. She did not hurry because somebody else's urgency had entered the room. When she finally turned, she held out her hand for the ledger. Inez gave it over. Vero did not open it at once. She weighed it, thumb on the scorched patch near the spine, and for a moment the room changed around her. The rudeness stayed, but something older rose under it.

"Your mother always tied the strap too tight," she said to Inez. "As if paper could run."

"What was M.D. stock?" Inez asked.

"Straight to the bone, then." Vero snorted. "Good. Saves me the trouble of pretending anybody here came for the weather."

She flipped through the pages with a speed that made clear she had seen the hands before, maybe even corrected them. When she reached the notes Silas and Inez had been tracing, she stopped. Her drifting eye settled. "You found the lost split entry."

"What does M.D. mean?" Silas asked.

Vero looked at him over the top of the ledger. "You hear one set of initials and already your mouth wants a proper noun. That is how men get robbed by their own habits."

"I can survive a lesson if it gets to the point."

"Can you."

She shut the book and jerked her chin toward the back room. "Bring that pail first. The one with the spoiled cloth. Since you want history, you can carry some stink with it."

Silas carried the pail. The spoiled cloth in it had gone sour with trimmed runoff and old mash. It smelled like hot coins left in ditch water. He hauled it into the back room, where the heat thickened and the walls were lined with older racks, older jars, older pieces of apparatus too useful or too broken to throw away. Vero followed with the ledger under one arm. Inez came last and set the bar across the door without being told.

That made Silas look at her. "We locking out saints or speculators?"

"Whoever reaches first," Vero said.

She crouched by the lowest rack and pulled out a shallow tray wrapped in damp burlap. The tray itself had once been decent tin. Time had hit it with dents, patches of solder, and a wire handle twisted from three different repairs. Under the burlap lay a thick mat of moss darker than the runaway city's patches, almost bottle green at first glance, until the room heat shifted and a hidden seam of color breathed under it: blue in the ribs, a blush of old rose at the tips, one pale gold thread where a split had recently healed.

Silas went still, not because the thing was magical in any storybook way, but because it had the face of a family member you only noticed after years away. The vein pattern along the frond edges, the little silvering at the root knots, the way the color did not sit flat but seemed to wait for company. He had painted the runaway in six streets and called it novelty. Here was its elder laid out in tin, alive and unimpressed.

"Mother tray," Inez said quietly.

Vero nodded. "The one that lasted."

Silas knelt without thinking. The floorboards were wet through the knees. "Christ."

"No," Vero said. "Women."

There was no softness in it. There was no joke either.

She set the ledger beside the tray and tapped two entries with a blunt fingernail. "M.D. was Mothers' Drain. Not official. Nothing written on a city map. Just the cut behind the wash houses where the digester runoff pooled after the old flood year. Fuel came thin that summer. Bought stock turned mean, smoked children black in the nose, burned too hot in pans and too weak in lamps. Men on the ridge kept saying supply would settle if we paid proper and waited proper. Meanwhile supper still wanted making."

Her mouth tightened. For one beat, when she said the next part, her English went almost formal. "So the women did not wait."

Then the roughness came back in. "We skimmed the scum from the cut, the good luminous strings that held after dawn. We fed half the city in our heads while we bred it. Tula Mays could tell heat from smell before a tray even blushed. Nena Ortiz washed her feet three times a day and still cursed like a carter. Your mother, God keep her troublesome, wrote everything down because she did not trust memory once men started asking questions."

Inez looked at the tray instead of Vero. "How many of you?"

"Enough to make a strain and not enough to own one. That is the point you should keep in your teeth." Vero scratched at the burlap edge. "Six steady hands. More on flood nights. We bred for kitchens first, stairs second, sickrooms third. Pretty was for people with money and mirrors. Useful was for us."

Silas touched the tin rim, careful not to touch the moss. It was warm, warmer than the room. "When did the buyers come in."

Vero barked a laugh. "They were there before the roots thickened. Buyers are flies. You don't ask when they arrive. You ask what they've already landed on." She leaned back against the rack. "At first they came with compliments. They said women in the low wards had a genius for domestic refinement. They said somebody ought to protect such promising work from spoilage. One patent man even brought sugared almonds in a box and spoke to us as if he was lowering himself into charity. I ate the almonds. Then I told him to take his polished shoes out of my mud."

Despite himself, Silas smiled. It died fast.

"We hid the better trays," Vero went on. "We split the names house by house so nobody outside the ward could hear one word and carry the whole use home. We kept the hot-burn notes in mouths, not on paper. We were not saints. We were tired and suspicious and already learning. But one split got away."

Inez lifted her head. "Taken?"

Vero's drifting eye went vague with memory. "Taken, traded, lost, sold. Pick the version that keeps your sleep. A tray does not need a villain with a silk hat. Sometimes it only needs a cousin who wants rent money. Sometimes a husband with a bottle in his eye. Sometimes a boy who thinks one clipping from the edge is not the mother herself. The split went upslope. A year later pretty little jars started showing up in refined windows. Less heat, more shine. Always the way."

Silas felt old shame move through him and find a newer place to settle. "And the runaway line."

Vero reached down and pinched a dead brown scrap from the mother tray, rolled it between finger and thumb, and let it fall. "Descended. Not identical. Not pure. Nothing alive stays pure in this city unless it's already dead. But yes. Same mother. Same old chorus temper in the bones. Same appetite for public mouths. They bred some steadiness out, some prettiness in, then commerce did what commerce does. It copied badly, cheapened fast, spilled often. Now the bastard is back on the walls, and everybody up-ridge is acting like the river invented it for their benefit."

"Does Gideon know?" Inez asked.

"Know this exact history? Probably not. Know he's selling stolen labor with a new ribbon on it? Men like him always know that much. They think if enough years pass between theft and sale, the taking goes clean."

Silas stared at the tray until the blue in its ribs throbbed faintly with the sound of voices in the next room. It was all there if a man stopped admiring his own cleverness long enough to look. The useful names split by house. The missing tray. The market prettying. The public instability. He felt almost insulted by how much of the city's suffering had been legible to anyone with patience.

"If Damaris prints this," he said, "if people see the tray, hear the history, maybe it cuts the ground out from under Gideon before the regatta."

Vero gave him such flat contempt he wished she had thrown something. "And then what. The city picks one holy widow to stand up in a clean collar and say this is mine? A judge asks which woman first touched the root and therefore owns the lamp in every kitchen? Foolishness. We didn't make it alone then, and we won't make it alone now."

"He can't own it either."

"Of course he can't. But markets don't need truth. They need a face and a word and enough fools repeating both." She pushed the tray toward him half an inch. "Don't use my dead against him as if I handed you a better brand."

That landed harder than any shouted blame. Silas took it because it was deserved. He had spent months turning other people's mouths into his trade. The instinct to fix this by finding the rightful author was only the cleaner version of the same hunger.

Inez put two fingers on the old tin. "Then why keep the tray."

Vero's expression changed again, a small dark shift. "Because work deserves witnesses. Because your mother is gone and Tula is gone and Nena is gone, and if every useful thing gets sold twice and remembered never, then the city belongs entirely to men with printers." She rubbed at the ferry rope around her waist. "And because it still works. We use splittings off the edge when a bed turns mean. History that does not light a stair is just vanity with dust on it."

The room hummed with heat. Outside, a child laughed, then yelped when someone shushed him. Silas looked from Vero to Inez and back to the tray. He had come in wanting proof. What sat before him was worse and better than proof. It was obligation that had outlived witness.

Vero jerked her chin at him. "Don't start apologizing now. I've heard enough men make sorrow sound like performance."

"Wasn't going to."

"Good. Save your mouth for when it can carry something besides itself."

She rewrapped the tray with more tenderness than she had shown either of them, though she did it roughly enough to preserve her reputation. When she rose, her knees cracked loud as snapped twigs. "Now get out. I want broth before the valve acts cursed again. And if you're taking this history anywhere near the ridge, go filthy. Let them smell where it came from."

***

Damaris's note found him on the walk back from Sump Ward, folded into a printer's proof and tucked beneath the strap of his good shoulder. He did not feel the newsboy do it. One minute he was crossing the narrow plank above the drainage cut, thinking about old theft and warm tin; the next there was paper against his neck and a boy already halfway down the alley yelling somebody else's headline.

The note was in Damaris's quick black hand.

If you want to see how Mr. Wren means to shove one word down the city's throat, meet me at the lower gate of Gilt Crescent at dusk. Comb your hair. They search for class first.

He laughed once when he read that, then folded the proof into his pocket. By the time the sun went red through the river haze he had washed, failed to make his shirt respectable, and borrowed a collar from Junie's best shelf that hated him on sight. The ridge climb put sweat under it anyway.

Damaris waited in the lee of a carriage arch where rich people could pretend they were outdoors without being touched by the street. She wore widow's black gone shiny at the elbows, and one glove had been mended along the thumb with blue thread so visible it looked defiant. Ink still lived in the creases of her fingers. When she smiled, the chipped front tooth flashed like a mean little lamp.

"You look like debt trying to pass for linen," she said.

"And you look like a funeral that learned shorthand."

"Fair. Come on."

She did not offer him an explanation until they were moving. That was Damaris at her best and worst. She treated secrets like headline type, worth more if held one breath longer than decency allowed.

"How'd you get this invite?" he asked.

"By being useful before the man remembered to despise me." She kept her pace easy, not quick, because quick meant guilt in neighborhoods built on watching. "He wants press after the launch, not before.
