He wants society women now, dry-goods men, ferry concessionaires, choir leaders, the kind of buyer who likes a product better if it arrives with music and a napkin. But one steward still owes me because I kept his sister's divorce off page three."

"You're a public service."

"At times."

The lower gate of Gideon's preview opened into a boathouse so polished it almost denied the river. The place sat on a cut off the main channel, under a glass roof beaded with evening condensation. Water held black and still in the basin beneath, thick enough to reflect the gas globes and the lantern boats moored in a bright row. Flower arrangements tried hard to conquer the algae smell and did not quite manage it. Under the sweetness sat mud, ferment, hot lamp metal, the real city refusing eviction.

Damaris passed her card to a steward with side-whiskers damp from the heat. "My copy clerk," she said, touching Silas's sleeve as if he belonged to her entirely. "He notices where phrasing goes wrong."

The steward barely looked at him. Class, as Damaris had promised, was the first search and often the only one. A man in a collar and cleanish cuffs could walk through more gates than a woman who had built the thing he was admiring.

They climbed a narrow service stair to the catwalk running above the basin. From there Silas could see the whole arrangement at once, which was exactly when he understood what Damaris had dragged him here to suffer.

It was not a regatta in any innocent sense. It was an occupation laid out in ribbons and copy.

Six sample skiffs floated below, each fitted with lantern cages twined in pale gold moss and each carrying the same gilt nameboard at the prow: CELADON CROWN in letters slim enough to flatter wealth and broad enough to read from a ferry. Behind the boats, printers' boys stacked bundles of handbills tied by route. One stack wore Ferry South in grease pencil. Another Bridge stalls. Another School allotment. On a long side table, paper sleeves held damp little splittings in sawdust, each one tagged with the same printed card and a line of usage instructions so simple they insulted the city: For elegant domestic light and refined evening display.

Silas stared at that line until disgust turned almost admiring. It was clean theft. It stripped out heat, labor, warnings, every practical distinction that had kept people safe, and replaced them with aspiration.

"Wait," Damaris murmured.

A small chorus took position at the far end of the dock, not church singers, not exactly. Girls in summer white and two boys whose collars sat wrong because they were river children hired up for the afternoon. A music man tapped the stand. They ran the refrain once, then again. The melody was simple enough for drunks and children, which was to say dangerous. By the third repeat Silas had it against his will.

Celadon Crown, carry it home.
Celadon Crown, let the whole house glow.

"Christ," he muttered.

"Yes," Damaris said. "A hymn for shoppers."

Below them buyers drifted between the skiffs with the pleased attention of people inspecting a future difficulty for somebody poorer. A dry-goods matron in pearl earbobs held one of the free cuttings up against her sleeve to judge the color. Two ferry men bent over a route map tacked to cork and argued in undertones about where the launch boats could catch the densest evening traffic. Nobody here was stupid. That was the filth of it. They understood perfectly that a word caught better when it came home in more than one form.

Gideon Wren stepped onto the dock with his gloves in one hand and the room quieted around him as if it had rehearsed that too. He was as neat as ever, silver at the temples, suit untouched by the climb or the damp. He did not raise his voice. He never needed to. Control did the carrying for him.

"My friends," he said, "the error of the earlier launch was not ambition. It was insufficiency. One name offered once is a suggestion. One name encountered from morning to night becomes habit."

He paced once along the dock. Gaslight touched the edge of his cheek and left the rest deliberate.

"People do not adopt by argument. They adopt by sequence. They hear a phrase at breakfast, they see it on the bridge at noon, they sing it without thinking by evening, and then they carry the living thing home in their own hands. After that, the market merely records what memory has already accepted."

Silas could have admired the construction if it were not aimed like a knife at half the city. Signs, songs, hand to hand cuttings, ferry routes, school allotments, house display. Every channel that strengthened a chorus strain, Gideon had bought or hired or seduced into orderly line.

One of the ferry men below asked about distribution to the lower wards. Gideon answered without a pause. "Especially the lower wards. A habit that flatters only the rich is fashion. A habit that enters the stair districts and the kitchens is ownership."

There it was, spoken plain enough to stink.

Damaris had been whispering smart little funeral lines all the way in. Now she went quiet. Silas glanced at her. Her eyes were fixed on the table of cuttings, and the theatrical amusement had drained off her face. He remembered what her voice profile might have called out if such things existed in life: she narrated until the story stopped being outside her. Then she got still.

"You knew about the song," he said.

"I knew there was a song. I didn't know he was feeding splittings into it."

Below, the chorus took up the refrain again while a steward placed sample namecards into buyers' hands and another uncapped a pot of printer's ink so fresh the metallic tang climbed all the way to the catwalk. On the far wall hung full-size posters already pasted to temporary boards for inspection, pale gold field, elegant lettering, a skiff all shine and no mud. In the corner, a schoolmistress examined little folded sheets printed with the refrain and the brand name in large type for copying practice.

Silas felt the collar sawing at his neck. He thought of Otis sounding out branded words at Junie's table. He thought of Vero's mother tray breathing in dark tin while this bright clean theft rehearsed itself under glass. Gideon was not planning an event. He was planning a week in which the city would not get a free mouth.

"How many papers?" Silas asked.

Damaris swallowed, then found her voice again. "Three friendly sheets for sure. Maybe four if he buys the morning boys. I heard two street song men downstairs comparing tempo. And those ferry allocations mean he's paying for the route callers too."

"Bridge stalls, school copy, free cuttings, songs." Silas looked over the rail at the route bundles. "Posters by every choke point worth the wall rent. He wants the word to hit a person before they know they've chosen it."

"Yes."

He looked down again at Gideon, who was smiling now in that composed devotional way he wore when speaking of markets as if they were weather sent by God to reward tidy men. Buyers laughed lightly at something he said about tasteful domestic illumination. A steward set one lantern boat adrift across the basin. The moss in its cages held a pale, disciplined gold, not because it preferred that color but because enough money had already started asking it to.

Damaris touched his sleeve. "You've seen it. Don't get greedy and stay for more."

"Greedy."

"Men like you and me always think one more look will turn into a weapon. Mostly it turns into being noticed."

She was right. He hated that she was right. They slipped back down the service stair while the chorus kept polishing the refrain. By the time they reached the gate, the tune had lodged under his ribs like grit. Damaris stopped under the carriage arch and pulled off the blue-stitched glove finger by finger.

"I can print some of this," she said. "Not all before he launches. He has bought too much silence too neatly. But I can make people laugh at the refinement of it."

Silas thought of the mother tray, of Vero saying proof was not a better brand. "Laughter won't stop the catch."

"No." Damaris looked toward the dark falling over the lower wards. "But it can rough the surface."

He studied her a moment. "Why show me."

Her chipped tooth worried at her lower lip before she answered. "Because I started this season treating your mess like copy, and now the copy is walking into kitchens. That makes it my business whether I enjoy the assignment or not."

It was not redemption. She was too honest for that. It was better.

"All right," he said.

"If you get us both hanged in print, at least spell my name right."

Then she was gone into the ridge traffic, widow black swallowing into carriage shadow. Silas stood another second under the hot stone arch, listening to the song spill faintly from the boathouse and thinking how cheaply a city could be taught to mistake repetition for truth.

***

Inez was waiting for him on the plank walk behind the wash houses, where the ward thinned into dark and the old drainage cut took over the speaking. The air down there smelled of wet rope, soap gone soft in its dish, and the green rot that never quite lost to usefulness. Somebody two houses over was frying onions. Farther off a ferry bell struck and let the sound travel through the boards.

She had her arms folded, not angry, just braced against mosquitoes and news. A lamp tray sat beside her boot on the rail, hooded against the damp. When he stepped onto the walk it tipped a little under his weight, and the water below made a low sucking sound against the pilings.

"You were gone long enough," she said.

"The rich schedule even their theft."

"You saw it."

He told her. Not all of it in order, because order made it sound smaller than it felt. He gave her the song first, because that was the filthiest part. Then the school copy sheets. Then the ferry allocations, the free splittings, the posters waiting on their boards, the buyers nodding over routes as if discussing weather. He gave her Gideon's line about habit, and when he repeated the word ownership he heard it come out of his own mouth like a bad taste.

Inez listened with her head a little bowed. In the half light he could make out the set of her scarf and the shine on the burn scar at her wrist where she held the rail. She did not interrupt until he got to the cuttings.

"Free?" she said.

"Tagged and damp and ready for anybody who wants to feel rich in a kitchen too hot to breathe in."

"So he'll put the word in their mouths and the thing in their hands together."

"Yes."

The drainage cut gurgled under the planks. A window opened somewhere above them and a woman dumped gray water into the night with a muttered curse at whoever had left soap rind in the pail. Silas thought again of the service catwalk, of rich people admiring a city as if it were a decorative appetite. He wanted very much to hit something expensive.

"Vero was right," he said. "He doesn't need the truth. He needs sequence."

"I know what he needs." Inez looked at him then. "What do you think we do."

He had been carrying one answer all the way down from Gilt Crescent and knew, even before he spoke it, that it was the old poisoned answer in a cleaner coat.

"Pike prints the history. We show the tray. We say where the line came from before he turns it into a parade."

Inez did not move. That was worse than anger. "And then."

"Then people know he's selling stolen ward stock."

"And then."

He exhaled. The plank rail was tacky under his hand from old damp and lamp ash. "Then maybe the city won't swallow his pretty lie whole."

"No." Her voice stayed level, which meant pressure with her. "Then the city asks who owns it instead. Who gets to stand up and say this one is the true mother, this one is the rightful hand, this one may license the rest. You heard Vero. That is only the same theft with a more honest face."

"So what, we say nothing? Let him march a choir through the wards and sell them their own drain water back with gold tied on?"

"I didn't say that."

"No, you waited for me to say the stupid thing first."

"You usually do."

He almost snapped back, then stopped. She was not being witty. She was being exact. That sobered him faster than any tenderness could have.

Below them, something moved in the moss along the cut wall. A strip of growth caught a voice from somewhere upriver and shifted blue at one edge, green at the other. Not dramatic. Just enough to remind a watching man that no single mouth ruled the stuff unless enough other mouths went silent.

Silas looked at it, then back at her. "If we prove the mother line, the market turns it into a claim."

"Yes."

"If we find one truer name, same problem."

"Yes."

"If Vero names it, they call her proprietor. If you name it, they call you local authority and sell around you until you're decorative."

"Yes."

He rubbed at the ache in his wrist and felt the old trough injury answer. The pain had become a kind of metronome in him, dull most hours, sharp when he reached the wrong way. It gave him no wisdom, but it did force him to stop reaching so fast.

"Then the answer isn't a better owner," he said slowly.

"No."

"It's making ownership fail."

That got her attention in full. Not approval, not yet. The tighter thing before it. "Say it cleaner."

He took a breath that smelled of onions, soap, river damp, and the sour sweet breath of the cut below. Words had always leaped for him. Now he made himself work for them. "Gideon's plan only holds if one name gets everywhere first and pushes the rest out. He needs people asking for the same thing in the same shape. If the city keeps more than one useful name in play, house by house, stair by stair, if every place keeps saying what it actually needs instead of what looks good on a card, then he has to buy silence over and over. He can't own a thing that won't stay singular."

Inez uncrossed her arms. The walk gave a little under both of them when she shifted closer to the rail. "That is better."

"That wasn't praise."

"No."

"Thought so."

She looked down at the lamp tray by her boot, then out across the dark where ward lights climbed the steps in uneven bands. "My mother did not keep that ledger so somebody could crown the correct widow after she was dead. Vero didn't keep the tray alive for a courtroom either. The work was shared before it was stolen. If we answer by finding one owner, we only finish what the buyers started."

"And if we just scream fraud, he still gets the habit."

"Yes."

"So we break the habit before it settles."

This time she nodded once. "Now you're talking like a person who has finally met a kitchen."

He laughed, low and tired. "That is the sweetest thing you've said to me all week."

"It isn't sweet."

"No. That's why I believe it."

For a little while they stood without speaking. The night moved around them in layers. Ferry chains knocked. A mosquito whined at his ear and he slapped it too late. Somewhere up on Lantern Steps a man called for more wick. Somewhere else a woman answered with a different moss name than the one Silas had heard in the boathouse song, then another voice corrected her for use, not for elegance. The growth along the cut wall breathed at the edges of color, refusing to settle into a single obedient glow.

Silas thought of Gideon's basin under glass, every skiff arranged, every route bundle tied, every poster already dried to the board. He thought of Vero's patched tin, warm through his fingertips. One room tried to make a city behave. The other had kept a city alive without ever pretending it belonged to one hand. For the first time since this whole mess had turned his mouth into civic weather, he could see the shape of the fight clearly. It was not falsehood against truth. It was monopoly against use.

"Pike can still print," he said. "Not as claim. As dirt under the varnish."

"Yes."

"Vero can talk if she wants, but not as a mascot."

"Try calling her that and see if your other wrist can survive."

"Fair."

He looked at her profile in the dark, the straight practical line of it, the mouth that had bitten his shoulder before dawn and was now set on strategy with the same seriousness. Whatever had opened between them in the shed had not thinned under the weight of the day. It had changed shape. That felt truer and more dangerous than comfort.

"We'll need more than one answer," he said.

"We'll need the city remembering itself out loud."

"That'll be noisy."

"Good."

The word settled between them with more force than anything louder could have managed.

Below the walk the drainage cut carried old runoff south toward the river. The water took scraps of light from every window it passed and broke them up on the surface. Green from one house. Blue from another. A bruised pink thread where some child had insisted on a name at supper and not been corrected. No single color held for long. The current would not let it.

Inez set her hand on the rail beside his, not over it, not asking for a vow. Shared weight was enough. "Then that's the work," she said. "Not proving who first owned it. Making sure nobody gets to own it alone."

He looked at their hands on the wet wood, her scar silver in the low light, his stained fingers, the city breathing around them through a hundred uncoordinated mouths. Under the deeper theft, under the heat and shame and coming spectacle, purpose finally took a shape he did not need to sell.


# Chapter 12

By the time Junie dragged the third table into place, the supper crowd had gone and Ferrymouth had dropped into its late noises: hulls knocking pilings, a bell from the upriver ferry, gulls disputing over fish skin in the alley, men on the slip laughing too hard because they were too tired to do it softly. The kitchen still held the whole day in its walls. Heat came off the digester stove in wet breaths, and the room smelled of eel broth, pepper vinegar, lamp smoke, yeast, and river mud. Silas had changed his shirt once and then given up on cleanliness as a profession.

Junie jammed a folded rag under a short table leg and looked at him over the stockpot. Flour lived in the old split of her left eyebrow again, making the scar look chalked.

"If you stand there like a hired mourner," she said, "I'll put you by the stove and let everybody sweat at you. Cut the bread. Thick. Nobody trusts a thin slice."

His right wrist was mostly good for hauling, chopping, and being sworn at. It still did not forgive elegance. He took the knife and started through the rye loaves anyway, leaning his weight behind the blade when the ache climbed.

"I wasn't planning a speech," he said.

"You plan them by breathing. Cut."

Otis, missing one front tooth and proud of the vacancy, sorted mismatched spoons by size because he had decided that honest people ought to get the heavier ones. Every few minutes he tested a word through the gap in his mouth to hear what the air did with it. Tonight he had chosen coalition, which came out with a pleased whistle.

Junie caught him at it. "If you whistle that at the table, I'll make you scrub the fish board."

"It whistles by itself," Otis said.

"Then let it do it quietly."

Silas watched his sister move through the room with the steady brutality of a woman determined to make hospitality do a job more delicate souls would have handed to prayer. She had set the good bowls out, which meant the thick white crockery with only two chips among them. She had fried plantain slices in a skillet black as a sin she'd stopped regretting years ago. Rice steamed in a lidded pan. There was eel stew, a heap of blistered green peppers, pickled onions, half a heel of hard cheese, and a slab of river-cake dense enough to stun a man if the evening went badly. Given the guest list, the evening still might.

She had invited people who had reason to dislike him, reason to dislike one another, and worse reason to need all the same thing.

"You still have time to claim plague," Silas said.

"No, I don't." Junie slapped the ladle against the pot rim and counted under her breath. "Eight bowls, two benches, one boy, one fool of a brother, one stove that still answers to decent names. We're committed."

"That sounded almost cheerful."

"It wasn't. Move the lamp."

He moved the lamp from the center shelf to the sideboard. The light in the glass had gone a working green, the kind Junie preferred when she wanted to see soup skin, dirty cuffs, and weak lies for what they were. She had refused the prettier shades from the first day of the craze. In her kitchen, beauty ranked below heat, rent, and whether a man had paid last week's tab.

The first knock was not a knock but the heel of Aunt Vero's hand hitting the jamb as if she had a long history of entering rooms that pretended not to want her. Inez came in behind her with a crock wrapped in a dish towel. Her dark hair was tied up in the work scarf he knew too well now, the knot damp at the nape from the heat outside. A faint crescent of old burn scar showed inside her wrist when she shifted the bowl higher against her hip. He felt that sight before he had any right to. Aunt Vero brought a jar of pickled okra, and the vinegar reached the room ahead of her.

Vero looked from Silas to the tables to the stockpot and clicked her tongue.

"You did put the thief indoors," she said. "I had a little hope for you, Junie."

"He isn't at the head of anything," Junie said. "He can slice bread till the hand gives out."

Inez's gaze touched Silas only once on the way in. It was not cold or soft, only measured, and it was almost worse that way.

"I brought black beans," she said to Junie.

"Bless you for your timing and not your morals," Junie said, taking the crock. "Set it there."

Aunt Vero set down the okra jar and fanned herself with her hat. One eye drifted a little when she was tired or amused; tonight it was doing both. "If that newspaper widow is here, I want her far enough from me that she has to lean to steal."

"You say that like leaning's hard for me," came Damaris Pike from the door.

She slid in wearing widow's black gone shiny at the seams, one sleeve shiny in a different place where ink had found it and made permanent claims. Her fingers were smudged blue-black to the first knuckle. Under one arm she carried a packet of spoiled proofs tied with string. The chipped edge of her front tooth showed when she smiled, which was often and not always because anything pleased her.

"I brought paper," she said. "Also a peppermint if the company goes bad."

"Keep the paper," Junie said. "Keep the peppermint. Wash your hands."

"They're editor's hands."

"They're hands."

Behind Damaris came Mara Tindle from Lantern Steps, narrow as a stair rail, with a blue loop of darning thread tied around her smallest finger so she would not lose track of whose mending she had promised by morning. She smelled of starch, wet linen, and fennel seed. Silas had seen her before on the landings above Lantern Steps, beating rugs with a kind of private vengeance. Tonight she held her jaw tight as if the room itself needed basting.

"I said I'd stay one bowl," she told Junie. "After that I've got shirts to turn and a lodger who snores like a drowned trumpet."

"One bowl's all I'm asking," Junie said. "If you stay for cake, that's between you and your weak nature."

Mara's glance caught on Damaris. "I didn't know Patent Row was invited."

Damaris set her bundle of proofs on the sideboard. "I didn't know Lantern Steps permitted wit after dark."

"It doesn't. That's why I came downriver for supper."

Junie turned before the heat between them could find a fresh draft. "Nobody here has to like anybody. You have to sit long enough to eat while the food's hot and your mouth's busy. If you want to leave angry afterward, leave stronger."

Otis, who had been instructed not to speak and had therefore been storing up a great pressure of language, said, "Can Uncle Silas leave thievy or does that wash off with dishwater?"

The room went still for half a breath. Then Aunt Vero barked laughter so sharp it startled a gull off the sill outside.

"Good," she said. "Keep the boy."

Silas cut another loaf. "I walked into that one."

"You walked into all of this," Junie said. "Set the bread out."

They sat because Junie made sitting feel less like agreement than like the next chore on a list nobody could afford to neglect. She put Vero beside Silas, which he understood as punishment and utility in equal measure. Inez went across from him. Damaris took the corner nearest the lamp and immediately pinched the flame tube with professional interest. Mara chose the chair that let her see both door and window. Otis wedged himself between Junie and the sideboard and reached for the plantains the instant grace failed to appear.

Junie smacked his knuckles lightly with a spoon. "Wait till everybody's got a bowl."

"That isn't a prayer," Damaris said.

"No," Junie said. "It's better."

She served like a woman bailing a boat. Nothing about it was delicate. Eel stew thumped into bowls, thick with tomatoes, onion, and rice. Black beans, peppers, and bread followed, and the table filled with steam so dense it made the lamp halo woolly.

Nobody spoke for the first minute except to ask for salt, and even that sounded like a tactical concession.

Silas had forgotten what a room of suspicious people could do once you gave them food hot enough to demand concentration. Soup put everybody on the same side for a little while. Tongues cooled. Fingers got occupied. Steam softened posture. Junie knew this the way other people knew liturgy.

Damaris broke first, because of course she did. "This is extortion by stew."

"It works," Junie said.

Mara tore bread, still watching the others. "I came because my top landing has changed color three times in six days and the old men on the sixth floor have started calling it fashionable instead of dangerous. That's how somebody dies stupid."

"That's also how rent goes up," Junie said.

"Everything in this city is how rent goes up," Damaris said.

Aunt Vero jabbed her spoon into the beans. "For you, maybe. Some of us are old enough to remember greed before you lot learned to print it nicely."

"I print it ugly on purpose," Damaris said. "That's my civic service."

Inez ate with the clean efficiency he had watched in the propagation shed, no wasted motion even with stew. "If we're doing this," she said, "we don't do it like a newspaper supper. Nobody takes a word out of this room unless the person who brought it says where it may go."

Damaris raised both hands. "I didn't come to steal."

Mara snorted. "That's exactly what stealing says."

"Not tonight," Damaris said, and for once the quick frame around her voice loosened a little. "Tonight I'm here because Gideon means to flood every decent surface in the city till schoolboys can chant his name in their sleep. I know what flood looks like when it's made of paper."

Silas kept his eyes on the stew, though he felt all of them glance his way in the wake of it. He had done flood in paint and slogans. He had helped teach it the route.

Junie cut through the silence before it could turn sentimental or righteous. "Fine. Rule one: nobody sells a word. Rule two: nobody asks for a true name, because that phrase makes me want to throw crockery. Rule three: if you say something useful, you keep eating while you say it so the rest of us know you're not performing."

Aunt Vero pointed her spoon at Silas. "Add rule four. If he gets hungry for the prettiest phrase, somebody hits him."

"Hard enough to improve him?" Damaris asked.

"Nothing's that strong," Mara said.

It got a laugh, even from Inez at the edge of her mouth, and the sound relieved the room of a pressure Silas had not realized he had been holding in his ribs. It was not peace; nothing so false would have survived the room. But the table gave a little around the joints.

Junie reached for the beans. "There. We can act like a city for one meal."

Outside, somebody on the slip shouted for more lamp oil. Somebody else answered with profanity and a joke about credit. Inside, bowls emptied. Vero demanded the pickled onions from Damaris rather than from Junie, and Damaris passed them without making a point of the surrender. Mara accepted pepper sauce from Inez after a pause short enough not to be called one. By the time the stew pot showed bottom and Otis had been allowed one plantain and denied a second, every chair was still occupied.

Junie had done what Silas could not have done with ten speeches and his best coat. She had given every grudge a plate and made it wait its turn.

***

The proofs Damaris had brought were spoiled on one side by a mangled alderman's face and an advertisement for corsets promising moral uplift through lacing. The reverse was mostly blank. Junie spread the sheets across the wiped table while Otis collected bowls to the sink and listened with his whole back.

"If anybody drips stew on the clean side," Damaris said, "I'll call it collaboration, but I won't enjoy it."

Silas reached automatically for the broadest sheet and set it square to himself, the way he always did before lettering, with a big phrase down the middle and room on either side for ornament and for the eye to be told where to go.

Inez leaned over, smelled faintly of soap, wet cotton, and the peppers she'd carried in with the beans, and turned the page sideways under his hand.

"Don't build another altar," she said. "Make columns."

His fingers paused around the pencil. "All right."

"Five of them," she said. "Name. Place. Use. What it does wrong. Who says yes."

Junie, carrying the pot to the cooling shelf, added without turning, "And what somebody owes if they borrow it. Make that six."

Aunt Vero grunted approval. "Now we're in church."

Silas ruled the sheet with the edge of the pencil and the side of his hand. The wrist complained halfway through the third line, a hot little warning down the bone. He kept going until six narrow fields stood waiting. The page looked ugly already. That helped.

Damaris, watching over the lamp chimney she had decided needed cleaning, said, "If I ever see my profession described in six columns, I'll kill myself from shame."

"You'll miss your own obituary," Junie said.

"I trust you to write a better one."

Mara had not touched the pencil or the paper. She kept rolling the blue thread around her finger till the tip went pale. "Before we begin," she said, staring at Silas and not blinking much, "I want it said plain. If anything from this table ends up prettied and sold, I'll tell every woman on Lantern Steps to send their mending elsewhere. You'll die dressed like a madman."

It was not the harshest threat anyone had made him in the last month. It had the advantage of being specific.

"Fair," Silas said.

"Not fair," Aunt Vero said. "Light. Fair would've been the river."

Junie planted both hands on the table. "Mara, say what you came to say. He writes what you say and only what you say. If he forgets where it came from, I'll open his skull and store onions in it."

That, apparently, was sufficient guarantee. Mara exhaled through her nose and looked at the columns.

"Pot first," Junie said. "My house, my order. Write potgreen."

Silas wrote the word.

Junie leaned over, saw the single naked phrase sitting there, and clicked her tongue. "What good is that by itself? Write the rest."

"Tell me."

"Ferrymouth kitchens. Dawn through dinner if you want beans to keep their shape and eel fat to mind itself. It holds a low steady heat. Doesn't spit. Doesn't sour broth. If some fool hangs it in a parlor because he likes how calm it looks, it turns greasy by the second day and makes the curtains stink. And if you borrow cuttings from my stove bed, you send back rice or lamp mash by week's end, because I don't run a charity for fashionable idiots."

Silas wrote as fast as the wrist would allow: Ferrymouth kitchens. Dawn-dinner. Long pots. Low steady heat. Not for parlors; throws grease, stinks curtains. Borrower owes rice or lamp mash by week. The language wanted smoothing under his hand. He let it stay rough.

"Better," Junie said.

"My turn," Mara said, before anyone could claim hers for her.

She sat straighter, one palm flat on the table. "Write stepblue. Lantern Steps, landings and stair rails, mostly after rain or fog. It catches the slick lip of the tread so people can see what will kill them. Not too bright, else old men miss the edge from glare and swear the light lied. You don't use it above a nursery because children think blue means cool and reach for it. And if you take it from our side of the city, you post the stair number with it. Otherwise some bastard paints it on a ballroom and says it came from nowhere."

Silas looked up. "Why the stair number?"

Mara's mouth tightened. "Because my brother split his chin open on the sixth landing before we learned what worked there. Because place matters, that's why."

He wrote that down too, not the brother exactly, but post stair number; place matters. Then, because the omission felt like a fresh theft, he added smaller: Learned after injury on sixth landing. Mara saw him do it and gave the smallest nod, not gratitude, just correction accepted.

Inez had been watching the columns fill. She pushed the lamp half an inch nearer the page so the pencil line sharpened.

"Washcool," she said. "Sump Ward wash lines and fever rooms. Night rinse, baby cloths, shirts you can't afford to scorch. It keeps the heat low enough that lye doesn't bite back through the rag. It also keeps glow steady through steam, which matters when half the room is wet. It doesn't go near fermentation shelves. If you mix it with mother trays, you'll wake to white fur and spoiled stock. If somebody outside the ward wants it, they say where they heard it and who sent them. Not because we're holy. Because we've had enough of strangers pretending they invented soap."

Her voice stayed even all through it. That was her pressure register, cleaner and sharper the more dangerous the subject got. Silas wrote with care, aware of her standing close enough that the burn scar inside her wrist hovered just above the margin while she pointed.

"Make a mark by that one," she said. "Shared only if the keeper says yes."

He added another note: keeper approval required.

Damaris made a dissatisfied sound. "That's not a column. That's a legal warning."

"Good," Inez said. "Maybe you'll finally respect one."

Damaris sucked the chipped edge of her tooth against a peppermint and thought. "All right. Put me in, then. Proof-pale. Patent Row pressrooms and compositor benches. We use it when the type's fine and the night is long and three different liars want three different editions before dawn. It throws enough white to show a cracked serif and not enough heat to soften lead or wilt the cheap paper. It makes every face in the room look recently exhumed, which is useful only during election season. You don't ask newsboys to carry it as a parlor novelty. They read ghost stories by it and then piss themselves in alleys."

Junie snorted into the sink.

"And what does somebody owe?" Silas asked.

Damaris looked almost startled that he had asked the question instead of assuming none applied to her kind of work. Then she shrugged. "If you borrow a pressroom name, you tell the boys who set type where you got it. And you buy them tea. Strong tea. Nobody steals from compositors twice if they want their letters spelled right ever again."

Silas wrote it in.

Aunt Vero had been quiet, which on her always meant either pity or calculation, and he knew better than to hope for the first. She ran a broad thumb along the rim of the okra jar and watched him making lines, notes, little cramped precisions where once he would have hunted for one phrase fat enough to sell to a wall.

"You're still listening like a shopper," she said.

The pencil stopped.

"Am I."

"Yes. I can hear it from your forehead. You're waiting for the one that'll beat his crown."

The room tightened again, not loudly this time. Just enough for the table to remember all its reasons for existing.

Silas did not attempt defense. It would have sounded too much like old trade. "I know that habit when it bites me," he said. "I'm trying not to feed it."

"Try harder," Inez said.

Junie wiped her hands on her apron and came back to the table with a dish towel over one shoulder like a banner for a state no one deserved. "Vero, either skin him properly or say your piece."

Aunt Vero took her time. Her drifting eye settled for a beat, the way it sometimes did when old losses walked too close.

"Write widow basil," she said at last.

Silas did.

"Wake rooms," she said. "Mostly Sump Ward before the investors learned to call grief a market. Low green with a little blue underneath. Keeps cut flowers from turning rank before dawn and lets old women see whose hand they're holding. You do not hang it in a dance hall because it is pretty. You do not bottle it for rich girls who want to feel tragic at supper. And if the widow house gives you cuttings, you send girls back on cleaning day. Two if you're strong, three if you're grateful. The obligation isn't courtesy. It's labor. The dead don't keep their own lamps."

Nobody moved for a moment after that.

Silas wrote slower than before. It was not from pain. It was from shame, and from the knowledge that the word itself had almost nothing to do with the thing if the thing was severed from the room that had taught it how to mean.

When he finished, Aunt Vero reached over and tapped the sixth column with a thick stained fingernail.

"There. That's the part you stole before. Not the word. The return path."

Something hot and ugly crossed his throat. It was not the usual dramatic kind. It was not the self-loving version that wanted witnesses. It was just the hard little fact of being described accurately by someone who had earned the right.

Otis, pretending to stack bowls and listening like sin, said, "What's the return path?"

Junie answered before anyone else could turn a child into metaphor. "It means if you borrow, you carry back. Plate to sink. Name to the people it came from. Coal to the kitchen if the kitchen kept you alive."

"Oh," Otis said. "Like spoons."

"Exactly like spoons."

Damaris leaned over the page. "It's too much for one broadside."

Silas heard himself answer from the old part of his trade. "No newsboy will carry widow basil clean. And stepblue dies if you don't know what stair."

Mara's chin lifted. "Then maybe the point isn't to make every mouth say the same damn thing."

"No," Inez said. "It isn't."

He looked down at the page. It held six columns and four names. Already the sheet felt crowded in the honest way a dock felt crowded: not decorative, just full of specific need. There was no center phrase, and there was no obvious winner. Every time his eye tried to choose one, the notes in the next columns dragged it back to place, task, cost, body.

"All right," he said, more to the paper than the room. "Then tell me how far each one may go."

That changed the table.

Junie sat. Mara loosened the blue thread from her finger and laid it beside the page. Inez pulled another spoiled proof from Damaris's bundle and flattened it with both palms. Even Damaris stopped performing amusement long enough to think like a woman who lived by routes.

They began again, and now the names came not as trophies but as permissions.

Some were for public use if the district stayed attached: potgreen for kitchens, stepblue for slick landings, washcool for fever cloths, proof-pale for pressrooms. Some were public only in part: a dock term for fog ropes that Junie said could travel if Ferrymouth stayed on the line beneath it and nobody pretended it worked indoors. Some were not for public walls at all, only for mouth and house: a nursery light from Lantern Steps Mara would describe but not surrender, because one lodger's twins had lived by it through a winter cough and she would not see it turned into fashion copy. Inez gave uses without certain deeper ledger names. Aunt Vero named categories of obligation before she named anything else. Every time Silas asked the wrong question, the room corrected him toward the right one, and the same set of questions kept returning: where, for what, unsafe for whom, who might pass it on, and what got sent back.

By the time they filled three sheets, the table was a mess of onion skins, pencil stubs, grease fingerprints, and an order of knowledge he had never once served in his professional life. He had made a career stripping words down till they flashed. Here they thickened under attention instead. The stories stayed with them like mud on boots.

And the strangest thing was that the work did not feel diminished by that. It felt more exact. Harder to sell, certainly. Harder to own. But alive in a way his neatest copy had not.

***

Near midnight the kitchen changed weather. The rush heat had gone out of the stove. River damp came through the cracked window over the sink. Otis fell asleep with his cheek against folded arms and one hand still sticky from plantain sugar. Junie tucked a dish towel under his face without waking him. Outside, Ferrymouth had thinned to late freight and men too broke to go home yet.

The papers covered most of the table now, full of names, uses, warnings, permissions, and return paths. The mess of it pleased Silas in flashes and scared him in others.

Damaris stood over the pile with both hands on her hips. "If we put all this in the street raw, Gideon'll call it proof the wards haven't got a thought between them. He'll say only one man in one clean waistcoat can make order from the swamp."

"He says that already," Junie said, scraping char from the bottom of the rice pan into a tin for tomorrow's stock. "Question is whether people hear him over breakfast."

"They hear what fits in the mouth," Mara said. "Nobody on Lantern Steps is stopping on the fourth landing to read a sermon while carrying coal."

"Nobody at Ferrymouth is reading delicate print through steam," Junie said. "If a board wants the dock, it needs words a man can catch while blowing on stew."

Inez had taken one of the cleaner proofs and started sorting the names into rough groups with the blunt end of the pencil: kitchen, stairs, wash, press, dock, wake. "Then don't ask one board to do every job," she said.

Silas looked up. "No?"

"No. That's his disease." She nodded toward the city beyond the wall, where Gideon existed like a well-paid rash. "One surface, one slogan, one answer for everybody. We don't beat that by making our own prettier version."

"We also don't beat it by hanging confusion till the whole town trips over it," Damaris said.

Aunt Vero, who had been at the sink rinsing out the okra jar with enormous seriousness, snorted. "You all talk as if the city isn't already confusion. It manages."

Junie pointed the spoon at her. "If you've got something better than insults, now's the hour."

Vero set the jar upside down to dry and wiped her hands on her skirt. When she came back to the table, she did not look at the papers first. She looked at the stove, the shelves, the cooling crock of beans, the lamp tray by the window where a thin strip of moss glowed the kitchen's chosen green. She thought with objects, Silas had learned. With what could break, spoil, scorch, or keep.

"When blight comes for a grow room," she said, "only a fool bets the season on one mother tray."

No one interrupted. They all knew she hated being hurried to wisdom. It made her meaner and less useful.

"You split the line," she went on. "One tray under burlap. One in the widow house. One at the wash room if the pipes are still kind. One with your cousin who forgets the saints but never forgets water day. Redundancy. A nasty ugly word. Keeps children lit." She pointed at the papers with a wet finger. "Do that with the names."

Junie frowned. "Spread them where?"

"Where mouths already queue," Vero said at once. "Ferries. Stoops. Wash lines. Market rails. Not one grand wall for rich men to stare at. Many surfaces, many heights, many chores. Let every board carry several names with the place and the use. Let women read them while pinning shirts. Let boys mouth them on the way to school. Let deckhands swear them over ropes. If one gets torn down, five more are already damp somewhere else."

Silas felt the shape of it before the words finished landing. It was not a poster or a launch. It was a network of practical invitations, with surfaces built to keep language plural on purpose.

"A chorus board," Aunt Vero said, as if she had only just now heard the name herself and approved its nuisance. "That's what it wants. Not a sermon board. Not a patent placard. A chorus board. Many mouths on one face, many faces through the city."

Otis, half asleep, murmured into the dish towel, "Chorus whistles less."

Junie smiled despite herself. "Go back to sleep."

Damaris was already moving. She took a pencil and drew rough boxes on a fresh sheet. "If it's a chorus board, it can't look like a ledger. People won't stop for accounting unless a sheriff's involved. It needs a handhold. Big route word at top. Then uses beneath. Maybe district marked in the margin. Something a newsboy can cry without becoming literature."

"And something rain won't eat before dawn," Mara said. "On Lantern Steps we'd need waxed edges or cloth ties. Paper alone goes to mush by the second mist."

"At Ferrymouth we can back it with fish-crate slats till we get proper wood," Junie said. "Not yet. For now I can hang proofs in the kitchen window and by the ticket rail. Men waiting on stew read anything if the pot's delayed."

Inez looked at the grouped names. "Not all of them. Only what the keepers release. District on the board. Use on the board. Warning on the board. If a name stays local, it stays local. We're not solving theft by doing theft with friendlier faces."

"Fine," Damaris said. "So every board gets three marks. Public. Local only. Ask first."

"Four," Silas said before he had thought through whether he had earned the right to add anything. Everybody looked at him, and he had the old nauseating urge either to flourish or to apologize. He chose neither. "Public, local, ask first, and return owed. If somebody borrows a name, the board ought to say what travels back with it."

Aunt Vero's expression did not soften, but it altered. "At last."

Junie nodded once. "Good. Because if Ferrymouth borrows a wash name, Ferrymouth can send soap. If Sump Ward borrows kitchen stock, Sump Ward can ask for rice. People remember what costs them."

"And what binds them," Inez said.

That settled more than one argument at once. Silas could feel it. The tactic stopped being a cloud of good intentions and turned under their hands into something rude enough to live. It was not unity. It was infrastructure.

He pulled one of the cleaner sheets toward him. Every instinct in his body wanted to center it, crown it, make the eye obey. Instead he drew no middle line. He marked a header strip for route and place, then left three broad bands beneath for names tied to task. Margins for warnings. A corner square for what was owed back. The design looked almost unfinished, all that deliberate room.

Inez watched the pencil move. "Leave space for changes."

"I was going to."

"No, you weren't."

He almost laughed. "I was going to after you said it."

Mara leaned over his shoulder, close enough that the starch of her sleeve brushed his arm. "Step numbers there," she said, tapping the margin. "Big. None of this tiny sign-painter vanity."

"I heard you the first time."

"Then I'll say it twice so it sticks."

Damaris pointed with her peppermint tin. "Route word at the top wants to be something nobody can mistake at three paces. For Stairs. For Pots. For Wash. If you get fancy, I'll hit you with journalism."

"That would be a first for the profession," Junie said.

Silas altered the sketch. The route words grew big, practical, and nearly blunt. The result was not beautiful in the old sense. Or rather, it was beautiful in a new mean way, with beauty measured by whether a woman with two buckets and no spare breath would catch the point on the move.

The lamp by the window had begun to gutter. Junie rose, checked the tray, and clicked her tongue. "It's hungry."

There was enough moss in the little side tray to test, if not prove. A strip no longer than two fingers, grown from kitchen stock and tempered to Junie's routines. Not private enough to offend, not public enough to matter much. Still, the sight of it shining there while the papers lay spread made the room lean toward experiment without admitting it was doing so.

Damaris saw the same thought. "Shall we let the city eavesdrop?"

Junie looked at Inez. Inez looked at Vero. Vero made a vague circling motion that meant yes if no one got stupid.

Junie took the draft sheet Silas had marked and pinned it in the open window with two wooden clothespins. The night air sucked at the paper. Outside, the ticket rail was nearly empty, but not fully. A pair of freight men lingered over beer by the post. Somebody farther down the slip was singing half a verse and getting the rest wrong.

"Not a demonstration," Inez said.

"No," Junie said. "Just reading."

She handed the sheet to Otis when he woke enough to blink and sit up. He peered at the block letters, sounding them first with his finger.

"For Pots," he read.

"Louder," Damaris said automatically.

Junie shot her a look. "Not like you're selling scandal. Like you're calling dinner."

Otis cleared his little chest. "For Pots. Potgreen. Ferrymouth kitchens. Long simmer. Not for parlors. Rice owed back."

One of the freight men outside said, "Rice owed back?" and laughed.

"That's right," Junie called through the window. "You borrow, you carry."

Otis read the next line slower. "For Stairs. Stepblue. Lantern Steps. Wet treads. Keep stair number with it."

"Use the number," Mara said. "Say the number."

Silas wrote Sixth Landing in the margin. Otis read that too. One of the men outside repeated it, not because he cared about Lantern Steps but because words on a board asked to be tried in the mouth. From farther down the slip somebody called, "What's stepblue?" and Mara, startled into ownership, answered before she could decide whether to.

"The thing that keeps you from breaking your neck if you've got any sense."

They moved through three more lines: Washcool, Proof-pale, and widow basil only as a category and not the whole obligation, because Vero had not released all of that one and nobody argued with her on it. Damaris gave the route words punch where they lagged. Junie corrected cadence whenever it turned theatrical. Inez cut anything that sounded like display. Silas wrote fast in the margins when the room sharpened a phrase by common use instead of individual brilliance.

The strip of moss in the tray did not leap. Private reading alone would not have done much. But this was not private now. The window stood open, dock traffic was hearing and repeating, and the sheet sat in public sight. Several mouths took it up along several routes, and no single chant ruled them. After the third pass, the green in the tray loosened at one edge. A cool blue seam appeared, then held. Near the center a paler wash rose and did not take over. The old kitchen green stayed where it had work to do.

Nobody cheered. That was not the mood of the room, and Junie's kitchen would have rejected cheering as vulgar optimism. They just watched the colors keep one another company without surrendering to a winner.

"Ugly," Damaris said softly, almost admiring.

"Useful," Inez said.

"Both," Junie said.

Aunt Vero folded her arms. "That's a city, then."

Silas looked at the draft in the window, the block letters thick from his hand, the margins already crowded with other people's corrections. There was nowhere on it for a signature. The emptiness where his name would once have gone no longer looked like loss. It looked like structure. The thing held because it did not have a center greedy enough to eat the rest.

Mara gathered her shawl. "One bowl," she said. "And now half the night. You're all thieves."

"Stay for cake," Junie said.

Mara hesitated just long enough to indict herself. "A small piece."

Damaris laughed through her nose. "Coalition achieved."

"Don't call it that in the paper," Junie said.

"I won't call it anything till we know which parts survive daylight."

That, too, felt correct. Nothing in the room claimed victory. They were not fools. Gideon still had money, walls, songs, debt papers, runners. But the shape on the table was no rival brand. It was a method that behaved like the people it came from: multiple, rude, practical, hard to evict all at once.

Junie cut the cake. Inez took the draft sheet down from the window before the damp could curl it useless. Aunt Vero began listing which wash lines in Sump Ward saw the most dawn traffic and which ones belonged to women who would rather bite through tin than help Patent Row by accident. Damaris sorted spoiled proofs by size and muttered about type weight. Mara counted stair landings on her fingers and argued about where to post first. Otis, fully awake now, asked whether chorus boards could whistle if the slats were thin enough. Junie said everything in this city whistled if the wind found the right crack.

Silas put a new sheet on the table and drew columns without being told.


# Chapter 13

By noon the regatta had made the river smell expensive. Someone up on Gilt Crescent had dumped soap, lemon peel, and brass polish over the usual breath of warm mud and algae mash, and the whole waterfront wore the effort badly. Under it all the Sallow still stank of sweet rot. The launches rocked against their ropes with garlands of cut glass and paper pennants, and every pennant carried the same pale green letters: CELADON CROWN. The words were printed so many times they looked less like language than wallpaper for a rich man's delusion.

Silas stood on the landing reserved for honored guests and felt his collar drinking him. Gideon's tailor had sent over a cream summer coat that fit across the shoulders and nowhere else, and the cloth held heat like spite. His right wrist, still not right since the trough, ached inside the cuff whenever he flexed his hand. Below the rail the quay churned with ferry crews, shop girls, buyers from the ridge, newsboys with bundles under their arms, and the sort of men who came to any public arrangement in hopes that free liquor might fall out of it. Brass from the municipal band skimmed over the water. Somewhere a barge choir was testing the first lines of the regatta hymn. Even the gulls sounded instructed.

He ought to have laughed. A month ago he would have laughed until his ribs hurt. Gideon had turned a living civic crisis into a floating advertisement and dressed it up like a bride from a warehouse catalogue. But laughter needed looseness, and Silas had come tight with purpose. He had a folded scrap in his inner pocket with three names and three uses in Inez's hand, not because he meant to steal them again, but because they reminded him what a name was for when it was not being sold by the yard. If he got the horn, if he got even half a clean breath inside Gideon's ceremony, he meant to spoil the neatness of it. Muddy the rhythm. Make the crowd answer back. Give the city room to remember its own mouth.

He went down the plank to the lower quay where the working vendors had been shoved under the ornamental bunting. Junie's scull sat wedged between a champagne boat for the ridge people and a fish seller who had tried to disguise his smell with mint. She had rigged a brass kettle over a little dock stove and was moving like six hands belonged to her by right: ladling broth, slicing bread, taking coins, knocking away a gull with the same spoon she was serving from. Flour streaked her forearm. The old split in her left eyebrow showed white whenever she frowned, which was most of the time today.

"You look like a corpse in pastry," she said without looking up.

"Good noon to you too."

"Lift that basket, then talk."

He lifted the basket of eel pies with his left hand. The rope handle bit into his palm. The stove under the kettle burned a dogged workman's green, but the flame sat lower than it ought to, breathing thinly around the iron plate.

"Pell shorted you," he said.

Junie snorted. "Pell sent half a barrel and a boy who kept squinting at my ledger like he meant to marry it. I've got enough mash till midafternoon if I stretch the broth and tell the crowd they're tasting elegance." She took the basket from him, counted under her breath, then looked up. "You eat?"

"Later."

"That's stupid. Eat now or you'll say something clever on an empty belly and get us all killed."

He nearly told her then, Tell me exactly how short, tell me what he's said, tell me if anybody's been sniffing round the stove room. But the quay swelled with customers and she had no spare surface on her body for his panic. A deckhand was already shoving coppers at her. She bent to him with a grin that was half hospitality, half threat.

Silas leaned closer. "If it turns mean up there, pack early."

"Turns mean? It's a river full of investors. It started mean." She tore a heel of bread off a loaf and jammed it into his hand. "And keep your mouth dirty. Clean speech is how those bastards win."

That was Junie: affection delivered as kitchen doctrine. He took the bread. It was still warm in the middle. He chewed and watched her feed the next customer. Each ladle came out a little more careful than the one before. Fuel arithmetic. Soup as counting.

When he straightened, he found Inez on the plank walk above him, one hand on the wet rail. Her dark scarf had been tied back tight against the river wind, but damp had already darkened the cloth at her temple. The crescent burn on the inside of her right wrist flashed pale where she gripped the rail. She had put on a clean blouse for the day and ruined its neatness within minutes by leaning against tarred wood. That did something low and stupid in him even now.

She came down only far enough to keep clear of the crowd pressing Junie's scull. "How's the flame?"

"Hungry," Junie said, before he could answer. "Like everybody else in this city."

Inez looked at the stove, then at Silas. Her voice stayed public and spare. "If the regatta catches, every kitchen in Ferrymouth will look like that by tomorrow."

"It won't catch if I can help it," he said.

She watched him another moment. In trust or bed she let warmth into the sentence. Here she did not. "Don't let him have a clean line. They've built this thing on rhythm. Break the rhythm and it stumbles."

He touched the inside pocket where her folded scrap sat against his ribs. "I know what to do."

"Do you?" There was no sneer in it, only workman's doubt. "Silas, if you get the horn, don't perform at it. Make it ugly. Make them argue."

Junie shoved a bowl at a customer and muttered, "For once in his life, that's sound advice."

The corner of Inez's mouth moved, not quite a smile. It hurt him more than if she had given him the real one. They had not had a private room since the supper. They had had strategy, quick touches in doorways, her knee against his under Junie's table when people were shouting over one another. Enough to keep him lit, not enough to quiet him. He wanted five minutes alone with her. He wanted a month. He got the river, the crowd, and Gideon's banners.

A widow-black figure elbowed through the newsboys and stopped under the bunting. Damaris Pike had come dressed for respectable mourning and profitable scandal in the same outfit as usual; her sleeves shone at the seams, and the chipped edge of her front tooth caught when she smiled up at him.

"Patent Row's saint of bad decisions," she said. "You do scrub up like a fraud promoted against nature."

"Mrs. Pike, if you've come to pray, aim elsewhere."

"I never pray before press time." She flicked a thumb at the barges. "He's got song sheets, Vale. Printed refrains. He's paying children to rhyme for him. If you mean to ruin the day, do it before the second chorus. After that it'll belong to the ferries and the schoolyards."

Inez's gaze sharpened. "How many barges?"

"Three I saw, maybe four if he's hidden one round the bend. The one with the choir master has a walrus mustache and a left boot that squeaks on every downbeat. He is trying to look important enough to ignore the rash chewing his neck." Damaris lowered her voice. "And Gideon's got a private tent up top. He sent for you two minutes ago."

"Me?" Silas said.

"You unless there's another overpromised sign painter in a cream coat I haven't been told about."

Junie clicked her tongue. "Go on then. And if he feeds you sugar water, spit in it."

Silas started up the plank. Inez caught his sleeve before he could climb past her. Her fingers closed just above his wrist, careful of the bad joint, and for half a second the whole quay narrowed to that pressure.

"Dirty," she said.

"Dirty," he echoed.

He went because he had been summoned like staff and because not going would have given Gideon the pleasure of sending two men to fetch him. The private tent stood behind the main reviewing dais, its striped canvas pegged above a stretch of dock that had been scrubbed so hard it smelled of wet hemp and lye. Inside, everything had been arranged to convince money it was attending nature without touching mud: polished crates draped in linen, a silver bucket sunk in river ice, bowls of sugared peel sweating into their own shine.

Gideon Wren stood at the folding table with his gloves off, which somehow made him look more clothed. His cuffs stayed unnaturally white in a city that stained anything it could reach. He was aligning a small stack of papers with the side of his thumb, tapping them flush as if their obedience mattered. When he looked up, his face held the public version of pleasure, all courtesy and no warmth.

"Mr. Vale. Thank you."

Silas shut the tent flap behind him. "If this is about the coat, it pinches."

"I can bear that disappointment." Gideon rested his fingertips on the papers. "We're late enough that I should be direct."

"You manage direct the way other men manage embalming."

Gideon let the line fall. That was one of his nastier talents, deciding in real time which insult deserved oxygen. "There are twenty-three vendors on the lower quay, seven hired song leads, four river launches, and a paid chorus of children waiting for a cue. I need one thing from you. The delivery must be clean."

Silas leaned one hip against the table because standing straight made him feel like a defendant. "I'm under no illusion about what you need from me."

"No," Gideon said. "You are under several more childish illusions, but not that one."

He slid the paper stack across the table with two fingers. They were not regatta programs. They were stained account notes, folded and refolded until the creases looked greasy. One carried the half-moon print of a thumb browned by broth. Another had a streak of flour ground into the corner. Junie's hand was all through them, blunt numbers marching down cheap paper, dates cramped to save space. At the top of the oldest note, Aram Pell's office stamp bled crooked blue ink through the fibers.

Silas did not pick them up. He knew them anyway. He had seen enough of Junie's scraps over the years to know the pressure of her pencil, the little hard kick on every seven. His mouth filled with the taste of the bread she had just handed him.

"What's this?" he said, though he knew.

"Your hinge," Gideon said.

Silas looked at him. "You bought her fuel paper."

"I bought arrears attached to a dependable appetite. Ferrymouth cooks regardless of fashion. It is one of the district's better habits."

Silas's hand closed around the edge of the table. His wrist answered with a hot, warning pull. "You couldn't make me dance, so you went after my sister's stove."

"I went after the mechanism that reliably moves you." Gideon's voice stayed almost gentle. "Please do not flatter either of us by calling it melodrama. This is accountancy."

He separated one note from the stack and set it forward. "Twenty-nine crowns, plus continuation on delivery. Pell was prepared to tighten her line this week even without me. I merely simplified the question. If you step to that dais and give the name clearly, her credit stands through the end of regatta season. The next barrels are released. Her kitchen keeps heat."

"And if I don't?"

Gideon met his eyes. "Then by evening the stove room at Ferrymouth is padlocked on arrears, her current mash is seized against the note, and every supplier I have touched this month declines further extension. She will not go under because she lacks courage, Mr. Vale. She will go under because soup cools."

For a moment all Silas could hear was the choir outside running scales on some bright civic lie. Soup cools. The bastard had found the ugliest possible phrase because it was true. Not rent, not bankruptcy, not a number on a page. Soup cooling in a dock kitchen while men with twelve-minute dinner breaks swore and walked elsewhere. Junie explaining to Otis why the stove stood dark.

"You're filth."

Gideon's expression altered by less than a teaspoon. "I am efficient. Had you been a solitary man, I would have no use for this."

Silas looked back down at the notes. One line item had been marked overdue in somebody else's neat clerk hand. Lamp mash, second delivery. Kitchen fuel. Emergency barrel. Every stroke smelled faintly of Junie's life, broth steam and cheap soap and the damp cupboard she kept her papers in above the spice tin. Domesticity laid out for leverage like exposed wiring.

"You could've come to me," Silas said. "You could've threatened my face, my hand, my contract. You didn't need to put your glove in her stock pot."

"I already came to you," Gideon said. "You embarrassed me in public, delayed me in private, and let yourself be educated into moral complication. That avenue has been tested."

Silas wanted to hit him. The desire arrived clean and stupid and almost relieving. He pictured Gideon's jaw breaking under the silver bucket. He pictured those perfect cuffs spotted red at last. But even if his wrist had been whole, which it was not, and even if the tent held no witnesses, which it did in all but flesh, hitting him would only have made the problem more physical and therefore simpler for Gideon. Men like Gideon loved simple.

"What exactly do you want said?"

Now Gideon smiled, very slightly. "There he is."

Silas felt the smile like spit.

"You will wait through the first hymn," Gideon said. "You will take the speaking horn from me and say, 'Nacre Bend, behold Celadon Crown.' Then you will repeat the name once more, without ornament, and hand the horn back. The choir has its answer. The vendors have their cry. The boys have their sheets. Clarity will do the rest."

Silas laughed once. It came out ugly. "You hired me for my mouth and now you want a clerk."

"I want authority, not style. Today even your vulgarity is only useful if it points in one direction."

Silas stared at the paper until the numbers blurred. In his inner pocket Inez's folded scrap pressed hot against his ribs. Dirty, she had said. Make it ugly. They had a plan thin as wire and maybe as useful. He could still do it, perhaps. He could step up and give the crowd five names instead of one, let the barges fight over echoes, turn Gideon's own saturation against him.

And Junie's stove room would be locked by dusk.

He heard himself ask, because a cornered man always wanted one last useless measurement, "If I do it, you keep your hands off her notes after?"

"I keep them solvent through the season," Gideon said. "I am not in the charity trade. But I remove the immediate blade. That is what you are buying."

Buying. With what? With half the city's light, with the ward names already buried under print, with Inez's face when she understood. He knew the price so well he could have painted it on a wall.

Gideon picked up his gloves, tugged them on finger by finger, and that was somehow worse than the papers had been. "We are done."

Silas did not move.

Gideon adjusted one cuff. "Mr. Vale."

Silas slid the notes back across the table because if he kept them in his hand he might tear them, and that would help no one. "If your boat sinks today," he said, "I'll watch."

"I am sure you will."

Outside, the band struck the opening march. The canvas walls shivered with applause.

Silas stepped back into the daylight like a man walking out of a confession booth to discover the church was built over a slaughterhouse. The quay had thickened. Buyers in summer linen leaned over the rails of hired launches. Ferry crews jammed shoulder to shoulder with wives in their better hats. Newsboys were already chanting the printed chorus under their breath to learn it fast. On the lower scull Junie had tied her sleeves higher and was moving even quicker now, the kettle burbling thinly. She glanced up at him once, saw something bad in his face, and set her jaw without asking in front of customers.

Inez waited at the foot of the dais steps. She had made herself a still point in the shove of bodies, which only made him feel his own shake more keenly.

"What did he want?" she asked.

He could have told her. That was the moment. He could have put the whole rotten thing in her hands as well as his own and let them decide it together, even if together meant running to Junie and then to Pell's office and then nowhere clean. He could have chosen not to choose alone. Instead he saw Junie's fuel notes on Gideon's table, flour-streaked and helpless, and he did what he had done too often in his life when shame came close: he tightened around it and called that control.

"He wanted the speech neat," he said.

Inez watched him long enough for the lie to become a physical thing between them. "And?"

"And I told him he'd have to work for it."

"That's not an answer."

"No," he said. "It's what I've got."

Her eyes went flatter. Not cold. Working. That was worse. "If you've changed the plan, tell me now."

He looked past her to the water. A barge of schoolchildren in white collars was taking position, each one holding a paper lantern cup with a nursery patch glowing faint green inside it. One little girl at the end of the row kept smoothing the ribbon on her own cuff with the serious concentration of a child trying to be elegant on command. A river hand on the next launch was picking black grit from under his nails with a bait hook while reading the printed refrain upside down. The whole city built from little human habits ripe for theft.

"I haven't changed it," he said.

It became true only in the meanest sense. He had not changed the plan. He had simply already begun betraying it.

The dais boards thudded under the weight of officials taking their places. Gideon mounted the center platform between two municipal men who looked relieved not to be the most hated figures in sight. Behind them, the regatta launches spread over the water in a fan: polished hulls, moss lanterns caged in cut glass, white streamers dragging their damp fringes across the river breeze. Every visible surface had words on it. Programs, pennants, crate labels, placards hung from oars. The printed phrase existed everywhere already, a city waiting with its mouth open. All it lacked was the moment somebody authoritative gave it breath.

Damaris had found a perch on a piling stub near the newsboys. She had her notebook open but was not writing. He noticed that because Damaris always wrote when frightened until the fear got too near the bone, and then the pencil stopped.

Gideon raised both hands. The band cut cleanly. The sudden absence of brass made the river noises come up hard: rope creak, gull cry, the slap of wash against pilings, Junie's ladle ringing the kettle rim below.

"Ladies and gentlemen," Gideon said, and his voice carried without strain. Of course it did. Everything about him had been designed to carry. "Nacre Bend has spent too many months at the mercy of accident. A city's light should not depend on rumor, whim, or neighborhood quarrel."

A few boos from the dockside. A few laughs from the ridge boats. Gideon let them pass through him like weather measured and priced.

"Today," he said, "we offer not novelty, but steadiness. Not confusion, but placement. A name you can ask for and receive. A name that travels from quay to kitchen, from parlor to ferry, without degradation."

Silas almost barked at that. Without degradation. The whole damned city was built on degradation properly distributed. But the crowd wanted to hear what came next. He could feel want moving through them like heat over marsh water. Buyers wanted certainty. Workers wanted the fuss to mean something besides another rich man's party. Children wanted a phrase bright enough to take home. Even people who hated Gideon wanted to know what shape the trap had this time.

The first hymn began from the choir barges. High voices, trained to a public sweetness that made the words feel clean enough to swallow. Silas caught fragments: crown of evening, crown of tide, crown of every window. He could have torn the lyricist's throat with his teeth. On the lower quay vendors took up the printed cry when the refrain marked it. "Celadon..." one called experimentally, not yet committed. The boys echoed from habit, practice, greed. A name half born, waiting for a midwife.

Silas stood three men down from Gideon, among aldermen, patrons, and one woman from the river commission whose feathered hat made her look like a furious heron. He should not have been the shabbiest soul on the platform in that coat, but he was by virtue of knowing what it had cost to put him there. His wrist pulsed. His inner pocket burned with Inez's folded scrap. Twice the crowd below spotted him and shouted something between a cheer and a heckle.

"Name us a better one, Vale!"

"Don't fall in the trough this time!"

"Paint us a miracle!"

He gave them his old grin once, purely by reflex, and heard how wrong it sounded coming out of him today. The grin got a laugh anyway. He had built too much of his life on those reflexes. That, more than Gideon's papers, might have been the real machinery.

The hymn ended. Applause moved across the water in staggered sheets. Gideon turned to him with the speaking horn in his gloved hand. It was not polished silver as Silas had expected. It was tin painted cream, practical under the ceremony, and the lip was wet from the last man's breath.

"Mr. Vale," Gideon said, just loudly enough for the near rows to hear. "If you would."

Humiliation had a way of clarifying edges. Silas saw the crease beside Gideon's mouth where satisfaction lived. He saw the council heron's annoyance that a tradesman would share the platform. He saw Junie below, arm-deep in bread crate, listening without ever looking up. He saw Inez by the rail, not moving at all now. He saw the schoolchild with the ribbon cuff mouthing along with the printed blank where the chosen name belonged. He saw Damaris holding still enough to count as prayer.

He could still wreck it.

That thought came with such force he nearly smiled for real. Of course he could. He was Silas Vale. He had made and unmade crowds for less than this. He could take the horn, turn Gideon's clean river pageant into a market brawl, crack the rhythm open, hand the thing back in pieces. Junie's stove room be damned?

No. Not damned. That was the problem. Not an abstraction. Not a noble sacrifice performed over somebody else's supper. Junie's stove room. Junie's kettle. Junie's hand on the ladle. Otis coming home to a dark kitchen and asking what broke. He had spent weeks learning that names landed on bodies and budgets, on women with blisters and men with twelve-minute meal breaks. Now the lesson had turned and bared its teeth at his own door.

He took the horn.

The tin was warm, slick with old spit and river damp. It pulled at his wrist. He stepped to the front rail. The crowd leaned in with one body. Even the launches seemed to hinge toward him.

He began where he had meant to begin. "Nacre Bend," he said, and the old public ease came back enough to carry the name of the city without a crack. "You've called this poor shining nuisance half the filthiest, prettiest things I've ever heard."

Laughter. Good. He had them.

"Patent Row called it one thing to sell pomade. Ferrymouth called it another when it fouled a stove. Sump Ward's had names for what it does when a child touches the hot side and when an old woman needs the cool side and when the wash line wants light but not blaze."

A murmur now, less easy. He felt movement at the edge of the crowd where Inez's people had scattered themselves. Ready. If he kept going, if he made the next sentence dirty enough, maybe the barges would miss their cue and the crowd would start answering in pieces. He could feel the opening. It was there. An actual live seam.

Then he looked down.

Not at Gideon. Not first. At the lower quay, where one of Pell's clerks had appeared beside Junie's scull with a leather satchel under his arm. He was young, maybe nineteen, with ears that stuck out and a fresh shaving nick still holding a bead of blood under his jaw. He was not evil. That was part of the obscenity. He looked like a boy sent to fetch butter. In one hand he carried a folded notice and a little brass padlock that knocked softly against his knuckles whenever he shifted. Junie, still holding her ladle, was talking at him through her teeth while serving with the other hand because custom did not stop for blackmail. The clerk glanced up toward the dais when he felt the crowd shift, waiting, obviously waiting, on whatever word came next.

Gideon had timed it to the breath.

Silas could have kept wrecking the sentence. He knew that. Nobody forced his jaw. Nobody bent his spine. He made the calculation himself with full sight of the sewer it ran through. If he tore the ceremony open now, Junie paid first. Not eventually. Before dark. Before her kettle cooled.

He hated that clarity. He hated himself for wanting it. A blur would have let him call it fate. This did not.

His next breath hurt on the way in. He heard Inez say dirty in the back of his skull. He heard Junie say keep your mouth dirty. He heard Gideon say soup cools. All the voices in the city reduced to three and one of them owned paper.

He made his mouth clean.

"The name," he said, and the river held still enough to hear it, "is Celadon Crown."

For one holy fraction of a second nothing happened. Silence sat there, shocked by its own obedience.

Then the choir answered exactly as paid. "Celadon Crown," the children sang, bright as cut glass.

The vendors caught it next because money trained faster than conscience. "Celadon Crown! Fresh cuttings! Celadon Crown!"

The newsboys shouted it because shouting was their trade and a new two-beat cry was meat. Ferrymen took it up to mock the ridge accents and found it sat just as well in a cracked mouth as a polished one. The little girl with the ribbon cuff lifted her chin to shape the second word. Three drunks on the public steps bawled it wrong, then corrected themselves because the crowd was already teaching them.

Silas could have dropped the horn. Instead, because cowardice liked completion, because half a betrayal still left room for hope, because Gideon's instruction had been precise and he had agreed to it in the tent, he said it again.

"Celadon Crown."

This time the name went through the regatta like a hook pulled under skin.
