You could hear the fit of it. Cel-a-don, tongue working against the teeth, a small show of refinement even from men without any. Crown, chin lifting on the round. The phrase adjusted bodies as it moved. Backs straightened. Mouths pursed pretty for the first half and opened royal for the second. Housewives on the quay tried it with broth ladles in hand. A coal heaver grinned because it made him sound expensive. A child said it purely for the pleasure of how the last word sat on air. The barges repeated. The printed sheets suddenly matched what people were already saying. Every channel Gideon had paid for locked at once.

Across the water the moss answered.

The lantern cups on the school barge blushed out of their work green into a pale, lacquered gold that looked rich enough to hang round a duchess's throat and useful enough to betray a kitchen. Ropes furred with living glow took the same color in streaks, then full. The garland on the lead launch brightened and thinned, shedding its old earthy heat for a prettier, colder burn. People gasped because beauty always got there first. That was another of the city's bad habits.

Down below, Junie's stove flame changed.

It happened at the edge first: the green going blond, the steady blue belly of heat lifting out and away. Then the whole fire sharpened into a pale, expensive gold that threw a lovely light and a mean boil. The broth stopped its heavy dockside roll. Junie saw it in the same instant Inez did. She swore, snatched the kettle half aside, and the ladle clanged overboard.

Inez moved before the splash finished.

She did not look up at him first. That landed harder than any stare could have. She vaulted the lower rail, boots finding the mooring post and then the scull deck with the easy exactness of somebody born to wet wood. Her clean blouse took a stripe of soot at once. She grabbed the stove valve bare-handed, flinched only when the crescent scar on her wrist kissed the hot metal, and snapped, "Not that air. Starve it. Junie, feed the dark mash, not the bright."

Junie was already hauling up the backup tin from under the bench, cursing so fast the words ran together. "I know what a starving flame looks like, woman."

"I know. Do it anyway."

The boy clerk with Pell's satchel had backed off two steps from the sudden labor, the notice still pinched in his hand, the little brass padlock knocking against his knee. He looked as if he had expected paperwork and found a kitchen emergency instead. Junie saw the padlock, saw Inez see it, and then all three of them looked up to the dais together.

That was when Inez finally found Silas's face.

He had wanted anger. Anger had room in it for the body, for a quarrel, for later. What she gave him was recognition so complete it felt like the removing of a bandage. She saw the clean line he had given them. She saw the clerk. She saw the stove turning decorative under her hands. Whatever still lived between them had to share air with that.

He tried to speak. Her name maybe. An explanation. The truth at last, too late and public.

She did not let him waste the breath. "Don't," she said, and even across the waterlogged noise of the quay the word came to him hard and flat. "I haven't got time to hear how you meant it."

That was the first blade.

Then, because Junie was wrestling the mash tin open and the crowd kept crying the new name above them like gulls over offal, Inez turned from him to the people who could still be helped. She shoved the clerk back by the chest with her soot-blackened forearm and said, "If you're waiting to close a stove, wait till she's fed the line you're profiting from." Then to Junie: "Keep your own books dry. I'll get the ward women off the free cuttings before they bring this shit home."

Junie, flushed and furious, looked from the padlock to Silas and understood enough to go white under the heat. She did not accuse him. That might have been mercy or simple lack of time. "If you've bought me dear," she shouted up, "you'd better pray I can spend it."

Then Inez was gone into the crowd, moving low and fast along the vendor line, knocking branded nursery cups out of hands, shouting practical warnings at any woman who looked as if she might actually listen. He heard pieces.

"Not for stoves."

"Keep the old trays separate."

"Don't mix it with kitchen stock."

There it was, not wounded pride but triage. Aunt Vero had to be somewhere farther back because suddenly another older voice was answering in rougher terms about pretty light and useless soup. A ripple of argument started at the edge of the quay where the working people stood. Too late to stop the catch. In time, maybe, to slow the burn. That was what Inez had gone to do instead of looking at him.

Gideon's gloved hand settled on Silas's shoulder with the intimacy of a hand on a coffin lid. The applause had begun, ridiculous and sincere in equal measure. People loved to clap for the completion of a thing, even if the thing was their own collar being fitted.

"Well done," Gideon said.

Silas shrugged him off so hard his bad wrist flashed white pain to the elbow. The horn nearly slipped. He shoved it back into Gideon's chest and came away with cream paint on his palm. Somebody on the launch below called for the name again, wanting to hear it repeated as if repetition itself were a blessing. The choir obliged. The newsboys obliged. The vendors obliged because they could smell which way the crowd's hunger had turned.

Celadon Crown. Celadon Crown.

The words went outward over water and inward through the city at the same time. He could hear them landing in different mouths and changing shape only slightly, not enough to break. That was the real victory Gideon had bought: not purity, but resilience. A dockhand with broth on his cuff said it with mock grandeur and then again because he liked the feel. Two girls from Lantern Steps whispered it into each other's bonnets and laughed. A man on the ridge barge tried it in French, failed, and came back to the common version. The name had legs already.

On the lower quay Junie got her stove breathing darker again by force and foul language. The flame held, but sullenly, and not before the first row of paying customers had watched her fight it. The boy clerk stood useless as a nailed bird. Junie kicked her day's cash box farther under the bench where no clerk could lay a hand on it without losing one, then set about serving with a face that promised murder in several practical forms. She had been saved exactly as Gideon promised: not from pressure, only from immediate extinction. It was almost enough to make Silas retch.

Damaris finally moved her pencil. He saw her write one line, stop, tear the page out, and crush it in her fist. That, more than her notebook, told him what sort of day it had become.

The regatta launches had begun their ceremonial pass. Oars dipped. Lantern garlands threw pale gold across the chop. The river took the new color greedily, broke it, returned it in trembling lengths against the pilings. Everywhere the light touched skin, people admired themselves in it first. That too was human. That too was part of the problem.

Silas stood on the dais in the coat Gideon had hired for him and listened to his own betrayal go common. He had thought there might be some theatrical instant afterward when the world marked him. A gasp, a blow, a curse from heaven. What there was instead was traffic. Repetition. Commerce. The real machinery of disaster, ordinary as a lunch rush.

When he looked for Inez again, he found only the space she had left in the crowd and the movement spreading from it: women turning their branded cups over, a vendor being shouted down for claiming the new glow burned hotter, Junie barking for somebody to fetch more dark mash from the crate under the bench. Work had taken her where love could not afford to stand.

Below, one little boy no older than Otis tugged at his mother's sleeve and asked to say the name again because it made him feel grand. She let him. Why would she not? It was only a word in his mouth. Only a word in a hundred mouths. Only a word crossing from paper to throat to lamp to stove to rent.

Celadon Crown.

By the time the second launch cleared the quay, the name had already learned the city better than he ever had. It rode the band brass, the sales cries, the school chorus, the mutter between two women comparing the new color to cheap jewelry, the private try of a laborer shaping it against broken teeth. It sat in ears and changed how people held their chins. It got into the shoulders. It turned the moss on the mooring ropes pale gold and made the light look rich while the heat went mean. Standing there with the river throwing that pretty useless shine back at him, Silas could feel the whole town beginning to wear the word on its body.


# Chapter 14

By the third morning after the regatta, Ferrymouth glowed like a rich man's tooth.

The light in Junie's kitchen had gone pale gold in the night and stayed there, thin and flattering and useless. It made the steam above the stockpot look expensive. It made yesterday's grease on the stove lids shine like lacquer. It made the floorboards look drier than they were, which was the mean part. The boards still sweated river damp, and when Silas put his bad wrist against the prep table to lever himself up from the bench, his palm came away with flour pasted to it and a smear of yellowish slime from the lamp hook.

Junie had been up long enough for anger to settle into method. She stood over the range in her apron and underskirt, hair pinned high with a trussing skewer because she could not find the proper comb in the dark. Steam had dampened the little healed split in her eyebrow white. She kept lifting the lid off the stew, staring into it as if staring harder could make the boil come back.

"It won't hold," she said.

Silas knew she was not talking to him in the first instance. She was talking to the firebox, to the pale-gold cakes stacked in the coal cradle, to Gideon Wren somewhere up on clean Gilt Crescent with his gloves and his sermon voice and his purchased city. Even so, the sentence landed on Silas because most things did now.

He crossed to the stove. The heat coming off it was wrong. There was warmth, yes, enough to comfort the skin if all you wanted from a stove was an agreeable hand. It did not have the hard push of kitchen fuel. It did not get under the pot and commit.

Junie thumped the ladle against his knuckles when he reached for the fire door. "You'll spill it."

"I was looking."

"You can look with your eyes."

He looked. The branded fuel sat in a crusted nest behind the grate, not green as household mash should have been, not blue-white as the cooler strains sometimes burned, but a pretty waxy gold like sugar pulled too thin. The cakes blistered and collapsed quickly, leaving a lace of bright residue that hissed without doing much work. Somebody in a parlor might have admired the color under a shade. In a kitchen it meant the stock never climbed higher than a fretful burble. Fat clotted. Bones sulked. The onions took forever to surrender.

Otis sat at the table with his slate and his one good school copybook, not dressed for school. He had one suspender hanging and a dark dock stain on the knee of his trousers where it never came out, no matter how Junie scrubbed. He was tracing the loop of a capital C, tongue tucked in the gap where his front tooth used to be.

"You said I could go if the lower steps dried," he said without looking up.

"They didn't."

"Mick Harlan went."

"Mick Harlan has a father who'll carry him if he breaks his fool neck."

Otis erased too hard and tore the page. He did not cry. He only rubbed the place with his thumb as if paper might heal if you were polite. Silas watched him do it and had the ugly thought that even the child had learned not to expect repair from him before breakfast.

Junie jerked her chin at the ledger propped open by the bread bin. "Read me that line."

He moved closer. Her accounts were written in the blunt clear hand of someone who did not romanticize numbers. Yesterday's fuel. Today's fuel. Two columns, same supplier, different world.

"Seven coppers a scoop yesterday morning," he said.

"And?"

"Twelve."

"Say the rest."

There were four scoops marked before dawn and three more with question marks beside them.

"If the heat keeps dropping, you need seven before the late ferry."

"At twelve."

"I can multiply, June."

"Then do it where I can hear it."

He did. Twenty-four, thirty-six, forty-eight, sixty, seventy-two, eighty-four. The numbers made the room feel smaller than the walls. Junie took each one with a short nod as if he were naming parts cut off a carcass.

"That's fuel only," she said. "Not fish. Not onions. Not bread. Not rent. Not the note that bastard bought just so he could stick his hand up your back and move your mouth."

She said it evenly. That was worse than if she had shouted. Her voice had gone dock-flat, the voice she used when tallying spoilage after a bad weather run.

Silas leaned his hip against the counter and nearly went down because the boards under him had been slicked with condensed steam. He caught himself on the flour barrel. The barrel rocked. Junie slapped it steady with the side of her hand without even turning.

"I'll go to the yards," he said. "There has to be common mash somewhere."

"There isn't."

"Somebody's holding stock back."

"Of course somebody's holding stock back. There are women on the ridge paying parlor prices to make their supper lamps look clean. Meanwhile I've got a stove full of saint's piss and a pot of eel that won't remember it's dead unless I threaten it for another hour."

The joke would have drawn a snort from him last week. This morning it only made him stare at the stew. The eel rounds rolled slow in the weak boil, skin silvering then dimming under the gold light like coins in bad water.

A man rapped on the side door and came in before being asked, because ferry crews treated Junie's kitchen as weather, not property. Doss Mercer, who unloaded flour barges with one shoulder permanently higher than the other, ducked under the lintel and stopped short.

"Christ," he said. "Looks like somebody boiled a chandelier in here."

He had a clove tucked in one cheek for his breath. He worried it with his molars when he was tired, and today the clove had stained a wet brown crescent at the corner of his mouth.

Junie pointed the ladle at him. "If you've come to praise the look, I'll feed you outside."

"I came to eat."

"Then you'll wait."

Doss glanced at the stove, at the fuel cradle, then at Silas. He was not a subtle man, but misery had made subtle men of half the city in three days.

"Same at ours," he said. "Pitch won't stay soft at the caulking shed. Miko blistered his thumb leaning too close because the lamp looked hotter than it was. Then the tar skinned over cold as spit. Boss had us scraping it off plank by plank."

The caulking shed had gone bad with everything else, one more place where elegant light made stupid damage. The city had no other conversation left.

Junie held out a bowl without looking at Doss. "You want breakfast, you get yesterday's heel and broth from the top. Hot enough if you don't dawdle."

"Pay you tonight."

"You'll pay me because your mother would rise out of her grave and box your ears if you didn't."

Doss gave a tired grin. "That she would."

He sat, hunched over the bowl as if to trap heat with his body. Otis watched the steam thread away and vanish before it reached Doss's face.

Silas reached for his coat. Junie saw it and did not tell him not to go. That, too, was worse. If she had stopped him, there might have been room for argument. She only said, "If you come back with pretty fuel, I'm throwing it at your head."

"I know the difference."

"Do you."

He left before the answer had to be spoken aloud.

Ferrymouth usually smelled of yeast, brine, rope, old soup, bilge, and whatever the river had decided to surrender in the night. The new gold phase had laid another smell over it, faintly sweet and powdery, the odor of something trying to pass for cleaner than it was. It came off lamp housings and packet shops and the wet strips of moss brightening along the dock pilings. Richer, maybe, if you were a fool. To Silas it smelled like paste left too long in a warm jar.

The light did wicked things to depth. It gilded puddles without showing their holes. It slid across the iron on ferry ramps and made dry rust look slick and slick rust look dry. Men who had worked these planks twenty years were planting their feet like strangers.

At the bottom of Lantern Steps, a woman in a red shawl had set down a market basket and was rubbing one knee with furious little circles. Two eggs had broken in the basket and glazed the turnips. She was old enough that her braid had gone steel gray under the shawl, but she still wore a hatpin shaped like a swallow's wing, absurdly jaunty for six in the morning. Silas knew her face from somewhere, one of the women who had once heckled a soap launch and improved it.

"You all right?" he asked.

She looked up and recognized him quickly enough to make the answer interesting.

"No," she said. "Your fancy corpse-light took the edge off the step."

He crouched, because standing over an injured woman while she cursed you was too easy a version of the moment. Her knee had already darkened under her stocking. She kept pressing it with the heel of her hand, then stopping because it hurt worse.

"Let me see."

"What for? You'll name the bruise and sell it back to me?"

He almost told her she had earned that one. Instead he took the basket and set the unbroken eggs straight.

"Mrs. Caskey," he said, remembering. "Your place is two flights up by the wash rail."

"Three now that I can't trust my own damn feet."

He offered his arm. She took it after a pause long enough to state terms. Her hand was light and dry, the fingers nicked from years of needlework, and she smelled sharply of lavender water deployed against the river with no hope of victory.

"My daughter hems collars for half Patent Row," she said as they climbed. "She starts before the sun because the boys carrying bundles knock by seven. This gold rubbish makes white thread vanish against white cloth. You know what she did this morning? She stitched a gentleman's cuff shut. The man had to rip himself out of it like he was being born."

On a better morning it might have been funny. Here it sat between them without lifting.

At the landing outside her rooms, a young woman with a tape measure looped around her neck stood by the door holding a shirtfront up to the lamp and squinting until one eye nearly closed. Her hair had escaped its pins on one side only, giving her the look of somebody who had fought half a battle before breakfast and lost the wrong half. Mrs. Caskey jerked her chin toward her.

"Told you," she said. "Nera, this is the bastard."

Nera Caskey lowered the shirtfront. "I know him."

She said it without theatrical hatred. That was almost unbearable. She sounded like somebody naming weather she had not invited but still had to work under.

Silas left them there and kept climbing because there was nowhere else for the motion to go.

By the time he reached the fuel yards above the market, the queue already bent around the block. Men with scuttles. Women with wrapped baskets. A barber from Sallow Cut carrying his lamp tray like an invalid. The branded packets were stacked under an awning in neat cream paper wrappers stamped with a crown and a field of curling leaves, beautiful work, his own lettering bastardized into something cleaner and more obedient. Common mash, the rough green household kind, sat in a single half-emptied bin behind the counter. A chalk sign over it read RESERVED.

Nels Tarrow ran the yard in the mornings. He had a cough that whistled on the inhale and one clouded eye from a childhood fever, which made it seem as though he was always looking at two truths and distrusting both. Today he was sweating through his vest before breakfast, tallying orders with a carpenter's pencil tucked over one ear.

"Don't tell me you're buying retail now," he said when Silas reached the front.

"Don't tell me you've sold your spine for wrapping paper."

Nels gave a dry little bark. "Spines don't keep stock. What do you want?"

"Common mash."

"You and forty others."

"That bin says reserved."

"That's right."

"For whom?"

Nels spread both hands. The gesture displayed the burns on his thumbs, old white ovals from years of handling hot pans. "For people with standing orders and advance payment."

"My sister feeds half this dock."

"Your sister owes half this dock, depending who counts."

Silas leaned in. "There has to be more in back."

Nels lowered his voice without changing his face. "There's more in back. It's going uphill by cart because Gilt Crescent wants matching table lamps and veranda bowls. They like the gold. Makes their wives look expensive at supper. You want me to say it slower?"

Silas looked past him. Two boys were indeed loading crates marked for the ridge, careful as altar servers with the branded cakes, careless with the rough stuff. One crate of household mash had split at the corner. Dark green clumps bled onto the cobbles. A woman three places behind Silas stared at that spill like a starving dog watching gravy run into straw.

"Give me four scoops of the rough," Silas said.

"I can't."

The words still landed stiff in his mouth. Fear had a way of straightening a man's speech.

"Then give me whatever will boil a pot for longer than a hymn."

Nels slid a wrapped gold packet onto the scale. "This is what's there."

"It's trash."

"It's trash that people with cash have decided is elegant. Which makes it stock."

The woman behind Silas laughed once through her nose. No one else bothered.

He bought two packets because pride had become a luxury item. The paper wrappers felt dry and costly in his hand, as if they had no business riding in his coat pocket beside cracked chalk and old tobacco. Nels counted out the change and, after a moment, pushed one extra loose cake across the counter.

"That one's crumbled," he said. "Use it first."

Silas looked up.

"Don't thank me," Nels said. "I'm only tired of hearing your sister call me a grave robber."

On the way back down he saw the city learning the new light with its shins and ruined breakfasts. A lamplighter on a side lane held his ladder farther out than usual because the gold glow erased the rung shadows. Two coopers outside a barrel shop had carried their steaming glue pot into the street to steal heat from the sun, swearing over a seam that would not set. In a basement laundry a woman stood with both forearms plunged into suds gone yellow at the surface, blinking hard over other people's collars because the pale gold made every stain look faint and every scorch look decorative until you turned the cloth and saw what was actually there.

When he came back through Junie's door, the kitchen had divided itself by necessity. The front half stayed lit. The rear tables sat in a dim greenish murk because she had extinguished two lamps to save fuel. Customers bent over bowls in alternating colors, gold at the counter and swamp shade at the back wall, as if the room had been cut into classes with a knife.

Junie took one packet from his hand, hefted it, and closed her eyes.

"How much."

He told her.

She did not swear immediately. She set the packet down by the ledger, flattened the wrapper, and read the crown stamp as if reading the face of the man who had printed it. Then she took up the pencil. Otis watched her write.

"Four scoops before noon, maybe seven by late ferry," she said. "At this price, by Thursday I'm selling broth for the privilege of smelling fish. By Saturday the bread goes smaller. By Monday I choose between fuel and rent. That's if the stove doesn't choke first on this painted sugar."

She looked at Silas then, properly. "This is what your big word bought. Not for the ridge. For here."

He had no answer that deserved air.

Doss Mercer, already back for seconds because men who hauled for a living were built around repetition, raised his bowl a little. "She's right. My mother says the upstairs lamp makes her saints look younger and the hallway dark as a ditch. She'd rather see the ditch."

"Eat," Junie snapped, and he did.

She broke open one of the packets with the carving knife. Inside, the fuel cakes were molded in little scalloped rounds fit for display. He hated them on sight. They looked like something a confectioner would pipe for a baptism. Junie rammed two into the stove with the poker as if trying to insult them into honest labor.

"Otis, put your books away."

"I didn't finish my page."

"You'll copy receipts instead."

He gathered the slate and copybook slowly. His mouth had gone soft around the missing tooth, the way it did when he was frightened and trying not to be young about it.

"Uncle Sile," he said, "is the gold sick, or are we?"

Junie made a sound in the back of her throat that might have become a laugh in a less tired house.

Silas took the boy's slate because his own hands needed occupation. The chalk dust streaked over the yellow residue on his fingers and turned paste-gray.

"The gold's not sick," he said. "It's spoiled."

"Can you fix spoiled?"

Junie answered before he did. "Not by talking at it."

Silas set the slate down. Outside, a ferry bell knocked twice through the damp and somebody shouted for breakfast from the alley. The day was beginning whether the kitchen was ready or not.

Junie tied a fresh rag around the spoon handle where the heat ought to have been too fierce to touch barehanded and was not. "Go see your shop," she said. "Boy from Murtagh Buildings came by at dawn. Said the wall's blooming through the paper and the landlord has found religion about clean tenants."

"I paid through last month."

"Then enjoy the memory."

"Junie."

She did not soften. Not because she had stopped loving him. Because love was what made softening impossible while the pot still needed coaxing and the room was full of men who wanted feeding.

"If there's anything left worth carrying," she said, "carry it before somebody else decides it already belongs to them."

He stood there another second, listening to the weak hiss of the branded cakes. They burned fast, bright at the edges, almost eager to be consumed. The stew finally rolled into something like a boil, but the sound of it had changed. It was not the deep working thump of honest heat. It was a fretful chatter, all surface, no force.

He went.

***

Patent Row had always looked as though language had been pasted over itself until the street forgot what its own brick color was. Usually that pleased Silas. He liked walls carrying old lies under new ones. He liked paint ghosts. He liked the vulgar confidence of paper slapped over paper, promise on top of promise, whole fortunes trying to dry before the rain found them.

This afternoon the row looked curated.

Celadon Crown placards had gone up in the night or the dawn, cream stock with pale-gold borders and the same obedient lettering everywhere. Too much breathing room in the design. Too much confidence that the eye would come where it was told. The gold moss had answered all over the district. It glazed cornices, window putty, sign hooks, the iron scrolls over awnings. It made the whole street look richer from a distance, tidier if you did not know what you were seeing. Up close the damage showed. Paste bubbled under posters. Paste boards swelled. Shop lamps glowed pretty in broad daylight because they no longer had enough practical business to do.

A jeweler on the corner had dragged his stool outside because the new light inside his shop made the brass backs of watch cases shine but left the pin springs invisible. Across from him, a map copier worked with the shutters open so wide the sheets kept trying to take flight. A line had formed outside a packet dealer selling half portions of branded fuel to clerks' wives and boardinghouse keepers who could not afford more and could not go dark. Every face in the line held the same insulted concentration, like people being overcharged for their own blood.

Silas's shop sat halfway down the block between a monogram printer and a dealer in patent lamp mantles. Or it had sat. The place was still there. The sentence was what had gone.

The window bearing his name had been scraped from the inside, not cleanly. The word VALE remained in ghosts and scratches, a few stubborn white loops where the brush had once ridden slick and proud. Pale-gold moss threaded through the putty lines in the pane, branching into delicate fans that would have been beautiful if beauty had not currently meant failure. The door stood open. Inside, somebody was moving his things.

Silas stopped hard enough to send pain up his wrist. He did not feel the wrist first, though. He felt the empty place where outrage ought to have been and found something colder.

Mr. Brindle, the building agent for Murtagh Buildings, stood near the counter with a handkerchief held discreetly over his nose. Brindle was a soft man made to look firmer by starch. His collars were always too tight, and when he grew flustered a red bloom crept up from under them in exact finger widths. Today the bloom had reached his ears. He removed the handkerchief when he saw Silas and folded it away as if manners might yet save the scene.

"Mr. Vale," he said. "I had hoped to catch you earlier."

"By taking the hinges off while I slept?"

"There is no need."

"There's every need."

Two boys had stacked his sample boards by the back wall. One of them, a freckled apprentice from the printer's next door, would not meet his eye. The other kept staring at the gold growth on the ceiling beam as if waiting for it to drip.

Silas crossed the room. The shop smelled wrong. Under the usual blend of oil, turpentine, damp paper, dust, and lamp smoke lay the same sweet false odor from Ferrymouth, stronger here because Patent Row had chosen to lean into it. The moss had climbed the wall behind his main worktable where old paste and summer damp met. It had got into a tray of rabbit-skin glue and turned the surface into a pale film like cooled marrow. Two bundles of handbills had fused together at the edges. His white lead had warmed toward butter. The brushes left overnight in rinse water wore gold threads along the hairs as if age had arrived all at once.

He lifted one and watched the bristles separate in a limp fan. No line with a point left in it. No good for lettering. No good for anything but smearing.

"Rent is sixteen days late," Brindle said behind him. "The contamination is worsening. The owner has accepted an advance from a new tenant who can make immediate use of the frontage."

Silas turned. "I can scrape the wall."

"It is in the joists."

"I can cut it out."

"You cannot work fast enough with one hand, Mr. Vale, and the owner is not obliged to finance your recovery."

There it was, crisp and bloodless. Silas almost respected the wording. Brindle had likely practiced it on the walk over.

"Who is it?" Silas asked. "Which genius needs my door so badly?"

Brindle hesitated. A woman standing just outside the threshold answered for him.

"Me, if it matters."

She was small, maybe thirty-five, maybe older in the way city hunger sanded people down into approximation. Her mourning dress had been brushed until the black went shiny at the hips, and she wore a spray of lacquered cherries on her hat that had lost one fruit and did not apologize for it. Under one arm she carried a flat tin cash box. Under the other, a sleeping child too big to be carried easily and too tired to object. The child's sock dangled half off one heel.

"Bess Morrow," she said. "I had a notions stall under the arcade until the damp got in. Verdancy's giving depot rights to small sellers now, on percentage. I can pay three months if I hang their packets and keep the lamp trimmed. That's the whole romance of it."

She did not sound proud. She sounded winded.

Silas looked from her to the cash box to the child. There was no villain there worth the expenditure.

"Congratulations," he said, because cruelty always arrived first. "You get a room full of spoiled brushes and a wall that answers to insults."

Bess shifted the child higher. "If the wall answers to coins, I'll call it darling."

Brindle took half a step between them, not from courage but because he feared raised voices in rentable spaces.

"Mr. Vale, if you remove your personal effects today, the owner is willing to forgive the disposal fee."

"How Christian."

The freckled apprentice snorted once and then pretended to cough.

Silas went to the counter because his knees had developed a private tremor he did not care to exhibit. Under the counter sat the strongbox where he kept sign gold, imported pigments, receipts, and the few clippings that proved he had once mattered enough to be reviewed. The box remained untouched. At least Brindle had not sunk that low. Or perhaps he had and found nothing worth pocketing.

He opened it. Two small tins of color were still sound, lampblack and river green. The clipping from Damaris's sheet announcing him "Patent Row's Mouth of Miracles" had gone tacky along one edge where damp had licked it. He almost left it. Instead he folded it once and put it back because hatred was still a form of possession.

At the worktable lay the mahlstick he had used on fine jobs before the wrist sprain. He picked it up with his right hand on instinct, dropped it, cursed, then bent too fast to retrieve it and banged his shoulder on the drawer pull. The pain came specific and stupid, not tragic. Behind him, no one made the noise of pity. For that he was grateful.

"I could still paint the front," he said, mostly to the room. "I could do one last board and rent out the back."

Brindle's silence answered first. Then Bess Morrow, from the threshold:

"Not with that wall you won't."

She nodded toward the sample rack by the window. He followed the line of her chin and saw that the gold growth had reached the finished pieces. A wedding banner half rolled on the rack now glowed pale at the edges where the white cloth should have stayed white. A funeral drape sample had picked up a warm sheen that turned solemn black into something almost festive. His trade depended on a color meaning the same thing two days in a row. Here the city had taken that away as neatly as if it had cut the handles off his brushes.

He stepped closer to the window. Outside, two women paused to admire the light in the pane.

"That's the color," one said. "Not so vulgar as the pink was."

"Makes everything look dearer."

They moved on.

Silas put his forehead against the cool glass for a second. The pane vibrated faintly with street traffic. Through the gold thread in the putty he could see his own reflection half erased, hat low, shirt sticking to the spine, fingers stained from work he could not properly do. Patent Row Silas. Mr. Vale. The man with a shopfront and a window and a chair customers waited in. He had thought the essential part of his ruin happened on the regatta platform when he gave Gideon the word aloud. That had been the mouth of it. This was the hand closing.

"Take the shelf hooks if you want them," Bess said after a moment. "Fresh iron costs stupid money this month."

That line, more than Brindle's notice, nearly undid him. Not because it was kind. Because it was practical. The city had moved past his shame into salvage, and salvage did not stop to admire a fallen man.

He stripped what he could in the next hour. Tins into the chest. Chalks wrapped in old proofs. The one serviceable mahlstick under his arm. Sample alphabets, though he nearly laughed packing them, all those beautiful confident letters with nowhere to go. The good sable liners were ruined. The wall easel had swollen at the feet and would not come free of the floor without splintering. He kicked it once, then stopped because tantrums were expensive in public.

The freckled apprentice finally found enough nerve to help him carry the chest to the door.

"Sorry, Mr. Vale," the boy muttered. He had ink under one thumbnail and a tiny silver medal pinned inside his vest, visible only when the cloth shifted. Some church prize, maybe, or a dead sister's token. Something he kept for luck while moving another man's life into the street.

"Don't be," Silas said. "Learn from it."

"From what?"

Silas looked around the shop, at the gold blooming through the paper seams, at Brindle with his handkerchief, at Bess setting her cash box on the counter he had stained with vermilion three summers ago, at the child waking and immediately reaching for the bright packet display because children trusted color before consequence.

"From how fast a nice name can eat a room," he said.

The boy frowned as if he would like to understand and could not afford the time.

When the chest was outside, Silas went back once more for the signboard that had hung over the door before he rented the place, a long painted panel with his own hand on it, the lettering flourished just enough to declare talent without admitting vanity. He had painted it the week the lease began. He had stood in the street afterward and admired the line of the V until dusk.

The panel came down heavier than he remembered. The back had gone damp and soft near one end where the gold growth had crept up the mounting bracket. As he lowered it, the softened corner tore off in his hand. A flake of painted wood drifted down onto his boot like dead bark.

He stared at it.

Brindle said quietly, "Mr. Vale."

Silas laughed then, a short ugly bark with nothing amused in it. "Of course."

He set the broken sign against the wall instead of trying to save it. There were losses too late for gentleness.

By late afternoon the light in Patent Row had turned richer, the way bad ideas always did once enough money stood behind them. Gold in the upper windows. Gold at the packet stalls. Gold edging the moss in the drains. From the ridge it would have looked like prosperity traveling downhill. On the street it looked like women buying half measures, men misjudging stair edges, glue failing in workrooms, stewpots refusing the boil, and a sign painter carrying his trade home in a chest because the room that had made him visible now belonged to packaged light.

He took the key Brindle held out and laid it on the counter anyway, though the gesture was theater and both men knew it. Then he picked up the chest with his left hand at the side handle and his right forearm braced uselessly under the weight. The strain bit into the old scar under his ribs and made him walk careful, as if he had aged twenty years since breakfast.

At the corner he stopped to rest and looked back once.

Bess Morrow had already propped a pale-gold packet pyramid in the window. The sleeping child, now awake, sat on the counter sucking two fingers and watching the street with solemn, fever-bright eyes. Behind them Brindle supervised the hanging of a cream banner that read CELADON CROWN in letters so trim and civil he could have spat.

Silas did not spit. Spit washed away. This was staying.

He turned toward Ferrymouth. The chest handle cut a fresh groove into his palm. Evening traffic thickened around him, workers, buyers, errand girls, boys with news cries, all moving through the same pale gold that made the ridge look fashionable and the lower wards look tired. Somewhere behind him, somebody admired the color again. Somewhere ahead, Junie was still feeding people on fuel that cost too much and did too little. The city had chosen a pretty word, and now kitchens paid for it, stairways paid for it, workrooms paid for it, and men like Silas discovered that a mouth could lose a shop long after it lost the argument.


# Chapter 15

By the time the first ferry bell struck the dark apart, Junie had three kettles going on a fire that should have had no business holding them. The branded fuel burned pretty and thin. It gave off a pale aristocratic glow that looked costly on a hotel balcony and behaved like an insult under a soup pot. She had wedged the cheapest coal she could still bully on credit under the grate, and the kitchen smelled of broth, damp burlap, onion skins, and that sweet-metal stink the Celadon phase left on every hot surface in town.

Silas came in from the yard with his sleeves rolled and his hair still wet from the pump. He had scrubbed at himself hard enough to raise a rash along his throat and still carried the city's slime in the seams of his hands. Paint used to live there. Fine blue, vermilion, lampblack, the honest dirt of his trade. Now the creases held pale gold gum and the gray fuzz of dead moss roots.

Junie did not look up at him right away. She was shaving burnt mash from the underside of a kettle with the back of a spoon, counting under her breath the way she did when numbers were all that kept rage from turning theatrical.

"Three sacks of onions," she said. "Two pounds of eel ends. Half a brick of coal I had to kiss Pell's clerk for, and that was with my mouth shut. Morning."

"Morning to you too."

"Take the bread before Otis wakes and sees it."

He took the heel from the table. It was yesterday's rye, the crust gone rubbery in the damp. He ate it standing. Across the room the chalkboard tab list had been wiped and rewritten so often the wood looked furred. Half the names on it were ferry hands who used to pay on Fridays and now paid whenever the light held steady enough for night loading. Junie had started adding little marks beside the worst of them, not hearts and not crosses, just a slanted knife-stroke that meant she was keeping score with the future.

She turned then, her hair pinned up with two skewers because she could never find a clean hairpin at this hour, the old split in her eyebrow silver in the stoveglow. Flour dust lived in that brow no matter how hard the city tried to coat everybody in moss.

"You're going to Lantern Steps," she said.

He swallowed. "Am I."

"You are if you plan to keep eating here."

"I thought I'd help with service."

"You thought wrong. I can use a pot boy. I can't use you haunting my stove like a bad debt with boots."

There it was, plain and brisk. No sermon, which made it hit harder. He set the bread down, suddenly too dry to finish.

"Who asked for me?"

"Nobody." She scraped the kettle once more, hard enough to ring metal. "That's the point. They asked for backs. Hands. Somebody who can haul bad trays and not die on the planks. Aunt Vero said she was short two bodies. I said I had one body I was tired of looking at."

"She said yes to that?"

"She said, 'If he talks like Patent Row, drown him shallow.' I took that for consent."

Despite himself, the corner of his mouth twitched. Junie saw it and cut it off with a look sharp enough to pare apples.

"Don't you start acting grateful because somebody found a funny way to insult you," she said. "Go useful. That's all."

He reached automatically for the rag hung by the sink, then remembered the weakness in his right wrist only when the rag twisted and pain ran up the forearm like a hot wire under skin. The sprain had settled into him, not dramatic anymore, just mean. It made fine brushwork stutter. It made a heavy pail slip at the wrong second. It had become one more small fact about his ruin, too boring to earn sympathy.

Junie saw that too. She saw everything in a room where food moved.

"Wrap it after the first load, not before," she said. "If you baby it, they'll smell the excuse on you."

"I wasn't offering one."

"Good. Save your breath. The Steps don't run on speeches."

She set a cloth packet on the table. Bread, an onion, a wedge of hard cheese that had gone slick at the corners. Beside it she dropped a pair of eel-hide gloves, stiff with dried salt.

"Those were Tomas Brigg's. He left them after a storm and then left this earth without coming back for them. They still fit a liar."

"Junie."

"What."

He had not known, walking in, what shape the morning might take. Some part of him had kept a fool's little shelf inside, a dry place where apology might be enough for one room, where suffering visibly could count as amendment, where the loss of the shop might begin earning him a softer face from someone. Junie did not even let him unpack the hope.

"If I go down there," he said, "I'm not asking anybody to forgive me."

"You'd better not. Forgiveness doesn't scrub a tray or cool a lamp. Keep up, keep your feet under you, and if one of the girls tells you to shut your mouth, shut it before she has to repeat herself."

He picked up the packet and the gloves. They smelled faintly of river fish and tobacco. As he turned for the door, she called after him.

"Silas."

He stopped.

She was already back at the kettles, shoulders broad under a washed-thin work dress, one hand on the ladle as if it were a tiller.

"If you see Inez, don't go giving her your face like it's a job she forgot to finish."

He stood with the cold morning off the yard pressing against his back.

"I wasn't planning to."

"Good," Junie said. "Then today might stay about work."

Outside, Ferrymouth was lifting itself awake in coughs and knocks and rope slap. The river smelled cooler than it had a week ago, though the city still sweated. A fish cart went by with its boards wet red. Two newsboys were arguing over a bundle of old sheets whose headlines had already gone damp and useless. Across the lane, a branded Celadon poster had bubbled off the wall in the night, leaving a white-faced lady with one eye missing and a strip of paste full of flies.

Silas tucked the food under his arm and walked south.

Patent Row would have been waking too, clerks unlatching shutters, paste boys smoothing fresh lies over yesterday's lies, men who still had shops standing arranging their brushes as if the world admired care. He did not turn that way. He went down toward the low planks and the rank sweet breath of the ward, where the city kept the work that did not count as display even when it saved everybody's supper.

***

At the base of Lantern Steps a handcart had been turned into a tool table. Hooks, scrapers, gloves, rags boiled to colorless strings, buckets of ash, baskets lined with wet sacking. People were already taking what they needed. Nobody looked at him with surprise. If Junie had sent word ahead, it had been in the plainest possible language.

Aunt Vero stood on the cart shaft, making herself taller by three inches and therefore impossible. Her thick white braid had been looped over one shoulder. Her broad hands were already stained green-brown up to the wrists, and the eye that drifted when she was tired had gone lazy with the hour. She wore a saint's medal tucked into the front of her dress and a man's old leather belt with two knives and a lump hammer hanging from it. The hammer handle had been wrapped in yellow prayer ribbon, dirty enough that only the knots still looked holy.

She glanced at Silas once, taking his measure in the same sweep she used for tools.

"You're late by useful standards and early by vanity standards," she said. "Take a hook."

"Morning, Aunt Vero."

"If you call me that before noon, I will put you in the cut up to your ears."

He took a hook.

Beside the cart a woman about Junie's age but harder in the face was rolling her sleeves over forearms thick as bread loaves. A dead pocket watch hung pinned to the front of her apron, the crystal cracked into a silver spiderweb. She caught Silas looking and rapped the watch twice with a thumbnail.

"It quit the day my husband had the decency to drown," she said. "I keep it because I liked the chain. Cora Nix. You're with me till I get sick of seeing you."

Before he could answer, a girl slid down the handcart wheel and landed in the mud like she had been born with knees made for impact. Sixteen maybe. Narrow face, black braid tucked through the back of a boy's cap, one boot sole cinched on with copper wire. A school primer stuck from her waistband, pages swelling from damp. She plucked it free, shoved it deeper, and squinted up at Silas with frank delight that had nothing kind in it.

"This him?" she asked Cora. "The crown bastard?"

"Pru," Cora said.

"What. He knows what he did."

Pru looked at Silas another second, then shrugged as if he were less interesting than advertised. "If he falls in, I've got first laugh."

On the other side of the cart a boy of fourteen or so was sorting nails from a coffee tin and rolling them over his knuckles like cards. His lashes were so fair they vanished unless he blinked. A strip of bright green scarf had been tied around his head to keep his hair out of his eyes, and the cloth had once been finer than anything that ought to end up on a labor crew. He noticed Silas noticing.

"My sister wore it for church," he said. "Now I wear it for filth. That's economy. Nico Perret."

"Enough courtship," Aunt Vero said. "We are not here to make friends with the man. We are here because every fool with a mouth helped turn the beds gold and now the city can't boil water. Steps first. Drain after. Cora, take him on the rail lamps. Nico, with Pru on the wash carts. If he talks too much, give him the heavy bucket."

"I can hear you all right here," Silas said.

Pru grinned. "Then save us the trouble."

They moved.

The Steps had never liked him. Even in better days, when he came through with money in his pocket and a line ready for every face, the place had always made a city man of his sort feel clumsy. The stair flights rose sharp and damp between houses pressed so close their wash lines crossed like arguments overhead. Mosslight clung to rails and landings in sick pale veils, pretty from a distance, treacherous under the hand. Local feet knew where to place themselves. Silas had to watch every board.

Cora walked up with a crate on one shoulder as if it were folded linen. "Rail cages first," she said. "The gold crust is keeping heat where hands go. Kids are blistering palms."

She showed him once, not slowly. Slide the scraper under the skin of growth, lift before it tears, shake the gummy pale fronds into the ash bucket, then wipe the iron with salt water so the old bed beneath could breathe. The trick was the angle. Too shallow and the scraper skated. Too deep and he gouged the older growth that still had a chance of answering to something useful.

He gouged the first one.

Cora slapped the back of his wrist, not hard but exact. "You shave a cheek like that, fine. You shave a lamp like that, somebody eats dark."

"Right."

"Don't say right. Do it right."

He tried again. The second cut held. Gold slime folded back off the iron with a sound like wet paper peeled from a wall.

Above them Pru was on the next landing, boots planted wide on slick boards, popping open cage lids and singing spelling words under her breath as she worked.

"Commerce," she said, scraping. "Com-mer-ce. Insolent. In-sol-ent. Catastrophe." She looked down at Silas. "That one's for you. School says say the hard ones twice."

"School says a lot of things."

"Yes," she said. "That's why I go."

Nico came past with a yoke over his shoulders and two slopping buckets of spent mash hanging from it. He had a smoker's cough without having earned it yet. "Don't mix the hot scrap with the cool scrap," he called. "Cora'll chew you."

"It all looks like hell to me."

"That's because you're looking with your face," Cora said. She pointed with her scraper. "Cool scrap there. See the flat blue under the gold? That's step-blue once we get the Celadon scum off it. Railings, landings, school stairs. Burns cool, holds a while."

"And the stringy green?" Silas asked before he could stop himself.

Pru answered from above, because of course she did. "Pot-green if you've got sense. Ferry kitchens, wash boilers, anybody who needs heat more than show. It smokes if you crowd it."

Nico thumped his buckets down and jerked his chin toward a narrow crate lined with burlap. "And that thin sad stuff goes wakelight. Mrs. Basco's sitting with her mother tonight on Marrow Street. Wakelight stays humble. Doesn't spit. Doesn't flare when people cry near it."

Silas looked from one bucket to the next. Under the pale rot the fronds were kin to one another, same slick flesh, same fine hair at the edges. The names divided them not by species but by promise.

"You can tell that by looking?"

Cora snorted. "No. We tell it by using. Looking's what rich men do before they buy the wrong thing."

He bent back to work.

The morning turned into an argument between his body and every object in reach. The scraper handle raised a soft blister at the base of his thumb. The bad wrist lit up each time a cage stuck and had to be yanked free. Pale slime coated the knees of his trousers and dried there like old glue. Twice he planted his boot too flat on the plank and felt himself sliding toward the open side before some stripped-down animal part of him found balance. Cora never grabbed him. She only said, "Toes first," in the tone a person used for weather facts and stubborn children.

People crossed around them all morning carrying the city's practical life in pieces. A woman with a basket of eels and curl papers in her hair stopped long enough to complain that her back room still smelled like expensive lamp oil and gave Cora two lemons in exchange for a handful of cleaned step-blue. An old man in a cooper's apron brought over cracked wick cages and spent ten minutes arguing about whether his daughter's shop needed pot-green or dock green, because the barrels were stored half inside, half out, and the moss behaved differently if the river breeze got ideas. Nobody called any of this magic. Nobody even called it strange. They called it work and kept moving.

By the fourth landing Silas had quit trying to be entertaining.

That was when he began to hear properly.

Pru did not just sort moss. She sorted words with the same quick contempt for waste. When a younger girl from down the alley brought up a tray and called all of it cooklight, Pru corrected her without looking up.

"No. Cooklight's what hotel fools say when they want the color and not the stove. Pot-green if you mean the kitchen. Say the thing you need."

The little girl nodded once, repeated it, and took the tray on.

Nico, meanwhile, had a whole private devotion to ferry bells. Every hour he tried to whistle the note sequence from the upriver boats and failed on the last turn, which irritated him enough to keep practicing. Between loads he used chalk to sketch little iron bridges on the side of the ash bucket and then rubbed them out with his thumb before anyone could accuse him of loafing.

At one point Cora paused to cough into a rag scented with orange peel. When the fit passed, she tucked the rag back into her apron and said to no one in particular, "If my lodger steals one more pickled pear, I'm marrying him off to a widow with seven children."

"Which widow?" Pru asked.

"The loud one with the bad feet."

"That doesn't narrow it."

Silas laughed before he remembered he was not here to belong. Cora looked at him sidelong.

"Careful," she said. "You'll think yourself company."

By midday they had cleared the upper rail lamps and moved down to the wash court below the Steps, where contaminated trays had been stacked in ranks against a wall. Here the heat got mean. A nearby ferment room pumped out a steady breath of yeast, hot copper, and pond rot. Flies clothed the rim of a runoff barrel. The court bricks sweated underfoot. On one side women were boiling rags in lye. On the other, three boys younger than Nico were shaving dead gold growth off pipe joints with oyster knives.

Aunt Vero sat on an overturned crate eating bread and raw onion, her belt knives dark with slime. When Silas came for his packet, she did not offer him a seat or tell him to leave. It was the closest thing to neutrality he had earned from her in months.

He leaned against the wall and opened Junie's cloth. The cheese had gone softer in the heat. He ate it anyway.

Aunt Vero said, "You drag the scraper like a priest blessing a sick goat."

"I've improved."

"From disgraceful to merely slow."

He chewed. "You're in a generous mood."

She took a bite of onion, eyes watering instantly, and smiled with all the warmth of a blade laid in sun. "No. I'm in a hungry one."

Across the court, Nico was showing Pru a trick with two bent nails and a washer, making the washer jump from one nail to the other with a flick of the wrist. Pru pretended not to be impressed, which fooled nobody.

"You teach him that?" Silas asked Vero.

"What, foolishness? The river teaches that free."

She watched the pair a moment longer. "His sister used to sing in chapel. Voice like a clean dish. Fever took her in June. He kept the scarf because he can't keep a song."

Silas looked away before pity made a spectacle of itself on his face.

From the lye pots came the voice of another older woman he had not yet worked beside, a heavyset laundress with two silver hair combs and a laugh that started low and climbed until it broke. She was wringing out rags with hands red from soap.

"Pru," she called, "if your mother asks, tell her I'm keeping the blue for her stairs and not for that idiot boarder she lets smoke indoors."

"She can ask it herself, Miss Dela."

"I would, but then I'd have to hear her answer."

The court laughed, not because the line was exquisite but because labor needed a place to put its breath.

Silas drank from the pump and let the iron taste sit on his tongue. The old itch arrived then, mean and familiar. Listening like this, hearing the names move hand to hand, he could feel the market part of his brain starting to sort. Step-blue had a nice edge to it. Wakelight carried grief without drooping. Pot-green was ugly in exactly the right useful way. He could have built a whole new campaign from the rhythm of these mouths if he'd still been the man who thought theft improved when it was artful.

He put the heel of the bread between his teeth and bit down until the thought lost some of its shine.

Aunt Vero did not look at him when she said, "If you're collecting again, I can still aim a hammer."

"I'm not."

"Good. Because if you were, I'd break the other wrist and save us all time."

He wiped his mouth with the back of his hand. "You always this tender after lunch?"

"Only with family and fools."

When the break ended, the real ugly work started.

The trays had to be stripped, boiled, resanded, and reset. Not one dramatic act, just repetition. Lift, scrape, dump, rinse, stack. Lift, scrape, dump, rinse, stack. The pale-gold Celadon skin came off in translucent curls that clung to arms and necks and made everybody shine sickly. Underneath, surviving beds showed their old mixed colors in bruised patches, blue at one edge, green at the root, a wet blush where the city still remembered the earlier name and hated itself for it. Silas worked beside Pru and one of the younger boys from the pipe crew, passing tray after tray to the boiling vats.

"Not that pile," Pru said for the tenth time. "That's dock green."

"Why is that one dock green and not pot-green? It came off the same fucking wall."

She did not stop working. "Because it's going to a rope shed. Smoke, tar, open boards. Dock green doesn't mind a draft and won't die if gull shit lands on it. Pot-green throws hotter. You put pot-green in a rope shed, it'll sweat itself stupid."

The boy beside her, ears sticking out under wet hair, added, "And step-blue goes to stairs because drunks fall cleaner when they can see."

"Thank you, theologian," Pru said.

He grinned. "My ma says I've got a gift."

Silas took the next tray. He had stopped hearing the names as rivals. That was the change, and it arrived so quietly he nearly mistrusted it. In Patent Row every new word crowded the last, one winner, one loser, that was how a man got paid. Here the names were not boxing for a purse. They were routes. Instructions. A map folded into the mouth. This tray to the kitchen. That basket to the wake house. That clipping to the stairs where children ran. Same family of growth, different demand, different handling, different little futures. Nobody here needed one splendid answer. They needed the right answer in the right hand before dark.

***

Late in the afternoon, Cora sent him and Nico down to the drain cut with a wheelbarrow full of spoiled posters, rotten slats, and dead tray backing. The runoff there had gone thick with paste from ruined advertisements. Whole faces drifted in the muck: a lady's mouth, a gloved hand, the golden corner of the word Crown floating like a fish belly in brown water.

Nico pitched a bundle into the barrow and said, "You painted some of these, didn't you."

Silas did not bother lying. "Some."

"You were better at letters than the fellows now."

That hurt more than mockery would have. "Thank you, I think."

"I didn't say you were any good at judgment."

Nico shoved the barrow into motion. The green scarf at his head had come half loose. He retied it one-handed while walking, a trick that made Silas think again of sisters and graves and the cheap way grief kept dressing itself as ordinary habit.

At the cut they unloaded the rotten paper and sorted the wood. Most of it was too soaked to save. Some had enough sound grain left for patching catwalk edges or lining new trays. Silas picked up one long ferry-plank from the side of an old schedule board. The painted times had blurred to ghosts. Beneath them, older layers showed through where damp had peeled the top sheet away: a flour notice, an election slogan, half a sermon, chalk sums, somebody's obscene drawing of a landlord with a rat's tail. The board had spent years taking whatever a city wanted from it and had not yet fallen apart.

He turned it in his hands.

"Leave that if it splinters," Nico said. "Pru stuck a nail through her palm on one last week."

"It's sound."

"Everything's sound till you lean on it."

Silas set the plank aside with the keepers.

When they got back, the court was in the rich dirty hour before quitting, when fatigue stripped everybody to fact. Cora's dead watch swung against her apron as she hauled the last crate. Pru had gone hoarse from reciting and now mouthed the spellings silently while she worked. Aunt Vero was crouched over a tray with a bit of charcoal, marking its edge before passing it on.

Silas came closer. She had written three words in a rough practical hand: STAIRS, BASCO WAKE, KITCHEN SOUTH.

"You label every tray?" he asked.

"Only the ones I don't want idiots switching," she said.

"That's most of them."

"You're learning."

She passed the charcoal to Cora, who added NIX LANDING in letters broad enough for a drunk to read. Pru took the next bit of wood and scrawled SCHOOL RAIL. Nico, seeing a free surface, put BRIDGE SHED with a little bell mark next to it before Aunt Vero smacked the back of his head for decoration.

Silas stood there with the charcoal dust in the air and the heat standing off the vats and watched one piece of scrap carry five different hands without complaint.

The old hunger rushed him then, sudden and vivid. He could see Bellglass Bridge in his head, iron lit against dusk, a board slung up where everybody crossing had to look. He could see himself painting one perfect title across it in a sweep so clean the whole city would have to follow. He even felt, for one ugly bright second, the old delight of imagining the crowd turning because his hand had told it how.

The feeling was so familiar it nearly took him by the throat.

Then Pru said, "Don't put Basco Wake by the kitchen lot, for God's sake," and Cora answered, "I'm old, not blind," and Nico was already rubbing space clear on the board's edge to add his bell mark back in when no one looked, and the whole vision of one beautiful commanding word turned rotten in his head. A single name would do what Celadon had done, only with better brushwork. It would take this mess of use and grief and errands and turn it into another stage.

He did not want a stage. He wanted, suddenly and with a force that made his palms prickle against the scraper handle, a surface that could hold the mess and keep holding it. Something wide enough for bad handwriting and crowded additions and names that needed to sit beside one another instead of murdering each other for room. Not a poster. Not a slogan. A board. Several boards. Ferry plank, stout nails, paper backing where paper helped, blank strips where chalk could take over, room in the middle and the margins both.

Infrastructure, Junie would call it if he brought her the idea dressed in an apron instead of vanity.

Aunt Vero was looking at him now, not kindly, not unkindly either. Measuring.

"What," she said.

He realized he had been staring at the marked tray as if it had started singing.

"Nothing," he said first, because the thought was still fragile and he did not trust his mouth around fresh things.

Vero waited.

He wiped his hand on his trouser thigh and tried again without performance. "Not nothing. I was thinking about a board."

"A coffin lid has the shape."

"Not that kind." He pointed at the tray edge, the layered labels, the charcoal shared hand to hand. "A public one. Wide enough for more than one name. Built for changing. Built so nobody has to win the middle of it alone."

Pru had stopped scraping. Nico had not, though his ears had tilted toward them.

Aunt Vero's drifting eye came straight for a moment, the way it did when something surprised her into full attention.

"And whose pretty signature goes at the bottom?" she asked.

There it was, the clean cut. If he flinched from it, he deserved it.

He looked at the plank by the drain pile in his mind, all those dead notices and live chalks over one another.

"Nobody's," he said. "Or mine can go on the back where the woodworms can admire it."

Nico barked a laugh.

Pru said, "Make it broad, then. People in this ward spell sideways when they're tired."

Cora, still hauling, added, "And use a face cheap enough nobody cries when it gets written over."

Aunt Vero took the charcoal from the tray edge and pressed it into Silas's hand. Her palm was hot and rough as kiln brick.

"Bring me a scrap tomorrow and show me you're talking about a board, not redemption," she said. "If I smell redemption, I piss on it."

"Fair."

"Nothing fair in this city," she said. "Quit standing there and finish the last stack."

They worked until the light thinned and the air over the cut turned almost cool. By then his shoulders felt scooped out. The blister at his thumb had burst and gone flat. His back was slick with old sweat under clean sweat. He had not been thanked. He had not deserved to be. He had done one day's labor among people who would be back tomorrow whether or not a man like him learned anything from them.

On the way down from Lantern Steps he carried the salvaged ferry-plank on one shoulder. It was awkward as sin and smelled of river mud, paste, and old iron nails. Nobody offered to carry it for him. That suited him.

Ferrymouth was closing into evening by the time he reached Junie's yard. She was out back skimming fat into a crock for tomorrow, apron streaked, eyes mean with fatigue. Otis sat on an upturned crate nearby, tongue out over a copybook, printing letters with painful care while the last of the kitchen steam climbed around his ears.

Junie saw the plank first.

"You stealing lumber now?" she asked.

"Salvage."

"That's a longer word for stealing."

Otis looked up. "Can I draw on it?"

"Not yet," Silas said, then heard himself and amended it. "Soon, maybe."

He leaned the plank against the wall and stood there another second, feeling the day in his knees.

Junie dipped the spoon, blew across it, tasted, and frowned at the fuel's weak burn as if she could insult it back into usefulness.

"Well?" she said.

He could have told her he had kept up. He could have said they did not throw him in the cut. He could even, in a cheaper mood, have offered the board like a triumphant reveal, watch me matter again, watch me come back useful through design. The shape of that performance was still in him. He knew all its joints.

Instead he went to the pump, washed his hands as clean as they would go, and came back for a bit of charcoal from Otis's copy slate.

On the plank's least-rotted face he did not write a name.

He marked out spaces.

Not elegant ones. Room for a broad hand at the top, smaller strips down the sides, a patch left blank where paper could be pasted and scraped away, another where chalk could take quick changes from stoop to ferry to kitchen. He measured with his thumb, not for beauty but for legibility at a run. He left margins big enough for arguments. He left the center unclaimed.

Otis crept closer, pencil forgotten. "What's it for?"

Silas kept working. The charcoal dragged over old paint ghosts and snagged on damp grain.

"For people to say what they need it to say," he answered.

Junie watched him a long moment. The yard was full of evening sounds, pans cooling, gulls fighting over offal, a ferry rope slapping wood somewhere out in the dark. At last she nodded toward the board.

"Then make it stout," she said. "The city won't use it gently."

He looked at the empty middle, at the black ribs of space he had left for other hands, and for once did not imagine signing the corner.


# Chapter 16

Silas spent the morning prying boat nails out of a dead ferry plank with his bad wrist wrapped in one of Junie's oldest apron ties. The cloth had once been blue, maybe. Now it was the color of dishwater left overnight, and it smelled like onion skins and old broth. Every time the hammer claw bit under a nail head, a line of pain ran up the inside of his forearm and sat in the elbow like a lodged fishbone. He kept going. The plank had river in it still, old tide and tar and passengers' sweat, and if this scheme was going to live anywhere it could live in that.

Junie had taken over the back stretch between her kitchen door and the alley wall as if she meant to feed a siege. A cauldron that had held eel stew two nights ago now held flour paste gone gray with salt and cheap glue. She stirred it with a paddle and counted under her breath at the same time, not trusting either numbers or starch unless both were kept under pressure. A fleck of bay leaf clung to the sweat on her forearm. She did not seem to know it was there.

"If you split that board, you can eat it," she said without looking at him. "I'm short on fire and patient enough already."

"I know how to lift a nail."

"You know how to name polish. Lifting a nail is newer."

He worked the claw deeper and got the rusted thing out in one pull. "There. A miracle."

"Sell it to somebody else."

The alley smelled of paste, river mud, coal smoke, and the thick green sweetness of cut moss warming in baskets by the kitchen wall. Somebody on the dock was gutting fish. The scales flashed in a pail like dropped coins. Beyond the sheds the Sallow kept hitting the pilings with that hollow knock that made Ferrymouth sound half built and half sinking. Silas had always liked the noise because it made the city seem busy even when he was broke. Today it sounded like a timer.

Inez came through the alley gate carrying a brace of narrow slats on one shoulder and a tin of lampblack in her left hand. She wore her scarf tied high, dark hair all but swallowed under it, and she had tucked a carpenter's pencil behind one ear as if she had been born with it there. The crescent scar inside her right wrist flashed pale when she shifted the tin. She set the slats down, tested the ferry plank under Silas's hand with one thumbnail, then leaned to smell the wet wood. It was a habit of hers he had only seen in grow houses and kitchens, people checking whether a thing was rotten by giving it the dignity of a close breath.

"This one holds," she said.

"I'm relieved to have passed inspection."

"You haven't." She took the hammer from him, drove the last bent nail back through from the opposite side with three clean hits, then handed the hammer back. "No borders."

"I wasn't thinking borders."

She looked at the brush bundle near his knee.

"I was thinking a small header."

"No flourishes either."

"Then why am I here at all?"

"Because you can letter plain without whining once you get tired enough."

That stung because it was exact. He had not heard warmth in her voice in days, but he had heard attention, which was worse in one way and better in another. He could not tell whether the difference kept him hungry or merely upright.

Behind her Aunt Vero came in with a roll of spoiled newsprint under one arm and a pair of dressmaker's shears shoved through her apron sash. Her thick white braid was looped twice around her head. She had tied a saint medal to the braid with red thread, either for devotion or because she kept misplacing it. Her wandering eye drifted toward Silas, amused already, then came back hard.

"Painter," she said, "if I catch one pretty curve on these boards, I'll break your fingers and save the city the trouble."

"You people know all the sweet ways to encourage a man."

"Sweet costs extra."

She dropped the roll on the table Junie used for chopping onions. The papers unfurled damp and showed the blurred corpse of one of Damaris's headlines beneath a wash of ruined ink. Some alderman's shame or some merchant's mistress had bled into gray clouds. Silas touched the page and felt the cheap wood pulp rise in wet pills under his fingertips.

Damaris herself arrived a minute later, pushing a handcart that squealed so sharply every turn sounded like a small accusation. Her widow's black had gone shiny at the elbows and hips from too much use. One boot was tied at the ankle with printer's twine where the buckle had given up. She had a peppermint tucked in one cheek, which made the chipped front tooth show when she smiled.

"Mind your gratitude," she said, seeing the papers. "That's three days of unsold scandal and one entirely libelous extra. I'm making a patriotic sacrifice."

Junie snorted. "You're making room in the pressroom."

"Both things can be true."

Damaris slapped the cart handle. More ruined bundles lay stacked there, along with two jars of paste and a crate of small tacks. "The back room roof leaked on the midday edition. Ruined the columns, blurred the names, sent half my subscribers into moral disappointment. Perfect for us."

Silas looked from the buckled papers to her. "Perfect how?"

She took one sheet, held it up to the light, and read from it with exaggerated delicacy. "'Society widow found in a compromising-' and then the rain took the noun. You can't help saying the words aloud when print goes bad. Your eye slows down trying to rescue it. Slow readers mouth what they see. Mouthed words count."

Inez set the lampblack tin on the table. "We're not asking the paper to work by itself."

"Of course not," Damaris said. "If paper could save us, I'd have ruled this city years ago. But blurred paper makes a person stop, and stopping makes room for a second mouth. That's all circulation ever was."

Silas almost smiled. It felt like theft, taking comfort from her trade even now, but he took it. He had spent half his life trying to make words stick faster than thought. The notion that delay could serve them had the vulgar charm of a dirty trick, which meant it might actually work.

Junie ladled paste into chipped bowls. "Talk less and spread more. Bellglass won't wait for your theories."

They set to it. Junie brushed paste in thick wet strokes over the plank faces. Damaris slapped ruined newsprint onto the paste and smoothed it with the flat of her palm, grinding yesterday's gossip into today's backing. Aunt Vero cut narrow strips from drier pages and sorted them in piles on the table edge, muttering as she worked. Some strips had names already copied in different hands from supper two nights before. Some were blank. Some carried use notes in abbreviated scrawls that looked more like kitchen orders than doctrine.

Silas took up a broad brush, held it in his left hand first, swore at the wobble, then shifted it back to his right and found he could manage if he kept the strokes blunt and short. No filigree. No hooks. No chance to show off. He painted the board header the way Inez wanted, heavy and plain enough for a dockman to read at a glance.

CALL IT HOW YOU USE IT

The letters sat there without one ounce of charm. He looked at them and felt, absurdly, a little bereaved. Then Inez nodded once, and the grief changed shape.

"Lower," she said, tapping the empty space beneath. "Leave room for additions."

"There's room enough for six."

"Leave room for ten. Nobody ever stops at six once they begin arguing."

That, too, was exact.

On the first board they pasted strips in two staggered rows:

STEP-BLUE on Lantern Steps, cool on wet stone

POTFAST in Ferrymouth kitchens, holds heat steady

DOCK-GREEN on gangplanks, clear in river fog

WAKE-BLUE in fever rooms, low light, no scorch

One from the lower cut that said BARGE-EYE, same use as dock-green, according to the cramped note beside it, but no one seemed interested in reconciling the difference. Another from Sump Ward read PAN-GREEN, which Junie said was close enough to potfast if you wanted to start a fight for sport. Aunt Vero insisted on adding a strip that read BRIDELIGHT for kisses and foolishness, then laughed so hard at Silas's face that her drifting eye nearly disappeared into the crinkles beside it.

"You object?" she asked.

"Only to accuracy."

"Too late for that."

By the time they had five large boards and a stack of narrower hand-copies, the alley had turned into a paste yard. Newsprint scraps clung to the soles of their boots. The kitchen door stood open behind Junie, belching steam and onion and pepper into the lane. Twice customers came to the threshold, took one look at the table full of planks and ruined papers and neighborhood matrons, and decided their business could wait until the stew was ready. Silas could feel the hour moving by the cooler edge coming into the air. Late summer was beginning to tire. Bellglass would answer more slowly tonight than it would have a month ago. That was why Gideon had chosen a blessing, no doubt. A ceremony could do what heat no longer did for free.

Damaris counted the smaller sheets and swore. "Not enough."

"Enough to start," Inez said.

"Starting is what respectable failures say before breakfast."

Junie wiped paste from her hands onto her apron and jerked her chin toward the cart. "What else did the leak ruin?"

Damaris considered the question like a merchant weighing whether truth ought to cost extra. Then she grinned around her peppermint. "A crate of half-set type forms, a stack of weather notices, and one sermon supplement no one was going to pay for anyhow. Give me twenty minutes."

"You'll be back?" Silas said.

"Darling, I own a newspaper. Running toward trouble is the only faithful habit I've got left."

She went out squealing toward Patent Row. Junie started another pot of paste without complaint, which in her case counted as optimism. Inez gathered the finished boards against the wall to dry, each one ugly as a patched door and twice as useful. Silas reached automatically for the corner of the first board, meaning to mark it, not with a signature exactly, but with something a little more his. A thicker tail on the last T, perhaps. A sly curl hidden in the brushwork.

Inez's hand came down on his wrist before the brush touched.

Not hard. Not intimate either. Just certain.

"Don't," she said.
