He looked at her hand on the apron tie and the old scar on the inside of her wrist, white against the heat-dark skin around it. "I wasn't going to sign it."

"You were going to make it remember you."

He might have lied a week ago. He did not now. "Yes."

She let go. "Let it remember the city instead."

The line landed clean. He set the brush down.

By noon Damaris came back with more spoiled stock, two newsboys, and a smell of wet ink so sharp it could have scoured a pan. After that the work sped up. The boards leaned in a damp row by the alley wall, drying under the back-window heat of Junie's kitchen. They looked too homely to sell anything. Silas took that as the first decent omen he had had in weeks.

* * *

By the time the boards were dry enough to carry, Ferrymouth was in full lunch ugliness, gulls screaming over fish guts, ferries bumping the slips crooked, men eating standing up because sitting wasted daylight. Junie cut a loaf into thick doorstops and shoved them at whoever had agreed to carry. She moved as if the whole campaign were merely another meal rush that had arrived badly timed. Her split eyebrow shone damp with sweat. She tucked a sugar lump into Otis's palm without pausing in her work and pointed at him with the bread knife.

"You don't stop to watch any fighting. You don't go near the rail. If a man in a green sash tries to take that board, you bite him and run."

Otis closed his fist over the sugar as if he had been handed a gold sovereign. His missing front tooth made his serious face look counterfeit, but the rest of him was all nerve and intent. One trouser knee was black with old dock dirt no scrubbing had ever beat out. He had two small boards slung across his back with onion twine and a pocket full of chalk stubs for copying when paper ran out.

"Do I bite first or after he reaches?" he asked.

"Use your judgment."

"That's a poor gift to give a child," Damaris said.

Otis ignored her and looked at Silas instead. "If they say two names at once, does the moss listen with both ears?"

"Moss hasn't got ears."

"Then how's it so nosy?"

Silas laughed before he could help it. The sound felt rusty. "Go ask it after we're done."

The children took the first wave because children crossed the city the way flies crossed a table, uninvited and impossible to sort. Otis led three others out of the alley, not because he was the oldest but because he already understood the value of moving before an adult could improve the plan. One was a wash-house girl from Lantern Steps with raw red knuckles and two onions tied into the corner of her apron because her mother had sent her for them and she refused to come home empty. Another was a ferry deckhand's son with arithmetic tables penciled up his cuff and a habit of clicking his tongue each time he changed direction. The third, a tiny dark-faced boy Silas knew only as Little Nuno, carried nothing but tacks and a hammer much too large for him, which did not keep him from looking pleased.

They scattered toward the slips, the stairs, the boarding houses, the alley mouths where people stopped to spit and gossip. Damaris's two newsboys followed with bundles of damp hand-copies under their arms, already shouting the header because she had told them to practice until it stuck.

"Call it how you use it. Call it how you use it."

The phrase made heads turn. Good enough.

Silas took a pair of larger boards and went with Inez toward the first ferry. He had to turn sideways to get between a crate of live eels and a woman carrying two basins of laundry. The board edge knocked his thigh with each step. The paper backing smelled of paste and ink and something faintly sour from the ruined sermon pages Damaris had sacrificed. He hoped a little blasphemy improved adhesion.

At the ferry shelter a deckwoman in a patched oilskin nailed one board to the wall beside the fare table before anyone could object. She held the nail between her lips while she worked and removed it only to spit into the river.

"My brother says dock-green," she said, reading the strip with her head tilted. "My mother says rope-light. They'll argue till winter."

Inez handed her a blank strip and a stump pencil. "Then give them room."

The woman grinned, showing a gold tooth filed flat on one corner. "Now that I understand."

By the time the ferry shoved off, three passengers had already read the board aloud to one another, not trusting their own eyes on the blurred print. One old man repeated potfast twice as if testing whether the word offended him. A pair of girls laughed at bridelight, then said it again softer when they thought nobody was listening. The river carried their voices under the deckhouse roof and out toward Bellglass with the churn.

It began like that all across the route. A board by a stoop where two women shelling peas stopped to correct the use note and wound up shouting their district's version at a cousin across the lane. A hand-copy pinned beside a kitchen hatch where the cook read it to customers while ladling stew. Another pasted crooked to the post outside a schoolyard gate, the children sounding out wake-blue with the solemn relish usually reserved for profanity or anatomy. Silas crossed three streets and heard the same header from four mouths, each one bent by class, habit, weather, and impatience into a different shape. The city was not harmonizing. It was picking up the pieces and biting them.

Then Gideon's runners found the first board on Patent Row.

They came in pairs, neat as clerks, green sashes over their coats and Verdancy labels stacked under one arm. One had pomade in his hair and little silver scissors on his watch chain, as if he might trim the world to shape given a quiet room and enough permission. The other smelled of violet water strong enough to make Silas's eyes sting. They stopped before the board nailed to a tobacconist's wall and laughed.

"What is this, then," the violet one said, "charity instruction for the semi-literate?"

Silas had expected anger. The mockery pricked worse, maybe because he knew how often he had once used the same tone on men selling practical things.

The scissors man peeled at the edge of the damp paper. "No source. No authority. No quality guarantee."

"No charge either," Silas said.

They turned. Recognition hit first, then a mean little pleasure. "Mr. Vale. Reduced to handbills."

"Reduced to walking, apparently," Silas said. "Hard luck for us both."

The violet one smiled. "Mr. Wren says confusion is a luxury the poor can't afford."

Inez stepped up before Silas could answer. "He's right. That's why one pretty lie is more expensive than ten useful names."

The man looked at her work scarf, the lampblack on her knuckles, and filed her instantly in whatever drawer he kept for women who ought not interrupt commerce. "Madam, naming for market is not your province."

"Neither are stairs till you break your neck on them."

He had no reply ready to that. He settled for tearing the board down.

For one bad second Silas felt the whole plan go soft in his hands. All morning's work, all that paste and stubbornness, and here it was coming away from the wall with one peel and a wet ripping sound. Patent Row had always been quicker with removal than memory. He knew this. He had made a living from it.

Then from across the street a fruit seller called to a customer, "Step-blue if you're taking Lantern Steps home, hear me?" and the customer called back, "Thought it was stair-blue."

"Lantern lot say step-blue," another woman said from her boarding-house stoop. "My aunt calls it shin-saver."

The runner still had the board in his hands when three names had already escaped it.

Otis shot past at that moment, barefoot for speed, onion twine slapping his calves. Little Nuno was behind him with the oversized hammer, and the wash-house girl had somehow acquired two more copies under her arm and a heel of bread in her mouth. They did not even look at the torn board. They were already past, down the alley toward the upper stairs, carrying duplicates.

"You can't strip every wall in the city," Silas said.

The violet runner lifted a shoulder. "We don't need to. We only need to keep people from trusting what they see."

Damaris, appearing from nowhere the way trouble always seemed to peel her out of the air, answered for him. "Then you're a half day too late."

She had stationed a newsboy atop a crate by the tobacconist and another on the cab stand railing opposite. At her signal both began reading the use strips aloud, loudly and badly, like auctioneers with fevers.

"Dock-green on gangplanks. Potfast in Ferrymouth kitchens. Wake-blue in fever rooms."

People laughed at the spectacle. Then people repeated the lines to correct them. Then the correction spread farther than the original. By the time the runners had reduced the board to a wet curl of paper and bare plank, a drayman driving toward Bellglass was saying potfast to himself as if he might need it later.

Damaris touched two fingers to the brim of her hat at the runners. "Pull faster, gentlemen. We're helping one another's circulation."

The scissors man swore under his breath and they moved on, hunting cleaner targets.

Silas stood in the little public mess they had left behind and felt the idea settle in his body in a new way. Not inspiration, not triumph, something rougher and more dependable. Gideon could own printers and choir singers and clean walls. He could not own the walk between a kitchen hatch and a ferry slip. He could not own the schoolyard mouths that liked saying the wrong thing on purpose. He could not own women on stoops correcting one another because correction was half the pleasure of being alive.

By midafternoon Bellglass was already seeded. Silas knew because he heard the names before he saw the bridge. They came off the traffic moving toward it, off cooks carrying baskets home, off ferry passengers stepping onto the road, off children who had clearly no idea what wake-blue meant and still enjoyed the shape of it. Every third person had a version slightly wrong. Good. Let wrongness travel too. It still split the monopoly.

When they reached the south approach, three chorus boards were already up, one tied to the iron fence with washline cord, one nailed to a signal post, one propped in the mouth of a side lane by a woman selling fried river cakes. She read from it between customers and corrected her own pronunciation each time. A boy on the ridge side shouted barge-eye down at the bridge and got dock-green shouted back. The argument made people stop. Stopping made them read. Reading made them mouth the words.

The city had begun.

* * *

Bellglass Bridge took a public blessing every year once the first cool edge came into the evenings. Ward crews checked lamp cages, merchants made speeches about safety and prosperity, and anybody with a product to tie to civic necessity tried to slip a sales pitch under the prayer. Silas had worked the edges of the ritual before, lettering banners or gilding donation boards, and he knew its smell: hot metal cooling, damp iron, river wind undercut by perfume from the ridge and frying oil from the lower stalls. It was piety with receipts under it.

Gideon had bought half the bridge.

Pale gold bunting draped the lamp posts. Verdancy men had set up a dais near the center arch with a lectern, a brass speaking horn, and crates of branded cuttings arranged like offerings. Even the city inspector's ribbon looked cleaner than the rest of Nacre Bend had any right to allow. Gideon stood near the lectern gloved as usual, silver at the temples, coat untouched by any weather lower than Gilt Crescent. He had a habit, when impatient, of smoothing each cuff once with the thumb of the opposite hand. Silas saw him do it twice while the crowd thickened and knew he was feeling the strain already.

The bridge was visible from three directions at once. Ridge people crowded the north entry in cleaner coats, sniffing at the drain stink and pretending they did not. Ferrymouth workers came up from the west with supper grease still on their hands. Southward, along the Sump side, women stood on the plank approaches with shawls and market baskets and the flat stillness of people who had not shown up to be dazzled. Bellglass itself held all of them in one iron throat.

Silas carried the first full chorus board under one arm and felt the old appetite begin to move in him as the crowd sound thickened. He hated that it still could. Put him in front of enough strangers waiting to be told what a thing was, and some mean bright part of him always lifted its head. The brass horn on Gideon's lectern shone like a bottle mouth in lamplight. One sentence into that horn and the bridge would lean his way. He knew exactly what kind of sentence would do it. Filthy enough to wake the ferries, sharp enough to draw laughter, practical enough to seem reformed. He could feel the shape of it in his teeth.

He tightened his grip on the board until the tack points bit his palm through the paper.

Inez came up beside him with a coil of line over one shoulder and a pouch of nails at her belt. She had changed nothing for the ceremony except washing the worst of the paste off her hands. Even clean, she looked like work. A smear of lampblack remained near her thumb knuckle. He found it comforting, which was ridiculous.

"When they start," she said, eyes on the dais, "you hold the ladder."

"That's all?"

"If you're lucky."

"And if I'm not?"

She glanced at him then. Not soft. Not cruel either. "Then you remember this isn't your mouth's evening."

He might have made a joke once. Instead he said, "All right."

Gideon saw them. Of course he did. He moved through the opening crowd with the smoothness of a man who believed passage was his natural condition and stopped just far enough away to avoid the paste on the board.

"Mr. Vale," he said. "You do choose interesting moments to embrace amateurism."

Silas shifted the board higher. "And you do choose expensive cloth for a damp bridge."

"Damp is weather. Your associates are strategy."

He gave Inez a brief, measurable look, as if assessing whether she counted as person or obstacle in the ledger. "Miss Cordero. I admire persistence when it remains local."

"Funny," she said. "I admire your silence under the same condition."

One corner of Gideon's mouth moved. Not enough to call it a smile. "This city is tired. It wants settlement."

"No," Silas said before he could stop himself. "You want settlement. The city wants light that works."

Gideon turned the full weight of his quiet onto him. "And yet one name gave people exactly that until interference made appetite look like principle."

Silas nearly took the bait. Nearly stepped into the old game of who could phrase theft more elegantly. The horn on the lectern seemed nearer than it had a moment before. He could win a line against Gideon. He had done it before. Winning a line was how he'd helped lose the city.

Inez reached and took the board out of his hands.

Not a request. Not even a glance. She simply removed the object and handed him the folded ladder.

"Hold this," she said.

It was the cleanest humiliation he had ever received, and because it was deserved it steadied him better than praise would have.

The ward clerk began the blessing with a bell and a page of municipal righteousness nobody listened to. The crowd shifted, coughed, muttered. Gideon's hired singers arranged themselves at the south rail, pale gold ribbons at their throats. When the clerk got to the line about prosperous continuity, Gideon stepped toward the lectern and laid one glove on the horn.

"Citizens of Nacre Bend," he said, his voice carried by money and training more than force, "you have seen what uncertainty costs. Tonight we bless a season of steadier light."

A murmur moved through the ridge side. Ferrymouth answered with less reverence and more curiosity. Silas planted the ladder feet against the iron rail where Inez pointed and held it while she climbed. Wet metal vibrated under his palms from all the bodies on the bridge.

Gideon lifted the horn.

Inez spoke first.

She did not have a horn. She did not need one. Her voice was not huge, but it was cut for working rooms where if you did not hear the first time you got burned.

"On wet steps," she said, tying the top corners of the board to the rail, "it's step-blue."

The line landed across the bridge like a thrown tool. People turned because it was not a slogan. It was an instruction.

Inez fixed the second tie and kept going. "On a crossing where iron sweats under your boots and your mother's carrying supper in both hands, it's step-blue. Call it pretty later if you've got spare bones."

Laughter hit first, then agreement, then a dozen corrections trying to own the truth of the thing.

"Lantern lot say it exactly so."

"We call it stair-blue on Clay Rise."

"My aunt says shin-saver."

Junie came up through the west-side crowd with a ladle still tucked through her apron string and shouted without ceremony, "At my stove it's potfast, unless anybody here enjoys raw eel."

That got a better laugh because everybody in Ferrymouth knew her stew and half the bridge owed her for it. A pair of kitchen women below her took it up at once, potfast, potfast, and a cluster of customers repeated it with their mouths still full of river cakes.

Aunt Vero, who had somehow planted herself just off the dais steps without being removed, cupped her hands and bellowed toward the lower ward side, "In a fever room it's wake-blue, and any silk bastard who says different can come wipe a sweating child with me till dawn."

The singers lost the beat. One of them, a narrow boy with an honest cowlick he had not managed to pomade flat, looked as if he might rather be anywhere else on earth. Someone on the ridge called dock-green from pure contrariness. Someone under the bridge shouted barge-eye back. The crowd did what crowds did when offered disagreement with license behind it. It multiplied.

Gideon put the horn to his mouth. "Celadon Crown," he said, clipped and clear. "A stable light for every district."

A few voices answered him out of habit, out of loyalty, out of wanting the evening to remain legible. Pale gold ribbons fluttered. The bridge held its breath between two uses of language.

Damaris solved that by setting her newsboys loose.

"Read the boards," she shouted from somewhere near the north approach. "Read them wrong if you must, but read them."

The boys obeyed with hideous enthusiasm. One stood atop a bench and mangled step-blue into step-bloo before being corrected by five strangers. Another read every use note as if auctioning livestock. The corrections flew thicker than the original cries. A woman from Sump Ward added PAN-GREEN to the board with a pencil stub while Inez was still on the ladder. A ferryman tied a strip beneath DOCK-GREEN that read ROPE-LIGHT and insisted both were proper depending on weather. Nobody paused to settle it. The board began acquiring names the way a wharf piling acquired posters and saints and warnings, by pressure and proximity.

Silas held the ladder and kept his mouth shut through the hardest thirty seconds of his adult life.

He could feel the crowd noticing him anyway. Patent Row knew his shoulders, his hair, the lazy slouch he had once sold like a patent cure. A few voices called for him by name, wanting the entertainment of his rescue or his further disgrace. The horn still gleamed at the lectern. He could have taken it. He knew he could have taken it. Even now some part of the bridge wanted one man to explain what all this meant.

Instead he handed a second board up to Inez.

"Left side," she said.

"Too high for you."

"Then stop talking and climb."

He climbed two rungs, held the weight, and let her tie the second board above the first. From that height he could see the city's channels converging. A ferry below them was docking with passengers still reading a hand-copy aloud. On the ridge approach three women had stopped under a lamp and were arguing over whether wake-blue belonged in a sickroom or only beside the dead. Ferrymouth boys were pasting smaller slips to the fence uprights. Southward, along the plank route, children carried duplicates like contraband prayer cards.

It was already too many mouths for a horn to master cleanly.

* * *

The first change showed low, where wet iron met foot traffic.

Silas saw it because Inez had just climbed down and he was still crouched by the ladder feet, breathing paste and river damp and crowd sweat. Along the edge of the third stair below the bridge deck, the old Celadon pale gold shivered, thinned, and took on a wash of blue cold enough to look useful. Not theatrical blue. Not bridelight's blushing nonsense. This was the color of shade under a water barrel, of clean steel, of evening before the lamps were all the way up.

"There," somebody shouted.

That was all it took.

The cry moved outward. People leaned, pointed, repeated whatever name had most recently pleased or convinced them. Step-blue gained one rail section, then another. Under the hands of dock men shouting dock-green from the west side, the moss on the lower truss thickened into a denser green that cut through mist and river spray like bottle glass held to sun. Near the food sellers where Junie's crowd kept chanting potfast between jokes, the lamp-cage growth warmed toward a hard cooking green with amber at its core, a color that promised a steady boil rather than fashionable shine. Someone on the south side kept saying wake-blue in a rough grandmother's rhythm, and cool blue islands gathered in the sheltered inner braces where sickroom quiet might plausibly belong.

Celadon Crown did not vanish. That would have been too neat and too kind. Gideon's singers and runners and ridge customers kept the gold alive in broad expensive patches along the center arch and the dais rail. It sat there like plated fat, elegant from a distance, thin and a little sickly up close. Around it, other colors held.

Gideon saw it happen. His face changed less than most men's would have, but the change was there if you knew where vanity lived. He took one step toward the nearest blue rail, stopped, and smoothed his cuff once, then again. When he raised the horn this time the polish was gone from his voice.

"Repeat one name," he said. "One. Or you'll have chaos in every kitchen and stair."

Junie barked a laugh so rude it cut straight across the bridge. "We've already had chaos in our kitchens, you expensive fool. That's how we learned the difference."

The crowd loved that. Not nobly. Not as a united people rising into historical purpose. They loved it because it was funny and true and aimed upward. Ferrymouth took up potfast harder after that. On the ridge side, where a few well-dressed women had been trying to keep Celadon Crown pure by sheer diction, a maid with her cap askew muttered wake-blue under her breath and then louder when she saw the sheltered brace answer.

Damaris was spinning in place, taking notes on her cuff with a pencil almost worn to skin. "Don't say rainbow," she shouted at one of the boys. "If you say rainbow I'll dock your supper. Say bands. Say islands. Say the damned thing as it stands."

"It stands ugly," the boy yelled back.

"Good. Print ugly."

Another runner from Verdancy lunged for the first board and tore away half the strip that read POTFAST. Before he could rip farther, Little Nuno darted between legs and hammered a duplicate to the fence post beside him with three wild, glorious blows. The oversized hammer almost took the runner's thumb off. The man swore, yanked his hand back, and the crowd laughed him smaller. Otis slid under the rail with paste and stuck two hand-copies lower where adults had not thought to guard.

"Mind the edge," Silas shouted, too late to be useful.

Otis twisted around, hair in his eyes, and called back, "I'm minding four edges."

He sounded delighted. That frightened Silas more than fear would have. Children loved contagion because they did not yet know how much labor it took to clean it afterward. Then again, children were how contagion got beaten sometimes too. The bookish little holy picture of that did not interest him. The practical fact did.

Names were coming in now from farther off than the bridge itself. Ferries passing beneath picked up whatever the passengers had heard and threw it up at the girders in work-thick voices. From the west embankment somebody shouted pan-green from a kitchen doorway. From a stoop on the north rise came stair-blue, then shin-saver, then laughter as if the name itself were less important than refusing to let one district's tongue rule the rest. Even bridelight came back, shrill and teasing from a pack of girls near the ridge rail, and pink freckles flashed in the moss by the lamp crowns where old flirtation still had customers.

Silas felt the whole bridge become legible in layers. Not simple. Never simple again. Use by use, pressure by pressure, neighborhood by neighborhood, the growth answered where it was being asked from. The colors did not blend into harmony. They held against one another. Blue veining the steps. Green thick on the handrails and lower truss. Gold still clinging in marketed bands where Gideon's saturation had money behind it. Pink blushing up in corners whenever memory or mischief revived the first stupid name he had ever given it.

That was the victory, if it deserved the word. Not purity. Not the clean replacement of one banner by another. Just the public ruin of the claim that one polished phrase could own all uses at once.

Gideon came off the dais at last. The crowd opened for him reluctantly, more from surprise than respect. He stopped before the nearest board and put one gloved hand against it, maybe to pull it down, maybe to steady himself, Silas could not tell. The paste gave under the pressure. Newsprint shifted. A streak of wet lampblack and flour glue smeared over the white kid leather.

Gideon looked at his hand as if the city itself had spat on him.

Inez saw it too. She was standing by the rail now, not looking at Silas, not needing to. Her face was set in the hard clean stillness she got when anger had already chosen usefulness and had no need to flare. She said, to Gideon and to anybody within hearing, "You'll have to keep buying every wall, every ferry, every stair, every cook's mouth in Nacre Bend if you want that gold to rule now."

He met her eyes. "Then perhaps I will."

Aunt Vero cackled from behind him. "You'll die broke and hoarse first."

That got another hard ripple of laughter. Not victory music. Better. Laughter meant people could carry the line home.

Silas did not move toward Gideon. That, too, felt like labor. There had been a time when the whole meaning of a public evening lay in whether he could cut the rich man prettiest. Tonight the cut had already been made and it did not need his signature under it.

Instead he picked up a fallen strip, found a dry patch on the second board, and held it steady while a woman from Clay Rise wrote STAIR-BLUE in square stubborn letters beneath STEP-BLUE. Beside them the ferry deckwoman added ROPE-LIGHT under DOCK-GREEN and underlined it twice for weather. A boarding-house keeper near the north end squeezed in NIGHT-KIND beside WAKE-BLUE, muttering that sickroom and mourning were not always separate things in cheap houses. The board became a city wall in miniature, layered, contradictory, useful because of the contradiction and not despite it.

Dark came up slow and public. One by one the lamp cages along Bellglass caught the new divided colors and threw them down onto hats, cheeks, sleeves, wet iron, river chop. People on the far bank stopped because they could see the bridge change from a distance. A ferryman below whistled, long and low, when the whole south rail banded blue-green-gold in separate holds instead of blending. Even the ridge watchers, who had come for a blessing and a spectacle they could purchase later, had to admit what their own eyes were doing to them.

No one stopped arguing. That was the last proof.

"Potfast and pan-green are close enough," one man insisted.

"Close enough burns babies," a woman answered.

"Dock-green on fog days, rope-light on clear."

"Stair-blue's for stone. Step-blue's for iron."

"Bridelight if you want kissing, you liar."

"Celadon Crown if you've got more money than sense."

The names flew, corrected one another, crossed one another, made pockets and bands and islands of color hold where the speech behind them held. Bellglass looked diseased and magnificent, like a civic bruise finally showing all the colors that had been gathering under the skin for months. Silas stood in the middle of it with paste drying on his fingers and his wrist throbbing under the old apron tie and understood, with a clarity that felt almost indecent, that he would never again be able to pretend authorship was the same as use.

By full dark the bridge carried six colors and more arguments than a council chamber. Everybody who crossed it had to admit the same ugly, liberating fact. The moss no longer answered to one clean mouth.


# Chapter 17

By morning Patent Row looked hungover from its own miracle. The blessing bills Damaris's boys had pasted for Bellglass Bridge hung in wet curls, and every slick wall along the hill held some leftover quarrel of color: pale gold in one seam, kitchen blue under it, a feral pink climbing the mortar where somebody had shouted bridelight just to see if the city would blush again. People were still stopping in the street to test words against the moss. A teamster in a cracked hat called one patch ferry-veil and got a stripe of green so cold it almost looked silver. Two laundresses laughed, tried widow's lantern, and turned the same stripe blue as skimmed bruises.

Silas kept walking.

He had slept a little at Ferrymouth, face-down on Junie's spare mattress with the ferry bells punching holes through what was left of the night. The chorus from Bellglass Bridge still ran in his skull, and not the sound of it only, though there had been enough sound to shake rust from iron. He kept feeling a whole city answer at once. He kept feeling what it had been to stand inside that answer and not have to own it. He did not know yet if that felt like relief or bereavement. His mouth tasted of old paste and lamp smoke and the bitter heel of envy. There were still parts of him cheap enough to miss the stage.

Damaris had sent for him at dawn by a runner with one suspender hanging and yesterday's ink on his ear. Gideon had sent for him too, by card, thick cream stock, Verdancy seal pressed in green wax. The card requested a private discussion at his immediate convenience.

That alone told Silas what sort of morning it was.

Damaris's pressroom stood open to the alley for air and got none worth having. The place smelled of hot iron, wet paper, stale chicory, and the human grease of boys who had slept in their shirts. The widow herself was at the long proofing table with her mourning collar unpinned and spread open because press heat raised hives on her neck. She had tucked the loose collar points down with a brass safety pin as if respectability were just another sheet to trim when it snagged. One compositor dozed upright on a stool. Another was setting type with his lips moving around the copy like prayer.

"You look awful," Damaris said without looking up. "Which is useful. A handsome man after a public victory makes people suspicious."

"Morning."

"It's late enough to be morning, yes." She slid a sheaf across the table. "Read that before you go uphill and let him try to wash you."

Silas took the pages. They were office copies on thin paper, violet with transfer ink in places where a clerk had pressed too hard. Verdancy House headings sat over distribution tallies and runner instructions. One list of ward names had, beside each entry, another column in a tidier hand: market substitute, simplified form, approved packaging color. He saw names he knew were never meant for labels because Inez had once told him what they were for in a room so hot the ferment pipes sweated on the walls. One meant keep children away from the heat lip. One meant burn only in damp weather. One meant funeral use, low and blue, for households sitting with a body through the night. Verdancy had turned it into moon-silk.

Under those was a stack of purchase notes. Arrears had been bought cheap from fuel men and landlords: kitchen paper, wash-house paper, digester liens. Junie's name sat halfway down one page in a clerk's obedient block hand, her debt entered like a crate of soap.

Silas looked up.

"Where'd you get these?"

"A clerk with a grievance and a fondness for cards," Damaris said. "Also for oyster pie, which is how grievances become print. Don't romanticize me. I'm a grocer of resentment."

He flipped farther. There were internal memoranda too. Language harvest from low wards before autumn cooling. Repeatable variants for district segmentation. Recommended use of Mr. Vale's public wit to convert resistance into aspirational difference. He could feel Gideon's mind moving under the page, neat as a knife through fruit.

"These are copies."

"Of course they're copies. I'm reckless, not stupid." Damaris finally looked at him, chipped tooth showing because she was not in the mood to hide anything. "What I don't have is the packet he will slide in front of you today when he explains why theft becomes vision if the stationery is costly enough. I want that packet. I want whatever names he thinks safe to show you now that he thinks the bridge business has made plurality fashionable. And I want the contract gone if you're not going to sign another. If he still owns your mouth on paper, he will pretend anything I print is a lover's squabble between businessmen."

"You think he brought me up there to hand you a confession."

"No. I think he brought you up there because he still knows exactly what kind of hunger made him money."

That landed because it was true, and because Silas had spent half the walk from Ferrymouth trying not to taste it. Damaris tapped the copy with one inked finger.

"He's going to offer you a cleaner version of your old sin. Maybe a smaller one. Maybe a prettier one. That's the dangerous sort." She leaned back and rubbed at the welt rising under her collarbone, irritated more by her own skin than by him. "If you take it, take it honestly and I will print around you. If you don't, bring me the papers and let the city see the seams. Either way, don't go up there thinking the man has discovered a conscience. He's discovered a pivot."

Silas set the copies down. The rollers thudded twice in the back room where someone tested a plate. He thought of his old shop on Patent Row, the window with his best lettering ghosted in reverse on the inside pane, the north light he had cursed all winter and loved every month he could pay for it. He thought of how simple it would be to have a door again, a bench, a rack of clean sable, a place where his name was not currently being shouted by newsboys as half a warning and half a joke.

"If I bring you the papers," he said, "you print all of it. Not only him."

Damaris's mouth tipped. "There you are. I was wondering if shame had finally made you dull. Sit with that comfort now: I've no intention of making you pretty. I'm a widow, not a seamstress."

He almost laughed. It came out as breath.

"All right."

"Good. Now go uphill before his courage improves."

Verdancy House kept its private rooms above the showroom in Gilt Crescent, where the ridge caught more wind and less honest smell from the river. The clerk downstairs knew Silas by sight and disliked him for reasons that had nothing to do with morality and everything to do with class. Silas had always had that effect on clerks who could not decide whether he was vulgar or enviable. Today the man only held the door with extra care, as if touch itself might leave a mark.

Gideon's office looked the same as the first day Silas had signed away his neutrality: blue carpet thick enough to quiet boots, green-shaded lamps, a coal grate polished around the mouth, paper arranged in weighted squares on a walnut desk broad enough to seat a family. The windows were cracked against the damp, but the room still smelled more of starch and lemon peel than of Nacre Bend. Gideon was bare-handed because he had been handling paper. The left cuff button of his shirt had worked loose again; he worried it with his thumb while he read, and the silk around it had worn a small grey halo from the habit.

"Mr. Vale," he said. "Please sit."

Silas stayed standing until Gideon looked up. Then he took the chair opposite the desk because he refused to play harder to get than he felt.

Gideon studied him with calm professional disappointment, as if last night's public defeat had chiefly injured the room's arrangement. "You should be congratulated. Few men survive a crowd by surrendering authorship and remain useful afterward. Fewer still do it by instinct."

"That what I did?"

"It is what the city thinks you did. The city is wrong in fascinating ways, but one can work with wrongness if it is energetic enough."

He slid a decanter and glass Silas's way. Silas did not touch either. His hands were steady enough until he looked at the desk and saw, off to one side, a ribbon-tied stack of papers thick as a fist. His own contract sat on top, watermark visible where the light caught it. His name. Gideon's. The old green wax seal he had once pressed like a man sealing up his own throat.

Gideon saw him see it and gave the smallest smile.

"I thought it best to keep the morning concrete."

"You always did like paper more than people."

"Paper keeps its shape longer."

There was the offer, then, laid out with the kind of civility men used when they expected greed to do the rough work for them. A new retainer, not exclusive in the old sense because the old sense had broken on Bellglass Bridge in front of God and ferry traffic. Better, Gideon said. More sophisticated. Verdancy House would cease trying to force one name and instead license district usage under a central arrangement. Public plurality, private ownership. Silas would serve as principal designer of public surfaces and authorized variants. He would have a monthly salary, a percentage on licensed boards, and a restored workshop on Patent Row under a lease held safely through Verdancy. Not the little room he had lost. Better rooms. Better glass. Better foot traffic. Damaris could be fed selected copy as needed. Junie's fuel notes would be returned and cleared. Miss Cordero, if she chose not to be foolish, could be retained quietly as a technical consultant.

As Gideon spoke, he laid mock-ups in front of him: paste-up sheets, sample layouts, a handsome broadside with layered type and ornamental borders. Beneath the printed names, in a script Silas recognized as his own old public hand because Gideon had paid him enough times to imitate it, the words read: Usage arranged by Silas Vale.

The bastard had even chosen the blue he liked best.

Silas felt the pull of it so hard it shamed him. It was not only the money, though the money was real enough: sixty crowns at signing, twelve a month, materials on account. It was enough to put meat in Junie's stew without the arithmetic scowl between her brows. It was enough to buy Otis boots that did not leak at the toes. It was enough to stand in a shop with north light on a fresh board and feel his own name come back into his body by the hand instead of by rumor. It was enough to stop being a man who lived on other people's spare corners and apology.

Worse than the money was the acquittal. Gideon was offering him a way to come back elegant. It would not be forgiveness exactly; forgiveness was for churchwomen and fools. But it would be explanation, reframing, installation again in the city as someone whose public failure had turned out to be foresight.

He hated how much he wanted it.

Gideon watched that hatred pass over his face and mistook it for deliberation. Men often had.

"You understand," Gideon said, folding his hands, "that last night's display changes nothing fundamental. People still require placement. They still require a trusted hand. They may shout six names from a bridge, but by market day they will want to know which packet to buy, which lamp to hang, which district mark means money instead of mold. There is no shame in order. There is only profit or waste."

"And the ward names?"

"What of them?"

"You strip them, polish them, sell them back."

"We refine them. Context is expensive, Mr. Vale. Most consumers do not buy context."

"Most consumers don't have to keep a stove on with it."

Gideon gave a slight shrug. "Then they are not the profitable consumers."

Silas looked at him. Really looked. The man's silver at the temples, the immaculate tie, the loosened cuff button under the worrying thumb. The office had the same green shade on the lamps as a hundred rooms below the ridge, but here the light was obedient. Here it answered to money first and mouths second. Last night at Bellglass Bridge the city had broken that obedience for one public hour. Gideon had come out of the wreckage already looking for a smaller cage.

"You'd sell them the rebellion too," Silas said.

"If rebellion becomes a habit, it is merely another route of distribution."

The honesty of it made Silas's stomach go cold.

Gideon leaned back. "You should not sentimentalize Miss Cordero's victory. Her kind of plurality is laborious. It soils the hand. It does not scale. What I am offering is an end to chaos with enough local ornament to satisfy the sentimental poor and enough coherent billing to satisfy the men who matter."

There it was: Miss Cordero's victory, the sentimental poor.

Silas heard the bridge again for a second, not as music this time but as proof of number. Children shrieking names wrong on purpose. Aunt Vero bawling something rude enough to make a priest choke. Inez's voice cutting straight through all of it because she had not asked the crowd to admire her, only to use the right words for the right light. Gideon had stood there and learned nothing except that next time he ought to invoice the noise.

He let his gaze drift to the ribbon-tied packet.

"What's in the rest?"

"Enough for you to see the architecture."

"Show me."

Gideon untied the ribbon himself. Perhaps that was the vanity of the thing. He wanted Silas to admire the mechanism. He spread the papers over the desk in neat fans: extraction schedules from Sump Ward and Lantern Steps, runners' reports on which names had commercial charm and which carried too much death or kitchen-specific use to sell cleanly, notes on debt acquisition timed to pressure local adoption, print orders for relabeled cuttings with color recommendations, a memorandum headed Language Simplification Strategy for Autumn Uptake.

Silas saw his own past work threaded through it like disease. Marginal notes in Gideon's hand beside phrases Silas had once brought back loose in his mouth, thinking himself a clever bastard and not yet a thief. One sheet had a list of possible taglines under his byline. Another had household warnings severed from the warnings and retained only for sound. The man had built a whole accounting language for theft.

And there, halfway down one ledger page, were Junie's notes, marked held pending leverage deployment, then transferred internal, then recoverable upon cooperation.

Silas tapped that line.

"Those come back first."

Gideon looked almost bored. "Of course. Provided we are speaking seriously."

"Put them with the rest."

"Mr. Vale."

"If we're being concrete, be concrete."

For the first time all morning Gideon seemed to consider not just the outcome he wanted but the shape of the man in front of him. Then, because he believed so thoroughly in appetite, he made the fatal choice and slid the notes out from the ledger pocket. Junie's fuel papers were soft at the folds from too much handling. Somebody had added Verdancy House marks in the corner where her own blunt numerals ought to have been left alone. Silas picked them up. Gideon did not stop him.

"You are angry," Gideon said. "That is not unusual. Men are often angry at the point of return. They call it conscience because they want a prettier noun. Take the morning if you require it. Take the papers and read them. By evening you will remember that outrage does not pay rent."

Silas kept holding the notes. With his other hand he lifted the old contract from the top of the stack.

The paper felt heavier than it had when he signed it. Maybe shame added weight. Maybe he had more hand to feel it with now. He looked at the scrawl of his name and remembered the first shock of that advance in his palm, how quickly compromise had felt like relief when Junie needed coal and Havelock Pigments wanted their money and every wall in Patent Row still pretended there was virtue in being paid well for ugliness.

He had told himself at the time that everyone sold something, whether it was brushwork or back or mouth. Why should he be the one bastard in Nacre Bend too pure to take a retainer?

Because sometimes the thing you sold kept reaching after you.

He stood.

Gideon did not move. "Sit down."

"No."

"Do not perform integrity for me. I funded you through better performances than this."

Silas smiled then, a mean little thing that hurt his own face. "That is the trouble, isn't it? You still think the room is yours if the carpet's thick enough."

He turned to the coal grate. Gideon rose fast enough then, chair legs scraping the blue carpet.

"Mr. Vale."

Silas fed the lower corner of the contract to the grate's open mouth.

The paper caught slowly at first, the edge browning, then all at once the green seal blistered and burst. Fire ran under his own signature until the ink rose black and curled. Heat bit his knuckles. Gideon crossed the room with a speed that would have looked ungentlemanly to anyone else. Silas stepped back before the man could snatch the sheet free and shoved the burning contract deeper with the poker. It broke at the fold. One half dropped into orange coals and vanished in a bright, ugly lick. The other hung for a second, his name splitting open along the S, before it went too.

For one absurd instant he felt grief. The grief was real, and it was not for Gideon's money. It was for the shape of himself that had once fit so neatly inside a signature and a reputation and a room full of men waiting to be charmed. It went up with the paper. He stood there and smelled the old life burn: rag cotton, sealing wax, ink, the fat paper rich men trusted more than they trusted bodies.

Gideon had stopped two paces away. He had not shouted for the clerks. He had not grabbed Silas by the coat. His face had gone very still, which on Gideon meant fury stripped of sermon.

"You are making a theatrical mistake."

"No," Silas said. "I made that when I signed it."

He took the packet of extraction papers off the desk with his burned hand. Gideon's eyes flicked down to it.

"Leave those."

"You just told me to take the morning."

"Those are not yours."

Silas tucked Junie's notes inside the packet. "That has not stopped Verdancy yet."

Now Gideon did reach, not wildly, just enough to show what he wanted. Silas stepped back again. The desk hit his thigh. Somewhere downstairs a bell rang from the showroom and went unanswered.

"If you print a scandal sheet with stolen office papers," Gideon said, every word clipped and exact, "you will ruin yourself beyond any possibility of repair."

"I think that part's already in motion."

"And Miss Cordero will still not thank you."

That found flesh, but it found it too late.

"I'm not asking her to."

Gideon's mouth thinned. "You should have stayed with what you were good at."

Silas looked at the grate where the last ribbon of contract had collapsed into cinder. "Maybe I am."

He left before the man could choose between dignity and pursuit. Gideon chose dignity. Of course he did. By the time the clerk downstairs half rose from his stool, Silas was already through the outer door and into the ridge wind with the packet under his arm and his right hand starting to smart in earnest.

Damaris did not say a word when he came back into the pressroom. She only looked from his face to the papers and then to the pink welt across his knuckles where the fire had kissed him. The rollers in the back had started for real now, a heavy turning heartbeat that made the floor hum.

"Well," she said. "That's a better expression."

He handed her the packet. She moved fast once the paper was in reach, spreading sheets, sorting, calling for copy boys, barking for the compositor to wake fully or go drown himself somewhere less expensive. She read as she moved, eyes hardening into pleasure of the coldest useful kind.

"Language simplification strategy." She whistled once through her chipped tooth. "He really did write the confession as a memorandum. Men with orderly desks should be horsewhipped."

Silas laid Junie's fuel notes on top. "Print those too."

Damaris paused. "You are certain."

"If you scrub me out of it, I will hate you."

"That would ruin my month." She gathered the notes in with the rest. "Don't fret. I know the difference between martyrdom and evidence."

He stayed because leaving felt cowardly and because the pressroom was the only place in the city where his old appetite and his new shame were both being put to use without pretending they were clean. Copy boys ran. Type clicked into sticks. Damaris wrote the opening in three bursts, crossing nothing out at first and only then going back to sharpen the nouns. She chewed anise seed from a twist of paper while she worked because the metallic taste of the press made her swear too much, and she swore plenty anyway.

She let him read the proof before the forme was locked.

Verdancy House Purchases Household Debts to Strip Ward Names for Sale.

Below it, in tighter columns, the packet laid open: the instructions to runners, the lists of practical names severed from use and repackaged for fashion, the bought arrears, the timed leverage, the note on Junie's kitchen. Damaris had not spared Silas. She named him as Verdancy's paid mouth, as the man who had helped turn ward language into product, and as the man who had now burned the contract and supplied the papers. There was no saint there, and no clever rebel scrubbed clean by one decent morning. There was only consequence, widened.

"Good," he said, and meant it through the wince.

"I know." Damaris sanded the page, shook it off, and slapped it toward the waiting boy. "Run that to the stone. Headline first, and if Barlow drops another comma I will break his thumb."

The first sheet came off wet and hot enough to steam faintly in the damp air. Silas took it by the edge. The ink marked him anyway. Outside, before the second dozen had been stacked, he could already hear the first newsboy trying the shout on his lungs.

"VERDANCY BOUGHT YOUR KITCHEN PAPER!"

Another boy answered farther down the alley, voice cracking with delight at the obscenity of good copy.

"WARD NAMES SOLD LIKE SOAP!"

Damaris listened with one hand braced in the small of her back, her face gone suddenly older and lonelier than it ever did while she was actively cruel. Then she shook it off.

"Go to her," she said. "If you wait until the city has finished reading, you'll turn this into another audience."

Silas folded the fresh broadside once, tucked Junie's freed notes inside his coat, and went.

***

By the time he reached Sump Ward, the paper had outrun him.

Two women were reading it aloud outside a wash house, taking turns where the type ran small. A ferryman had pinned one copy to a post with a fish knife and was explaining the column on debt leverage as if he had invented debt himself. A boy on Lantern Steps was testing Damaris's headline at different volumes to see which version made the moss nearest the rail ripple green. The city had got hold of the scandal in exactly the way Gideon feared most: not as a private disgrace among respectable men, but as something with legs and mouths and cheap shoes.

Silas crossed the plank walk into the grow-house yard under a sky already thinning toward cooler blue. The night's damp was coming earlier now. Autumn was somewhere upriver, not here yet but thinking about it. The yard smelled of wet burlap, pond heat, and the faint sweet rot of cut stems left too long in a bucket.

Inez was at the long worktable under the lean-to roof, one boot off, rubbing a thumbful of camphor tallow into the arch of her bare foot before she shoved it back into the leather. Wet planks made her feet seize by evening; he had seen her hide the limp before, but never the maintenance of it. Her scarf was off and hanging from a nail. Dark hair had come loose at the nape in a damp rope. On the table beside her sat three boards sanded clean, a tin of white ground, a pot of blue-green paint, and the first broadside from Damaris folded under a stone so it would not crawl away in the damp.

She looked up once.

"You are late," she said.

He stopped. "You knew I was coming?"

"Damaris printed you in half the city. It seemed likely the other half would deliver the body." She set her boot down and pulled it on. "Come here. The edge on this plank is rough."

That was all the welcome he got. It was more than he had any right to expect.

He came to the table. The plank was for Ferrymouth by the size and scuff of it, scavenged ferry wood with old bolt holes filled in. One end had already been underpainted. She had ruled pencil lines for three stacked fields of text and left the bottom strip blank.

"What's the blank for?"

"What people add when they aren't asked permission." She tipped her chin toward the paper under the stone. "I read your morning entertainment. Did it hurt?"

He looked at his hand.

"Some."

"Good."

He almost smiled. "You don't waste sugar."

"I've kitchens for that." She slid him a scraper. "Take the old paste off the back. Not with the burn."

He obeyed. The work was ugly and small, which was probably why she had chosen it. Old newsprint and paste came away in gummy curls. The board smelled of river salt and something older, maybe fish oil, maybe rope. Inez opened the broadside with flat fingers and read one paragraph again, though he could tell by the set of her mouth that she already knew it nearly by heart.

"Damaris made him sound taller," she said.

"She made me shorter."

"That part was overdue."

He scraped. She sorted brushes by stiffness and laid them in a row. Her movements were exact without ever looking delicate. The burn scar on the inside of her right wrist flashed when she reached for the paint tin, pale against the rest of her skin like a crescent moon somebody had tried to file down.

When she spoke again, her voice had lost the dry edge just enough to be dangerous.

"Junie?"

"Got her notes back."

He took the folded papers from his coat and laid them beside the broadside. Inez looked at Junie's blunt numerals, at the Verdancy marks in the corner, and then at him. She was not grateful. She was measuring.

"All right," she said. "That is one thing where it belongs."

"And the contract's gone."

"I read that too."

"I burned it in his grate."

Something moved in her face then, quick and gone. Satisfaction, maybe. Or only the relief of hearing a fact out loud instead of through Damaris's appetite. She dipped two fingers in the tallow pot and caught his burned hand before he could pull it back. The ointment was cool, greasy with camphor and something minty underneath. She spread it over the red welt with competent pressure, not tender enough to flatter him and not rough enough to punish.

"You should have done it months ago," she said.

"I know."

"I'm not saying it so you can agree prettily."

"All right."

She let go. "Good. Then listen."

That word changed the yard. Even the drip from the gutter seemed to move aside for it. She picked up the board, set it straight between them, and tapped the three ruled fields.

"This goes to Ferrymouth tonight if the paint dries fast enough. Top line for the common use-name there. Second line for heat warning. Third for cuttings source and the night it was last checked. The blank strip stays blank unless the kitchen adds something after. We are doing two more tomorrow, one for Lantern Steps and one for the lower wash path."

The word was we.

Silas kept his scraper still.

"If that is an offer," he said, "say it like one."

"If it were only an offer, I would not bother saying it at all." She picked up the pencil and wrote on the back of the plank in tight practical script: white, blue-green, lampblack, paste, nails, two labor hours. "You asked me, once, what a future with me would look like if it did not depend on speeches. It looks tedious at first glance. That is one of its virtues."

He felt the old instinct rise, the one that wanted to turn a line like that into flirtation, wanted to make her smile and himself forgivable in the same motion. He let it die.

"Tell me the terms."

She nodded once, as if a student had finally found the right side of the question.

"First: no sole credit. Not on a board, not in print, not in a room full of investors, not in your own head. If a sign comes from ward knowledge, the ward gets named or the sign stays bare. If the work is ours, it is ours. If it is mine, your name does not crawl onto it because you are handsome with a brush."

"Fair."

"Second: no stripping context. If a household gives us a name and a warning with it, the warning stays with the name. If the story matters to use, the story stays too. If somebody says no, that means no. You do not harvest a thing because it sounds expensive."

"Fair."

"Third: money gets written down every time. Materials first. Shared labor next. One part in six goes to the grow houses until the mother trays are steady through the cool weather. If Junie's kitchen takes a job board, she pays when she can, not when some man in a clean office decides to teach her discipline."

"All right."

"Fourth." She set the pencil down and looked at him properly for the first time since he had come into the yard. "You do not come back to me because you burned one paper and felt noble watching it. I am not a medal for improved conduct."

He met her eyes. "I know that too."

"Do you." It was not a question. It was a blade laid flat, waiting to see whether he would press his own throat to it. "If you want my body, you come to it awake. Not hiding. Not as a way to wash off what you did. Not to prove to yourself that a woman with sense has pardoned you. You ask me. I answer. Some nights I will say no, and you will not mope like a dock boy denied a kiss behind the fish shed."

Against his will, despite everything, he barked a laugh.

"I wouldn't dare."

"You have dared stupider things."

"True."

Her mouth twitched and settled. "If I say yes, you stay the night through. No crawling out before dawn because remorse has made you dramatic. If you are in my bed, you are there because tomorrow morning you are still on the ladder beside me, or under the sink fixing paste, or out in the yard cutting dead growth with your wrists aching. Work before declarations. Shared signage before shared mythology. Life before speeches about life."

The last of the light had dropped low enough that the yard lamps were starting to answer. One tray near the drain gave off a stripe of diluted gold that the blue paint on the table made greener by contrast. Somewhere beyond the fence Aunt Vero was shouting about somebody's useless nephew and a stolen bucket. The city did not stop to make room for a turning point. It went on being damp and rude.

Silas put the scraper down very carefully.

"And if I say yes to that," he said, "what am I to you?"

Inez leaned both palms on the board. There was paint under one thumbnail and a little crescent of dried mud at the edge of her sleeve where she had wiped a hand hours ago and forgotten it. He loved her most, he thought, when she looked most like a person halfway through the day's real work.

"A man I want," she said. "A man I do not trust cheaply. A man who is useful when he tells the truth and dangerous when he starts admiring himself. A partner, if you can stand the second word without trying to crawl back into the first."

The first word was author, perhaps, or genius, or star, or bastard. Maybe it was all of them.

He looked at the blank strip on the bottom of the board. Room left for other mouths.

"I can stand it," he said, and knew as he said it that standing it was not the same as liking the loss. The honest future was smaller than the one Gideon had laid out in blue mock-ups and monthly numbers. Smaller and wetter and more public in the least glamorous way. No brass plate on a shop. No easy version of innocence handed back. No sole crowd. He grieved it. He did. The grief did not make the answer less true.

"I want the work," he said. "I want you. I don't know yet how not to miss the rest, but I'm done selling the missing."

Inez studied him long enough to make the yard feel narrow.

"That is almost a decent sentence."

"You know how I feel about almost."

"I know how you feel about applause."

She came around the table then, close enough that he could smell the tallow on her hands, the wet plant smell caught in the cotton at her shoulder, and the faint salt of her own sweat under all of it. It was not perfume or anything bought to mean desire. It was the body that had spent the day doing necessary things and had not apologized for it. She touched the front of his shirt where the broadside was folded in his inner pocket.

"Are you keeping that as proof," she asked, "or as a souvenir of your own reform?"

"Proof."

"Good."

She slid the paper out, unfolded it, read the headline once more, then set it under the stone again. After that she took his face in both hands and kissed him with none of the softness he had spent the morning half craving and half despising in himself. It was not absolution. It was selection. Her mouth was warm and a little rough from coffee. When he made the mistake of turning the kiss grateful, she bit his lower lip hard enough to correct him.

"Do not do that," she said against his mouth.

"Do what?"

"Thank me with your face."

"I wasn't."

"You were."

He put his forehead to hers and laughed once because the only alternative was something worse. "All right."

She kissed him again, slower this time. His burned hand hovered useless for a second until she caught it and put it at her waist herself. The motion was deliberate enough to feel contractual in the cleanest possible sense. He felt the heat of her through the worn work dress, the solid line of her side, the breath she took when his thumb found the knot of muscle above her hip. Her own hand went under his coat and up along his ribs until she found the old scar there and pressed it once, not gently.

"Still there," she said.

"Most things are."

"Not the contract."

"No."

She stepped back first. "Good. Then earn the rest of the evening."

He made a noise low in his throat that would have embarrassed him yesterday and did not now. "Cruel woman."

"Tired woman. Hand me the white ground."

So they worked.

He laid in the top line while she mixed the warning blue darker for Ferrymouth's damp. The board was for Junie's kitchen after all, though Inez had not said so until the first letters began to show. FERRY-BED over the top, in a plain strong hand fit to be read through steam and fatigue. Below it, KEEP THE BLUE STRIP FOR STOVE HEAT. DO NOT CUT GOLD GROWTH INTO THE POT BEDS. The last line named the source house in Sump Ward and left the date blank for morning's inspection. The bottom strip stayed empty. While the paint set, Inez ruled two smaller boards for Lantern Steps: one for safe rail light, one for children's fingers.

The work steadied him more than any speech could have done. He brushed, lifted, breathed, turned the stroke before the grain caught, and remembered his wrist before it complained. Inez corrected spacing without apology and once took the brush from him to tighten an S that had tried to become showy. He let her. Outside the fence, voices kept lifting with pieces of Damaris's broadside: Verdancy, kitchen paper, bought debts. Some of the shouts were gleeful. Some were angry. None of them sounded like private catharsis. Good.

When the Ferrymouth board was done they carried it inside to dry on chairs in Inez's room because the night damp had turned mean. Her room was narrow and warm with stored heat from the wall pipes. A chipped blue cup sat on the sill full of screws and clipped nail heads because she saved what could still be useful. Her work scarf hung over the bedpost. On the little table by the bed lay the oilskin ledger, closed and tied. Beside it she had set out another book, cheap brown paper boards, blank except for two ruled columns on the first page.

She saw him looking.

"That one is for jobs," she said. "Not names. Hours, materials, who paid, who did not."

"Romantic."

"I told you what my virtues were."

He set the drying board down across two chairs. The room had only one lamp lit, shaded with tin so the glow fell low and useful. It turned her face into planes and left the corners in green shadow. He could hear the ward outside through the thin wall: a baby objecting to sleep, somebody laughing at the broadside again, a bucket knocked over, the far river answering in hull knocks and one bored bell.

Inez took the cheap ledger to the table and opened to the first page. In the left column she wrote Ferrymouth kitchen board. In the right: shared labor. Then she held out the pencil.

"If you're saying yes," she said, "write it by doing something boring."

He took the pencil and added the materials in his own hand. White ground. Lampblack. Blue-green. Nails to come. His lettering wanted to flourish out of habit. He kept it plain. When he set the pencil down, Inez nodded as if witnessing something more intimate than a vow.

"All right," she said.

"That all?"

"For tonight."

The want in him had not gone anywhere during the work. It had changed shape. Less plea, more hunger with its boots on. He came to her slow enough to be asked away if she wanted. She did not ask. She put one palm flat on his chest and felt the beat under it like she was testing a wall for hidden pipe.

"You are staying?" she said.

"If you'll have me."

"I just spent an hour making rules for it. Do not make me repeat myself."

He kissed her again, and this time when she opened to him there was no gratitude in it, no theatrical wreckage. Only desire with its eyes open. He got her dress loose at the throat and put his mouth on the burn scar inside her wrist because he had watched that pale crescent flash at him for months and wanted to know the skin around it. She hissed once and tipped his head where she wanted it, which was better than tenderness, better than pardon. Her fingers found the buttons of his shirt and fumbled only once at the burn on his hand before choosing the other side. By the time they reached the bed they were both half-undone and still arguing in the smallest practical ways.

"Mind the lamp," she said.

"I'm watching it."

"You are watching me."

"That too."

"Less poetry. More care."

He laughed against her shoulder. Then she pushed him down on the edge of the mattress, stripped the coat from him, and kissed him hard enough to shut him up. The bed ropes complained under their weight. Outside, somebody in the alley tried out a new name for the bridge light and got no answer at all.

Later, when the lamp was turned low and the Ferrymouth board dried in the chair shadows like a third quiet witness, they lay under one blanket with the damp night pressing the window. Inez was on her side facing away, not asleep; he could tell by the change in her breathing when voices rose outside. After a while she reached back without turning and put her hand on his forearm.

"We start at dawn," she said.

"I know."

"This is not repaired."

"I know that too."

Her hand stayed where it was. "Good."

Silas looked past her to the jobs ledger on the table, to the oilskin name book beside it, to the chair where the board for Junie's kitchen waited for morning. He could still feel the ghost of the old life burning in Gideon's grate. He probably would for a long time. But here was the smaller thing, the harder thing: a room with no brass plate on the door, a ledger that would record hours instead of glory, a woman who had made space for him without pretending the making cost her nothing, and work already drying for the morning.

Down in the alley, two drunk men argued over what to call the same strip of moss climbing the drainpipe. One said saint's broth. The other said dock-gleam. Neither won. The strip held both colors badly and stubbornly at once.

Inez's heel found his shin under the blanket, warm and accidental or maybe not. He lay there listening to the city refuse to settle, and when sleep came it did not feel like absolution. It felt like having somewhere exact to wake.


# Chapter 18

By early autumn the damp had stopped trying to smother the city and settled for living in its pockets. The window above Junie's kitchen stood open on a slice of pear-colored morning, and the air that came through it smelled of river mud gone cool at last, yeast, old boards, and the onion grease already waking below. Silas lay for a moment with one arm over his eyes and felt the ache in his right wrist before he felt anything else. Cold always found the old sprain first. That was one of the small jokes the body kept for itself.

Inez was already up. She stood in her shift and petticoat with one stocking on, tying her dark hair into the work scarf she favored when there was no reason to impress anyone and no point pretending there might be. Her oilskin ledger sat on the chair beside his rolled brushes. His tobacco tin rested on top of it as if the two objects had been born to share a surface. Her boots were under the bed with his, toes touching.

"You awake," she said, not looking around, "or are you planning to bill the morning for your thoughts?"

"Depends who the client is."

"The south ferry line. They pay in bad coffee and complaints."

"Then I'm fully alert."

That got him half a smile. She bent to pull on the second stocking, and the crescent burn scar inside her right wrist flashed pale against her skin before the scarf fell over it. He had learned the scar the way a man learned a favorite bend in a stair rail, by use rather than announcement. The knowledge sat deeper in him than half the names he had once sold for money.

"Captain Lusk wants the new board up before the noon traffic," Inez said. "Some fool scraped off the warning slat at Lantern Steps and now people are asking for pretty light where they need cook heat."

"A fool or a salesman."

"Same breed."

Silas pushed himself up. The blanket slid off his hips. The room was narrow enough that when he stood he had to turn sideways to reach his trousers hanging from a nail. It had started as a storage room for sacks of meal and had become, through a month of use and very little ceremony, a place where his shirts hung beside her spare scarf and his extra brushes dried in a jar next to her hairpins. No one had made a speech over it. No one had promised anything impossible. He was grateful for that in ways he still did not say cleanly.

He crossed behind her to reach the washbasin and put his mouth against the back of her neck on the way. Her skin was cool from the window air. She made a small sound that was not surprise and not invitation either, only acknowledgment, and caught his wrist before he could let his hand wander lower.

"Not with your fingers full of sleep grit," she said.

"Cruel woman."

"Wash."

He did. The water in the basin had gone cold enough to sting. When he straightened, he saw his own face in the little spotted mirror Junie had nailed crooked to the wall: dark hair flattened on one side, scar under the left ribs pale in the glass where the blanket had dragged low, the look of a man who had once made a profession of entering rooms at an angle and now mostly entered them carrying equipment. He liked this version less some days. He trusted him more.

From below came the ferry bell at the landing and Junie shouting at somebody to either sit down or stop leaning on her hatch. In Junie's voice the two commands carried equal affection.

Inez was wrapping the ledger in its oilskin. "Bring the thick brushes. The river's been chewing paint this week."

"If the river wants lettering, it can pay my old rates."

"Your old rates are dead."

"They are."

He said it without flinching and found he meant it. That was newer than the room, newer even than the way her scarf ended up over his chair most nights they worked late enough to stop lying about going separate ways.

Downstairs, Junie's kitchen was already loud. Steam filmed the front panes. The long counter wore its usual dents and knife scars, but the room had changed the way a face changed after illness: not beautified, not restored, only forced into a new honesty. The chalkboard over the pot shelf no longer offered a single proud lamp source in tidy script. It carried three columns in three hands. `Dock-green` in Junie's blunt chalk. `Pan-hold` in Inez's narrower block letters. `Tourists may call it crown if they enjoy eating in the dark` in Silas's own slanted paint, smaller than the rest because Junie had threatened to box his ear if he turned her breakfast wall into a performance.

Under the chalkboard a row of mismatched jars held moss cuttings with rag tags tied around their necks. Some tags named color. Some named use. One just said `Not for frying, ask first.` Another said `Lantern Steps calls this widow-blue in fog.` None of it was elegant. All of it was alive.

Junie stood at the stove with her sleeves rolled and her hair pinned up in a hurry that had let one dark coil escape against the healed split in her left eyebrow. She stirred the oat pot with one hand and, with the other, flicked a bit of fried onion from the cutting board onto the floor for the three-legged dock cat she denied feeding. The cat took it under a bench with priestly dignity. Junie caught Silas seeing it.

"You mention him and you're washing pans for a week," she said.

"I wouldn't dream of exposing your criminal softness."

"Eat."

She shoved two bowls across the counter, thick oat mash loosened with broth and topped with yesterday's eel scraps and pepper. The smell came up hot and rough and absolutely sufficient. At the far end of the room two ferry hands in wet caps were arguing over a bundle of glow cuttings one of them had bought cheap off a Patent Row cart.

"He said it was bridge-pearl," one of them complained, prodding the rag-wrapped bundle with obvious distrust.

"He said that because your face told him you'd pay extra for anything with a pearl in it," Junie said. "What do you need it for?"

"My girl wants a cleaner light in the room."

"Cleaner than what?"

"Than the green."

Junie shrugged. "Then call it cleaner if you like, but don't cook over it and don't trust a man selling lamp stock from a hatbox."

The second ferry hand snorted into his bowl. "Told you."

"If your girl wants to sew by it, that's one thing," Inez said as she took her spoon. "If she wants to boil potatoes, tell her not to be stupid for the sake of color."

"She's not stupid," the first man said, wounded on behalf of somebody absent.

"Then she'll listen."

That settled it better than any kindness would have. The man nodded. Silas watched Inez eat standing up, one hip against the counter, speaking the way she always did when the matter was use and there was no profit in prettying it. He had spent half his life trying to make language glitter. She made it hold weight.

Otis was at the back table with his school satchel open and a stub of blue chalk in his hand. He had a permanent smear of dock dirt ground into one knee of his trousers, and he had threaded three punched brass ferry tokens onto a string that hung from his vest button like a little saint's charm for bad travel. He was copying letters off a broken warning slat, tongue caught between his teeth in concentration.

`KEEP DRY / DON'T STACK HOT`

His version had become `KEEP TRY / DON'T STARK HAT`.

Silas set his bowl down beside the boy's elbow. "That'll save lives in a way no one expects."

Otis grinned, the gap from his lost front tooth now mostly replaced by a new one that had come in crooked enough to look argumentative. "I can fix the hat."

"Leave it," Junie said. "Might improve the city."

Otis looked from one adult to the next, weighing the chance of indulgence. "Can I ride out with you?"

"You've got lessons," Junie said.

"Miss Berrick's got the chest cough again. We only do sums when she can breathe."

"That sounds like a lie."

"It sounds like Tuesday," Silas said.

Junie cut him a look that admitted he might be right and resented it. She ladled more mash into a waiting bowl for a carter at the hatch. "You'll ride if you keep clear of the winch and don't repeat any dock language in front of God or your teacher."

"Which God?"

"Any of them feeling sensitive."

The room laughed. Otis looked pleased with himself and then, because he was a child and could not leave a live wire alone, pointed with the blue chalk toward the window where a new handbill had been pasted over an old Verdancy poster.

"That man out there says the proper name list costs one bit and keeps you from embarrassment."

Silas turned. Outside on the landing, a neat little clerk in a glazed collar stood under the ferry notice board with a sheaf of folded pamphlets under his arm. `APPROVED NAMES OF THE AUTUMN STRAINS`, the cover declared in damp blue ink. Below it, in smaller print, `For Visitors, Investors, and Serious Householders.` Someone had already written `and fools` across the bottom in charcoal.

The clerk was doing brisk enough trade among two women dressed too cleanly for Ferrymouth and one warehouse runner who had probably mistaken the pamphlet for authority. The sight put a sour little smile in Silas's mouth. There it was. The city had escaped one monopoly only to sprout a hundred petty cousins, each hoping to rent order by the page.

"You should buy one," Junie said. "Would save me hearing your guesses."

"If I spend a bit on proper names, I want a receipt fit to wipe with."

"You always did."

She slid a paper packet across the counter toward Inez without looking at her. Fried crusts for the ferry, wrapped in yesterday's scandal sheet. That was how the peace lived now in this kitchen: not with apologies reheated until they turned thin, but with food passed hand to hand, ledger numbers shared, cuttings traded, and an understanding that no one in the room had become innocent.

Silas finished his bowl and set it in the wash pan. On the shelf above it lay his working kit: no lacquered sign case, no shop chest with his name in gilt, only an oilcloth roll of brushes, two tins of paint, a bundle of rag, charcoal, hooks, and the little spirit level he had once used for window commissions in districts too rich to tolerate a crooked line. He took the roll down. The kitchen smelled of pepper, river damp, and the faint sweet rot of the cuttings in their jars. It smelled, to his surprise, like enough.

When they stepped out into the morning the boards underfoot were slick, but not with summer's greasy sweat. Autumn had sharpened the damp instead of thickening it.
